The field said *bread.* The field said a labourer's children eating in February. The field said rents paid and a country that need not, this generation, go to the wall for want of nitrogen in its dirt. He believed in the figure entirely and he served it, but he had not given his life to chemistry for the love of a burette. He had given it because he had been a curate's son in a parish where the poor ate turnips in a bad year and worse than turnips in a worse one, and he had worked out, with the cold of it never leaving him, that the difference between the full plate and the empty one ran through this — through the dull invisible element and the men who could conjure it back into the ground. He was not a sentimental man. He distrusted sentiment in science the way he distrusted damp in a reagent. But he knew what the work was *for*, and the white stuff in the jar was the nearest thing to an answer he had found in forty-six years, and when he breathed it in at half past eight in the morning, that was what lifted his heart. Not the stink. The mercy in the stink.

*

At noon he walked over to the rooms in Hanover Square, because the Society was sitting and Marsden had asked him to bring the figure himself, and the figure was the sort that did better in a man's mouth than in a circular.

The fog had not lifted; it never lifted in that week of November; it lay over the streets the colour of unbleached calico and turned the cabs to shapes and the horses to breath. Fenwick walked it with his collar up and his case under his arm and he did the other sum as he walked, the one that had nothing to do with the burette, because the burette's figure was only the half of it and the lesser half. The greater half was the country's appetite, and he carried it about with him the way a verger carries the dimensions of his church, knowing every figure to the foot. England's arable, twelve million acres and falling under the plough as the towns ate the edges of it. The yield she got and the yield she might get with the nitrogen restored — the difference, spread across twelve million acres, ran into a figure of quarters of wheat so large he had given up startling people with it and now simply said *we could feed ourselves again, gentlemen, which we cannot at present, and the proof is that we import our bread and the price of a loaf is set in Odessa and not in Mark Lane.* And the means of it, the white means, came in at the docks now in tonnages that had not existed five years before and would, the contractors swore, only swell — for the islands held a quantity that the trade circulars, with the flat confidence of men who had never had to count a thing they could not see the end of, described in a single adjective. *Inexhaustible.* He had read it that morning in the *Mark Lane Express*, in a notice of a fresh cargo landed at Liverpool: *the supply from the Chincha deposits being, for all present purposes, inexhaustible.* It was a comfortable word. It meant a man could build on the thing — could put down capital, lay out land, write a contract for ten years' delivery — and not lie awake. Fenwick was a chemist and not a merchant, and he had no particular reason to doubt the word, and he did not doubt it, and he carried it into Hanover Square with the rest of the arithmetic as a sound foundation carries a house.

The room was warm and full and smelled of wet wool and snuff and the coal fire, and they were glad to see him, which was a pleasure he had earned late and did not pretend to disdain. Marsden took his arm. There were perhaps thirty of them, the agricultural interest of half of England in one heated room — squires with ten thousand acres and a worried bailiff, the secretaries of two county societies, a Member who sat for a farming seat, a manufacturer of drill-ploughs scenting trade, and at the far end, by the fire, the men who were not agricultural at all and never pretended to be: the money. Two partners of a City house that financed the trade, and a broker, and a man Fenwick did not know in a very good coat who turned out to represent interests in Lima, which in that room meant interests in the islands themselves. They had come, these last, not for the chemistry. They had come because the chemistry set the price, and the price was the whole of their attention, and Fenwick's assay was, to them, a thing that moved a market by a shilling either way.

He gave them the figure. He gave it plainly, fifteen parts in the hundred, the methods stated, the two runs, the field-trial cited; and he watched it land, and it landed the way he had known it would, a low stir going round the warm room like wind in standing barley. The squires leaned to one another. The drill-plough man wrote in a little book. And at the fire the money did not stir at all, which was how he knew it had landed hardest there, for he had learned that the men who feel a figure most are the men who show it least.

"Then it is the richest thing in the world," said the Member, pleased, settling back. "Bar none."

"As a fertiliser, sir, in its class, by a wide margin," Fenwick said. "There is nothing near it. The Ichaboe stuff off Africa ran half this and is worked out already; the saltpetre is a different article and inferior for most crops. This is the best the earth has, and the earth made very little of it, in very few places, and we have found the richest of those places, and we are bringing it home." He could not keep, quite, the warmth out of it; he did not greatly try. "Gentlemen, I have spent twenty years telling rooms like this one that our land was dying under us and that I did not know the cure. I know the cure. It is in the jar. I find that I am glad to be alive to say so."

That got a murmur, warmer than the figure had. The squires liked him for it. He was, in that moment, the most popular man in the room, and he knew it, and a small clean vanity in him enjoyed it, and there was no harm in the vanity that he could see, for the thing he was glad of was true and good and would put food in mouths, and a man might be permitted to be glad of that.

*

It was over the wine, afterward, that the money came and found him, as the money always did, by the fire where the good coats gathered.

The man from the Lima interests was named Caulfield, and he was easy and well-fed and shrewd behind the ease, and he praised the assay handsomely and then, in the way of his kind, turned at once from what the thing *was* to what it would *do*, which was to say, to the figure that mattered to him, which was not fifteen but eleven — eleven pounds the ton, landed, and climbing, the price having moved up better than two pounds in three years on the strength of just such assays as Fenwick's and the bottomless want behind them.

"You have put another shilling on it this afternoon, Dr. Fenwick, with that fifteen," Caulfield said, smiling, holding his wine. "I do not say it to flatter you. I say it because it is so. The Society will print your figure, and the *Express* will copy it, and every land-agent from here to the border will read it to his farmers, and the demand will tighten another turn, and the price will follow it up. Eleven this month. I should not be astonished at thirteen inside two years, nor fourteen after. There is no top to it that I can see." He said it the way a man says a thing that gives him deep contentment to say. "A continent that has run out of the strength in its own soil, sir, and one country that holds the strength to sell — and holds it, mark me, by the grace of God in a quantity that will not run out in our time nor our sons'. I have seen the estimates of the deposit. They beggar belief. There is more of it on those three rocks than the whole of Europe could spread in fifty years."

"So I am told," Fenwick said. "Inexhaustible was the word I read this morning."

"Inexhaustible," Caulfield agreed, savouring it. "It is the rarest position in all of trade, Doctor — to sell a necessity that cannot be exhausted, to a market that cannot be satisfied. There has been nothing like it. The spice trade was a trifle to it. We are watching, you and I, the making of fortunes that will outlast the men who make them, and the beauty of it" — he was warming now, the wine and the figure in him together — "the beauty of it is that it is *clean*. It is not the slave-grown sugar your father's generation had to swallow its conscience over. It is honest commerce in an honest article that does nothing in this world but make the wheat grow and the poor man's bread cheaper. Where is the harm in it? I ask you as a man of science. Where is the single grain of harm?"

And Fenwick, who was a careful man and an honest one within the borders of what he had ever been given to see, considered the question seriously, as it was seriously meant, and found that he could not locate the harm. He turned it over with the same scruple he gave a doubtful reading. The article was wholesome. It fed the hungry. It cost no man his liberty that Fenwick knew of; it was dug, he supposed vaguely, by labourers in the usual way that distant things were dug, by men with shovels in the employ of the company, in the manner of a mine or a quarry, paid their wage and going home at the end of the day to wherever such men went — he had never thought about it and had no occasion to, the labour being, like the fourteen thousand miles of sea and the bones of the dead birds that had made the stuff over the slow uncounted centuries, simply one of the silent terms of the equation, present in the result and invisible in the working, the way the cost of the candle is present in the price of the book and no reader thinks of the chandler. He went looking, at Caulfield's prompting, for the harm, and his honest eye lit on nothing, because his honest eye had never been shown the place where the harm was kept, and the figure that named it had never come up the river to Cox's Quay in any jar.

"I confess I can find none," he said. "It feeds people. I have given my life to less."

"Just so," said Caulfield, satisfied, and touched his glass to Fenwick's, and the small clear ring of the two glasses meeting was the only sound in the warm room that meant anything at all, and neither man heard anything in it but agreement.

They talked a while longer. The fire was good and the wine was good and the figure sat between them like a thing they had jointly made, and Fenwick was happy in a way he was rarely happy, among men who valued his work and a discovery that would do nothing but good, and the future, when he glanced at it, was a clean broad sunlit thing — England fed, the land green again, the price climbing not as a cruelty but as the simple honest signal of a world that wanted what he had helped to give it, and somewhere out past the fog and the Horn three white mountains standing inexhaustible in a hard clean light, pouring their slow accumulated mercy into the holds of ships and the holds of ships pouring it into the dead fields of home. He did not see the men on the mountains. There were no men in the picture he saw; the picture had no room for them and no need of them; they were below the level at which his mind, looking from London at a figure on a burette, was built to see. He saw only the white, and the wheat, and the bread, and it was, to him, who meant nothing but well and had spent his life on the side of the full plate against the empty one, a vision very near to beautiful — and he carried it home through the calico fog at five o'clock with his case under his arm and his heart still lifted, the smell of the morning's assay faint and ineradicable on his fingers, where it would stay through the washing, the one part of the islands that ever reached him, riding home on the hands that had weighed their worth and found it, in parts per hundred, very high.

The jar he had left on the bench, stoppered again, fifteen grains the lighter. In the morning he would write the figure up fair for the Society, and the Society would print it, and the *Express* would copy it, and a land-agent in three counties would read it aloud to a room of farmers, and the want would tighten its turn, and the price would climb its shilling, and on a white cliff fourteen thousand miles away that no man in Hanover Square had ever stood upon, the line would come up a barrow faster to meet it, and the breath of the broken face would go up a little oftener into the eyes of men whose number Dr. Josiah Fenwick, who meant nothing in the world but good, would have been genuinely astonished, and then sincerely grieved, and then, being a busy man with the nation's hunger to feed, made to forget by the press of better news, to learn.


## MANIFEST I — The Discovery

### Chapter 6 · Mei Loong-fa
*Chincha North, off Pisco · 1852 · guano ≈ £11/ton · Chincha working face ≈ 170 ft*

The mountain was ten feet lower than the morning he had landed, and he was the only man on the island who knew it.

He knew it the way he knew the number of sheds and the names on the stick, by keeping it. Three years before, standing in the grey light with his eyes already running, he had read the high face of the northern island against the sky and made it a hundred and eighty feet, sheer up from the surf, and he had folded that number away with the rest. Now, in the white forenoon of his fourth year, he stood at the head of the chute with his good eye on the painted weather-line the old hands called the snow-mark — the faint horizontal stain, high on the cliff above this season's cut, where the cliff changed colour from the bone-white of the new face to the weathered cream of the part the barrows had not yet reached down to — and he saw that the working face had come down past where it had stood when A-Yün walked off it, and down again past where it had stood the season after, and that the difference between this year's face and the snow-mark above it was the height of three men, near enough, and growing. He did not say it. There was no one to say it to who would not either laugh or report it. He filed it with the rest, ten feet a year, give or take, and behind the filing the cold part of him that the broker had smelled in the wineshop did the sum it always did, without being asked, *a hundred and seventy feet, ten a year, and the lower islands not yet opened,* and gave him a figure, and the figure was a number of years, and the number was not large, and he put it away unspoken with all the others he carried and could tell no one.

He was twenty-two. The left eye had gone the way he had known on the third day it would go — not to blindness all at once but by a clouding, a grey film come down over the sight of it across the first months until the white mountain on that side was a soft doubled smear and a man's face at three paces was a pale shape with no eyes in it — and he had learned to turn his head a little to bring the right eye to bear, a habit that the new men read, wrongly, as suspicion, and that the overseers had stopped reading at all, because Mei turning his head to look at a tally was as much a part of the scale-house as the beam. He had kept the right eye. He stood crooked at the face, as the old hands stood, and the crook had cost him an inch of height and saved him a sight, and he counted it a fair trade, the only fair trade the island had ever offered him, and he had made it himself, without asking, which was the only kind that came fair here.

He was not at the face today. He had not been at the face, except to read it, for two years. The overseer's note in the company book had done its slow work: the man who could carry a true count in his head over a long afternoon, who could read a hand of figures in two scripts and was learning a third off the guards and a fourth off the Cantonese gang in the far shed, had been taken off the barrow and set to the tally — to standing at the chute-head with the slate, marking the barrows down as they tipped, holding the gang's true number against the overseer's and the overseer's against the ship's, so that the company's books would balance and no boat's master could swear he had been shorted a ton he had in fact received. It was clean work, by the island's reckoning. It did not scald the lungs. It kept a man out of the worst of the dust and let him eat the half-ration of the indispensable instead of the quarter-ration of the spent. And it had made him, in three years, the clerk of the place — the man who knew, better than the heavy Englishman who paid him, exactly how much of the mountain had gone down to the boats and exactly how many of the men who had carried it down were left to carry the rest.

The barrows came up the line and tipped, and Mei marked them, and below him on the slate the morning's tally grew, and the white plumes jumped off the cliff and slid down the canvas tongue to the lighters, and over the seaward face the birds went out and came back in their pouring column that did not end, eighteen million of them, more, a darkness in the bright air that he no longer looked up at the way he had in the first year. In the first year he had looked up at them because they were the one free thing. Now he marked the barrows and kept the count, and the birds were only the noise behind the work, the high far roar of the number too large to be a sound, and he had stopped envying them, because a man who envies what is free has not yet finished understanding that he is not.

*

He saw her at the water-cask at noon, and his hand on the slate stopped, and he did not let it stop for more than the space of one barrow, because a man whose hand stopped at the chute-head was a man with something in his face, and Mei had spent three years training his face to hold nothing.

It was the third time. The first had been a week before, a glimpse only, a slight figure among the carriers who brought the rice and the water up from the stores to the sheds — there were a handful of women on the island, very few, kept apart and put to the carrying and the cooking and the things the company did not write in the books, and Mei had marked the figure the way he marked everything, filed it, *a girl, small, quick, not new, has the cough already,* and gone on with his count, and only after, lying on the boards, had the shape of her come back to him and sat in his chest and would not be filed. The second time he had been nearer, near enough to see the set of the shoulders and the way she carried the water-jar braced on her hip with the weight thrown just so, the way their mother had carried it up the lane from the harbour well, the exact way, a thing the body learns in one house and keeps for life, and he had stood with the slate in his hands and felt the island tip under him, and had said nothing, because he was not sure, because he could not be sure of a thing he wanted this much, because a man who let himself be sure of a thing he wanted this much on this rock was a man the rock would teach.

The third time she set the jar down at the cask and straightened and turned, and across forty feet of glaring white, out of his one good eye, he saw her face, and it was A-Lian's face, fourteen years old now and aged past it by the rock the way the rock aged everyone, thinner, harder, the cheeks fallen in and the eyes gone to the watchfulness that the island ground into a face within a year — but it was her face, his sister's face, the youngest of them, the one who at eleven had carried water up the lane and laughed at him for counting the fish into the basket twice to be sure, the one he had last seen on the deck at Macao in the press of bodies, lifted up by a man's two hands over the heads of the men and passed forward toward the women's hold, screaming his name once and then not screaming it, going quiet the way the island would later teach them both to go quiet, and he had not been able to reach her, had been held in the press of the men, had called her name into the noise and the name had gone nowhere, and the ship had taken them both across the water in different holds and he had not known, in three years, in a thousand and more days of the stick, whether she was on this rock or another rock or under the white of one or drowned off the side of a hold somewhere past the curve of the world, and he had not let himself ask, because to ask was to learn, and there was only the one thing he could have learned that he could bear.

She saw him. He watched her see him — watched the same tipping go through her that had gone through him, watched her face do the thing his had not let his do, the jolt and then the iron coming down over it fast, faster than his, she had always been faster — and then she bent and took up the jar again and braced it on her hip and went on up toward the sheds, not looking at him, not faltering, a girl carrying water, because she too had learned what a face must hold on this rock, and that a brother and a sister who fell on each other's necks at the water-cask in front of the guards were a brother and a sister who had just told the company exactly where its leverage on each of them lay.

He marked the next barrow. His hand did not shake. He had not let it shake at the chute-head the day he counted A-Yün out of the living, and he did not let it shake now, and the not-shaking was the whole of him, was the thing the island had made him, a man who could hold his own sister forty feet off after three years of not knowing and mark the barrow and keep the count, because the count was the thing that kept them both, and a man who broke the count to weep had not understood that the weeping was a thing the company would weigh and bill.

*

They found the lee of rock that evening, the way the few who had a thing to say on that island found a place to say it — not by arrangement, which the guards watched for, but by the slow drift of two people each going where the other was, in the hour after the chutes stopped, when the light went off the sea and the guards drew in toward the stores and a margin of the island, narrow and grey, belonged for a little while to the people on it. There was a fold in the seaward cliff below the women's shed, a place where two slabs leaned and made a roofless room with a floor of old guano gone hard, out of the wind and out of the line of the guard-post, and A-Lian was in it when he came, sitting with her back to the rock and her knees drawn up, and he sat down across from her with the width of the little space between them, because even here, even now, they did not touch first; they were their mother's children and their father's, and the body kept its house-manners where the heart could not.

"I made you dead," he said. It was the first thing. It came out of him before the greeting, before the thousand things, the plain hard fact at the bottom of the three years. "On the ship, when I could not reach you, I made you dead in my head, so that I could bear the not knowing. I have carried you dead for three years."

"I made you dead too," she said. Her voice was lower than he remembered and there was a catch in it that was not feeling; it was the cough, sitting in the chest, waiting. "It is easier. A dead brother does not need you to find a way to him across an island full of guards. A dead brother you can grieve in the dark and then go to sleep." She looked at him, the watchful island look, and under it, far under, the old thing surfaced for a moment, the sister, the laugh she did not laugh. "You have one eye now."

"I have one good eye."

"You count with one eye?"

"I count with no eyes. I count in the dark." He had not meant it to be the old game, the thing they had done as children, she teasing and he answering flat and exact until she gave it up laughing, but it came out as the old game, and he watched it land in her, watched her take the small blow of it, the brother she had buried answering in the voice she had buried, and her face did a thing then that the island had not yet been able to stop, a small breaking at the mouth, quickly held — and she looked away at the grey sea and breathed carefully, around the thing in her chest, and did not let it become the other thing, and he saw that she had learned the same lessons he had learned, in a worse school, and that they had each survived by becoming a person the other almost did not know.

She told him, in the spare flat way they had both come to, where she had been. Not all of it. He did not ask for all of it. There were things a brother did not ask a sister on this rock and things a sister did not say, and the not-saying was its own account, kept between them, that neither would ever read aloud. She had been landed on this island in the first season, the same as he, and put with the women, and the women's work was the carrying and the cooking and the worse, and she had survived the worse the way a person survives it, by going somewhere else inside while the body stayed, and by being useful in the daylight hours so that the daylight hours were the most of her life — she cooked, it turned out, well enough that the overseer's own mess wanted her at it, and a woman wanted in a man's mess was a woman a little harder to spend, the same trade Mei had made with the count, the same coin, and they looked at each other across the grey little room with the understanding of two people who had each bought their lives in the same currency and knew exactly what it had cost and would never tell another soul the price.

"How long is yours," he asked her. The paper. The years. The old question, the first question, the one A-Yün had answered in the dark of the shed three years gone.

She laughed, the not-laugh. "I did not get a paper. They do not give the women a paper. A paper is for a thing that is contracted. I am a thing that was taken." She said it flat, without the heat he could not have stood, and he understood that she had carried that too, the difference, that she had stood on the deck and watched the men be handed their lying papers with the lying eight on them and had understood, at eleven, that she was a thing below even the men below even the cargo, a thing the books did not trouble to write down, and that this too was a count she kept, and that her count, like his, had no end-figure on it that anyone would honour.

He took the stick out of his shirt then. He had not planned to. He had carried it three years against the folded paper with the eight on it, the length of hard pale wood smoothed by the hands of dead men, and it had grown its notches in the dark of the shed, one and one and one, the count the company's books were built to lose, and he held it out across the little space so that she could see it in the last grey light, the rows of small square cuts running down it, more than a hand of them, more than two.

"What is it," she said.

"The dead. The ones I knew the name of. I cut one when one goes that I knew." He turned it so the notches caught what light there was. "A-Yün is the first. A tile-maker, from the village a half-day west of ours, the one that says water with the tongue forward. He had a son of eleven. He set his bar down so it would not fall and trouble anyone, and he walked off the face into the sea, and I counted him out of the living at the chute-head with my own hand because that is the thing I am now, and then that night I cut him into this so that one of us would have counted him as a man and not as one short to close up." He had never said it aloud. He had not known he would say it to her, of all the people in the world, the youngest, the one he had failed to reach over the heads of the men. "I tell myself it is a thing I am carrying home. So that when I stand at last in the grey house on the lane and the kitchen-god has his three sticks burning and mother asks me what the country was like, I will not have to say it was only white, it was only the smell. I will be able to set this on the stone table and say a number, and a name, and another name."

A-Lian looked at the stick a long time. She did not reach for it. When she spoke her voice had gone very low, under the cough, under everything.

"There is no grey house, brother." She said it the way the island had taught them both to say the thing that could not be borne, flat, fast, without a tremor, so that it might pass through and be done. "I have not had a letter either. But a man came last year, off a ship, a Fujian man, a steward, and I cooked for the officers' mess and I heard him talking in the galley, and he was from Amoy, from the wharf-end, near the fish market, and I asked him. I did not say who I was. I asked him as a girl asks for news of a place. The rice factor's quarter, I said, the lane that runs to the harbour well." She stopped. The sea worked grey at the foot of the white cliff below them. "He said the lane was sold. A debt. Two years gone or three. He did not know the family. There were too many families sold for debt in those years, he said, after the factors failed, for a man to know one lane from another." She looked up at him, and the iron was on her face, and behind the iron was the thing she would not let through. "So I do not count my eight years either. I count nothing toward home. There is no home to count toward. I made you dead to bear it. I made the house dead the same way. It is easier."

Then she did a thing that answered the stick. She reached inside her jacket, to the breast, the same place his own hand went, and brought out a small flat bundle of cloth folded so often the folds had worn to a grey furred softness, and held it on her two palms without opening it, the last grey light not enough now to have shown what was in it even had she opened it, which she did not. "Mother's," she said. "It is in here. The comb she wore. She put it in my hair the morning the broker took me, because I was crying, and she said a girl with a comb like that was a girl from somewhere, and no one could make her be from nowhere. She was wrong. They made me be from nowhere by the second week. But I kept it. It is the one thing of the lane that came out — the one grain of the grey house that the ship did not take and the rock did not take and the men in the mess do not know I have." She did not pass it to him; he did not put out his hand for it; they were their mother's children, and a thing kept against the breast was a thing the keeper unwrapped, or did not, and the other did not ask. She folded her hands shut over the bundle and put it away again where it had been. "You keep the dead in the wood," she said. "I keep the house in the horn. Between us we have carried the whole of it across the world, and there is nowhere to set it down."

Mei sat with the stick in his hand and the news going into him the way the air went into him at the face, as a thing and not a word, a burning that closed the throat. He had known. Some cold clerk under the clerk had always known, had run the figure of the debt and the years and the failed factor and got this answer and put it away unread, because there was only the one thing he could have learned that he could bear and this was the other thing. He had built the whole of his survival on the grey house. The eight years were a column with the grey house at the bottom of it; the stick was a thing he was carrying home; the keeping, the counting, the not-weeping, the held face, the bought life — all of it was scaffolding around the one room with his mother in it burning three sticks to the kitchen-god on the right days, and his sister had just told him, flat, in the grey light, that the room was empty, that it had been empty while he counted toward it, that he had carried a coal through his sleeve for three years to keep a fire that was already out.

He did not weep. He had decided about weeping a long time before. But something in him that had been holding a shape let the shape go, quietly, the way A-Yün had set the bar down, and he understood, sitting there, that the want had not died with the house. It had only lost its object. He still wanted, with the whole banked force of three years, and there was nowhere left for the wanting to go but back into him, into the count, into the rock — and a cold thing he did not like the taste of said, far down, *then the only home left is to not be the one the book walks down. To hold the pen. To own the thing instead of be it.* He put the thought away unspoken with all the others. It would keep. It would render its account at a time of its own choosing.

"Then we carry it for each other," he said at last. "If there is no house, we are the house. You are the lane and I am the well." It was not a thing their father would have said; their father had not been a man of words. But it was true in the way the stick was true, and A-Lian took it, and her hand came across the little grey space at last and lay on his sleeve over the place where the stick and the paper sat against his chest, light, the courtesy of two people who did not touch first; and his own hand, when he raised it, came to rest the same way against her breast over the worn cloth and the horn inside it, so that for a moment each of them held the place where the other kept the unkeepable thing. They sat in the roofless room out of the wind with the cough sitting in her chest and the sea working white below and the birds pouring back to the cliff in the dark, and they were, for the length of one held hour, somewhere real.

*

He learned about the Englishman's son the way he learned everything, by keeping the count.

There was a young man new to the island that season, off a ship in the early year, a Cornishman, eighteen or nineteen, sent out by the agent whose chair the hard man Penhale had taken — sent out, the guards said, by Penhale himself, who was his father, to learn the trade from the bottom the way the father had learned it, which on this rock meant the young man was put to the loading and the lighters and given no soft place, because the father, they said, did not believe in soft places. Mei marked him the first week, the way he marked all the new entries: *young, the father's frame on him but the father's face not finished, watches the men at the face the way the others do not watch them, as men.* That last was the thing Mei filed and turned over. The English on the island did not look at the diggers. They looked at the work, at the barrows, at the tally; a digger was a unit of barrows, a thing that made the weight or did not. This one looked at the faces. A man who looked at the faces was either a danger or a door, and Mei did not yet know which, and so he watched.

He saw the young man find A-Lian the same way he had seen A-Lian find him, by the slow drift in the grey hour — saw it across three weeks, the boy contriving to be where the water-carriers passed, the words exchanged that Mei could not hear and did not need to, the way the boy's hard-set Cornish face came undone a degree when she answered him, the way A-Lian — who had survived the worse by going somewhere else inside, who had no reason on this earth to spend a soft thing on any Englishman — looked at the boy a beat too long when his back was turned. Mei watched it with a dread that sat under his ribs like the dust, because he knew exactly what it was and exactly what it cost on this rock — a thing across the lines the island drew hardest, the lines between the company and the cargo, a thing that could get a woman beaten and a young Englishman shipped home in disgrace and could get them both, if the wrong guard saw it the wrong way, much worse than that — and because he knew that he could not stop it, that A-Lian had not been able to be stopped from anything since she was four, and that he had no more standing to forbid her this than he had had to reach her across the heads of the men on the deck at Macao.

And under the dread, unwelcome, a second thing, that he liked even less than the cold clerk's thought about the pen. The boy was the agent's son, a thing the guards would not touch — and a woman the boy watched was a woman a little harder to spend. Mei sat with it and hated it and could not make it untrue: his sister might buy a further measure of her life with this English boy's undone face, and the measure might be the difference between her living and her going under the white, and the cold part of him that kept all the counts ran the figure and did not flinch. He put it away with the rest, unspoken, because saying it would have made him a man who weighed his own sister's last refuge as cargo, and he had sworn, on the third day, watching A-Yün go into the sea, that whatever the island made of him it would not make him that.

It was already making him that. He knew it. He filed it.

*

The cough took her in the lee of the rock the next time they met, and it took her the way the island took everything, by degrees, so that you did not see the day it began.

She was telling him a thing about the officers' mess, a small bitter funny thing, the kind she had always been able to make, a story about an English captain who would not eat the fish because he said it tasted of the birds and ate the rice that tasted twice as much of them and never knew — and she was almost the old A-Lian telling it, faster and funnier than Mei had ever been, the laugh nearly out of her — when the laugh turned in her chest and became a cough, and the cough did not stop. It went on. It bent her over her knees in the grey little room and shook her and brought up, when at last it eased, a thing she spat to the side onto the hard old guano floor, and Mei looked at it in the last light, because he looked at everything, and it was streaked.

She wiped her mouth with the back of her hand and straightened and saw him looking at the floor, and she put her foot over the place, fast, the way a person hides a thing, and looked at him with the iron full down.

"It is the dust," she said. "Everyone has it. The cooking-smoke and the dust. It is nothing."

"It is the dust," Mei agreed. He did not say the rest. He had counted enough men on the stick to know the look of the thing that began as the dust and was nothing and ended as a notch — had cut A-Yün, and the boy with the bad lungs from the second shed, and the old Hakka man who had coughed for a year and a half and then one morning had not got up — and he knew that the streak on the floor was the first entry in an account that had a figure at the bottom of it the way the company's mountain had a figure at the bottom of it, and that the figure was not large, and that he could no more say it to her than he could say the height of the snow-mark to the overseer. He looked at his sister, fourteen, who had dug the dust into her chest at eleven, who had survived the ship and the worse and the three years and had found him again across an island of guards, who had just told him with her foot over the streak that it was nothing — and he understood that he had been wrong, sitting in this room a few nights gone, when he had thought the want had lost its object with the grey house. It had a new object now. It had her.

He took the stick out again, but not to show her a name. He turned it in his hand in the dark and found the rows of notches with his fingertip, A-Yün at the top and all the others under him, the count he had told himself was a thing he was carrying home, and he understood now that there was no home to carry it to, that it would never stand on his mother's stone table, that the stick was not a thing going anywhere — it was only the dead, kept, because someone must keep them, in a country whose every beam and book was built to weigh them and forget them. And he understood, his thumb on the lowest notch, on the blank wood below it where the next one would go, that he would cut them all. Every one he knew the name of. For as long as the island spent him. And that the figure at the bottom of the account that began tonight with the streak on the floor — the one he would not say, the one she had put her foot over — was a notch he had already, in the cold clerk's books under his ribs, begun to leave room for, low on the stick, in the blank pale wood, where his fingertip rested and would not move.

He put the stick away against the folded paper with the eight on it. He walked his sister back as far as he dared toward the women's shed, the width of the grey space between them, not touching, their mother's children. Then he went to the men's shed and lay down on the boards with his one good eye open in the dark and listened to the cough start up, small, from the direction of the women's shed, under the surf and the endless roar of the birds, and he did not let himself feel the thing he was feeling, which was that the island had found, after three years of taking his eye and his sleep and the grey house and the man from the village a half-day west, the one thing left in the world it could take from him that would finish the work, and that it had all the time it needed, ten feet a year, give or take, to take it.


## MANIFEST I — The Discovery

### Chapter 7 · Thomas Quick
*Chincha North, off Pisco · 1853 · guano ≈ £11–12/ton, climbing · Chincha working face ≈ 165 ft*

The new quota came out from Pisco on the same boat that brought the rice, which Quick took for a kind of honesty. The company sent you what it wanted of you and what it would pay you with, in one bottom, and you could read the whole of its mind in the lading if you had the eye for it. He had the eye. He stood on the little timber jetty in the dawn with the manifest open against the wind and ran his thumb down it the way another man would run his thumb down a wife's letter, and he found the figure four lines from the bottom, between the casks of salt fish and the kegs of nails, and he read it twice to be sure the clerk in Pisco had not slipped a digit, because clerks slipped digits, and a slipped digit was a thing the men paid for at this end and never the clerk.

Five hundredweight a man, it said. Per diem. Up from four.

He shut the book on his finger and looked up at the working face, where it stood gone grey-gold in the first light, a hundred and sixty-odd feet of it now above the cut, and he did the arithmetic the company had not troubled to do for him, the way he had been doing the company's uncounted arithmetic for nine years. Four hundredweight to five was a quarter more again off every back on the island, every day, with the same backs, the same rice, the same hours of daylight God allowed off this latitude in winter. The company in Pisco — and the house in Lima above it, and the house in London above that, none of whom had ever stood downwind of the cut with their eyes running — had looked at a price climbing past eleven pound the ton toward twelve, and a thousand ships rotting at anchor for want of cargo, and had reasoned, soundly, that if the stuff was worth more then more of it should come down the cliff, and had written *five* where last season they wrote *four*, and had put it on the boat with the rice. There was no cruelty in it. Quick wished to be plain with himself on this point, as he was plain with himself on most, alone, at dawn, before the day made a liar of every man on the rock. There was no cruelty in the figure. The figure did not know the men existed. The figure was only the price, turned around and pointed at their backs.

What it would cost he also knew, to the head, near enough, because he kept the rolls and the rolls did not flatter. He could have told Pisco what five hundredweight bought. He did not tell Pisco things Pisco had not asked. That was the whole art of remaining the man who weighed and not the man who was weighed: you answered the manifest and you did not improve upon it.

He folded the book under his arm and went up to the scale-house to make the day balance.

*

The scale-house was the one square of order on the island and Quick kept it like a chapel, though he would not have used the word; he had no chapel in him and was easy about the lack. It was a long shed of ship's timber, open down the seaward side to take the light, with the great beam slung from the king-post and the two pans hanging, the iron weights ranked on their shelf small to large like a choir of graduated children, and the ledger on its sloped desk where the barrows were entered as they came and the men were entered as numbers and, in a separate column kept in a separate ink, exited. He had a Liverpool clerk's hand and a Liverpool clerk's love of a column that came out even, and the islands had given him, in exchange for everything else, a place where columns came out even or a man answered for it, and he had decided long ago that this was not the worst bargain a poor man had ever made with the world.

The tally-men were already at the desk when he came in. Two of them were Peruvian, jornaleros up from the coast who could be trusted with a number and a cudgel and not much else, and the third was the Chinaman, the one the company's books had as a contract number and Quick had, privately, by a name he had heard the others use across the barracks and had filed because Quick filed everything — Mei. The clean one. Not clean in his person, no man on the rock was that, but clean in his counting. Quick had pulled him off the face two seasons gone for the plain reason that the man could carry a tally in his head across a whole gang and lose nothing, and a tally-man who lost nothing saved the company more in a month than his rice cost it in a year, and Quick had reckoned it a sound piece of management and had been proved right and took a flat satisfaction in it still. He had also, though he did not dwell on this, taken the man off the face the week the man's eye began to go — the left one, clouding from the bottom like milk poured into water — and Quick had told himself it was for the counting and not the eye, because the counting was a reason the company would understand and the eye was not, and a man did the right thing for the reason the company understood or he did not last to do it twice.

"New figure," Quick said, and laid the manifest open on the desk and put his finger on the line.

Mei looked at it. He could not read the English — Quick was nearly sure of that, though the man had a way of seeming to read everything that put a small cold draught on the back of Quick's neck if he watched it too long — but he could read a number, and he could read Quick's finger, and he could read, Quick supposed, the whole machine off the single digit faster than the jornaleros would read it off an hour's shouting. The Chinaman's face did not move. That was the thing about him that Quick had stopped trying to get past. A man who had been on the rock four years and still had a face that did not move had learned to spend nothing he did not have to, and Quick, who spent nothing either, recognised the economy and disliked recognising it.

"Five," Mei said.

"Five."

"The men cannot."

"The men will be asked to," Quick said, "and the ones that can't will be docked the difference in their rice, by the contract, and you'll keep the count straight so I know which is which, and that's the whole of it." He heard his own voice come out level and reasonable and he was glad of it; a man kept the level voice the way he kept the ledger, because the day a manager began shouting at the work was the day the work knew he had lost the figure, and they would smell it the way they smelled weather. "You'll go down the line with me after the weigh and you'll tell me, man by man, who's short and by how much. Not who's tired. Who's short. I don't keep a column for tired."

Something happened in the Chinaman's face then, or did not happen — a thing held so still it amounted to an expression by the violence of its stillness — and Quick, watching it, had the sensation he sometimes had with this one man and never with another on the island, the sensation of being read back. Of his own figure being run down by a thumb. He did not care for it. He squared the ledger to the edge of the desk and broke the look.

"After the weigh," he said.

*

They weighed the night's cut first, because the cut paid and the men did not, and a manager who did not know in his bones which was which had no business on the buying side of anything. The barrows came down the line from the high face in the grey light, the men leaning into them with their cloths bound over their mouths and noses against the dust, and at the scale-house each barrow was tipped into the pan and the iron run up the beam and the weight called and entered and the guano carried on to the chutes and tipped over the cliff in its white sliding plume to the lighters working the swell below, and the whole of it had a rhythm Quick had learned to hear as music if he was honest, the scrape and the call and the slide, the scrape and the call and the slide, the sound of a price being turned into a cargo one barrow at a time. He stood at the beam and watched the weights and let the rhythm carry, and for an hour the island was only what it claimed to be on the manifest, a quantity coming down a cliff, and a man could stand inside that hour and be nothing but the eye that watched the iron, and it was the nearest thing to peace the rock afforded and Quick took it where he found it.

Then the rice was weighed out against the same beam, into the same pans, with the same iron, and that was the part the company in its wisdom had arranged and that Quick had never once heard a soul above him remark upon — that the cargo and the ration went over one scale, that the thing the men dug and the thing that kept them digging were measured by the same weights on the same morning, so that a man might watch his own day's food balanced against a five-pound iron not an hour after he had watched two hundredweight of the white stuff balanced against the same. Quick had thought about this more than he let on. He had decided it was efficient and let it lie. One beam was cheaper than two. The company did not build a second scale-house to spare a digger the sight of his supper weighed on the cargo's iron, and the company was right not to, because the sparing would have cost money and changed nothing, the man would eat the same weight of rice whether it was weighed apart or not, and Quick had a horror of the kind of sentiment that cost money and changed nothing. That was the worst kind. It made the sentimental man feel clean and left the digger exactly as hungry and the books a column light.

He weighed the rice short. Not by much — within the contract, always within the contract, a manager who went outside the contract was a thief and Quick was not a thief — but the contract gave the company a hand's breadth of grace on the ration and the company expected its managers to take the grace, and Quick took it, and entered the true weight in the ledger and the contract weight in the issue book and the difference belonged to the company and was, in the run of a season, not nothing. He did it with the same hand he weighed the cargo. He did not feel it as two acts. The white dust was on the pans from the cargo when the rice went in, a film of it, the same film that was on his hands and on the desk and on the page of the ledger where his thumb had smudged the morning's column grey, and the rice took up a little of it the way everything on the island took up a little of it, and the men ate it with their food and breathed it with their air and Quick weighed it into them and out of them and entered both in the proper columns, and the columns came out even, and that was the day, and the day was the work, and the work was not cruel. He would have known if it were cruel. He had a horror of cruelty as he had a horror of sentiment, and for the same reason: both were unprofessional. A managed thing was neither cruel nor kind. It was only managed.

*

The man who could not make the figure was called Iquana on the barracks and was a number, 114, in the book.

Quick had him by the second day. The roll told him before the man did — 114 had been a four-hundredweight man, steady, no trouble, one of the ones who came up from the coast as a convict rather than over the water in chains, which made him, in Quick's grading, the better class of labour, a man with a sentence to work off and a country to go back to when he had worked it. But four hundredweight steady was not five, and the rice-dock for the short measure took the strength that would have made up the difference, the way it always did, the way the figure in Pisco could not see that it did, and by the third evening 114 was a three-hundredweight man falling, and Quick entered him short three days running and on the fourth went down the line to see him, because a manager saw a falling man before the man fell, the way a master saw a sprung plank before the ship opened, and acted while acting was still cheap.

He took Mei with him to interpret, because 114 had the coast Spanish and a little of the trade-pidgin English and none of the company's, and because Mei could put a thing to the men in a way that did not start a riot, which was a skill Quick valued and had come, over the seasons, to lean on more than he would have set down in any report. They went up the line in the wind with the dust coming off the cut and stinging, and they found 114 sat against the barrow he could no longer fill, not resting — a resting man got up when the manager came — but past the place where a man got up, looking out at the water the way the falling ones did, the way Quick had seen too many of them look out at the water in nine years to mistake it for anything but what it was. The sea off the cliff was very blue that evening and the column of the birds was pouring back to the rookery off the seaward shoal, two million of them or some number Quick did not keep, a darkness in the air that the men sat under as if it were the only roof, and 114 was looking past the barrow and past the cliff-edge at the blue and the birds, and his face was the face of a man doing a sum, and Quick knew the sum, and put himself, with his body, between 114 and the edge, the way a manager learned to without deciding to.

"Tell him," Quick said to Mei, "that he's short three days and he's docked, and that the dock comes off when he makes the figure, and that there's a man takes him off the face and onto the chutes if he asks for it, which is lighter, and which I'll grant, but he asks for it standing up and at the weigh and not sitting against a barrow looking at the sea. Tell him the sea's not on offer."

He had meant the last as nothing — as the flat managing thing he said, the level voice, the figure pointed at a back. But it came out of him with a heat he had not put there, and he heard it, and disliked hearing it, because heat was a kind of sentiment and sentiment cost money and changed nothing, and he had just spent some, on a convict numbered 114 who would or would not make five hundredweight regardless of what Quick's voice did.

Mei spoke to the man. Quick watched the Chinaman's face while he did it and saw, for once, the stillness break — not much, a thing about the mouth, the man Mei saying to the man 114 something longer than Quick had asked for, in the coast Spanish, low, and 114 turning his head from the sea to look at Mei, and a thing passing between the two of them that Quick was outside of entirely, the way he was outside of most things that passed between the men, and that he had taught himself to be outside of, because the day a manager got inside what passed between the men was the day he could not weigh their rice short with the cargo's iron and sleep, and a manager who could not do that was no good to himself or the company or, Quick would have argued if anyone had asked him, which no one ever had, to the men, who needed the rice issued and the cut weighed and the columns balanced by a man who did not lie awake, far more than they needed a man who lay awake and could not, in the morning, manage.

"He will stand at the weigh," Mei said.

"Tomorrow?"

"Tomorrow."

"And he'll ask for the chutes?"

Mei looked at 114, who had got himself up off the barrow now and stood, swaying a little, a three-hundredweight man on his feet, which was all Quick had come up the line to buy and Quick had bought it. "He will ask," Mei said, and did not say *and it will not matter,* and Quick heard the not-saying as clearly as if the man had shouted it, the count the Chinaman kept and would not speak, and Quick chose, as he chose every day, a hundred times a day, not to add up the silence, because the silence was not in any column he was answerable for.

They went back down to the scale-house. Behind them the column of birds went on pouring back to the cliffs in the falling light, and 114 took up the barrow, and the rhythm started again, scrape and call and slide, and the day balanced, and Quick entered it.

*

He almost let it in that night.

It came at him the way it did perhaps twice a season, late, in the scale-house, with the lamp lit and the ledger open and the iron weights ranked on their shelf and the wind working at the open seaward side and bringing the smell up off the cut, ammonia and old fish and the sweetish mineral underneath of a thing that had been piling up longer than there had been an England. He was squaring the day's columns and they came out even, they always came out even, he was good, and the goodness was the whole of what he had, and he sat with his even columns in the lamplight and the thing came at him from the side the way the falling men looked at the sea.

It was only this: that he had come up the same way. That there had been a Liverpool dock and a hungry boy on it and a man with a book who had looked at the boy and seen a quantity, a back, a thing that could be put to a figure and shipped, and that the only difference between Thomas Quick at the scale-house beam and the number called 114 against the barrow was the book — that Quick had got his hands on a book and a beam and a place where the columns came out even, and 114 had not, and the getting had not been virtue, Quick was too plain a man to call it virtue, it had been luck and a hard stomach and a willingness to weigh the rice short with the cargo's iron and not lie awake, and any man on the island with the same luck and the same stomach would be standing where Quick stood, and Quick, with the smallest turn of the wheel that had brought him here, would be the one sitting against the barrow with his face to the water doing the sum.

He knew it. He let it stand in the lamplight one moment, the whole of it, the wall down, the men on the rock his own kind and his own face under the dust, the rice he weighed short going into bodies that were a back like his back had been, the count the Chinaman kept that was, said plainly, a roll of the dead and Quick the man who balanced it against the cargo. He let it stand the one moment because a man could not hold it off forever and the holding cost less if you let it through clean once in a while and out the other side.

Then he shut it. He had a horror of it the way he had a horror of cruelty and sentiment and for the same reason — it was the thing that, let in and kept, would unfit him to manage, would put his food in his throat at the weigh and his hand off the iron, would make him, in a season, a man who could not do the work, and the work would still want doing, the company would send another beam and another book and another manager who could weigh the rice short and sleep, and the men would be exactly as hungry and the cut exactly as deep and the only thing changed in the whole of it would be that Thomas Quick had gone soft and lost his place and was, himself, on a dock somewhere being looked at by a man with a book. He had seen it happen to managers. He had replaced one. There was no version of letting it in that helped a single man on the rock and every version of it ended with Quick on the wrong side of the beam, and Quick was not going to be on the wrong side of the beam, not for any kindness, not for any sum the Chinaman kept, not for the boy on the Liverpool dock who was thirty years dead in everything but the lamplight. He took the flask from the shelf where the small irons ended and drank the wall back up, two swallows, three, until the boy went down the way the falling men went down, and the columns came out even, and he was the man who weighed and not the man who was weighed, and he closed the ledger on the day.

*

The nest he did not even see at the time. He had it afterward, from the report of the work, because the work that day had reached the new ground.

The cut had come, that season, to a stretch of the high face on the seaward shoulder of the island where the old deposit thinned over a shelf of the living rock, and where the birds — driven up off the lower workings season by season as the men cut higher and the white mountain came down to meet them — had taken to nesting on the bare ledges and the loose top of the new ground, wing to wing, the way the guanay did, in a press so dense a man could not set a boot down between them. The company wanted the ground. There was guano under the nesting and on the ledges below, good guano, the rich dark crumbling kind off the deep old beds, and the price was past eleven and climbing toward twelve and a thousand ships lay at anchor and the new quota stood at five hundredweight a man, and so the barrows went out onto the new ground at first light to take it, and the men did what the men did, which was to clear what was in the way of the cargo, and what was in the way of the cargo, that morning, was the rookery.

They rolled the barrows over it. There was no other way to work the ground and no instruction needed; a thing in the way of the cargo was moved or it was gone over, and a nest was not a thing the company kept a column for any more than tired was. Quick had the figure that evening — the new ground had given better than four hundredweight a man for the first time that week, the rich old beds under the nesting making up what the thin upper cut had cost, and Quick entered the better figure with a flat professional satisfaction and noted that the seaward shoulder would carry the gang another season at the new quota, which was the kind of thing a manager noted, and was glad of, and put in his report.

What he did not put in his report, because there was no column for it and the company had not asked, was the one barrow he happened to see when he went up at the day's end to look at the new ground for himself, the way he looked at any new ground, to read what it would yield. A jornalero ran it down off the shoulder full and heavy and Quick stood aside to let it pass, and the wheel of it went over the last of the cleared nesting at the lip of the cut, over a place where the press of the birds had not all got up in time, and under the iron rim of the wheel, in the white churned mess of trodden ground and broken shell, there was a sound — not a sound, Quick told himself, a wet small nothing under a wheel that was carrying two hundredweight of the richest cargo on earth — and the barrow went on down the line to the chute and tipped its white plume over the cliff to the lighters, and the rhythm did not break, scrape and call and slide, and the column of the birds went up off the disturbed face into the evening in a roar that was no different from the roar of any other evening, two million of them or some number Quick did not keep, pouring out over the blue water toward the shoal, indifferent to the ground, indifferent to the wheel, indifferent to which of them had got up in time, and Quick stood at the lip of the new ground with the better figure in his book and the white dust on his hands and watched the cargo go down the cliff, and did not enter what was under the wheel, because it was not, in any account he was answerable for, a loss.

---

*The cut had reached the nesting ground. For a thousand years the birds had made the mountain, layer on layer, a fall of white in the rainless dark, and had nested on the top of what they made, and the deposit and the colony had been one thing, the living and the spent of it stacked together two hundred feet into the sky — eighteen million of them now over the three islands, a little fewer than the season before, though no one on the rock kept that count. This season the wheels came onto the top of the mountain where the nests were, because the rich beds lay beneath, and the men cleared the nesting the way they cleared the rock, and a barrow-wheel passed, this once, over the eggs of one ledge — the first of them, the first the mining had touched, on a shoulder of Chincha North in the winter of a year the price stood near twelve pounds the ton. The colony rose off the broken ground and poured out to the shoal and came back at dusk to the cliffs as it had every dusk since before the rock had a name, and nested again, a little higher, on the new bare ground the men had opened for them by taking the old. There was guano under the nests. There had always been guano under the nests. The two had never before been a question a wheel could answer.*


## MANIFEST I — The Discovery

### Chapter 8 · Liddell Penhale
*Chincha North, off Pisco · 1854 · guano ≈ £12/ton · Chincha working face ≈ 160 ft*

The American captain wanted the *Marianne* loaded inside the fortnight, and Penhale had the *Marianne* loaded inside the fortnight, and it cost the man a hundred and forty pounds over the going rate to jump the queue, and he paid it grateful, which was the part Penhale liked.

He liked it the way he liked a clean wind. There were fifty ships at anchor off the northern island that morning, more than fifty, riding light and empty in the swell with their crews up in the tops to breathe, and every one of them was burning money by the day it lay idle — wages, victuals, the freight already promised at home — and the chutes worked only so fast and the labour was short and a berth at the loading was the one thing in all that pewter water a man could not buy with patience. So they bought it with him. The *Marianne*'s master had come aboard the *Petrel* with his hat already in his hand, a hard Maine man who had doubled the Horn eleven times and would not have taken off that hat for the Queen, and he had taken it off for Penhale, because Penhale held the berth and the berth was the whole voyage, and a hundred and forty pounds against a voyage was nothing, was a rounding, was the price of not rotting another month at anchor with the dust salting down into the bread. Penhale had named the figure low and even and watched the man do the sum he had already done, and the man had paid, and that was the morning's first profit, made before the sun was a hand off the cliff, and it had not cost Penhale the lifting of a single barrow. That was the trade at its best. You did not move the mountain. You stood beside the only door the mountain could come through, and you charged the rent.

He was thirty-six now and he was not the inspector who had stepped dry-shod onto the shingle five years back with his account-book under his arm and a senior agent's chair to climb toward. The chair was his. He had it by reading the house's correspondence faster than the house read it, by knowing the bond-market lag in London a full packet ahead of the men who outranked him, by being right about the labour when the consignee called him a Cornish doom-crow for it — *the throat is the men, not the ground; whoever solves the men owns the season* — and the consignee had not solved the men and Penhale had, after his fashion, by being the agent the company could trust to keep the worst of its arithmetic off its own books and on someone else's. He took a slice of every cargo that went down those chutes now. Not a large slice. A slice did not need to be large when the cargo was the whole white side of an island and the price climbed every quarter. Twelve pounds the ton it stood at this season, twelve, up from the nine he had smelled across the water in 'forty-nine, and it had not climbed because the stuff had got better. It had climbed because there was a continent of exhausted soil that could not stop wanting it and a finite number of berths to bring it through, and Penhale had positioned himself, with five years of patience and no scruple he could afford to keep, precisely in the narrow place where the wanting met the finite. He was, that morning, on the *Marianne*'s hundred and forty pounds alone, a wealthier man than his father had died, and his father had not died poor by St. Just's reckoning; his father had owned the cottage and the net and one bad share in a fishing lugger and a Bible with a brass clasp, and had drunk most of it, and had sold his son for the rest. Penhale did the comparison without bitterness. He did it the way he did all his sums, to know where he stood, and where he stood that morning was very high, and getting higher, and he allowed himself, for the length of time it took the boat to pull him in to the beach, the rare clean pleasure of knowing it.

*

The trouble came ashore, the way trouble did, wearing a familiar face.

His son was on the beach. Daniel had been on the islands a year now, sent for by Penhale himself out of a Cornwall where the boy had been going to seed at twenty in a counting-house in Penzance, sent for to be made into something — a Penhale who could hold the buying side of a scale, an heir to the slice and the chair and the patience. The boy had his father's frame and his mother's softness through the face, Tamsin's softness, dead these fourteen years and still surfacing in the son's jaw whenever the son was about to be a fool, and Penhale could read the coming foolishness in the jaw before Daniel opened his mouth, the way he could read a green sky.

"There's a thing I'd have you know," Daniel said, "before you hear it crooked from Quick."

"Then say it straight and spare me Quick."

The boy said it straight, or tried to. It came out in pieces, and Penhale stood on the shingle with the dust settling on his good coat and assembled the pieces into a shape while the boy was still laying them down, because that was the work, that was always the work, taking a thing as it fell and seeing under it to the value and the liability before the man who brought it to you had finished bringing it. The shape, assembled, was this: that his son Daniel Penhale, of the Penhale line, sound and quick and worth raising, had bound himself in some manner the boy was at pains not to call by a plain word — *an understanding, a thing between us, I mean to do right by her* — to one of the Chinese. A woman of the Chinese. Younger than the boy, the sister of one of the labourers, of the very tally-man in fact, the half-blind one Penhale had taken note of years back and lifted off the face into the count because the man could hold a sum in his head better than most of the company's clerks — that one's sister. The girl had come off the same lifting as her brother, years back. She had survived the islands five years, which by itself told Penhale a thing about her, because women did not survive the islands five years, the islands used women up faster and worse than they used the men, and a woman who had come through five years of what the islands did to women was either lucky past all probability or she was made of something the islands could not easily spend. And his son had got himself into an understanding with her.

Penhale was quiet a moment. He looked past the boy at the white cliff and the chutes working and the line of men leaning into their barrows, and he did the only thing he knew how to do with a fact, which was to value it.

He valued it cold and he valued it fast and the valuation came out, on the company's side of the ledger, all liability. A senior agent did not couple his son and heir to the labour. The labour was the throat of the trade and the trade ran on the labour being labour — bought at the port for three hundred pesos the head, worked to the weight, closed up and replaced when it failed, a line of entries that balanced only so long as no one on the buying side of the scale looked at any single entry long enough to see a face in it. The whole apparatus, the chutes and the quota and the rice weighed on the beam that weighed the cargo, the entire profitable machine, depended on a wall between the men who held the pen and the men whose teeth got looked at, and Penhale's own son had just put a ladder against that wall in front of fifty ships and a company that would hear of it by the next packet and a consignee in Lima who would dine out on it — *Penhale's boy, you know, has gone native with the coolies* — and there was money in that, money it would cost him, in standing, in the next contract, in the thousand small frictions a man's reputation oiled or seized. All liability. He had the figure almost before the boy stopped talking.

And then, underneath the figure, in the room of his head where the hymn lived and where he did not often go, a smaller thing turned over, and he was annoyed by it, because it had no place on a ledger.

He had seen the man. Years back. The tally-man, the sister's brother, standing at a chute-head holding a true number up against a false one on his fingers, not arguing, only showing, the way you show a sum to a man who can be shown a sum — and Penhale had recognised him. Not as a Chinaman and not as labour. As a man who held the pen, or who would have held it, in some other arrangement of the world; a man whose whole bearing was the bearing of someone keeping his own books in secret against the day the lie in someone else's books could be made to cost. Penhale knew that bearing from the inside of his own ribs. He had been quiet about it for thirty-six years. And it crossed him now, unwanted, that if his son had to be a fool over one of the labour, the boy had at least been a fool over the right family — that the girl who could survive five years of the islands was the sister of the one man on the rock Penhale had ever looked at and thought *that one is not a barrow, that one is a clerk born to the wrong line* — and the thought was no use to him, it valued nothing, it would not unspend the standing the boy had cost him, and Penhale put it down the way he put the hymn down, by force, and turned the whole matter back into a sum he could act on.

"You mean to keep her," he said. Not a question. He was establishing the figure's terms.

"I mean to marry her."

"That word costs more than you have."

"I've thought what it costs."

"You have not," Penhale said, "or you'd not be telling me on a beach with the dust in your teeth and your mother's chin on you." He said it without heat. He never said anything with heat; heat was a thing you spent and got nothing for. "You've thought what you want. That's a different sum, and it never balances." He looked at the boy and saw Tamsin in him and saw, behind Tamsin, the long error of having loved Tamsin himself once, briefly, before he had learned what loving cost a man who could not stop appraising — and he made the decision he made, which was the only decision the ledger left him, and which he would think of for the rest of his life, when he thought of it at all, as the decent one. He did not forbid it. Forbidding it would have made the boy a martyr and the girl a cause and put Penhale on the wrong side of his own son for nothing bought; and worse, it would have meant standing in front of his son and saying, in plain words, *that woman is labour, she is an entry, you do not marry an entry* — and some part of Penhale that had once been an entry himself could not get the words up his throat, found them stuck there like the hymn, would not say to his boy the exact sentence the man with the book had not quite said to an eleven-year-old on a deck thirty years gone. So he did not forbid it. He did the colder thing, which was to price it.

"Here's the whole of what I'll do," he said. "I'll not stop you. You're a man, after your fashion, and a man may ruin himself; that's the one freedom none of us can be taxed out of. But you'll do it off the company's ground and out of the company's sight. Not here. Not on the rock where every soul I trade with can watch a Penhale walk a coolie woman to a parson. You'll take her up to the main, to Pisco, to Lima, where she's only a Chinawoman a man's kept, which the whole coast does and no one logs. And you'll have nothing from me toward it. Not a sol. Not the chair. The chair was for the man I sent for, and if that man wants to spend himself on this, then he's spent the chair as well, and I'll find another to keep my books." He let that sit. "Or you'll put it down, and be sensible, and inherit the largest fortune any St. Just man ever scratched off a rock, and keep the woman as men keep women, quiet, and marry where it pays. I'll think no less of you for either road. I'm not your conscience. I'm your father, which is a smaller thing and more use. The price is named. Choose it knowing."

The boy's jaw worked. Tamsin's jaw. "You'd cut me off over a parson's word."

"I'd cut you off over a sum that doesn't balance, and call the parson incidental. You'll find I'm consistent." He almost added something. He almost said the true thing, the thing under the ledger — *I had a wall built once between the man with the book and the boy whose teeth he looked at, and the wall is the only reason there is a chair to inherit, and you are putting a ladder against it for a feeling, and feelings are the one cargo I have never once seen turn a profit* — but it was too near the room with the hymn in it, and he did not go there in front of his son, or anyone, ever, and so he said only, "Think on it. The *Marianne* sails Thursday. I'll want your answer before she clears, so I know whether I'm training an heir this season or a stranger." And he walked up the beach toward the scale-house, leaving the boy on the shingle with the dust settling on him, and he did not look back, because not looking back was a thing he had practiced until it was easier than looking.

*

He worked the day. The day was good. A Bremen ship paid to jump the queue; a dispute over short weight on a Liverpool cargo was settled in the company's favour because Penhale produced the tally-man and the tally-man produced a count no master could argue with; the price held at twelve and a rumour came in off a packet that it might touch thirteen by the turn of the year if the American demand kept on, the southern planters buying it now by the shipload for their cotton and their cane. Penhale filed the rumour and did not let it warm him, because a man who let a rumour warm him made his next trade with his blood up, and a trade made with the blood up was a trade made by a fool. He kept the blood down. He kept the blood down all day, the way he kept everything down, and it was only at night, when the loading stopped and the ships rode dark and the chutes hung empty and the surf-roar of the birds thinned to the night sound it made, that he went up the cut face alone with a lantern, the way he did once or twice a season, to do the sum he did not let anyone see him do.

He climbed the workings in the dark. The new face that season had been cut back a long way from where it stood in 'forty-nine; he had to climb up over the rubble of the spent ground, the picked-over flats where the white had been taken down to the harder stuff beneath, and the lantern threw his shadow huge and broken across it. At the high face he stopped and held the lantern up, and there they were, the marks. The high-water marks.
