The faint horizontal lines weathered into the cream of the cliff where last year's cut had stood, and the year before's, and the year before that — the lines he had read from the deck of the *Petrel* his first morning and understood, alone among the eleven hundred ships and the whole bankrupt government in Lima, to run both ways. He read them now in the lantern-light, close to, the way another man might read a draught-scale painted up the side of a hull to know how deep she swam.

He read down from the top of the deposit as it must once have stood, before any pick, two hundred feet of it if the old hands were right, the full sky-high mountain of a thousand years of birds — and he read down the marks the trade had cut, season by season, each line a year of the queue and the chutes and the men leaning into the barrows, and he counted the marks, and he measured by eye the white that stood below the lowest mark, the white that was left, the white not yet cut. And he did the sum.

He did it the way he had done the *Marianne*'s berth, low and even and without permission from any softer part of himself. So much taken in five seasons. So much standing. The price climbing, which meant the demand climbing, which meant the cutting would climb, not hold — fifty ships now and a hundred coming, the southern planters in it, the whole world's soil hungry and only the one trough to feed at. He carried the figures in his head the way he carried everything, and the figures resolved, up there in the dark with the lantern hissing and the birds making their low far roar over the seaward cliff, into a number he had been circling for five years and had never once let himself land on.

A hundred and sixty feet stood below the lowest mark. He made it a hundred and sixty, give or take a fathom. And the cutting was going to accelerate, not hold, because the price said so. And a hundred and sixty feet of it, cut faster every season as the world's hunger and the price drove the chutes harder — that was not the inexhaustible mountain the trade circulars sold and the consignee believed and the bondholders in London had mortgaged a nation against. That was a finite quantity of weeks. He could very nearly count them. He stood with the lantern up and he very nearly counted the weeks until the lantern would shine on bare rock, and the part of his mind that had never once been wrong about a price told him the answer was a number of years a man could live to see. Not a hundred years. Not even fifty. The white mountain that fed a queue of fifty ships and made him richer than his father had died and propped up the whole staggering credit of Peru — it had a bottom, and the bottom was close enough that a man of thirty-six, standing on the cut face in the dark, might reasonably expect to outlive it.

He was the first to know. He was almost certainly the first soul to stand on that rock and do that sum and land on that number, because every other man in the trade had a reason not to — the company wanted it inexhaustible, the government needed it inexhaustible, the diggers could not afford to think past the day's weight, and the bondholders in London had built an empire of paper on its being inexhaustible, and a thing that a great many powerful men need to be true has a way of staying true in every head but the one head cold enough to have no stake in the lie. Penhale had a stake only in being right. He had built his whole self on never being on the wrong side of a sum again. And the sum said: the mountain ends.

And here was the thing that he would not have been able to explain to his son or to Quick or to any man living, the thing that was the whole of him: knowing the mountain ended did not make him want to cut it slower. It made him want to cut it faster.

Because a finite thing, a thing with a bottom you could see coming, was a thing to be *taken* — taken hard and taken now, before the bottom came, before the other men did their own sums and the scramble started and the price either crashed from glut or the ground simply gave out under everyone at once. The Indians, the old hands said, had put a man to death for troubling the birds in the nesting season; had let the mountain stand and only skimmed the new top of it and called the birds sacred and never touched the deep old wealth at all. Fools, Penhale thought, up on the dark face, fools with a fortune under their feet and a superstition keeping them off it — and then a colder thought, because he was always honest in the room with the hymn in it: not fools. They had known something he did not have a word for and did not want one. They had known the mountain was the birds, somehow, the live thing and the dead thing the same account, and they had not spent the principal. And he was going to spend the principal. He had always been going to spend the principal. A man who had once been the principal himself — sold, logged, a quantity entered in a book and spent — did not stand on a mountain of spendable wealth and protect it for a future he would not be alive to bank. He spent it. He spent it the way the man with the book had spent him, without cruelty and without mercy, because it was there and it could be done and the only safe place to stand was the side of the scale that did the spending.

The lantern hissed. The birds roared, low and far and endless, the river of them pouring even now in the dark off the seaward cliff to the shoal and back, a tide of the living laying down, drop by drop, the next inch of the mountain he meant to cut to nothing inside his own lifetime. And up there alone, with the figure landed in him at last and no softer part of himself permitted in the room, Liddell Penhale was afraid.

He felt it come up the way the smell had come up his first morning, ahead of his consent, from underneath. Not fear of the bottom for the trade's sake — he would be out and rich long before the trade felt it. Fear from lower down, from the deck, from eleven years old. Because a thing with a bottom was a thing that ran out, and a man who stood on a mountain and felt in his own chest that it ran out felt, in the same breath, the bottom of every other thing he stood on — the chair, the slice, the dry-shod ownership of the world that was the only wall between Liddell Penhale and the line of men on the cliff he had refused, his first morning, to count. Owning the mountain did not make him safe; it only put him ahead of it. And somewhere under the slice and the chair and the fifty ships he was still the boy stood in a line with his chin tipped up to the light, and the only thing five years and a fortune had bought him was that now he held the lantern that did the looking — and the lantern showed him the bottom, and a hand he had not given the order to opened and closed on the cold rock at his side.

And the hymn came up.

It came in his father's drink-thick voice off a chapel bench fifty years gone, the way it only ever came when he was afraid, uninvited, complete — *we are but strangers here, we do not crave a home* — and on its heels the next line that he had not let himself hear in years, *Heaven is our fatherland, Heaven is our home* — and he stood on the cut face with the lantern up and the bottom of the world showing in its light and for one moment, one only, the hymn was louder than the figures, and he wanted, the way the men on the cliff wanted when they set down the bar and walked to the edge, a home that was not a quantity, a place a man could stand that did not run out, that could not be spent or cut or weighed or taken down to bare rock a barrow at a time —

He crushed it.

He crushed it the way he crushed everything, by force, in the dark, alone, and he was angry as he always was when it happened, because it told him the one thing the figures could not, which was that the wall had a crack in it and the crack let the deck-water in. He brought the figures back. He brought back the twelve pounds a ton and the fifty ships and the hundred and forty the *Marianne*'s master had paid grateful that morning, and he stood on the bottom-line of a mountain and made himself a plan with the blood gone cold again: cut faster. Take more of the slice. Press the company to lift the quota, work the chutes by night when the moon served, buy in more labour through the brokers at Macao because the labour was the throat and a man who solved the throat owned the season and the seasons left were countable and a countable thing was a thing to be emptied before the count ran out. Get out at the top, so far up the buying side of the scale that when the rock at last showed bare no bottom could ever reach him again.

That was the plan. It was a good plan. It was the plan of the only man in the trade clear-eyed enough to see the floor, and it was going to make him a great deal of money, and every inch he cut the harder drove the birds the further off the broken face — the same birds whose slow white falling had laid the mountain down in the first place, drop on drop, a thousand years of it. That sum Penhale did not do. There was no line for it in any book he kept. He had read the marks on the cliff his first morning and they had told him true: a quantity, going out, with a bottom. The marks counted the white. They did not count what made it. There was no mark anywhere on that cliff to read the live thing leaving, and a man reads the figures there are.

He came down off the face with the lantern. At the foot of the workings he stopped and looked once across the dark anchorage at the riding-lights of the fifty ships, his ships, the rent on the only door, and the chest was quiet again, the hymn down, the fear down, the figures cold and standing. He had his answer for the season. He hoped the boy would have his by Thursday. Either way the *Marianne* would sail, and the price would touch thirteen by the turn of the year, and the mountain would stand a hundred and sixty feet and then less, and Liddell Penhale would cut it faster than any man alive, knowing the bottom — which was, he had decided up there in the dark, the one thing he had been made for.

He went down to the boat. Behind him the birds did not stop. They poured off the cliff all night, laying the mountain down a feather's weight at a time, faster than he could ever cut it and slower than he would ever believe, and over the water the bell of the *Marianne* struck the watch, and a man up in her tops, come out of the hold to breathe, heard it strike and did not know what he was breathing or what it had once been or what, three lives on, it would still be costing in a girl's two lungs who was not yet born; and Penhale's boat ground up onto the shingle, and he stepped out dry-shod, the way a man steps who means to own a place, and went to find his son and learn the figure for the season.


## MANIFEST II — The War

### Chapter 9 · Inés Penhale
*Callao and Lima · 1864 · guano ≈ £12–13/ton (war premium) · Chincha working face ≈ 120 ft*

The Hamburg agent had a habit of speaking to her father's empty chair, and Inés let him do it for a full minute before she moved the chair.

She did it without comment. She rose, crossed behind the long table in the counting-room over the Callao mole, took the heavy chair her father sat in when he sat in this room at all, and drew it from the head of the table to the side, against the wall, where it could be looked at and not addressed. Then she came back and sat where she had been sitting, at the foot, with the open books in front of her and the harbour glare coming up white off the water through the dusty window, and she folded her hands on the ledger and waited for Herr Brauer to understand that the chair was gone and only she was left to talk to. He was a slow man and it took him the better part of another sentence. She used the time. She was thirteen years old and she had learned that the most expensive thing a person could give a man who had decided not to see you was your impatience, because impatience was a price, and a child who could not yet be seated at the table on her own name could at least refuse to pay.

"Señorita," Brauer said at last, having run out of chair. "With respect. I will wait for Don Liddell. This is not a matter for—" He stopped himself before the word. He was not a cruel man, only a busy one, and he heard the word coming and did not like the sound of it any better than she would. He had children of his own in Hamburg, she happened to know; she made it her business to know small soft things about hard men, the way her father made it his business to know the bond-market lag; it was the same trade conducted with different stock.

"For a girl," Inés finished for him, in English, easily, so the word would belong to her and not to him. "No. It is not. It is a matter for whoever holds your bill, and your bill is in this book, under my hand, and my father is on the islands, and the islands"—she paused, exactly one beat, the beat she had practised against the glass in her mother's room until it came without showing—"are not where any man with cargo afloat wishes to be thinking about, this week, of all weeks."

That landed. She watched it land. It went into the German the way a cold draught goes under a door, and his eyes did the thing she had been waiting for, the small involuntary cut sideways toward the window, toward the water, toward the open Pacific past the breakwater where, two hundred and forty kilometres down the coast, the thing that had happened was still happening. He had heard. Of course he had heard; the whole port had heard for four days now and talked of nothing else, in the chandlers' and the consignees' offices and the cafés along the Calle del Comercio and the fish-market and the cathedral steps; it had come up the coast the only way news came, by ship and then by mouth and then by the broadsheets that the criers were selling at a real apiece with the ink still wet and the type set crooked from haste. *Los españoles han tomado las islas.* The Spaniards had taken the islands. A squadron of the Spanish Pacific fleet had stood in to the Chinchas on the fourteenth of April and put men ashore and run up their flag over the white, and now the source of the whole revenue of the Republic of Peru—the guano, the islands, the entire treasury of a nation that had borrowed against nothing else and mortgaged its bread and its railways and its army's pay against the droppings of three little rocks off Pisco—sat under foreign guns.

Brauer had a barque at the Chinchas. Inés knew that too. She had it from the harbour-master's clerk, whom her mother fed twice a month and who would have crawled across glass for Rosa de la Cruz, and who told the señora things, and the señora told her daughter, because Rosa had understood some years ago that the daughter was the one who could do something with them. Brauer's barque, the *Concordia* out of Hamburg, was lying empty at the anchorage off Chincha Norte waiting her turn at the loading when the Spanish came in, and a ship caught at the islands by a war over the islands was a ship that might load and might not, might be seized as good prize or held or simply rotted at anchor for a season while admirals exchanged letters, and every day of it cost Brauer money he had promised to men in Hamburg who did not care whose flag flew over the dung.

"You are afraid for the *Concordia*," Inés said. Not unkindly. She had learned the unkind way did not pay; her father did everything cold and even and got more for it than the men who shouted. "You should be. So is every house on this mole. That is why you will settle the short-weight with me today, at the figure in this book, and not wait three weeks for my father to come up from the south through a war to tell you the same figure in a deeper voice. Because in three weeks the figure will be worse for you, not better, and you know it, and you are not a man who likes to pay more for waiting." She turned the ledger a quarter-turn so it faced him, and laid one finger—she had her mother's hands, fine and quick—on the line. "Twelve pounds eight the ton, the cargo your master disputed. The dispute is the moisture. He claims a third of it sea-damp and over-weighed. The scale-house at Chincha says no. My father's tally-man says no, and my father's tally-man does not make scale errors, which the whole coast knows, which is the only reason your master thought to try it on—a clean count is worth disputing because it is the one count nobody can prove crooked." She let that sit a half-second, because it was a small piece of flattery aimed at no one in the room, aimed at the absent tally-man, and she had put it in deliberately, the way you salt a thing, because she had met the tally-man and meant to be remembered by him as a person who knew the worth of a clean count. "I will give your master his moisture. Two per centum off the gross, today, on my hand, and the bill paid before sundown into this house. Or he may have nothing off, and his lawyer, and the war, and a barque at the islands that the Spanish may decide is a German ship carrying Peruvian guano and therefore exactly the sort of thing one detains to make a point. Two per centum is cheaper than a point. Decide."

She stopped. She had learned to stop. The hardest thing she had taught herself, harder than the figures, which came to her the way breath came to other people, was to stop talking at the moment the figures had done their work and let the silence do the rest, because a person who kept talking past the close was a person who did not believe the close, and Brauer would smell the doubt the way a dog smells fear. So she stopped, and folded her hands again, and looked at him with her father's eyes set in her mother's face—she had been told often enough that this was the disconcerting thing about her, the cold pale appraising Cornish eyes looking out of a soft Limeña girl, and she had stopped minding it and started using it—and she waited.

Brauer looked at the chair against the wall. He looked at the books. He took out a handkerchief and pressed it to his mouth, not from the dust here, only habit, the gesture every man on this coast had, and Inés noted that too, the white cloth, the way they all carried it, and filed it without yet knowing it was a thing to file. Then he sat down—in the side chair he reached for the wrong one, the head one, then caught himself and took the lesser, which was its own small surrender, and they both pretended not to see it—and he said, in German, half to himself and not meant for her, *Gott, was für ein Kind,* God, what a child, and then in English, "Two per centum. Today. You will have your sundown."

"You will have your *Concordia* loaded the day the islands are free, ahead of two ships that disputed and a third that did not pay," Inés said, and rang the small bell for the clerk to write it up, and did not smile until Brauer was looking at the paper and not at her, and then she let herself, one half-second, at the window, at the white water, the way you let yourself swallow only after the bitter thing is down.

*

Her father had been gone ten years and was in the room.

That was the trick of him; he managed it from the islands the way he managed everything, by having arranged the room before he left it. The counting-room over the Callao mole was his and bore him in every fixture—the long table he had bought at the bankruptcy sale of a house the guano had ruined, because he liked to buy the furniture of men the trade had eaten; the great brass-cornered ledgers he kept the company's books in and the smaller private book, the one with the cracked black spine, that he kept his own in; the framed thing on the wall that strangers took for a license or a charter and that was in fact the first manifest, his first, the bill of lading he had written and signed his first season on the rock in '49, the *Petrel*, one hundred and forty tons of Peruvian guano consigned, in a young man's hard upright hand with the down-strokes pressed deep—he had had it glazed and hung where he sat so that he might look at it, Inés had decided long ago, the way other men looked at the portraits of their dead: not from love, from accounting, to see how far the figure at the top had grown. She had grown up under that frame. She had learned her sums on the company's ledgers at the foot of this table while her father called figures from the head of it, and she had understood before she was nine that the figures were the only language in which he ever said anything true to her, and so she had become the best speaker of that language in the house, better than the clerks, better, this last year, than the men he hired to keep what she could have kept for nothing, and she had done it for a reason she did not examine because examining it cost more than it returned: she had done it to be looked at by him the way he looked at a sound cargo. To be a thing he appraised and found, in the one register he possessed, worth keeping.

He had been on the islands the better part of two years now, this stretch, down at the workings and the Lima dealings, an old man—he was forty-six and she thought of him as an old man, with the easy cruelty of thirteen—a man who ran the southern end of the house's affairs himself now, more from habit than need, because he could no longer quite trust the world to do the buying side of a scale without his standing on it. He came up to Callao and Lima in the seasons and went down again, and between the comings he managed the room from afar, by letter, by the figures, by the wall he had built around his absence out of furniture and her, and she had filled the absence so completely and so well that there were weeks when the men who came up the mole forgot the chair at the head was empty until they were halfway through addressing it.

She rose now and went and put the chair back. She always put it back before she left the room for the day; she was careful never to be found with it moved, because the moving of it was a thing she did, not a thing she was permitted, and the difference between a thing you did and a thing you were permitted to do was, she had worked out, the whole of her position in this house, and she meant one day to abolish the difference, by being too useful to refuse, which was a long sum and she had begun it early.

She squared the chair to the table. Under the framed manifest the little private book lay where she had left it, and she opened it once more before she closed the room, not to read—she had read it, of course she had read it, she read everything in this room, it was hers more than it was his now in every way but the law—but to add the line. The Brauer line. Two per centum, the *Concordia*, the sundown payment. She wrote it in her own hand under her father's, small and exact, in the place where the day's true figures went that the company's grand glazed ledgers were too proud and too public to hold. Her hand under his hand. She had been writing in the private book for a year and he had never once said she might and never once said she might not, which was, she had decided, his way of saying it: the one inheritance he would acknowledge was the one she took without asking. She blotted the ink. She thought, not for the first time and without self-pity, because self-pity was a price and she did not pay it: *I keep the book he cannot say he loves me in, and that is the love, and I have learned to read it, and it is enough,* and then she shut the book, because the other thought, the one that came in behind that one in her mother's tongue—*pero no es bastante,* but it is not enough—was a thought she did not let finish, the way her father did not let the hymn finish, though she did not know that about her father and would not have recognised the resemblance if she had.

*

She went up to Lima in the afternoon, in the hired carriage, with the war in every mouth along the road.

The road from Callao to Lima ran for three leagues across the dun coastal plain between the port and the capital, and it was busy as a fair, busier, with the war on it: post-riders, an artillery train going the wrong way with its mules balking, a Franciscan walking with his sandals white to the ankle, families in carts with their goods, going where she could not tell, away from the coast perhaps, as though the Spanish meant to bombard Lima itself, which they did not, yet, though by the time the thing was over they would have shelled Valparaíso and stood off Callao and a boy at a gun would learn what the islands cost in a coin the ledger did not keep. Inés watched it all through the carriage window with the particular attention she gave to everything, which was not curiosity—curiosity was a child's thing and she had put it down with the other child's things early—but assessment: who was afraid and of what, who was profiting, where the fear would move money and which way. The fear was moving money toward her. That was the plain truth of it and she did not flinch from it because flinching was a price. A nation under foreign guns over its one cargo was a nation whose every house with cargo afloat or contracts pending was, this week, more frightened than usual and therefore more biddable, and the price of guano in London had jumped on the news the way it always jumped on a war—twelve and climbing toward thirteen, the war premium, the buyers in Liverpool and Hamburg and the American South bidding the stuff up precisely because the supply was now uncertain, because uncertainty was the one thing that moved a price faster than scarcity, and the islands' being seized at gunpoint had made the whole world's farmers afraid they might not get their dung, and a frightened farmer paid. She had done the sum the morning the news came, before her mother was up, and the sum had told her the family was about to make money out of the seizure of the very islands the family's money came from, and she had felt nothing about it except the clean small pleasure of having seen it first, which was the only pleasure she fully trusted.

Her mother was at the house in the old centre, near Santo Domingo, the house the island money had built and that her father came home to less and less and that her mother ran like a small fierce embassy of respectability in a city that had taken thirteen years to half-accept the Peruvian woman a Cornish guano-agent kept and had still not entirely forgiven her the smell. The smell. It was in the house still, under the wax and the flowers Rosa kept in every room, faint and ammoniac in the curtains and the silk, the thing the wealth was made of and could not be scrubbed out. Inés had grown up in it and could not smell it at all except on the days she came in from the clean dust of the road and met it at the door—one breath of where the carriage and the carved Spanish chairs had come from, and then it was gone. She lost it now, coming in. That was the thing about it nobody said: you stopped smelling it, and then you were a person who lived in it and did not know.

"They've taken the islands," her mother said, by way of greeting, from the sala, where she sat with her sister-cousin the Widow Errázuriz and two other women of the old families, the good families, the ones who came now, finally, because the money had at last grown too large to keep snubbing—and Inés heard in her mother's voice, under the drawing-room calm, the thing her mother would never say in front of the Errázuriz woman, which was fear, real fear, not for the cargo but for the man, because Liddell was on the islands or near them and the islands were under guns. Rosa loved him. Inés had observed this fact about her mother the way she observed the war, from outside, with assessment and a little wonder, the way you might observe a man who keeps a debt he has no hope of recovering on the books out of some sentiment the ledger has no column for. Her mother loved a man who could love her only as a sound asset, and had built a whole life and a whole house and a whole standing in Lima around the asset-love, and Inés had grown up watching it and had drawn from it the lesson her mother had not meant to teach, which was: do not be loved that way. Be loved for the thing itself. Be so good at the thing that the love and the appraisal become the same act and no one, not even you, can find the seam.

"They've taken the islands," Inés agreed, and crossed the sala, and kissed her mother, and made the small grave courtesies to the women, in the soft fast Lima Spanish she put on for them like a different coat—she was a different person in this language, warmer, rounder, a girl who said *Dios la guarde* and meant the room to think she meant it—and she sat, and took the cup of thick chocolate the maid brought, and listened to the women be frightened in their drawing-room way, and said, when there was a gap, the one thing she had come up the road to plant, dropped lightly, to her mother but for the room: "Don Echeverría's house is short of freight to bring the Lobos cargo up, now the Chinchas are closed. They'll pay over the odds for bottoms this month. If Papá's *Petrel* and the two charters were to be offered to them at the war rate before the others think of it—" She did not finish. She had learned not to finish. She let it lie in the chocolate-scented air of the sala with the candles and the smell nobody smelled, a thirteen-year-old girl's idle remark about her father's ships, and watched her mother's face do, for half a second, behind the social mask, the thing she had come to see it do: understand, and approve, and be a little afraid of her own daughter.

*

It was the smallest of the guests, the child nobody had introduced, who undid her for a moment, which was not a thing Inés permitted often.

There was a little girl in the doorway between the sala and the inner court, a child of seven, dark and quick-eyed, who had wandered in from the back of the house where the maids and the kept children of the household lived, and who stood now in the doorway watching the grand women with an attention Inés recognised because it was her own—the watchfulness of a child cataloguing a room she has not been asked into, reading it for where she stands in it—and Inés knew who she was. Everyone in the house knew who she was, and no one in the sala would have said. She was Carmen. She was the tally-man's grand-niece, the daughter of the Chinese woman Daniel Penhale had married off the islands against Don Liddell's whole ledger—Daniel, Inés's own half-brother, a man twenty years her senior whom she had met perhaps four times in her life and who was at this moment somewhere in the volunteer levies for the war, gone to the guns for a country that was not his over islands his father got rich on—and the child's mother was in this house too, in the back, sick, had been sick a long time now, the cough, the women said in the kitchen, the islands' cough, the one the diggers got, though no one could say how a woman who had got off the islands eight years gone still had the islands in her chest. Inés had passed the sickroom. She had heard the cough through the door, the particular tearing depth of it, the same sound she had heard, this last year, beginning, in the men who came up the mole and pressed the white handkerchiefs to their mouths, the same sound—she realised it now, sitting in the sala, with a small cold drop of understanding she did not want and could not use and therefore set aside—the same sound the cargo made, somehow, when it had got far enough into a person; as if the white going out and the cough coming up were two ends of one long account that ran through the curtains and the chocolate and the carved chairs and out the back of the house into a sickroom, and a child of seven in a doorway.

The child Carmen was looking at her.

Not at the grand women. At Inés. The dark quick eyes had picked her out of the room and were reading her with exactly the cold cataloguing attention Inés was at that moment using on the room, child to child, two watchers recognising each other across a sala full of people who watched nothing, and for one moment—one only, and Inés crushed it after, the way she crushed everything that did not pay—Inés saw the whole shape of the thing she did not have a column for. She saw that the child in the doorway was her own blood, her brother's daughter, her niece, a Penhale by half and so by the other half the granddaughter of the very rock the wall above the Callao mole was the first manifest of; she saw that the cough behind the back door and the war off the coast and the price climbing toward thirteen in London and the little private book under her own hand and the smell nobody smelled were all one thing, one account, kept in two languages by two grandfathers who would not look at the same page; and she saw, with the part of her that was her mother and not her father, that the watchful little girl in the doorway was going to grow up to be the one who asked what the account came to—because she had the eyes for it, the eyes that read a room, the family eyes, and she had, behind the back door, in her mother's chest, the figure the ledger never carried, already, at seven, in her own breath. Inés saw all of it in the length of a held look. And then she did with it the thing she had taught herself to do with everything that cost more than it returned: she put it down. She gave the child the smallest nod—one watcher to another, *I see you, you see me, neither of us will say*—and turned back to the women and the war and the freight that Don Echeverría's house would pay over the odds for, and did the next sum.

The child held the doorway a moment more. Then a maid's hand came and drew her gently back into the dark of the inner house, toward the sickroom and the cough, and she was gone, and the sala closed over the gap she left the way the gang on the cliff closed over a man, and the women talked of the war, and the chocolate cooled, and somewhere down the coast the Spanish flag hung over a hundred and twenty feet of white that had been a hundred and eighty feet of white when Inés was born, and Inés Penhale, thirteen, finished her chocolate, and rose, and made her courtesies, and went to write to Don Echeverría's house about the freight before any other house in Lima thought to—and she got there first, which was the only place she had ever wanted to be, and the wanting of which was the one figure in the whole accounting that did not, however she worked it, balance.


## MANIFEST II — The War

### Chapter 10 · Mei Loong-fa
*Chincha North, off Pisco, under Spanish occupation · 1864–65 · guano ≈ £13/ton · Chincha working face ≈ 115 ft*

There was a flag over the rookery now, and the birds did not care, and Mei stood at the chute-head with the slate in his hands and watched the two facts sit side by side and refuse to bear on each other, the way two columns sit on a page and add to nothing until a hand draws the line.

It was a Spanish flag. It had been a Spanish flag for two months. In April the squadron had come up out of the south in the white forenoon, four ships of war and a transport, and had put a boat ashore at the landing below the scale-house, and a Spanish officer in a clean coat had read a paper to the heavy Englishman who ran the company now and to the few guards and to nobody else — the men at the face did not stop; the chutes did not stop; a thing read aloud to the air over a working island changed nothing a barrow could measure — and at the end of it the small Peruvian ensign that had flown over the stores came down and the flag of Spain went up the same halyard, and that was the war, the whole of it, as it reached the rock: a different cloth on the same pole, and the price of a ton gone up two shillings on the rumor of it before the cloth had finished rising. Mei had marked it. He marked everything. He had stood at the chute with the morning's slate and watched the new flag take the wind and had thought, in the cold flat place where he kept the things he could tell no one, *they have taken the island; they have not taken the work; the work belongs to whoever owns the gun, and the gun has changed hands, and tomorrow I will stand here and count the same barrows down to a different master's boats, and the master will call it his, and the men will call it nothing, and the birds will not look down.*

The birds did not look down. That was the part he kept coming back to, the way his thumb kept finding the lowest notch. The guanay went out over the seaward face in the grey of every morning the way they had gone out the first morning he landed, a pouring column black against the bright, fifteen years of it now, and came back full from the shoal at the curve of the day, and went out again, and the column was thinner than it had been — he knew it was thinner the way he knew the mountain was lower, by keeping it, by holding this year's darkness against the darkness he had folded away in the first year, and the difference was not nothing — but it was still a number too large to be a sound, still the high far roar behind all the work, and not one bird of the millions had broken its line for the squadron or the flag or the two shillings. Spanish smoke or Peruvian smoke; the shoal was where the shoal was; the birds fished it. A nation could lose its whole revenue at gunpoint in a single forenoon and the cormorants would feed their chicks that evening exactly as if no man had ever come to the rock to count what fell from them. Mei had watched empires for fifteen years now, from the one place on earth that all of them wanted and none of them would dig, and he read in the birds the thing he had learned and they had not, because they had never needed to learn it: that the thing being fought over and the thing that mattered were not the same thing, and never had been, and that the men with the guns had the first and would never have the second.

He was thirty-four. The left eye had been a clouded smear so long he no longer thought of it as a thing he had lost; it was only the side he turned away from the face, the way a man favors a knee. The cough had come into his own chest the winter before last and he had given it the same treatment he gave everything, had filed it, *the dust, fifteen years of it, it begins as the dust and is nothing,* and had learned to take it standing very still with the breath shallow so that it did not become the long bending thing in front of the men who now took their counts from him. He masked it the way A-Lian masked hers — had masked hers; he made himself use the present, still, the way a man keeps a chair set for someone not yet gone — by going quiet and breathing around it. He had cut a great many notches into the stick since the grey little room. The stick was heavy now. He had carried it fifteen years against the folded paper with the lying eight on it, and the eight had long since stopped meaning a count of years and become only a thing he kept, the way a man keeps the first coin of a debt that was never honored, and the stick beside it had filled and he had had to begin a second, shorter, newer, the pale wood not yet greyed by the hands, and between the two of them they held more dead than he could find with a fingertip in a night, more than he could set on any stone table, in any house, that did not exist.

*

The Englishman who ran the company now was not Quick. Quick was still on the island — Quick would die on the island, Mei thought, the way the men at the face died, only slower, of the drink instead of the dust, the only difference between the overseer and the overseen being which of the two killings the company paid for — but Quick had been passed over when the old consignment changed hands, set to the stores and the punishments, kept on because a man who knew the rock was worth keeping and because there was nowhere for such a man to go, and a younger company man named Pessell ran the loading now, a thin precise person who had come out with the new contractors and who did not look at the diggers any more than the others had, but who looked at Mei, because Mei was the man whose count no master could argue with, and a man whose count could not be argued with was a thing worth a chair near the scale even to a man who would not have given him the time on the path.

Pessell had a problem, which was the war, which was that the Spanish held the island but did not work it, having no diggers and no wish to land soldiers in the killing air, and so the strange arrangement held that the company went on cutting the mountain and loading the boats and the Spanish let it, because a guano island that did not produce guano was a stone of no use to anyone, and the question of who got paid for the cargo and on whose books it stood and at what price in a market gone mad with the war premium was a question that ran in the air over the chute-head like the dust, and Pessell needed a man who could hold three counts in his head at once — the gang's true cut, the company's claim, and the figure the Spanish prize-officer would want to see — and keep them each separate and each defensible, so that when the accounting came, whenever it came, however the war ended, the company could prove what it had loaded and to whom and under what protest, and not be stripped twice, once by the diggers' deaths and once by the lawyers.

So Mei kept three slates now where he had kept one. He stood at the chute-head with the gang's number and the boats' number and a third number that was the truth under both, and he understood, holding the three of them in the failing light, that he had risen as high as the island would ever let a man like him rise: he was the keeper of the counts that the masters did not trust each other enough to keep, the one man on the rock that the company and the Spanish and the lawyers to come all needed and none owned, and the rising had not freed him an inch. He was still the clerk of the men who died to make the figures. He was only, now, the clerk of more masters. He marked the barrows. The morning's tally grew on the first slate, and on the second, and the true one underneath, and below him the white plumes jumped off the cliff and slid down the canvas tongue to the lighters, and the lighters carried the mountain out past where the Spanish ships of war lay at anchor with their guns run out and their ensigns hanging in the still air, out to the merchantmen waiting their turn at the trough the way they had waited it for fifteen years, the flags on them of every nation and the holds of them empty and high, and the price on the cargo going into them higher this season than Mei had ever marked it, thirteen pounds the ton, the war and the scarcity each putting their shilling on it, the fortune at its very loudest in the one season the mountain stood lowest he had ever seen it.

He had read the mountain that morning, the way he read it every morning, the working face against the snow-mark high above it where the new white met the weathered cream, and the face stood now at a hundred and fifteen feet, near enough, where it had stood at a hundred and eighty the morning he landed. Sixty-five feet of it gone in fifteen years, down to the boats, out past the guns, into the fields of a continent he would never see. He did the sum without being asked, the cold clerk under the clerk doing it the way the lungs took the air, *a hundred and fifteen feet, and faster now than the ten a year of the early time, because the price drives the cutting and the price has never been higher,* and the figure it gave him was a smaller number of years than the figure it had given him in the grey little room, and he put it away unspoken with all the others, because there was no one to say it to who would not laugh or report it, and because the one person he had ever said such a thing to was lying in a bed in Lima with the thing in her chest that had begun as the dust on a floor twelve years gone, finishing its account.

*

A man went off the face that month. It was a new gang — the new contractors had bought a fresh lot at the port, Mei had counted them off the tender in April the way a hand on the beach had once counted him, by the number and not the name, the company paying now near five hundred soles the head where it had paid three hundred pesos in his year, the war and the dwindling supply each putting their shilling on a man the same as on a ton — and one of them, a young one, not three weeks on the rock, stopped at the face in the white forenoon with his bar in his two hands the way A-Yün had stopped, and looked at the sea.

Mei was not near enough to say the man's name. He did not know the man's name; the man was three weeks on the rock and had been counted off the tender as a number and would be counted off the roll as a number, and the only place his name would have stood, if Mei had been near enough to learn it, was the stick. He saw it from the chute-head. He saw the bar come down — not flung, set, the same courtesy, the tidy gentleness of a man who will not leave a tool in the dirt even now — and he saw the young one walk to the lip past the head of the chute, four steps, five, unhurried, the way a man walks out of a room he is finished being in, and he opened his mouth to call out the only thing there was to call, the dull small thing to put in front of the man's eyes, *the barrow, brother, the barrow* — and the words were fifteen years old in his mouth and he knew before they left him that they would do nothing, had done nothing for A-Yün, did nothing now, and the young one went off the cliff into the white surf two hundred feet down and the column of birds did not break and the chutes did not stop and Pessell, who saw it from the scale-house door, made a small dry sound and turned back to his papers, and a guard called up the line for the gap to be closed, *one short, close it up,* in the trade-Spanish that was the only tongue the rock had ever fully agreed on.

And it was Mei the gap came to. Of course it was Mei. He was the keeper of the counts. The gang's true number had to be set right against the one that had walked into the sea, and it was Mei who held up the slate and struck the young one out of the living, one stroke, the live number falling by one, the working number, the man subtracted as clean as a barrow gone down the chute and not come back up, and the second slate adjusted, and the true one underneath, so that the company's claim and the Spanish prize-officer's figure and the truth would all three balance over the body, and Mei made them balance, with the hand that did not shake, because he would not let it shake in front of Pessell, because he had not let it shake the day he counted A-Yün out of the living with the same hand fifteen years gone, and the not-shaking was still the whole of him, was still the thing the island had made and the war had only confirmed.

He did not cut a notch that night. He had no name. He sat on the boards in the dark of the shed with the heavy stick in his hand and his thumb on the lowest grey notch of it and the blank pale wood of the second stick below, and he found that the not-knowing of the name was a worse thing than the death, because the stick was the one account in the world that did not lose the men, and a man it could not hold — a man three weeks on the rock who had gone into the sea before Mei could learn what to call him — was a man the company's books and Mei's books both lost, doubly lost, gone with no figure at the bottom of him anywhere, and Mei sat with that, the fifteen years of it, the gathering certainty that the stick which he had begun so that one of them would have counted, so that the dead would have somewhere to stand, was losing them faster than it kept them now, that there were more nameless ones than named, that the rock was making men disappear quicker than one half-blind clerk could carve them in, and that the account he had sworn to keep was the one account that could never, now, be made whole.

He put both sticks away against the folded paper with the eight on it. The eight was very worn. He could no longer feel the character with his thumb the way he once had; the salt and the years had softened it; but he knew it was there, the lie at the bottom of everything, the number a broker had drawn in spilled liquor in a wineshop in Amoy for a boy who had believed there were honest brokers, and he kept it the way he kept the dead, because to throw it away would have been to say that the lie had not happened, and the lie had happened, and someone must keep it.

*

He went to Lima in the last month of the year, on the company's leave and the company's coin, because the company needed a man it trusted in Callao to stand at the bonded warehouse and reconcile the cargo that had been run up the coast ahead of the war, and Mei was that man, the war having made him more useful than ever, the way the island had always made him useful by needing the thing he could not stop doing. He stood three days at the warehouse with the slate and made the figures balance. And on the evening of the third day he was let, at last, to go up to the house.

It was a good house. He noted it the way he noted everything, *brick, two floors, a tiled court, a fig in the court, the smell of the islands in the curtains under the smell of the candles and the cooking, the money plain in the room and trying not to be,* a house that the white mountain had built, that A-Lian's English boy had bought her with the wage of the loading and the lighters, and she had gone off the rock at last on the arm of a Cornishman and into a brick house with a fig in the court, and had borne a daughter and then a son, and had thought, Mei knew, that she had carried one thing out of the worst place on earth that was hers — a marriage she chose, children she named, the girl named Carmen for a future the girl might belong to in a way her mother never could — and she had been right, and it had not mattered, because the thing she carried out of the island that finished her was not in the curtains or the court. It was in her chest. She had taken the rock with her in her lungs, the way Mei carried it in his, the way the boy Asher carried it who had been born in the dust of it, and the brick house and the fig and the Lima name could not reach the place where it sat, doing, slowly, in a good bed in a good room, the same thing it had done on the hard guano floor of the roofless room twelve years before, by degrees, so that you did not see the day it finished.

She was very small in the bed. The cough had worked her down to almost nothing, the child's frame the rock had aged early now aged past all of it, and her eyes in the candlelight were the only quick thing left, the watchful island eyes, and they found him in the doorway and did the old thing, the jolt and then, this time, no iron coming down over it, because she was past the need of the iron now, past the rock's reach, dying in a bed where no guard would weigh her weeping, and she let the brother she had buried and unburied see her face do what it did, which was to break a little at the mouth and then to smile, the old fast crooked smile, the one the island had not, in the end, been able to stop.

"You came up the coast to count boxes," she said. Her voice was a thread under the cough. "Fifteen years and they still cannot make the books balance without you. They are fools. I always said they were fools."

"They are fools," Mei agreed, and sat by the bed, the width of the courtesy between them still, their mother's children to the last, though he could have crossed it now and she would have let him.

"Don't," she said, watching his face, reading it the way she had always read it faster than he could hold it. "Don't make the count. I can see you making the count. How many years, how many months. Don't. I made you dead on the ship to bear the not knowing; you can make me dead a little early to bear the knowing. It is easier. You taught me that."

"You taught me that," he said.

"Then we are even." The smile again, fading. "Brother. The girl." Her eyes went to the door, where a child stood, seven years old, dark and quick and watchful, A-Lian's own eyes set in a face that was the Cornish boy's and the Amoy girl's at once and foreign to both, a child caught between in the bones of her, standing very still in the doorway the way Mei stood still, the way A-Lian had taught herself to stand. "Carmen. Come. This is your uncle." And her hand came up off the coverlet — slow, costing her — and in it was a thing Mei had not seen in fifteen years, a small comb of dark wood, worn pale at the teeth, a thing from before the ship, a thing from the grey house on the lane, the one object she had carried through the press at Macao and the hold and the rock and the marriage and the brick house, her one thing from home, the home that was sold, the lane that was gone. She had kept it the way Mei kept the stick. She did not give it to the child. She only held it where the child could see it, and closed her hand over it again, and lay back, spent, and looked at her brother across the small courteous space, the two of them who had bought their lives in the same currency and knew the price and had never told another soul, and there was nothing left to keep from each other now, and so they kept nothing, and that was the worst of all.

He did not weep. He had decided about weeping a long time before, in a wineshop, watching a man draw the lie of eight. He sat with his sister while the candle burned down and the cough came and went and the child stood in the doorway holding the frame, and outside in the Lima dark the bells rang for some saint and the price of the cargo he had counted in the warehouse went up another two shillings on a telegram from London that no one in the room would read, and somewhere south of them the squadron lay at anchor over the rookery under the flag they had raised, and the birds, which did not stop even at night, poured back to the cliff in the dark the way they had poured back the first night he lay on the boards with his one eye open, the high far roar of the number too large to be a sound, fishing the shoal that the warm wrong water of a year not yet come would one day take from them entirely, indifferent to the flag and the price and the woman dying in the brick house, feeding their chicks, making, grain by grain through the rainless dark, the white mountain that the men below were spending and could not put back.

He stayed the night. In the morning the company needed him back at the warehouse, the cargo would not reconcile itself, the war would not pause for a dying woman, and he stood in the doorway to go, and A-Lian was awake, and she looked at him, and she said the last useful thing she would ever say to him, in the spare flat voice, the sister's voice, the island's:

"Own it." He did not understand her. She saw he did not, and the smile came, the last of it, crooked and fast and almost cruel with the truth of it. "The island. The count. I have watched you fifteen years not let yourself want the thing you want. You think to hold the pen makes you the thing that killed A-Yün. It does. Hold it anyway. Stand where the heavy man stood. Better you than another fool who cannot even keep the books." The cough took her then, and bent her, and when it eased she only had breath to add, very low, the thread of it nearly gone: "I am the lane. You are the well. Carry it." And he carried it out of the room and down the stairs and back to the warehouse and the slate, the comb left behind in the dying hand, the count carried against his ribs, the thing she had said set now where he could not unhear it.


## MANIFEST II — The War

### Chapter 11 · Asher Mei
*Chincha North, off Pisco · 1865 · guano ≈ £13/ton · Chincha working face ≈ 115 ft*

There was a right way to count a colony and a wrong way, and Asher had worked out the right way himself, which was the thing he was proudest of in the world, more than his name, more than the boots he had bought with his own coppers, more than the English he could read now if the words were not too long. You did not try to count the birds. That was the wrong way, the way a fool did it, standing on the cliff with his mouth open at the whole pouring darkness of them and saying *thousands, thousands,* a word that meant nothing, that was only a man admitting he could not count. The birds would not hold still to be counted and there were too many and they all looked the same. So you did not count the birds. You counted the *ground.*

You took a square of the rookery — he used the length of his own forearm laid down twice each way, a square he could pace, a square he had cut into the rock once with a nail so he could find it again — and you counted the nests in that one square, the low foul cups of muck and feather and fishbone, packed so close their rims touched, the guanay sitting them wing to wing with no more room between two birds than between two cobbles in a Lima street. He had counted that square eleven times across the spring and got, on the good days, between nine and eleven nests in it, and he took the middle, ten, and he had paced the breadth of the seaward shoulder where the guanay nested thickest, two hundred and forty of his paces, and the depth of it back from the cliff, ninety, and a boy who knew his sums — and he did, he had taught himself off the tally-clerks, off the very arithmetic the company used to weigh the cargo — a boy who knew his sums could lay the one square over the whole ground in his head and come out with a number that was not *thousands.* It was a number with a shape. It was, this spring, on the north shoulder alone, something past two hundred thousand nesting pair, and that was one shoulder of one island, and there were three islands, and the boobies nested separate on the open ledges and the great pelicans separate again on the low rocks to the south, and when he had done all of it, all the shoulders and all the ledges of all three, and added the birds at sea that were not nesting, the unmated and the young, the river of them that poured out at dawn to the shoal and poured back at dusk — when he had done the whole sum he got a number he wrote very small at the back of the book because it frightened him a little to have it down in ink, and the number was twelve million, near enough, and he had got it himself, out of one square of muck the length of his arm laid down twice.

He thought it was the best thing anyone on these islands had ever done. He did not say so. There was no one to say it to.

The book was new. He had bought it in Pisco off a chandler's counter in the same hour he bought the boots, a real bound book with a marbled cover and pages sewn not pasted, meant for a ship's accounts, and the chandler had looked at the boy putting down good coin for a ledger and asked him what he kept in it and Asher had said, in his careful counting-house English, *birds, sir,* and the man had laughed, and Asher had not minded, because the man was a fool who counted the wrong way. He had ruled the first pages himself with a borrowed straight-edge: a column for the date, a column for the place, a column for the count, a column he headed *Remarks* because he had seen that word at the top of a column in a tally-clerk's book and thought it the most beautiful word in the language, a column where a man might put down anything at all that he had truly seen. Under *Remarks* he had so far written, in his small upright hand: *the chick is grey and the parent black and white. they feed it by the throat. a square holds ten. they do not mind the guns.*

They did not mind the guns. That was the thing he had come out today, against all sense, to see whether it was still true.

The guns had been going since before light. They came across the water from the north, from off Callao way and from somewhere nearer, a flat thudding that you felt in the rock through the soles of the boots before you heard it in the air, and then the air would catch up and carry the sound itself, a long rolling cough out at sea, and twice in the morning a column of grey had stood up over the water to the north and leaned and spread and gone. The Spanish were doing something to somebody. They had been on the islands a year now, the Spanish, ever since the morning the boy Asher had woken to find a flag he did not know flying over the scale-house where the company's house-flag had flown, red and yellow, and grown men weeping in the barracks-lane — not the diggers, the diggers wept at nothing the Spanish did, the diggers had been weeping at this place since before the Spanish were born — but the Peruvians, the foremen and the boatmen and the lighter-crews, men who had cursed Peru every day of their lives standing in this stink, going about that morning with wet faces because some far-off thing called Peru that owned them had been taken at gunpoint and they found, to their own surprise, that it had been theirs.

Asher had felt nothing about the flag. This was a thing he knew about himself and did not examine too closely. He had been born on this rock, in a shed in the barracks-lane, of a mother who was dead and a father who did not speak, and no flag had ever flown for him, not the company's and not Peru's and not the dragon-flag of the country his father came from that he had never seen and did not intend to. He belonged to no one who could be taken at gunpoint. When the men wept for Peru he had stood at the edge of it and thought, with a coldness he was a little ashamed of even at the time: *I will belong to myself. I will be a man of my own making and no country will weep for me or need to.* He was twelve when he thought it. He was thirteen now and he had not changed his mind, only bought boots.

The shoulder was a half-hour's clamber from the lane, around the seaward side where the company did not work because the cliff was too sheer there to run a chute, and that was why the guanay had it, that one strip of the island the barrows could not reach. He went down the goat-path he had found, the boots gripping where his bare feet used to slip, the book buttoned inside his shirt against his chest with the pencil — a real graphite pencil, English, the third great purchase, he had been very rich for one afternoon in Pisco — and he came out onto the lip above the rookery into the wall of it, the noise and the reek that even he, who had breathed this air since his first breath, even he had to stand a moment under.

It was not the company's smell. He knew the difference the way a Lima man knows wine. The company's smell, the smell off the cut face where the diggers worked the old packed deposit, was the dead smell, the buried smell, ammonia and a thousand years of it pressed to stone, the smell that had taken his father's eye and put the cough in his father's chest and would, Asher supposed without much feeling, put it in his own one day. But the rookery was the *live* smell, sharp and wet and loud with it, the smell of two hundred thousand pair of birds making the deposit right now, this morning, while the guns thudded — fish and salt and the hot green stink of fresh muck and under it all the warm dusty smell of feathers and of the grey chicks panting in the sun, and it was, he thought, a thing he would never say to anyone, a *good* smell. The company sold the one and could not have sold the other and they were the same smell forty years apart. He did not have words for what that meant and he did not try to find them. He took out the book.

He found his square. He knelt. He counted. *Eleven.* He wrote it under the date.

And the guns went, away to the north, a long roll of them, three and then a fourth, and Asher lifted his head and watched the colony to see what they would do.

They did nothing. That was the wonder of it, the thing he had come to confirm, the thing he could not stop being amazed by though he had seen it a hundred mornings. A grown man hearing those guns went still inside; the diggers up at the face stopped their barrows and looked north; even his father, who looked at nothing, would turn his good eye toward the sound. But the birds did not stop. The guanay on the nests sat their nests. The guanay at sea kept fishing the shoal. The dawn-river of them poured out over the grey water to the north *through* the rising smoke of whatever was burning out there, and Asher watched a whole long skein of cormorants fly straight over a standing column of gun-smoke, fly through the very top of it, and come out the other side and bend down to the water and dive, and not one of them so much as faltered. They did not mind the guns because the guns were not in the book. Hunger was in the book. The shoal was in the book. The warm or cold of the water and the hour of the tide were in the book. Two empires had come a third of the way around the world to fight over the ground these birds were standing on, and to the birds it was weather, less than weather, it was nothing, because no gun had ever once kept a guanay from a herring, and the birds kept only the accounts that fed them.

He wrote it under *Remarks*, very pleased, getting the smoke and the skein exactly. *The guns are loud today. They fly through the smoke to the shoal and do not break the line. They keep only the count that feeds them.* He read it back. It was the truest thing he had ever written and he had got it himself.

He did not let himself think the other thought, the one that came up the underside of the true one like a fish coming up under a lure, the thought that he kept only the count that fed *him*, the boots and the book and the coppers and the English, the man-of-his-own-making, and that there was a column he did not rule and did not keep, headed with the names of the people he had come out of, and he had left it blank on purpose, and a boy who left a column blank on purpose was not so different from a bird flying through smoke. He did not think it. He was very good at not thinking it. He turned the page and went down the shoulder to count the boobies.

*

> *Twelve million birds over the three islands, near enough, and the smoke of the war is one more weather over the shoal. A schooner burns to the north and the cold green water does not warm by a single degree, so the anchoveta hold where they have always held, a half-fathom down in the upwelling, and the guanay go to them through the smoke as they go to them through fog, through gale, through the falling of nights past counting. They have done this since before there was a Pisco to lie off, before there was a flag to lower or raise, fishing the same shoal and roosting the same rock and dropping the same white onto it grain by grain by grain until in a thousand years of dropping it stands a hundred and twenty feet into the air. They are not building it. They do not know it is there. It is only what is left of every herring they ever ate, going down behind them onto the stone, the long slow sediment of their hunger — and it is worth, this year, thirteen pounds the long ton at the chute, and the men below are killing the men below over the right to carry it away. The birds keep the only ledger that has ever balanced on these islands: a herring in, a grain of the white down. They do not look up at the smoke. They look down at the water, where the food is.*

He had been at the booby ledges an hour, where the count was harder because the *piqueros* nested looser and you could not lay a square on them, when he found the girl.

She was sitting where no one sat, out past the last ledge on the flat shelf of rock above the deep water, a Lima child in a Lima dress with her shoes set neatly beside her and her bare feet over the edge, and she was doing a thing he had never once seen another human being on this island do. She was watching the birds. Not waiting, not lost, not crying, not being minded by anyone — there was no one anywhere near, which on these islands meant either she was a fool or she had got away on purpose, and she did not look a fool. She was watching the boobies plunge. When a *piquero* folded and dropped — they hit the water like a thrown knife, from forty feet up, a white needle and a small hard splash and gone — she followed it down with her whole head, and when it came up she watched it come up, and she had been doing it, he could tell by her stillness, for a long time.

Asher stood with his book and considered her the way he considered a bird he could not name. She was eight or nine. Dark, quick, with her hair coming loose from under a comb that did not match the dress — a plain dark comb, foreign, old, the kind of thing a poor woman might have carried a long way, and it sat oddly in the careful Lima hair, and he noticed it the way he noticed everything, and filed it, and did not yet know it was a thing he would remember for the rest of his life. She had a cough. He heard it while he stood there, a small dry catch she put her wrist to without seeming to notice she did it, the way you scratch in your sleep, and Asher, who had that same small dry catch and the same wrist-habit, felt something he had no column for at all.

"You're counting them wrong," she said, without turning around.

He had not known she knew he was there.

"I'm not counting them at all," he said. "I counted them in the spring. I'm counting the *piqueros* now. The white ones."

"The ones that fall down."

"They don't fall. They dive. It's called plunging. They go in after the —" he had the word, he was proud of the word — "after the anchoveta. The little fish, in the shoal. They can see it from up there and they fold their wings and drop on it."

She turned around then and looked at him, and he saw she had been crying earlier, hours earlier, the way you can see weather that has passed, and that she had stopped and made herself watch the birds instead, and Asher understood that exactly, because it was the thing he did, it was the *only* thing he did, and he had never met another person who did it. You could not bear a thing, so you counted a thing. You put the unbearable down and you picked up the count.

"My mother died on this island," she said. Flatly, a fact, a thing you would put in a ledger. "Up there. In the lane. Two nights ago. They wouldn't let me see her after, only before, and before she couldn't talk, only the cough. So I came down here. There's nowhere on this whole island that isn't —" she stopped. She did the wrist to her mouth. "I found the birds," she said. "The white ones falling. So I'm watching the white ones."

Asher stood very still. He knew who she was. He had not put it together until she said *the lane* and *the cough* and he had seen the comb, but he knew now: she was the digger-woman's girl, A-Lian's girl, the one married off years back to a Cornishman, the agent's son — the girl who was, by a tangle of blood Asher had never bothered to trace because he traced nothing of that kind on purpose, some kind of cousin to him, because A-Lian was his father's sister, and so this coughing Lima child watching the boobies fall was the nearest blood he had in the world besides the silent half-blind man at the face who had made him. And her mother had died two nights ago in the lane. His aunt. The cough had finished his aunt, the same cough the child had, the same cough he had, and he had been a half-hour's clamber away on the seaward shoulder these two days, counting nests, getting eleven to the square, writing *they do not mind the guns,* and he had not known, and no one had told him, because there was no one whose business it was to tell Asher Mei anything.

He did not say any of it. The wall in him that he had built so carefully, the man-of-his-own-making, held — but he felt it, for the first time in his life, as a *wall,* a made thing with another side to it, and not simply as the edge of the world.

"Anchoveta," he said instead, because it was what he had, "is what they all eat. The white ones and the black ones and the big ones with the pouches. All of them. The same little fish." He came and sat down on the rock, not close, a careful arm's length, and set the book open on his knee where she could see the columns if she looked. "If you want," he said, "I'll show you how to count them the right way. Not the birds. The birds you can't count, there's too many. You count the ground."

She looked at the book. She looked at the columns, the date and the place and the count and *Remarks,* and at the small upright writing, and Asher watched a Lima child who two nights ago had lost her mother in the worst place on earth bend her dark wet-lashed eyes over a column of figures about birds, and go still over it, and stop, for one breath, being a child who could not bear a thing, and become a person doing a sum. He knew the exact moment it happened because it was the moment it always happened to him. Something in her unclenched.

"Ten to a square," she read out, slow, off the *Remarks.* "Is that — is that always? Ten?"

"Nine to eleven. I take the middle." He felt his face do a thing it did not often do. "Most people," he said, "say thousands. Thousands is what you say when you can't count."

"I can count," the girl said.

"I know," said Asher. "I could tell."

*

He showed her the square. He paced it out for her on the booby shelf, two of his forearms each way, and she paced it after him solemnly with her own small arms and got a smaller square and they argued about whose arm to use, and settled, after some negotiation that was the first ordinary conversation Asher had had with another young person in his whole strange life, on hers, because the square was hers to count. She counted the boobies in it twice and got three and then four and was furious at herself for the difference until Asher told her the right way was to take the count many times and that the difference was not a mistake, the difference was the *truth,* the birds moved, a good count had a difference in it, and a count with no difference was a lie a man had told to look tidy. She thought about that for a while, frowning at the shelf.

"My grandfather keeps a count," she said at last. "The Cornish one. In a book. He has a man write it." She paused. "It's tidy."

Asher said nothing. He did not know yet, and would not for years, what she had just told him.

"My other grandfather keeps a count too," she said. "Not in a book. On a stick. He won't show me what it counts."

And Asher knew that stick. Everyone in the barracks-lane knew the stick, the tally-man's private stick that he kept against his ribs and would let no man see, and Asher knew whose it was, and his face closed without his meaning it to, and the girl, who counted the wrong way at the birds but the right way at faces, saw it close.

"You know him," she said.

"He's my father," Asher said. The first time he had said it to anyone in he could not remember how long. It came out flat and hard, like *the chick is grey, the parent black and white.* A fact for the *Remarks* column.

The girl looked at him a long moment with her mother's watchfulness that she did not know she had inherited any more than she knew where the comb had come from, and then she looked back at the book on his knee, and she did the wisest thing anyone had ever done to Asher Mei: she let it go. She did not say *then you're my cousin* or *then we're family* or any of the things a Lima drawing-room would have made her say. She put her finger on the booby column instead and said, "Write four. We took it twice and got three and four, and four was the last, so write four. And put under *Remarks* that they argue with you about the square."

He wrote four. Under *Remarks* he wrote: *C. counts well. We could not agree the square. Hers is smaller and we used hers.* He did not write what the C. stood for because he was not sure yet that he was allowed to, and because writing the whole name would have made it a thing in the book, a kept thing, a column he had sworn not to rule. But he wrote the C. He let the one letter stand.

*

The guns had stopped. He noticed it the way you notice a wheel that has been squeaking go quiet — a wrongness, an attention freed up. The smoke to the north had thinned and gone and the afternoon had come down hot and flat and white over the water, and the dusk-river of the birds was beginning, the long skeins coming back from the shoal to the rookeries to feed the grey chicks, pouring up off the sea and bending in over the cliffs, more of them and more, the air filling and darkening with the homing of them until the whole sky over the islands moved.

They watched it together, the boy who had sworn off all belonging and the girl who had just lost the woman she belonged to, sitting an arm's length apart on the rock with the book between them, while twelve million birds came home over a mountain that two empires were killing each other to own and that had been a hundred and eighty feet high in the year of Asher's birth and stood, he had paced its shadow against the old high marks that very week, hardly more than a hundred and fifteen now, sixty-five feet of it gone in his own short lifetime, gone out past the queue of waiting ships at thirteen pound the ton, gone into the soil of countries neither of them would ever see. He did not say that either. He did not yet have the whole of it, only the pieces — the dropping mountain, the climbing price the foremen recited like a catechism, the count of the birds he had got himself and written so small at the back because it frightened him — and he did not know that the three numbers belonged to one sum, or that a girl beside him would one day be the one to weigh them all against each other and find them out. He only knew it was beautiful, the coming home of them, and that for the first time in his life there was someone beside him who had stopped saying *thousands* and was watching it the right way.

"It's the most birds in the world here," the girl said quietly. "Isn't it. It must be."

"It's twelve million," said Asher. "On the three islands. I counted it. Off one square." And then, because she had let his father go, because she had used her own arm, because she had a cough he knew and a comb he would remember and was the nearest blood he had and had not made him say so — because of all of it, he heard himself add a thing he had never said aloud and had not known he believed until it was out of his mouth in the failing light: "It was more, before. There's fewer every spring. I've got it in the book."

The girl turned and looked at him.

"Fewer," she said. Not a question. She was eight, and she had a tidy grandfather and an untidy one and a dead mother in the lane, and she had just learned to count the right way, and Asher watched the word go into her and settle, the way his own frightening number had settled into him, a fact taken down in ink that you cannot afterward un-know.

"A little fewer," he said. "Every spring. I don't know why." This last was the only lie he told her, and he told it to be kind, because he was beginning to know why, he had been on the seaward shoulder the spring before last and seen the company's cut reach the old guanay ground on the north face and the barrows go over the nests to get the deposit beneath, and he had said nothing then and had not put it in the book, the one thing he had ever seen and not written down, because to write it down was to keep it, and he did not want to keep it, and he was learning that there were things you could decline to write in a book and still, exactly, know.
