He closed the marbled cover on the small frightening number and the C. that stood for her name and the birds that did not mind the guns, and the two of them sat on while the colony came home, not speaking, counting in the failing light the only thing on that whole island either of them could bear to count, which was the birds, and not the dead.


## MANIFEST II — The War

### Chapter 12 · Rosa de la Cruz
*Lima · 1865 · guano ≈ £13/ton (war premium) · Chincha working face ≈ 115 ft*

The sickroom faced the wrong way, and Rosa had known it for a fault in the house since the day she bought the house, and had never thought she would be glad of it.

It faced the patio and not the street, so that the cart-noise and the bell of the water-seller and the priests going up to the Mercedarian church did not come into it; and it took the afternoon sun late and weakly off the whitewashed wall opposite, which meant it was cool in the worst of the day, which the doctor — the real doctor, the Frenchman, not the herb-woman from the Malambo whom Rosa had also sent for and paid more and trusted further — had said was the one thing that could be done. *Keep her cool, keep the air moving, keep her propped.* He had said it the way a man says a thing he knows will not work but that costs him nothing to recommend, and he had charged eight soles for the visit and looked at his hands in the patio afterward as if he wanted to wash them, and Rosa had paid him and read in the not-quite-meeting of his eyes the figure he had not said aloud, which was: *weeks. Perhaps not weeks.* She was good at the figures men did not say aloud. She had built her whole life on the reading of them.

So the sickroom faced the patio. And A-Lian, who had spent five years in the worst-aired place on the earth and was now dying of it in the best-aired room a guano agent's money could buy in Lima, lay propped on the bolsters with the late sun coming weak and gold across the foot of the bed, and watched the wall, and breathed.

That was the thing about the room you could not get used to. The breathing. It had a sound now that it had not had a month ago, a wet pull and a click at the bottom of it, like a pump working a well that has gone low, like the suck of the tide going out over shingle — Rosa had grown up on the Pisco mole and knew that sound from the wrong end, from the side that drowned men — and it went on, and on, through the whole afternoon, and you could be three rooms away in the cool of the sala with a guest and a tray of *alfajores* and the talk of who had taken which pew at the Cathedral for the feast of the Asunción, and under all of it, if you were Rosa and the house was yours and the dying woman in it was yours to keep, you could hear it. The pump. The well going low.

*Madre,* Rosa thought, the way she thought it forty times a day, the way her grandmother had thought it over a gutting-board, *Madre de la Merced, ten piedad.* And meant by it nothing the Frenchman would have recognized and everything her grandmother would. Have mercy. Not on the soul. Rosa would see to the soul; the soul had a price and a procedure and she had begun on both. Have mercy on the *breathing.* Make it stop being a thing a house could hear.

She had not asked for any of this. She wished to be honest with herself about that, in the one room of her own head she kept honest, the room with the columns in it where she did the sums no one saw. She had a husband on the islands — common-law, the priests would say, and she had heard the word *manceba* said behind a fan at the Aliaga's fifteen years ago and had outlived the woman who said it and bought her dead husband's box at the Cathedral, which was its own kind of answer — she had a husband on the islands, gone three months now and not expected before the rains, and a war on so that no one knew when the ships would run clear of the Spanish, and two daughters of her own to settle in a Lima that would always, always make them pay the surcharge for their father's trade and their mother's birth; and she had not asked, on top of all of it, to nurse her husband's son's Chinese wife to death in the good front room.

But here was the truth of it, the figure under the figure: she had asked. Not in words. But when Mei had come to her — Mei, the husband's man, the tally-keeper, the one whose name Liddell said the way you say the name of a sound tool — when Mei had come to the house in Lima with his clouded eye and his stillness and the thing he could not say sitting in his throat like a stone, and had stood in her patio with his hat in his two scarred hands and looked at her and not asked, because asking was a debt and the man would die before he took on one more debt he could not pay — when he had done all that, Rosa had looked at him and known exactly what he was there for and exactly what it would cost her and exactly what the city would say, and had said, before he could humiliate himself by finding the words, *Bring her here. There is a room.*

She had done it for the children, she told herself. That was true and it was not the whole of it. There was a thing she did it for that she had no column for. The man had stood in her patio unable to speak, and she had recognized the silence. She had spent her life among men who could not love and could count to the last centavo, and here was a man who could not say *please help my sister* and would carry every grain of dung off that island on his own back if it would buy her one more cool morning, and Rosa, who had married salvage and knew exactly what she was and what she would always be to the man on the islands, had wanted — fiercely, against her own interest, against the room and the fan and the surcharge — to be, just once, on the giving side of a mercy that did not have a price stamped on it.

So. The good room. The Frenchman's eight soles. The well going low.

*

The children were not to come in. Rosa had ruled it on the first day and broken her own rule by the third, because you could not keep a child of eight from a mother by ruling against it; the child only learned to do it in secret, and a thing done in secret bred a worse thing. So Carmen came in the afternoons, after the rest, when A-Lian had her breath the most under her, and sat on the low stool by the bed where Rosa could watch them both from the door.

The girl was a strange one and Rosa had given up pretending she understood her. Eight years old, dark and quick, with her mother's watching eyes and a stillness that did not belong to a child — she would sit on the stool for an hour without fidgeting, the way no child of Rosa's own had ever sat, and she did not chatter at her mother the way a frightened child should, did not ask the loud blunt questions — *are you better, when will you get up, why does it sound like that* — that Rosa had braced for and known how to deflect. She watched. She had a way of watching that was the dead image of the tally-man's, and Rosa supposed she had it from him, from the hours he must have sat with her, the two of them counting whatever it was they counted in their silence; or supposed, in the honest room, that the child had it from the same place he did, which was the place where you learn early that no one is going to do your noticing for you.

"You are holding your breath," A-Lian said to the girl.

Rosa, in the doorway, went still. It was the most A-Lian had said in a stretch all day. Her voice came thin off the top of the wet pull, costing her, and she spent it on this.

The girl said nothing. But Rosa, watching from the door, saw her let it out — saw the small shoulders come down a quarter-inch — and understood that the child had been doing it for some time, breathing shallow and slow in time with her mother, the way you match your step to a person you are walking close beside, the way Rosa had caught herself doing in the night when the pump-sound got into her own chest; that the girl had been sitting there in the gold light breathing as little as her mother breathed, keeping her company down at the low end of the well.

"You do not have to do that," A-Lian said. There was something under the thinness of it that took Rosa a moment to place, and then she placed it and it went through her like the cold off the Pisco water: it was amusement. The woman was *amused.* "I am not racing you, *ya-tou.* Breathe like a fat priest. Breathe like the French doctor. He breathes like a man who has somewhere to be."

The girl made a sound that in another room, in another house, would have been a laugh.

"There," said A-Lian, and closed her eyes, satisfied, and let the wall have her again, and the breathing went back to being the only thing in the room.

Rosa stood in the doorway with the figure rising in her that she would not look at, the figure that had no column. She had thought, when she took this on, that she was sheltering a victim. She had been wrong, and she had been wrong in a way that shamed her, because she of all women should have known better — she who had walked into the Aliaga sala four months pregnant and friendless and made the old families warm a half-degree toward her by sheer refusal to be pitied — she should have known that a person does not get *to* the worst-aired place on earth and *out* of it again and into your good front room by being a thing that suffering happens to. The woman in the bed had a will in her that the islands had spent five years and had not been able to spend, and only the lungs, in the end, were going to take it, and she knew it, and she lay there spending what was left of her breath making her daughter laugh and the doctor a figure of fun, and Rosa — Rosa who had buried more of herself in this city than anyone living knew — understood that she was not nursing a death. She was attending a person who had decided how she meant to do the one thing left to do, and meant to do it on her own terms, and would no more take Rosa's pity than Mei would take her charity, and that the kindest thing the good room could offer was to let her.

*Madre, ten piedad.* On me, this time. For not having known.

*

Liddell sent money. That was Liddell's love and Rosa had stopped, long ago, waiting for the other kind, and had learned to read the love that came in the only tongue the man had.

The letter came up from Callao with the ship's mail, in his hand, dry as a bill of lading and about as warm, and it was three lines about the war — the Spanish still sat on the Chinchas, the loading thrown into the lesser islands, the freight and the insurance gone to the devil, the price holding at thirteen pound the ton and would hold while the cargo could not be got out clean, *and a man who has guano in a bottom that runs the blockade this season will name his own figure* — and then one line, the fourth, that was the love. *Spare nothing on the woman. Whatever the Frenchman wants, pay it. The boy's wife is to want for nothing.*

Rosa read it twice. *The boy's wife.* Not A-Lian; the man had likely never once said her name; she was a position on his board, *the boy's wife,* a fact attached to his dead son's life, and the instruction was the same instruction he would give about a fouled anchor or a sick horse — *spend on it, do not stint, it is mine* — and it was, Rosa knew, the largest thing the man was capable of, the absolute top of his love, *spare nothing,* and she had married it with her eyes open and she sat now in the cool of the sala with the letter in her lap and the well going low three rooms off and did the cruel small sum she could not help doing, because she was his and she did the sums too: that thirteen pound the ton, holding, while the war kept the white locked up on the rock under the Spanish guns; that a man who got a bottom out clean this season would name his figure; that the worse the war went, the higher the price stood; that somewhere out past the blockade the mountain that had put the cough in this woman's chest when she was eleven years old was at this moment, by the very war that was killing the trade, worth more by the ton than it had ever been worth in the history of the world.

The white was on the banknote folded in with the letter — a draft on the house in Callao, drawn for two hundred soles against the doctor's bills, and across the fold of it, when Rosa unfolded it, lay the faintest powder, a dust so fine it was almost nothing, that had come off the cargo in the hold of whatever bottom had brought the mail up from the islands and salted itself into the paper the way it salted into everything that came off the rock. She brushed it with her thumb. It left a pale smear on the pad of her thumb and a fainter one on the green of the draft. Two hundred soles, and a little of the white, riding up together from the same island in the same hold.

Three rooms away the pump pulled and clicked. She did not make the connection a thing she said. She was not a woman who said such things; saying them was a weakness, like pity, like hope. But she had a memory shaped like a ledger, and it kept its columns whether she willed it or no, and it entered the two figures side by side without being asked — *two hundred soles* and *the well going low* — and ruled no line between them, and left them there, the way you leave two coins on a counter for a man who has not yet come to take his change. Then she folded the draft and went to send for the Frenchman, who would want it, and to pay him out of it.

*

A-Lian sent for her on the eighth evening, after the girl had gone, in the hour when the light failed and before the candles, the hour Rosa hated in a sickroom because the room went grey and the breathing got louder in the grey.

"Doña Rosa." She had never called her anything else; she had a courtesy in her, an exactness about the forms, that Rosa had not expected and had come to value — the woman knew precisely what she was in this house and precisely what Rosa was, and used the right word for it, the way Rosa used the right word in a drawing-room, two women who had each learned that the forms were a kind of armor you could not afford to be careless with. "You have been good. I will not waste your breath thanking you, because you do not like it, and because I have not got the breath to waste." A pull, a click, a rest. "I want to ask you a thing, and you will say yes too quickly, the way you say yes, so I will tell you the cost first."

"Tell me the cost," Rosa said, and sat on the stool the girl used, because she understood that the woman was paying her the compliment of doing business, and meant to do it back.

"When I am dead," A-Lian said — flatly, the words costing her no more than the other words, a tile-maker's daughter laying a tile straight — "Liddell will want my children sent to him. To the English house. He will not say it unkindly. He will say it the way he says everything, that it is sense, that he can give them what the rock cannot, that a Penhale child is a Penhale child." The grey eye, the only thing in the failing room with any light left in it, came around to Rosa. "And my brother will want them. He will not say anything at all. He will only be there in the morning, every morning, in the patio, with his hat in his hands, until someone gives them to him or tells him to go." The faintest twist at the mouth, the amusement again, terrible now in the grey. "You see my difficulty. One of them cannot speak and one of them cannot stop pricing, and between them they will tear my Carmen in half and call it providing for her, and neither of them will once have asked the child what she wants, because to one of them she is an asset and to the other she is a debt, and a man does not ask his asset or his debt what it wants."

Rosa said nothing. It was, to the centavo, the truth, and she had thought it herself and not said it, and to hear the dying woman say it aloud — *to one of them she is an asset and to the other she is a debt* — was to hear named the thing Rosa had married and lived inside for fifteen years, the cold tongue, the love that could only count; and to hear it named not in bitterness but in a kind of clear-eyed, dying tenderness, by a woman trying in her last days to do the one piece of business that mattered, was almost more than the room could hold.

"What do you ask," Rosa said.

"Keep them in Lima." A-Lian's hand came up off the coverlet — slow, the hand a child's still, the knuckles too large for it, a hand that had cut dung from a cliff at eleven and would never grow into itself now — and it found Rosa's wrist and held it, with no strength at all, with the weight of a sleeping bird. "Not the English house in Callao, where she will be a Penhale and learn to look at a person and see a sum. Not handed only to my brother, who loves her past saying and will raise her in his silence and his counting and his grief until she is as walled-up as he is. Here. Your house. Where there is a war on and a smell in the curtains and two old men who built their whole lives on the same stinking rock from the two ends of it, and where, at least, between the lot of you, the child might be — " A pull. A click. A long rest, and Rosa thought she had finished, and then she had not. " — might be *more than counted.*"

The hand fell back. The effort of it had cost her the next four breaths; Rosa sat and held her own and waited, the way the girl had taught her without meaning to, until the woman had them back.

"She is named Carmen," A-Lian said, when she could, "because I named her for this city and not for mine. I had nothing of mine to give her that this city would not take the surcharge on. A name was the one thing they could not charge me for. So I gave her a Lima name, so that she would belong somewhere all the way, the way I never have and my brother never will and you, Doña Rosa — forgive me — the way you have spent your whole life paying to and never quite — " The twist at the mouth, gentle. "We are alike in that. I knew it the first time you spoke to me. Two women who married into a belonging and got the bill instead."

Rosa found that she could not answer. She, who could answer anything, who had answered the Osma girl and the Errázuriz widow and a city full of women with knives behind their fans, sat on the low stool in the grey light and held the wrist of a dying woman she had known eight days and had no answer.

"There is one more thing," A-Lian said. "It is small. It is the only thing I brought out. Everything else they took or it rotted on the rock." Her chin moved, the smallest gesture, toward the little table by the bed where the medicines stood and the water-glass and the rosary Rosa had put there which A-Lian had not touched and had not asked Rosa to remove, a courtesy on both sides. "In the cloth there. Take it."

Rosa rose and unfolded the square of worn cloth on the table — it had been folded so long the creases had gone to cracks — and inside it lay a comb. A woman's comb, the kind for the back of the hair, carved of pale horn gone amber with handling, the teeth of it close and fine, and along the spine a line of small flowers cut into the horn by a hand that had known what it was doing, plum-flowers, Rosa thought, though she had never seen a plum-tree; little five-petalled flowers worn nearly smooth now where a thumb had run over them across — how many years. It was the only object in the room, Rosa realized, that had come from across the sea. Everything else in the good front room was Lima — the bed, the bolsters, the whitewash, the Frenchman's bottles, the rosary A-Lian would not pray on, the city's whole apparatus of dying-well — and this one small comb of amber horn, with its plum-flowers worn down by a thumb, was Amoy, was the grey house on the lane that her brother had told Rosa, once, in his three or four sentences, was sold now for a debt and gone; was the one grain of a homeland that had made it across the floating hell and through the five years on the rock and into the good front room in Lima, in a square of cloth, to lie now in the palm of a Pisco fish-girl who had married salvage.

"It was my mother's," A-Lian said. "She put it in my hair the morning the broker took me, because I was crying, and she said a girl with a comb like that in her hair was a girl from somewhere, and no one could make her be from nowhere. She was wrong about that. They made me be from nowhere by the second week. But I kept the comb." The eyes were closed now. "Give it to Carmen. When she is older. When she asks — and she will ask, she is a child who asks things to herself and not aloud, you have seen it — when she asks where she is from, give her the comb, and tell her it is from somewhere, and that her grandmother put it in the hair of a girl who was crying so that the girl would know she was from somewhere too. Do not tell her it did not work." A breath. "Let her find that out the slow way, like the rest of us."

Rosa closed her hand around the comb. The horn was warm — it had lain in the cloth in the warm room — and worn, and very light, and she thought, holding it, of the weight of it, which was nothing, which was less than the dust on the banknote, less than a single barrow of the white going down a chute into a lighter; and thought how a thing could weigh nothing and be the whole of a person's home, the only thing the sea and the rock and the empire had between them failed to weigh and take; and did not say any of it, because saying it was a weakness, and because the woman in the bed had not the breath for it and had asked her for a deed and not a sentiment.

"I will keep it for her," Rosa said. "And I will keep them both in Lima. I give you my word, and you may trust my word, because I am a woman who has never in her life given anything away that I had not first reckoned the cost of, and I have reckoned this one, and I am giving it to you anyway. That is the most I have. It will have to do."

A-Lian smiled — a real one, the last full one Rosa would see her make — and said, "It is more than enough. It is more than either of them would have offered, and they have all the money in the world." And then, because she was who she was and would not end on the soft thing any more than Liddell would: "Light the candles, Doña Rosa. I do not like this grey. It is the color of the island in the morning before the sun, and I have seen enough of that to last me."

Rosa lit the candles.

*

She died four days after, in the small hours, with Rosa in the chair by the bed and the candles burned down and the patio dark and the city quiet but for the watchman's far call and a dog; and there was no drama in it, no death-rattle of the kind the romances promised and the priests half-wanted; there was only the pump-sound, the wet pull and the click at the bottom of the well, going slower across the last hour, and longer between, until Rosa — who had been listening so long and so close that the sound was in her own chest, the way it had got into the girl's — heard a pull, and waited for the click, and the click did not come, and waited for the next pull, the room utterly still, the candle-flames straight as plumb-lines, and the next pull did not come either; and the silence that came instead was the loudest thing the good front room had ever held, louder than the breathing had ever been, because it was the breathing's exact shape with the breathing taken out of it.

Rosa sat in it a while. There was no hurry now; the figures had all been done; there was nothing left to read in the not-meeting of anyone's eyes. She said the words her grandmother would have said, in the form her grandmother had used, not because she was sure of the soul's road but because the saying was owed and she paid what was owed: *Madre de la Merced, ruega por ella. Have mercy. She is from somewhere.* And found, saying it, that her face was wet, which surprised her, because she had not given this woman leave to cost her anything she had not budgeted, and grief was a thing she had a strict column for, and here it was running over the column and down her face in the candlelight for a Chinese woman she had known twelve days, who had refused her pity and made her daughter laugh and named her child for a city that would charge the child a surcharge all her life, and had given Rosa, in the end, the one small worn amber thing she had carried out of the wreck of everything, to keep.

When it was light she sent the boy to Callao for the priest and the box and to carry the word to the house, and she went out into the cold patio, into the grey she had been told the woman could not bear, the color of the island before the sun; and Mei was there.

He was in the patio by the well, with his hat in his two hands, and Rosa understood that he had been there some part of the night — that he had come up from the islands or wherever he had been and had not let them wake her, had simply stood in the cold patio in the grey with his hat in his hands the way A-Lian had said he would stand, *every morning, in the patio, until someone gives them to him or tells him to go* — and that he knew already, had known the moment Rosa came out into the grey with her face the way it was, because the man read a face the way his sister had said he read a count, and there was nothing to tell him.

He did not ask. He did not weep; she had not expected it; the man's grief, she saw, went the way of his love and his asking, down into the stone in his throat, where it would sit and be carried and never once be set down. He stood and looked at Rosa with his clouded eye and his good one, and the not-asking was so loud in the cold patio that Rosa, who had buried her own answer to that not-asking thirteen days ago when she said *bring her here, there is a room,* found that she had one more thing to give him, and gave it.

"She asked me to keep the children in Lima," Rosa said. "In this house. Not the English house. She asked me, and I gave her my word, and Liddell will fight me on it and I will win, because it is the one fight in fifteen years I mean to win against him, and he knows me, and he will let me have it because it costs him only a thing he does not value, which is a child near him, and he will tell himself it is sense. So they will be here. With me. And you — " She stopped. She was not a woman who offered things; she had offered two in two weeks and it had nearly unmade her. But the man stood in the grey with his hat in his hands and his sister four days dead behind the wall, and Rosa was the giving side of this, had chosen to be, and would finish it. "You will come whenever you come. There will be no morning you stand in this patio and are turned away. The girl knows you. She watches the way you watch. She will need the one of us who counts and the one of us who — " she heard herself reach for A-Lian's word and take it — "who lets her be more than counted. Between us. Do you understand me."

The man's jaw worked. Whatever was in the stone in his throat moved against it and did not come up. He looked at the well, and at the grey wall, and at last he did the only thing he had: he took, from inside his coat, against his ribs, a length of pale wood the size of his forearm, worn smooth, cut along one edge with notches, more notches than Rosa could count in the grey light — a great many, a column of them, close-set and deep — and he held it a moment in his two hands the way he had held the hat, and Rosa understood that she was being shown a thing that was not shown, that this was the most the man could say and that it was an enormous thing he was saying, though she did not know its grammar; and then with the small knife that lived in his coat with the stick he cut, low down on the wood, in the last clear span of it, below all the others, one notch. Clean. Square. Deep enough, Rosa thought, watching, to find with a fingertip in the dark.

He did not say a name over it. But his lips moved, once, on a shape Rosa could not read — a sound in his own tongue, his sister's, the one the city had charged her a surcharge for and the comb had failed to keep her out of *nowhere* with — and then the stick went back inside his coat against his ribs, and the man put his hat on, and Rosa knew that the books had been made true, his and the city's both: the priest would enter A-Lian into the parish register at the Mercedarian church under a Christian name she had taken to marry a Penhale, *A-Lian Penhale, china, esposa,* the date and the cause, and the company in Callao would close out, somewhere, a contract that had never properly existed for a woman who had been given no paper; and here in the cold patio her brother had entered her too, in the only book that had ever counted her right, low on a stick worn smooth, below all the others, a clean square notch with no name said over it that Rosa could hear, in a grammar Rosa would spend the rest of her life not quite learning, for a girl whose grandmother had put a comb in her hair so that no one could make her be from nowhere, and who was now, four days into the silence, from nowhere at last, and counted.

Inside the house, behind the wall, in the good front room that faced the wrong way, the child Carmen had got up in the dark without being heard, the way she did everything, and had gone in to the bed, and had found her mother, and had not cried out — Rosa would learn this later, would find the girl when she went back in, sitting on the low stool in the dark by the bed where the breathing was not, very still, holding nothing, watching the place where the sound had been — and Rosa stood in the patio with the comb in her pocket and the tally-man putting on his hat, and across the sea the war held the white mountain at a hundred and fifteen feet under the Spanish guns, locked up and unmineable and worth, this season, thirteen pound the ton and rising, more by the ton than it had ever been worth or would ever be worth again, and not one of the men who would name his figure for it this year would ever stand in a cold Lima patio in the grey and watch what it cost, weighed out in the one currency the islands had always paid in and the books had always been built to lose.


## MANIFEST II — The War

### Chapter 13 · Tomás Reyes
*Callao · 2 May 1866 · guano ≈ £13/ton · Chincha working face ≈ 115 ft*

They had given him a sponge on a pole and told him it would keep him alive, and he believed it the way he believed everything they told him that week, which was not at all and entirely at once.

The gun was a Blakely, English iron, three hundred pounds to the shot, mounted behind the seaward wall of the tower they called the Merced, and Tomás Reyes was nineteen and the youngest of the seven men who served it and so he was the sponge-boy. His work was to ram the wet sponge down the bore after each round to kill the embers, because a man who loaded fresh powder onto a live ember went up with the gun and took the others with him, and the sergeant — a black Callao stevedore named Bambarén, broad as a door, who had loaded guns under sentence once and now did it for sixpence a day and the right to walk the mole a free man — had said it to him plainly on the first morning. *You sponge it dead every time. You sponge it dead like your mother's life is in the bore.* Tomás had said yes. He had not slept since. Three nights now he had lain on the stone of the battery with the sea moving black below the embrasure and counted the men around him and tried to learn which of them would die, the way you learn the weather, so that it would not surprise him when it came.

He still did not know what the war was about. He knew it was about the islands. He knew the islands were white and far to the south off Pisco and that they were made of the dung of birds and that for fifteen years the country had lived on selling it, and that two years ago the Spanish had sailed in and taken them at gunpoint, the way a man puts his boot on the throat of a fallen dog and keeps it there, and that this was the reason a fleet of Spanish iron now lay out beyond the breakwater in the grey forenoon with its broadsides run out, and the reason he, Tomás Reyes, who two months past had been a barefoot net-mender on the beach at Chorrillos, was standing behind three hundred pounds of cold English shot waiting to be killed. He had asked Bambarén, the second night, low, *what is it worth, the islands, that they would come this far for it.* And Bambarén had looked at him a while in the dark and said, *more than you. More than this tower. More than every man on this wall and the wall itself.* And that was the only figure Tomás ever got, and it was enough, and it told him nothing.

The morning was the colour of a dead fish. The fog had burned off the harbour by eight and left the sun white and flat over the water, and out past the line of the moored hulks and the boom the Spanish fleet lay in a long curved line, seven of them, eight, dark and low and patient on the swell, and the biggest of them — the iron one, the one without masts to speak of, the *Numancia*, they said her name down the wall like a curse passed from mouth to mouth — sat broadside-on to the batteries with her gunports open like a row of black teeth. Tomás counted the ships. He counted them three times and got eight and then seven and then eight, because they shifted, because one was working in closer, and his hands, when he looked down at them on the haft of the sponge-pole, were doing a small steady tremor he could not stop and did not show.

"They're slow," said the man beside him, a carter from the Rímac named Esquivel, missing two fingers off his left hand from the work that gave him to the army, and he said it to comfort himself and not Tomás. "Look at them. They're frightened of the towers. They'll fire a while at long range to say they did it and then they'll go home and tell their king they won."

"Sponge it dead," Tomás said. He had stopped being able to say other things.

He thought of the beach at Chorrillos instead, because a man at a gun thinks of the place he is not. He thought of his mother's hands on the net, faster than his had ever been, the way she could find a parted mesh by feel in the dark and knot it shut before his eyes had found it at all; of the smell of the morning catch laid out on the wet shingle, silver and ordinary; of his three sisters and the youngest one who was simple and laughed at the gulls. They had taken him in March. A press of soldiers had come down the coast road with a list and a need for men, and his name had not been on any list, no list in the world had Tomás Reyes on it, but he had been on the beach and he had been sound and quick and worth carrying off, and that, he had learned in two months, was the only entry a poor man ever made: not in a book, but in a hard man's eye, the moment it found you and judged you fit to be spent. He had left his mother nothing. He had not even left her a word. He had been mending net one hour and the next he was walking north between two muskets, and he understood now, behind the gun, that the islands had reached all that way up the coast and taken him off the shingle as surely as if a bird had dropped on him out of that white southern sky and carried him off in its claws.

*

At ten minutes before noon the *Numancia* fired.

He did not see the gun. He saw the smoke — a single great rolling ball of it blow out from the iron ship's side, white going to grey, and then he heard it, not as a sound but as a thing that hit the whole front of his body at once and went through it, and somewhere along the wall to his left a section of stone the age of his grandfather's grandfather came apart and went up in a cloud of dust and old mortar and a noise like the world's spine breaking, and a man screamed in it, one long note, and Tomás found that he had not moved, that he was still standing with the wet sponge in his two hands and his mouth open, and that Bambarén was bellowing at the crew and the gun-captain had the lanyard and they were going to fire back.

After that there was no more counting. There was only the work.

The work saved him; he understood that even inside it, that the seven motions of serving the gun were a rope thrown to a drowning man and he took it with both hands and did not let go. Load. Ram. The gun-captain's hand up. Stand clear of the recoil — clear, *clear*, a man who stood in the path of three tons of iron coming back on its slide was paste, Bambarén had a word for it that Tomás would hear in his sleep for the rest of his life — the lanyard, the world hit again from the inside, the carriage roaring back to the breeching-ropes, the smoke, and then his work, his one work, the sponge driven down the bore wet and turned and drawn, the hiss of it on the hot iron, *sponge it dead, sponge it dead like your mother's life,* and the fresh charge after, and the shot, and up again. He did it. He did it through the noise that was not noise anymore but a pressure on the body that never lifted, through the smoke that filled the gun-tower until the sun was a brown smear and the men were shadows coughing at their stations, through the moment the gun two embrasures down took a shot full on its muzzle and was simply gone, gun and carriage and the four men on it, gone into a red wet ruin Tomás did not look at twice because looking cost time and time was the bore and the bore was his mother's life. He sponged it dead. The bore took the wet and gave it back as steam and he loaded behind it and the gun spoke. Eleven times. Twelve. He lost the count, he who had counted everything for three nights to keep from going mad, and the losing of the count was the most frightening thing that happened to him all day, more than the iron, because it meant he had gone somewhere a man should not be able to go and still call himself a man.

*

There was a gap.

He never knew why. The smoke thinned — a wind off the land, a lull, the iron ship hauling off to cool her guns — and for the length of a few breaths the wall could see again, and the men sagged at their stations and a water-carrier came down the line with a skin and Tomás drank and the water tasted of powder and his own blood where he had bitten through his lip and not felt it. And in that gap he saw the Englishman.

He had seen him before, on the wall, in the grey before the fleet stood in: a tall fair foreigner in a plain dark coat, not a soldier, no uniform on him, moving along the batteries with one of the harbour-company men and a Peruvian officer, and Tomás had taken him for one of the merchants who had business in the port and a reason to want the towers to hold — the warehouses and the moles and the bonded guano in the sheds, all of it hung on whether the Spanish iron could be kept beyond the breakwater. The foreigner had stayed. That was the thing Tomás could not place. The merchants and the consuls had been gone from the seafront before the first gun, up to Lima or down into the cellars, and this one had stayed, in the open among the conscripts and the convicts and the stevedores, with no iron over him and no reason Tomás could see, helping to clear a fouled tackle on the next gun, his coat off now and his sleeves rolled and his fair forearms grey to the elbow with mortar-dust, working like a deck-hand and shouting something in bad Spanish to the powder-boys that made one of them laugh in the middle of the worst morning of his life.

Tomás watched him a moment in the lull. There was something in the man's face he knew and could not name — and then he named it, and it cooled him worse than the iron had. It was the face of a man who had decided about a thing. He had seen it on the older men in the village the year the fever came, the ones who walked down to the boats anyway. He had seen it that very dawn on Esquivel, for one second, before Esquivel put it away. The Englishman had it settled into him like a stone in a wall: a man who had reckoned what the day might cost and paid it in advance, in his head, so that the rest would only be arithmetic. Tomás did not know the man's name. He never would. He knew only, watching him wipe his grey arm across his grey face, that the foreigner was here for the islands too — that this Englishman had a piece of the white mountain, had crossed an ocean and made a life on the strength of it, and had come down to the seafront to stand in the iron and defend the thing his fortune was hung on, and would die for it if it came to that.

He thought, in the dull bright clarity of the lull, with the water of powder in his mouth: *we are the same, him and me. Only he chose it and I was caught.* And then he thought it the strangest thing, that an Englishman in a good coat and a fisherman's son from Chorrillos should both die in one morning over an island of bird-leavings neither of them would ever own a foot of, and he almost laughed, the way the powder-boy had laughed, the laugh that is the other side of weeping, and then the smoke rolled back over the wall and the iron ship came round again and there was no more lull and no more thinking, only the bore.

*

What killed the Englishman was the tower itself.

That was the part Tomás carried after, when he was old, when his grandchildren asked him about the war and he told them only the small true things and never the worst of it: that in the end it was not the Spanish iron that did most of the dying on the wall, but the towers the men were defending, turning on the men inside them. Toward the middle of the afternoon a Spanish shot — or a fault in a charge, or the heat, no one ever knew, no one was left at the spot to know — found the magazine of the Merced, the deep stone room where the powder was kept, two embrasures and a turn of the wall from Tomás's gun, where the foreign man had gone with a party to bring up a fresh supply because the powder-train had been broken by a hit and the guns were going silent for want of it and someone had to carry the charges up by hand through the smoke, and a tall fair man with mortar-grey arms who had already decided about the day was as good as any to do it.

Tomás felt it before he heard it. The stone under his feet jumped, a single hard buck like a ship striking, and then the sound came — not a gun's sound, deeper, a sound with the whole weight of the tower in it, a sound that did not pass through his body the way the broadsides did but lifted his body off the stone and set it down again wrong — and a column of fire and smashed masonry and the powder itself going up all at once stood straight up out of the seaward angle of the Merced into the white sky, higher than the masts of the ships, and hung there, and came down. It came down on the wall and on the water and on Tomás's gun-tower in a rain of hot stone and splinters and things that were not stone, and the man Esquivel was bleeding from the ear and shouting words Tomás could not hear, and the gun-captain was down and Bambarén was hauling him up by the collar, and the world had gone to a high singing silence with the battle still raging soundless inside it like a thing seen through thick glass.

When the smoke cleared off that angle of the wall there was no party at the magazine, and no magazine, and no fair foreigner with grey arms and a settled face. There was a gap in the tower the shape of a bite, and rubble sliding into the sea, and the harbour-company man Tomás had seen the Englishman walking with on the wall at dawn was on his knees in the broken stone with his hands pressed flat to the rubble as though he could hold the tower together with his palms, his mouth working, and you could not hear him, you could not hear anything, and Tomás, who had counted the men around him for three nights to know which would die and had not once thought to count the foreigner because the foreigner was not one of theirs — Tomás understood that the count had been wrong from the start. That it had always been wrong. That the dead of the day would not be the men he had ranked in the dark by the look of them, but men chosen by the bore and the heat and the powder and the towers, without reason, without ledger, the brave and the caught and the foreign all subtracted out of the living together and given no receipt.

He did not have time to be afraid of it. The gun was going silent for want of charges, the same as the others, and now the powder-train on his own side was broken too, and Bambarén turned and looked at Tomás Reyes through the singing silence with blood on his great black face, and Tomás knew, before the sergeant's mouth had finished making the words he could not hear, what he was being told. Someone had to carry the powder up by hand, through the smoke, from the deeper magazine that had not yet gone, the way the foreigner had. Someone had to be the man who walked to the powder, the way the tile-makers and the fisher-boys and the foreign merchants all, in the end, walked to the thing that would take them, because the alternative was the gun going silent and the wall going quiet and the iron ships standing in unopposed to the moles and the warehouses and the bonded white in the sheds. Tomás set down the sponge. He had been the sponge-boy. He understood he was not the sponge-boy any longer. He looked once, out past the broken angle of the wall, at the Spanish fleet on the bright water, the iron ship turning ponderous to bring her cool guns to bear, and he could not for his life have said what was in those sheds that was worth this, what the white islands held that two empires would burn a port and a generation of poor men to keep, and he found, standing there with his ears full of silence and his mouth full of powder and his mother's far-off life suddenly very clear in his head, that it did not matter what it was worth. It would have him either way. He had only the choosing left — whether to walk to it or wait for it — and he had seen, that morning, a foreigner choose, and laugh, and go.

He went down toward the powder.


## MANIFEST II — The War

### Chapter 14 · Thomas Quick
*Chincha North, off Pisco · 1866 · guano ≈ £12–13/ton, the war premium easing · Chincha working face ≈ 110 ft*

The Spanish had left the island the way a tenant leaves a let house at quarter-day — owing nothing they could be made to pay, and having broken what could not be itemised. Quick wanted the island back in its columns; a thing a man could not count was a thing that could rob him. So he walked the workings the first morning after the fleet stood off, with the manifest of the company's losses open against the wind, and made his inventory, item by item, because somebody had to.

He totted it as he went. Two lighters scuttled at their moorings, one of them carrying off with the swell, scratch that off. The seaward jetty fired and gone to charred piling, which the company would rebuild because a jetty was the throat of the trade, and the company would spend to keep the throat clear before it spent a centavo to widen a digger's ration: a man could be had off the tender for three hundred the head, and a jetty could not be had for three hundred anything. The powder-magazine the garrison had built into the old guano and then blown when they ran was a hole now, a crater with the white standing round it in a frozen splash, and Quick looked into it and thought, with the part of him that could not stop, that a man had finally found a use for the mountain the diggers had not: the Spaniards had thrown a few hundred tons of the most valuable substance on earth into the sea in one bright thump, and the price had gone up a shilling in London that same fortnight for the scarcity of it. He had read it in a circular. He would not have said it aloud to a living soul.

The flag over the rookery was Peru's again. He noted that too, because it bore on the labour: a Spanish flag meant requisition and idle men paid out of the company's pocket, a Peruvian flag meant the consignment resumed and the men back on the figure. He had got them onto it himself before the smoke was off the water. A manager who waited for orders let two weeks' cut stand in the cliff while he waited, and two weeks' cut at a war-premium price was a sum Quick carried in his head, and it was reason enough to put the barrows back up the line the same dawn the guns went quiet.

The mountain had come down while the empires fought over it, which the war had not changed and could not change, and Quick saw it from the workings the way he saw everything, in feet. A hundred and ten foot of the high face stood above the cut now, near enough; he had read the marks his whole working life and did not need a rod. When he had landed on this rock the face had stood at a hundred and eighty, a thing the old hands had spoken of as outlasting Lima, outlasting the Republic, outlasting the want of bread in England that paid for it. Seventeen years, and seventy feet gone. The two years of occupation had barely shown — a season's worth the Spaniards' idleness had spared, and the company had got that season back in a month of double gangs the instant the flag changed. Quick looked up the white face and did the arithmetic the company still did not trouble to do for itself, and it came out: he could say, if he chose, how many seasons stood between the cut and the bare rock under it. He did not choose. He shut the book on his finger. It was not a column he was answerable for; the bottom of the mountain was the house in Lima's affair, and London's, and the manager who told the houses where their fortune ended got a passage home for his honesty and another man on the beam by the next tender.

He went down to the scale-house to make the day balance, because that, at least, had not gone anywhere.

*

The scale-house had taken a single round through the seaward wall — a ship's gun, ranging on the jetty, throwing long — and the ball had gone clean through and out the other side without touching the beam, which Quick took, against his own principles, as a thing very near to luck. He had had the hole boarded the same week. The great beam still swung true from the king-post; the iron weights still ranked on their shelf small to large; the ledger still sat on its sloped desk with the men entered in one column as numbers and exited in another in a separate ink, and Quick stood in the doorway the first morning and looked at the order of it and felt the thing settle in him that nothing else on the island settled, the war least of all. He had no chapel in him. He had said so to himself for forty years and was easy about the lack. But a man could stand in the one square place where the columns came out even and let the rest of it — the crater, the scuttled lighters, the flag that had been the wrong flag for two years and was now the right one again, all of it weather, all of it outside the beam — let the rest of it stand off at the door, the way he made his own body stand off the falling men from the edge, and Quick stood in the order of his scale-house and let the war stand off at the door and got out the issue book.

The broker came to him at the desk in the middle of the morning weigh, and the broker was Mei.

Quick had known he would. The consignment resuming meant the brokers resuming, the men who stood between the company and the labour and the lighters and the Lima houses and took their fraction off each passing of the cargo from one hand to the next, and Mei was the broker the company dealt with on the matter of the gangs now, had been for some years, and Quick had watched it happen the way a man watches the tide come up a beach he is standing on — without alarm, because each inch of it was reasonable, and then all at once to the knees. He had pulled the Chinaman off the face himself. Seventeen years ago, near enough; a contract number with a clouding eye who could carry a tally across a whole gang and lose nothing, and Quick had marked him for the tally crew the way he marked any sound tool for the use it was good for, and had reckoned it a clean piece of management, and had been right, and had taken a flat satisfaction in being right that he took still. The tool had kept the company's count for nine years. Then the tool had been freed by the running-out of a contract that the company had stopped pretending to honour and started pretending it had honoured, on paper, in arrears; and the tool, free, had not gone home — none of them went home, the home was a thing the broker in Amoy had drawn in spilled liquor to sell the passage and there was no home at the end of any of these men's eight years, Quick knew that better than the men did, he kept the rolls — the tool had not gone home, and had not died, and had instead done the one thing Quick had never seen a freed digger do in forty years on the rock. It had got the far side of the beam.

"You're short on the *Esperanza*'s gang," Mei said. He stood at the corner of the desk with a slate and did not sit, because Quick had not asked him to sit and Mei did not take a thing that was not given, which was a habit off the rock that the rock did not unlearn in a man even when he could have bought the chair. "Eleven men. The Spanish took fourteen for the magazine work and gave back nine, and two of the nine cannot stand to a barrow."

"Then they'll be docked to what they can do and entered short," Quick said, "and you'll bring me the count of it, man by man, who's short and by how much, the way you always have."

"The way I always have," Mei said, and there was nothing in it, no edge a man could put his thumb on, and Quick felt the old cold draught off the back of his neck that he had felt off this one man and never off another in his life — the sensation of being read back, of his own figure run down by a thumb — and he squared the ledger to the edge of the desk and did not look at it too long.

Because the count was no longer Quick's to ask for, not as a master asks a tally-man. It was a courtesy between two men who needed the same numbers, and Quick knew it, and Mei knew it, and the knowing sat in the scale-house between them like the white film of dust that sat on everything, too fine to brush, in the food and on the page and in the air a man breathed whether he agreed to or not. Mei could have sent a clerk with the slate. Mei had a clerk now, a Cantonese boy with a good hand, and could have sat in Pisco and let the boy stand where the broker stood. He came himself. Quick had wondered at it the first season of the reversal and had stopped wondering: the Chinaman came to the scale-house himself the way Quick walked the crater himself the morning after the guns, because a man who had spent his life keeping count came to look at the count with his own eye, the one he had left, and would not be told a number he could go and see. Quick recognised the economy. He had always recognised it in this one. He disliked recognising it still, because recognising it meant recognising that the difference between the man at the beam and the man with the slate was the beam, was the book, was the one turn of the wheel of luck that had put Quick's hand on a ledger off a Liverpool dock and put Mei's hand on a barrow off a tender, and that the wheel had turned again since, a half-turn, not the whole, but a half — the digger to the broker, the owned to the owning — and that it might, given another season and another premium and the right small corruptions on both sides, turn the rest of the way.

"The stick's full, then," Quick said. He had not meant to say it. It came out of the part of him that totted things, that could not leave a count uncounted, and he heard it land in the order of the scale-house and could not call it back.

Mei looked at him.

"I've seen you cut it," Quick said, plainer, because there was no unsaying it. "Years. A man keeps a private tally and a manager notices; I notice everything entered and I noticed that. Not the company's count. Your own. The men." He nodded at the issue book, the contract numbers, the separate ink of the exit column. "I keep mine in there. You keep yours in the wood. It's a longer stick than it was."

For a moment the stillness in the Chinaman's face did the thing it did, the thing held so still it amounted to an expression by the violence of its holding, and Quick thought he had said too much, had let a thing in at the door that did not belong inside the beam. Then Mei reached into his coat — not the rags of seventeen years gone, a broker's plain dark coat now, good cloth, the cloth of a man the company paid — and brought out the stick. He laid it on the desk between them, on the sloped wood beside the issue book, and he did not say anything, and Quick looked at it.

It was a length of hard pale wood the size of a forearm, smoothed past smoothness by the hand that had carried it, dark where the hand held it and pale where the notches were, and the notches ran the length of it in a close even file, square-cut, deep enough to find in the dark, and at the foot of them, low on the wood, there was a fresh one, the cut still bright, and above it a small clear space, and then the file resumed and ran up to the top where the first of them stood, the oldest, worn nearly round with the years of the thumb that had found it. Quick did not count it. He found, with the part of him that he had spent forty years teaching not to listen to, that he did not want to count it, the way he had not wanted to count the men on the cliff the first morning he ever stood downwind of this place; but the part that totted things had the number before he forbade it, the way the eye has the weight before the iron settles — somewhere past a hundred, a clean round file of them past a hundred, and Quick stood in the scale-house with his own death-rolls in the book at his elbow and looked at the same dead counted again in the wood, and understood that he had two ledgers of the men on this desk now and they were the same ledger, that every number he had exited in the separate ink was a notch in this stick, that the company had bought the labour and spent it and entered the spending as wastage, and the man across the desk had carried the wastage in his coat for seventeen years against his ribs because somebody had to and the company would not keep the column.

"That's a deal of them," Quick said.

"Yes," said Mei.

"More than I'd have set down." Quick heard the heat come into it that he did not put there, the heat that was a kind of sentiment and cost money and changed nothing, and he disliked hearing it, and went on anyway, level as he could get it, because the stick was on his desk now and a man did not look away from a count laid open. "You'll have me on it. Somewhere up the top. The years I kept the figure and you kept the slate and we put them onto the boats short and entered the short ones in the proper column." He did not make it a question. "A manager's on every one of these, if the count's honest. You'll not have left me off for the courtesy."

Mei picked the stick up off the desk. He turned it once in his hand, the worn old top of it under his thumb, and he looked at Quick with the one eye that the place had left him, the milk-clouded one tilted a little away the way the old hands all stood, favouring what they had kept, and he said: "You are not on it."

"I'm on it."

"You are in the book," Mei said, and laid his hand flat on the issue book a moment, the contract numbers, the separate ink. "The book is the company's. The company spent them and you balanced it. That is the book's count and it is true." He put the stick back inside his coat, against his ribs, where the cloth had a shape worn into it for the carrying. "The stick is not for the men who balanced. It is for the men who were balanced. You weighed them, Mr. Quick. You did not dig."

And Quick, who had stood downwind of this rock for seventeen years and weighed the cargo and the rice on one beam with one iron and slept, who had let it in twice a season and drunk the wall back up before morning, who knew in his body that he had come up off a dock the same hungry boy these men came off the tender and had got the far side of the beam by luck and a hard stomach and nothing he would call virtue — Quick stood in the order of his scale-house and was told by the man he had pulled off the face that he did not belong even in the wood. That the dead were the diggers' dead and not the manager's; that a man who weighed was outside the count of the men who were weighed, outside it the way Quick had spent his whole life making himself outside the men, the wall up, the kinship drunk down — and that the wall, which he had built to keep himself on the buying side, kept him off the stick too, kept him out even of the dead, so that when the count of the men on this rock was finally read, the manager who had balanced them would not be among them, would be in the other book, the company's, where the men were numbers; would be, himself, in the end, an entry the way the boy on the dock had been an entry, on the wrong page, counted by the machine and not by the conscience, and let the conscience pass him by.

He had thought the wall kept him safe. He saw, for one moment in the lamplight of the morning weigh, that it kept him alone.

He squared the ledger to the edge of the desk and broke it.

"After the weigh," he said. "The count of the *Esperanza*'s gang. Man by man."

"After the weigh," said Mei, and went out into the white light to look at the gang with his own eye.

*

The orphans were settled in Lima, which Quick had no part in and went to see anyway, because the company had a writing to be witnessed and a manager's signature was cheaper to send than a notary's, and because — though he did not put it to himself in those words — he had carried the news of the children up and down the trade, the dead mother and the dead father: the woman gone of the lungs the year before, and Daniel Penhale, the founder's own son, a volunteer at the Callao batteries this spring who had no need to be there and stayed anyway, gone up at the wall with the powder when the train to one of the guns broke — a Cornishman blown to nothing over the islands his father got rich on. A man who had carried a thing wished to see where it was set down.

It was a paper, in the end. It was always a paper. Quick had spent his life on the far side of papers and was not deceived by this one: there was the girl and there was the boy, and there was a Penhale house and a Mei house, and there were monies, and there was the language of wardship and maintenance and the disposition of the children's interest in this lease and that consignment, all of it drawn in a Lima lawyer's careful hand so that two fortunes might raise two children without either fortune conceding a point to the other, and Quick read it the way he read a lading, down the side with his thumb, and found in it exactly what such a paper held, which was the children entered as the company entered men: as a charge, an asset, a thing to be valued and provided for and logged against the proper account. He signed where the lawyer pointed. His was a steady hand; it had entered a great many things.

The boy was not in the room. The boy was four, the lawyer said, and asleep through the heat of the day with a woman somewhere in the back of the Lima house, too small to be entered into anything but the second line of the paper, where he stood as *and the male child, Samuel*, a figure provided for and not yet aware he was a figure. The girl was a different case. The girl was in the room. They had not meant her to be, Quick thought; a child was generally got out of the room when the disposition of the child was being signed, the way the cargo was not consulted on its consignment; but she had come in after the comb she had dropped, a Lima girl of nine in a dark frock, and she had found it under the chair where the men sat with the paper, and instead of going out again she had stayed, in the corner, by the long window with the cold sea-fog standing white against it, and watched.

Quick watched her back, because he watched everything in a room and entered it.

She had the look of the trade in her, which he had seen in no other child and did not at first know what to call. It was not the colour, though the colour was its own thing, the dark quick eyes set in a face that read as one thing in this drawing-room and would read as another entirely on a Cornish quay, a face the city would never quite decide about, the way it had never quite decided about her grandfather's woman. It was the watching. She stood in the corner with the comb in her two hands — a small plain comb, old, foreign, not a Lima thing, with a worn place where a thumb had held it that Quick's eye went to before he knew why, because a worn place was a record of use and use was the only truth an object kept — and she watched the men sign the paper that said where she would live and on whose money, and she did not cry and she did not fidget and she did not ask, and Quick, who had stood at the beam while four hundred men learned not to spend what they did not have to, knew the stillness when he saw it. He had seen it across a desk that same week, tilted away over a clouded eye. The girl had got it off the old Chinaman. She had got it the way a child gets a turn of speech off a man who half-raised her, without being taught, by being near it; and Quick stood in the Lima drawing-room and understood that the count had got into her too, the noticing, the keeping of the figure, the refusal to look away from the paper that valued her — that she was watching them enter her in the ledger and was, in the corner, with her dead mother's comb in her hands, entering them back.

He found he wanted to say a thing to her and had no idea in the world what it was.

He had no children. He had a flask and a ledger and a horror of sentiment, and the wall, the good wall, seventeen years of it, that had kept him on the buying side of every scale he ever stood at and had kept him, he had been told that very week, off the only honest count of the dead on the island; and he stood with the pen still in his hand and looked at the orphaned girl in the corner and felt the thing come at him from the side that came at him twice a season, late, in the lamplight, with the smell up off the cut — only it was the middle of the day, and there was no cut, and the smell was only the faint ammonia that came off the money itself, off the silk and the carpet and the drawn paper, the stink that the trade put into everything it touched and never came out, even here, a hundred miles from the rock, in a room built entirely out of it. She would grow up in this smell. She would grow up in the rooms the white mountain had bought, in the dresses and the schooling and the careful Lima hand, and the same dust that had come down the chutes off her grandfather's island and turned, somewhere in London, into the figure on the paper Quick had just signed had been in the air her mother breathed at eleven, at the face, and was very likely in the girl already, the faint film of it laid down in a child's chest on some visit to the workings to see what the money was. She had the comb and she had the watching and she would have, he did not doubt, in time, the cough. The whole account would come to her. He had just witnessed the paper that handed it over, and the paper had a column for the leases and a column for the monies and no column at all for the thing that would actually be paid, which was the only true entry in the whole disposition, and which would render itself, in its own time, in a currency the lawyer's careful hand did not know how to write.

The girl looked at him across the room — caught him watching, and did not drop her eyes, which a Lima child of nine should have done to a sunburnt Englishman with broken veins and a pen, and did not — and Quick, for one moment, let the wall down. Let it stand off at the door the way he let the war stand off at the door. Looked back at her plainly, the orphaned girl in the room full of the smell of the money her mother and father had died making, and let himself see her not as the charge in the paper, not as the asset entered and provided for, but as what she was, which was a child, his own kind, the way the men on the rock were his own kind and the boy on the Liverpool dock had been his own kind, a small human thing that the machine had already valued and logged and would, in the end, collect.

Then he shut it. He had a horror of it and shut it, the way he had shut it twice a season for seventeen years, because there was no version of letting it in that helped the girl by so much as a centavo, and every version of it ended with Thomas Quick a soft old man no good to the company or himself; and the company would send another manager who could witness the paper and not look at the child, and the child would be exactly as orphaned and the trade exactly as deep, and the only thing changed in the whole of it would be that Quick had gone soft at the last and lost his place and was, himself, an old broke man on a quay somewhere being looked at by a hard one with a book. He capped the thing the way he capped the flask. He set the pen down, neat, so it would not roll. He gave the girl a short nod, manager to charge, the kind of nod he gave a sound piece of work, and the girl, after a moment, gave it back — the watchful tilt of the head she had off the old Chinaman, the entering nod, the nod of one count acknowledging another — and Quick took up his hat and his copy of the disposition and went out of the room.

In the street the fog had come up white over Lima, off the cold sea, the way it did, and it stood in the air the way the dust stood in the air on the rock, fine and total and in everything, and Quick walked down through it toward the harbour where the lighter waited to take him back to the island and the beam and the resumed cut and the eleven short men on the *Esperanza*'s gang. He did not think about the girl. He was good at not thinking about a thing; it had turned the largest profit a poor man ever turned. He thought instead about the day's weigh and the figure he would enter and the mountain at a hundred and ten foot coming down at the rate it came down, and somewhere behind all of it, in the part he did not answer for, the part that totted what no column held, a number stood that he had not chosen to do and had done anyway, walking the crater the first morning — the seasons between the cut and the bare rock, a finite number, a bottom under the mountain — and he carried it down through the white fog to the boat the way the broker carried the stick against his ribs, the one count on the island neither book would keep.

He went back to the rock. The cut had a figure to make and the figure did not know the children existed, and Quick weighed it, and the day balanced, and he entered it.


## MANIFEST III — The Boom's Apex

### Chapter 15 · Carmen Penhale-Mei
*Lima · 1869 · guano ≈ £13–14/ton, the apex · Chincha working face ≈ 45 ft, stumps*

The sum would not come out, and Carmen had been at it three days.

She kept it in the back of an arithmetic copybook her tutor believed she used for the practice of long division, which she did, in the front, in a careful hand, because a girl who did her division wrong was watched and a girl who did it right was left alone, and being left alone was the whole point. The front of the book was for the world. The back of the book was hers. In the back she had been trying, since Thursday, to add up what the dresses cost.

Not the price of the dresses. She knew the price of the dresses; it was eleven soles to the modiste on the Calle de Mercaderes for the blue one with the second flounce, and she had it written, because writing down a figure was the first thing both her grandfathers had taught her without either knowing the other had taught her anything. She meant the other cost. She had a column for the soles and she had begun a second column beside it, narrower, with no heading, because she did not know what to head it, and into the second column she had been trying to put the things that were not soles, and the second column would not add, because she did not know the unit. You could not add eleven soles to a man. The page kept refusing her. She would set down the price of the cloth and then she would sit with her pencil over the second column and the column would look back at her empty, asking her for the figure, and she would not have it, and after a while she would close the book.

It was a stupid sum. She knew it was stupid. No one in either house was doing this sum; that was precisely the thing she could not get past. Two houses full of grown people who did sums all day — her grandfather Penhale who could price a thing with his eyes shut and her uncle Mei who counted everything that moved and a great deal that did not — two houses of counters, and not one of them had ever once put the cloth in one column and the rest of it in the other and tried to make the page come out. It seemed to her, at twelve, the most obvious sum in the world, the first sum a person would do, and she could not understand why she was the only one in Lima attempting it, unless the reason was that everyone else already knew the answer and had agreed, the way grown people agreed about God and money, never to write it down.

She heard her grandfather Penhale come into the house below.

She knew him by the floor. She had learned both houses by the floor before she was old enough to be trusted on the stairs of either — the Penhale house here on the Calle Núñez with its imported boards that her grandfather walked the way he was said to have walked a deck, heel first and certain, a man who had decided the floor would hold him; and the other house, Rosa's older house behind the cathedral where her uncle Mei kept his two rooms when he was up from the islands, where the floors were tile and Mei crossed them without sound at all, in soft shoes, the way he did everything, conserving. She could tell from the boards which grandfather she had. This was Penhale. He had been to Callao to see a ship in and he was pleased, she could hear the pleasure in the weight of him, and she put the copybook under the loose board where she kept it and went down to be appraised.

*

"There's my best cargo," he said, which was what he said, and which she had worked out years ago was the highest thing he could say to a person, and she let him take her chin in his big hand and turn her face to the window-light the way he turned everything to the light, and she watched him do it, because she had learned to watch him do it the way you watch a man weigh a coin he suspects, with interest, without taking it personally. He was looking to see that she had not got thinner, that her eye was clear, that the thing he had invested in was holding its value. She let him look. It cost her nothing to be looked at and it told her a great deal about him, and Carmen had decided long ago that she would rather know her grandfather than be loved by him in some other way she would have to imagine, since the loving was not on offer and the knowing was.

"You're pale," he said.

"I read indoors."

"Read in the sun. You're no use to me pale." He said it dry and he meant it as tenderness; she had cracked that code at about eight, the year her mother died, when she had needed very badly to understand exactly how much was meant by the few things that were said, and had learned to read her grandfather Penhale the way you read a barometer, not for what it felt but for what it foretold. *No use to me* was the warmest words he had. She filed it, the way her uncle filed things, in the place where she kept the true value of what people said as against the price of it.

He had brought a thing for her, which he did when a ship came in well — not a present, he did not give presents, he gave evidence; he set it on the table, a little carved fan of sandalwood with a Chinese landscape on it, mountains and a bridge, that had come off a vessel out of Hong Kong in the China-goods trade, and she understood at once that he had not chosen it for the mountains or the bridge. He had taken it in part-payment of something, or it had come cheap in a lot, and rather than let it go to Inés or to the modiste he had carried it home to the granddaughter, because the granddaughter was an account he kept up, a thing he provisioned, and the fan was a figure entered to that account. She picked it up and opened it and the sandalwood breathed out its faint sweet ghost into the room, into the other smell, the one that was always under everything in both houses and that she had stopped being able to smell the way you stop hearing your own clock — the dry ammoniac whisper that lived in the curtains and the carpet and her grandfather's coat and, she did not doubt, in the very banknotes folded in his pocketbook, because she had once, as a small girl, watched him count out a payment and seen the white film come off the notes onto his thumb, and seen him lick the thumb to take the next note, and had said nothing because she had not yet known there was anything to say.

"It's pretty," she said, of the fan, because that was the receipt he wanted for it, and he grunted, satisfied, the transaction closed.

"Your aunt's downstairs going at the Dreyfus papers. Go down to her, learn something. She's worth ten of your tutors."

This was true and Carmen went.

*

Inés had the long table covered. She was eighteen and she had been the principal of the counting-room in fact if not in name for two years, and she sat at the head of the table the way her father sat at the head of a meal, and she did not look up when Carmen came in but she said, "Sit. Don't touch the left-hand pile, it's sorted," which Carmen took as the warmth it was, from Inés, who was only six years older and treated those six years as a tariff wall.

The papers were the new contract, or news of it, the thing the whole city was drunk on that summer. Carmen had heard the word *Dreyfus* a hundred times in three months, in the sala, in the street, from the pulpit nearly, the way you hear a word that has stopped being a man's name and become a kind of weather. A French house had bought the trade — bought the right to sell the islands' whole cargo into Europe, two million tons of it, and against that the French were going to lend Peru more money than Carmen could make a figure of, money for railways, money that was already in the city, you could see it, the new gas-standards going up the Jirón and the talk of a line of rail over the mountains to the interior, the impossible mountains, a railway to be flung across the Andes on the strength of what the birds had left on three islands off Pisco. Inés thought it was the cleverest thing she had ever read and the stupidest, both, and she was trying to work out which of the two it was going to be for the family, and she let Carmen sit beside her and watch her do it.

"They've borrowed against the next ten years," Inés said, half to herself, sliding a paper across. "Read that. They've sold the guano that hasn't been dug yet. The guano that hasn't been *birded* yet." She tapped a column with a sharp nail. "Here is what they say is on the islands. Here is what they have promised to deliver. You see?"

Carmen saw. She was good with figures the way you are good with a face you have known all your life; she did not so much calculate as recognize. She read the figure of what was promised and she had, from the back of her own copybook, the other figure, the one nobody at this table would have, because the men who wrote these papers had never been out to the workings and she had, once, as a very small girl, and remembered it the way you remember a fever.
