She knew what the white mountain had looked like when her father took her out to see it, and she had heard, since, from Mei, who never lied about a number because lying about a number was the one thing he could not make himself do, what it looked like now. She put the two together and she felt the thing she always felt when the two ledgers in her head were laid side by side, the small cold vertigo of the page that would not come out.

"It isn't there," she said.

Inés looked at her then.

"The two million tons," Carmen said. "It isn't on the islands. The islands aren't — " she stopped, because she did not have the grown word, and reached for the child one, "—the islands aren't that tall anymore."

For a moment Inés did not answer, and Carmen watched her aunt's clever face do a thing she had never seen it do, which was to be, for one second, afraid of a sum. Then Inés took the fear and put it away somewhere, the way Penhale put away the thing the hymn was for, and she smiled her tariff-wall smile and said, "No. It isn't there. Which is why the clever thing to do is be the house that *sells* the paper, niña, not the house that has to deliver the tons," and she went back to her sorting, and the conversation was closed, because Inés had answered the only version of the question she could afford to hear — *how does the family stay on the winning side* — and Carmen's question, the back-of-the-copybook question, *what is the true figure*, had glanced off her and fallen on the floor between them, and Carmen left it there, because she had learned that you did not pick a thing up off the floor in front of Inés. You noted where it fell. You came back for it alone.

She sat the rest of the afternoon and watched her aunt build, out of the gap between what was promised and what was there, a position that would make the family money whichever way the thing broke — and she understood, watching, that Inés had seen the same hole in the bottom of the islands that she had, the exact same hole, and had looked into it and seen, not a horror, but a draught, a current to ride. The hole was the opportunity. That the cargo was running out was the very thing you traded on. Carmen sat with the sandalwood fan in her lap and the dry whisper of the guano under the sweet ghost of the wood, and thought: *they have all done my sum. Every one of them has done my sum. They did it years ago and they liked the answer.* And the thing she could not yet say, that lived in the narrow second column with no heading, was that the answer they liked was the answer that frightened her, and that she did not yet know whether the difference between herself and her aunt was that she was better, or only younger, or only out at the workings once, long ago, as a child, with the dust going into a chest that was still going in and out of her now, at this table, a small dry catch under the breath that she swallowed before Inés could hear it, the way she had learned to swallow it in company, the way she had learned to swallow most things, in a house where the things you could not put a price on were not, by general agreement, written down.

*

She went to the other house in the evening, to her uncle, because she had a thing she wanted to look at and he was the only person in Lima who would let her look at it without first asking her why.

Mei was up from the islands that week — he came up less than he used to and stayed up longer, and she had heard Rosa say, not to her, that the air down there was finishing what it had started on him, though no one said it to his face, because you did not tell Mei a thing about his own body that he had not first told you. He was in the smaller of his two rooms behind the cathedral, by the one window, in the last light, doing the thing he did, which was nothing — sitting with his hands still on his knees and his head turned a little so the clouded eye pointed at the wall and the good one at the door, breathing in the careful shallow way she had thought, as a small child, was a kind of patience, and understood now was a man not spending more breath than a breath was worth. He did not get up. He never got up for her, which she had come to understand was the highest courtesy he had, the same in its way as her grandfather's *best cargo* — Mei did not treat her as a guest because a guest was a person you had not decided to keep.

"You're pale," he said, in the careful Spanish he used with her, that he had built late and exact out of the islands the way he built everything, never a word he had not first heard used correctly.

"My grandfather says read in the sun. You both say I'm pale."

"You are pale." He looked at her with the good eye. "You read the wrong things."

She sat on the low stool that was hers, that had always been hers, since she was small enough to need it, and she did not ask him about his body and he did not ask her about her copybook, and they sat a while in the particular quiet of his room, which was not Penhale's silence, the silence of a man between deals, but the other thing, the held quiet of a man who had a great deal to say and had decided, a long time ago, in a place she had only seen once, not to spend it. The room held very little. A bed, a chest, a shelf with a few books in a script she could not read and a tin of the black tea he drank, and on the wall a single strip of red paper with two columns of characters she had asked him about once, years ago, and he had said it was a thing you put up for the kitchen-god, for luck in the house, and she had asked which god and he had said, *the one who watches what the house eats and tells*, and had not explained further, and she had kept it, the way she kept everything, the god who watched what the house ate and told, and had thought of it often since, at the Penhale table, where a great deal was eaten and a god who told would have had things to say.

On the chest, where it always lay, was the stick.

She had known the stick all her life. There were two of them now, she had noticed this year — a second one, paler, newer, lying beside the old dark one — and she had never been told what they were and had never asked, because the not-asking was part of the thing between them, and because she had worked out, slowly, the way she worked everything out, most of what they were, and had been waiting, without knowing she was waiting, for the evening she would be old enough to be told the rest.

She looked at the sticks. She did not pick them up. You did not pick up a thing in this room any more than in Inés's, but for the opposite reason — not because it would be noted as a trespass but because the thing was his, was so entirely his that touching it without leave would be like touching the clouded eye.

"What are they," she said. Not *what are those.* She had decided, on the stool, in the last of the light, to ask the true question for once, plainly, the back-of-the-copybook question, to a person who might not put it away.

Mei did not answer for a while. The good eye stayed on her. Then he reached over — it cost him a small effort she pretended not to see, the reach — and he took up the old stick, the dark one, worn smooth and brown where his hand went and pale in the cuts, and he held it a moment in both hands the way she had seen Rosa hold the rosary, not praying with it, just letting the count of it pass under the thumb, and then he held it out to her, across the little distance of the room, and let her take it.

It was heavier than it looked. It was the size of his forearm and smoothed past smoothness, and the notches ran the length of it in a close file, square-cut, deep, and she ran her own thumb down them the way she had just seen him do, and they passed under her thumb one and another and another, and she did not count them because counting them felt, suddenly, like a thing you would have to have permission for, but she had the sense of the number the way you have the sense of a depth of water you are looking down into, that it was past a hundred, that it went on, that the file of them was longer than a tally of anything she could think of that a person would want to keep a hundred of. At the top, worn nearly round, the oldest, was one she somehow knew was the first, the way you know which is the eldest tree. And low down, near the foot, among the newer cuts, her thumb found one that was not like the others — clean, deeper, set a little apart, with a space of blank pale wood above it as if room had been left, once, on purpose, and then used — and her thumb stopped on it, of its own accord, the way her chest had stopped on the catch at Inés's table, and she did not know why, and then she did.

She looked up at him. He was watching her with the good eye and he had seen her thumb stop, and he did not look away and he did not explain, and in the not-explaining she understood it whole — that the stick was a count of people, that each cut was a person, that her uncle had carried in his coat for twenty years a tally of the dead the way her grandfather Penhale carried a tally of the cargo, two old men out of the same trade keeping their two books, and that the books were the same book kept twice; and that the one her thumb had stopped on, low down, set apart, with the room left above it, was her mother.

She had not been told. She knew. She held the stick in her two hands and felt her mother's notch under her thumb, the deep clean cut he had made, she did not know when, somewhere, in some room, with the little knife, the way he had made all of them, alone, for no one to see, because somebody had to keep the column the company would not keep, and she understood, sitting on her child's stool in the last light with the count of the dead in her hands, the thing the back of her copybook had been asking her for three days and could not name. The second column. The unit. It was not soles and it was not men. It was *this* — the cut in the wood, the name under no name, the thing kept in a coat against the ribs for twenty years because there was nowhere else in the whole apparatus of two houses and a trade and a continent's bread to keep it. That was the figure. The dresses cost eleven soles and they cost this, and the two would not add because they had never been meant to add, because the whole genius of the thing, the thing her grandfather was a genius at and her aunt was becoming a genius at and the French house in its papers and the men with the railway and the gas-standards going up the Jirón all depended on, was that the two columns were kept in different books, in different houses, in different tongues, by men who would die before they laid them side by side, and that she, Carmen, on a stool, was the only place in the world the two books had ever, by the accident of her blood, been kept in one head.

She did not say any of it. She had no more words for it than she had had for the page. She handed the stick back to him, carefully, the way it had been handed to her, and he took it and laid it on the chest beside the new one, and neither of them said anything about her mother, because there was nothing to say that the wood had not already said better, and the not-saying was the truest thing that passed between them, the way it always was, the way it had been on the islands she could not remember and in the lane behind the cathedral she would remember all her life.

Then Mei said, in the careful exact Spanish, looking at the wall with the clouded eye, "Your grandfather is happy this week."

"A ship came in well."

"Mm." A long breath, shallow, careful. "He prices the bottom too. He has priced it for years. He does not write it down." The good eye came back to her. "You write it down."

It was not a question and it was not praise. It was the nearest thing to a blessing he had, and it was also a warning, and she took it as both, because she had learned from him that the two were generally the same thing said once. *You write it down.* She thought of the narrow second column with no heading, and understood that he knew about it — not the copybook, he had never seen the copybook, but the thing in her the copybook was for, the refusal she had inherited from somewhere, that he had seen in her the way her grandfather saw the clearness of her eye, each man reading in her the part of her that was his.

She went home through the dark to the other house, the Penhale house, past the new gas going up bright on the Jirón, the city lit and loud with the money the birds had left, and she carried it home the way her uncle carried the stick and her grandfather carried the figure he would not write — the thing she now had the unit for and still could not add. In her own room she got the copybook out from under the board and opened it to the back, to the two columns, the soles and the nameless other, and she sat over it a long time with the pencil. She did not write anything in the second column. She understood now that she never would, that it was not a column that took figures, that the having of it was the whole of what could be done with it and the doing of the sum was a thing that would take, she saw, the rest of her life, and might not, even then, come out.

She did, before she slept, turn to the front of the book, to the practice of long division, the part that was for the world. And under the last sum, in the careful hand that got her left alone, she wrote, very small, not as a figure, as a note to herself, a thing she would not be able to explain to her tutor or her aunt or her grandfather and did not mean them ever to see: *Ask Uncle why he left the room above her.* Because she had felt it, the blank pale wood above her mother's notch, the space left and then used, and she had understood that her uncle had left room on the stick before her mother died, knowing, the way they all knew the bottom of the mountain, and not writing it down — and that the room he had left, that he had since had to fill, had been left for one person, and that there might be, lower yet on the stick, in the pale wood, room he was leaving still.

She closed the book and put it under the board and lay in the dark of the house the money built, and listened to her own breath go in and out with its small dry catch, in and out, in the room above the city of light, and did not yet know what the catch was the receipt for, only that it was a receipt, and that her body, like her uncle's stick and her grandfather's ledger, was keeping a count of something in a hand she had not yet learned to read.


## MANIFEST III — The Boom's Apex

### Chapter 16 · Carmen Penhale-Mei
*The open Pacific, off Macabí · 1870 · guano ≈ £14/ton · Chincha working face ≈ 45 ft (stumps)*

She had carried the sum out from Lima with her, the way she carried the copybook, and it still would not come out. Her grandfather Liddell's column anyone could do — so many tons at fourteen pounds the ton, less the freight and the Lima house and the dressmaker who had let out the bodice of her green merino twice this year because she was growing — and what it left was a number so large it stopped being money. Her uncle Mei's column had men in it, ran down the worn pale stick under his thumb, and went the wrong way against the first. The two would not cancel because they were not the same unit, and she had stopped, this last year, believing that the fault was in her carrying.

So she stood at the rail of the *Esmeralda Dos* with the spray salting the figures in her head and did not try, this once, to make them agree. She let them stand side by side and looked out at the thing they were both about.

*

They had been two days out of Callao, beating south and west under a hard clear sky, and the captain — a dry Genoese named Bóssola who ran cargo for the house and treated the owner's granddaughter aboard his deck as a hazard of the trade, like weather, to be civil to and not consulted — had said they would raise Macabí by the afternoon watch, and that she should not expect much. There was not much, he said, on Macabí. There was guano on Macabí, which was why there were forty sail standing off it, but it was not the Chinchas, he said, in the tone a man used for a thing that had been better once. The Chinchas they would pass in the night, he said, if she cared to be up, though there was nothing to see of them now in the dark and not a great deal by day.

She cared to be up.

She had asked to come. That was the part the Lima house could not get over, that Rosa worried at like a loose tooth — that the girl had asked. Other girls of thirteen in the gilded houses did not ask to go out to the workings; they asked for the bustle and the box at the opera that the railway money had built and the French dancing-master, and Carmen asked for those too, was not a saint about it, liked the green merino and the box and the ices at the confitería as much as Connie did — but she had asked, also, plainly, at the supper table with Liddell and Inés and Rosa all three turning to look at her, to be taken out to see where the money came from, and Liddell had laughed his short laugh and said, *She'll see how it's got, then, and be glad enough of the getting,* and had let her go, because Liddell could refuse her almost nothing and called the failing fondness and Carmen called it, privately, the only soft place in a man otherwise built entirely of scale.

But that was the lie she had told them, and she knew it for a lie even as Liddell granted it, the way she knew most things, half a beat after they were already true. She had not asked to see where the money came from. She knew where the money came from; it came from the white, everyone knew that. She had asked to see it because she had been out to the white once before, when she was small, and she could not remember it, and she had begun to need very badly to remember it, because of the cough.

*

The cough had been hers as long as she had a self to attach it to.

It was not much. That was what she said when anyone remarked it, *it is not much,* and it was true and it was the trained thing to say, the thing she had learned to say in company the way she had learned to keep her hands still and her face pleasant. It came on her dry, low in the chest, a catch and then a small hard run of it, three or four, no more, and she could stop it now by pressing the back of her wrist hard against her breastbone and holding her breath against the catch until it passed, and she did this so smoothly now, in a drawing-room, mid-sentence, that no one saw it but Mei, who saw everything, and who, the one time their eyes had met across a room while she did it, had not smiled and had not looked away, had only held her look for a moment with his one good eye and then looked down at his own hands, and she had understood that he had the same thing in his own chest, the same catch, and that he had been doing the same trick with it for twenty years, and that he had not wanted her to know he knew she had it.

She had asked Rosa once where it came from, the cough, and Rosa had said the Lima fogs, the *garúa*, that wet grey winter that sat on the city for months; everyone coughed in the *garúa*. And Carmen had let it stand, because Rosa wanted it to stand, but she had done her sum on it even then, small as she was, the watching part of her that did sums on everything: that Connie did not cough, and Connie grew up in the same fogs; that Inés did not cough; that Sam, her own brother, did not cough; that the only two people in either house who had the dry catch low in the chest and the trick of pressing it down were herself and the half-blind old man who had come up off the rock and whom she was to call uncle, and that the *garúa* did not single out one girl and one digger and pass over a whole gilded house besides.

*

The memory she did have was not of the white. It was of the green merino's grandmother — the first good dress, the one before this one, sea-green silk with a velvet sash, made for a child of six by the same Calle Mercaderes house, the dress she had worn the day her father took her down to the boat.

She could see the dress. She could not see the island. This was the maddening shape of it. The dress she could see perfectly, the exact green of it, the way the velvet sash pulled when she was lifted, because the dress had been the event for her then, six years old and got up like a doll because the men were taking her out and a Penhale child must be seen to be a Penhale child even on a guano boat — and she could see her father's hands, which she could see almost nowhere else in any memory she owned, big fair hands lifting her down into the lighter, a fair man, an Englishman, *the Englishman,* she had only the word for him now and the hands, the rest of him had gone the way the island had gone, into the white blur where the small child had not been keeping count — and she could see, she thought, the chutes, the long pale tongues of them coming down the cliff, though she could not be sure she had not built those out of the talk since.

What she could not see was the standing in the dust. She knew she had stood in the dust. The whole of the present voyage was built on the certainty that she had stood in the dust, because the cough had to have come from standing in the dust, and her father — *to see what built her dresses,* he had said, she had this secondhand from Rosa, who had it from a guest who had thought it a fine merry thing for a father to say and had repeated it at a supper years after he was dead, *he took the little one right out to the face, "to see what built her dresses"* — her father had carried her, in the sea-green silk, up to the working face of the Chincha North, where the men were cutting, so that a child of six could see the cliff of white that was paying for the silk on her back, and she had breathed there what the men breathed, for an hour, for a morning, however long a fond cruel proud young father held a doll-dressed daughter up to a wall of cutting dust to admire it.

And the dust had gone into her the way it went into them, only less, only an hour's worth, only a child's lungful where they had years — and it had stayed. It had stayed the way Mei's eye had stayed clouded and Mei's chest had stayed caught, a small permanent subtraction made in her in a single sea-green morning she could not even remember, against a fortune she would spend her whole life unable to remember the price of, and this was the thing she had come out to the water to stand inside again: not the white, the *standing in it,* the morning the account was opened in her body with her father's fair hands holding her up to be enrolled.

*

They raised the Chinchas in the last grey light before the moon, and Bóssola was right that there was not a great deal to see, and he was wrong about why.

She had expected mountains. Liddell still called them the mountains; the house called the trade *the islands* and the Chinchas *the mountains,* the way you went on calling a bald man by the name he had when he had hair. Her grandfather, the night before she sailed, telling her what she would see with a kind of rough pride, an old wrecker showing a child his beach — *three of them, girl, white as bone, two hundred foot out of the sea, you'll smell 'em before you see 'em, biggest thing of its kind God ever* — and then he had stopped, the way he stopped, and changed it, and said only *you'll see,* and crushed something, and hummed half a bar of one of his chapel tunes under his breath the way he did when a thing got near him, and she had filed that, because she filed everything, that her grandfather had begun to describe the islands to her in the present tense and had not been able to finish.

Now she saw why.

There were no mountains. There were three low dark humps on a moonlit sea, flat-topped, hardly higher than the swell seemed to want to wash them, and around them the riding-lights of the fleet that still came — fewer than the forty she'd been told once stood off them, a dozen now, a baker's dozen, waiting on the scrapings — and the white of them in the moon was not the white of a mountain. It was the white of a thing that had had its mountain taken off it. She did not have the figure for the Chinchas; Bóssola gave it to her, unasked, leaning on the rail beside her with his pipe, in the flat way he gave her the soundings, because she had stood there long enough that he had decided she was the kind of hazard one talked to. *Forty foot,* he said. *Forty-five at the high face, what's left of it. There was a hundred and eighty foot of it when the old man came out, your grandfather. He'll have told you.* He drew on the pipe. *They took a hundred and forty foot of it down to the boats. In one man's working life. You can stand on the cut now and see clean over the top of what used to be a mountain.*

A hundred and eighty to forty-five. She did the carrying. A hundred and thirty-five feet of it, gone, down the chutes, into the holds, onto the fields of England in one man's working life — her grandfather's working life, which was also, she understood, doing the second sum without wanting to, the working lives of however many men it took to send a hundred and thirty-five feet of a mountain to England by the barrow, the men whose count Mei kept on the stick, the men who were the difference between the two columns that would not agree.

She watched the three flat dark humps go by to starboard in the moonlight, and somewhere on the largest of them was the cut where her father had held her up in a sea-green dress to admire the cliff, and the cliff her father had held her up to was a hundred and thirty-five feet ago, was gone, was on the wheat fields of a country she had never seen, was, a part of it, however small, in the bottom of her own chest, the only piece of that mountain she would carry to her grave — and she pressed the back of her wrist to her breastbone, because the catch had come, dry and low, on the cold night air, and held her breath against it, three, four, and let it pass, and Bóssola, beside her, did not look at her, which she understood was his kindness, the same kindness as Mei's, the kindness of a man who knew the sound and would not make her account for it.

The islands went into the dark astern. She did not watch them out of sight. She had seen what she came partly to see, which was that the thing was finite, that it had been a mountain and was now a stump, that you could spend a mountain — and she found that knowing it as a number on Liddell's wall and seeing it as three flat humps where mountains had stood were not the same knowing at all, and that the second had a floor under it the first did not, a hard ground her grandfather's fourteen-pounds-the-ton had been floating over her whole life like the sea floating over a wreck.

*

Macabí, by daylight, was worse, because Macabí was the present tense.

It was a meaner rock than the Chinchas had been, lower, the guano on it thinner and dirtier, mixed with the rock, Bóssola said, not the clean ancient depth of the Chinchas but a poorer, harder-won deposit they had not troubled with while the great mountains stood and troubled with now because the great mountains were stumps. The *Esmeralda Dos* worked in among the waiting fleet and anchored, and a boat took Carmen ashore, because she had asked, and because the house's man on Macabí — a tired Scot named Ferrier who managed the loading for the consignment and who clearly thought a thirteen-year-old girl ashore on his rock was an affliction sent to try him — could not refuse the owner's blood, and walked her up from the landing toward the face with the air of a man showing a guest a thing he would rather she did not see.

She had thought, going up, that she was prepared. She had the figures. She had the two sums. She had her grandfather's hard practical view of it, which she had decided to wear like a coat — *this is how it's got, and you'll be glad of the getting* — because the coat let a person look at a thing without the thing getting into her, and she meant to look.

The coat did not last the climb.

It was the air that took it off her first, before she had seen anything, the way Bóssola had said the islands were a thing you smelled before you saw — but the Chinchas she had only smelled across water, and this was the face itself, this was standing where they cut, and when the men a little above her broke into the deposit with their bars and a cloud of it lifted on the wind and came down across where she stood, she understood in one breath, in the actual burning passage of one breath down her own actual throat, the thing that no figure on a wall had ever told her and that the cough in her chest had been trying to tell her since before she could speak. It was not a smell. A smell was a thing that happened in front of you. This happened *in* you. It went up her nose and down her throat and it was a scald, dry and total, and her eyes ran at once, helplessly, the way a knife-hand wept over an onion, not from feeling but because the body wept when a thing was being done to it, and she coughed — not the trained small catch she pressed down in drawing-rooms but a real cough, torn up out of her, doubling her, so that Ferrier put a hand under her elbow with a long-suffering courtesy and said something about going back down, the wind was bad today, the lady ought not — and she straightened, and wiped her eyes with the back of her glove, and did not go back down.

Because she had seen the men.

There were perhaps thirty of them at the face, cutting, in rags and straw hats with cloths bound over their faces against the dust, and they were Chinese, most of them, and some were not — she could not have said what the others were, Peruvian, some of them, convicts maybe, she did not know the marks of it yet — and they did not look at her. That was the thing she had not been prepared for, in all her preparing: that they did not look at her. She had been, her whole life, a girl that rooms turned toward, the owner's odd dark watchful granddaughter, half of one thing and half of another, looked at in Lima and looked at in the talk and looked at by Inés and by Liddell and by Mei; she was a thing the world appraised; and here on the face were thirty men who had not the breath to spare to look at the owner's granddaughter come up in her travelling dress to see how it was got, who were spending their breath on the bars and the barrows and the weight they owed before the rations were docked, the same weight, she thought, the exact same arithmetic, that her grandfather had described to her at supper as *how it's got* — and she stood in the dust she had been brought up the once at six to admire, and watched the men breathe what she had breathed, only without stopping, only without a fair-handed father to carry them back down to the boat after a morning, only for the years it took the air to do to a whole man what it had done in a single morning to a small part of her, and she did her sum.

She did the honest one. She made herself. She had come out here to find the floor under her grandfather's number, and here it was, breathing, thirty of it, at the face — and she set against the column of pounds that was being made out there in the holds the column that was being spent up here in the chests, and she found, standing in it, weeping helplessly from the air, that the second column was not a cost the first could be made to cover. It was not a price. A price was a thing you paid and were quit of. This was not paid; this was *taken,* the same substance taken twice, once as the white going down the chute to make the pounds and once as the breath going out of the men to make the white move — and her grandfather, who had a rate of exchange for everything in the world, had none for this, and she saw now, in the burning air, that he had none for it not because he had failed to work it out but because there was none to work, because the two things had never been the same thing measured twice but two different ledgers kept side by side in two tongues that did not translate, the way she herself was kept, half in one and half in the other, and could never make the halves agree.

She had thought, coming out, that knowing the true cost would let her decide something. That was the shape her whole young certainty had: that a girl who did the sum honestly, all the way down, on both sides, would reach the bottom of it and find an answer there, a clean figure, a thing to do. She stood at the bottom of it now, at the face, in the dust, with the men cutting and not looking at her and the white lifting and coming down across all of them alike, the owner's child and the spent men, the same white settling on her travelling dress and in her opened lungs and on the bound cloths over the faces of the men who would die of it — and she found there was no clean figure at the bottom. There was only the white, and the breath, and the two columns that would not agree, and the cough, hers now, the same as theirs, the small piece of the mountain she would carry to her grave, opened in her at six and not yet closed and never now to close.

She turned and let Ferrier take her down at last, because she had stopped being able to see for the weeping of her own eyes, and because she had seen what she came for, which was not what she had told them and not what she had told herself. She had not come to remember the standing in the dust. She had come to find out whether the sum could be done, and it could not, and she carried that down the rock with her as carefully as her father had once carried her up it, holding it close enough to keep and not yet near enough to know what she would do with a thing that did not balance, in a family whose whole great floating fortune was the belief that it did.

At the landing she stopped and looked back up at the face one time, the men small against the dirty white, the dust lifting off the cut and hanging on the wind over all of them, the spent men and the travelling dress alike. Then she pressed the back of her wrist to her breastbone — not because the catch had come, but because she had worn the gesture so smooth it had become the thing her hand did when there was a thing she could not yet say — and held her breath against nothing, three, four, and let it go, and turned, and got down into the boat with the figure she had come for shut up in the one place she had no column to keep it.


## MANIFEST III — The Boom's Apex

### Chapter 17 · Liddell Penhale
*Chincha North, off Pisco · 1871 · guano ≈ £14/ton · Chincha working face ≈ 40 ft*

The island had grown shorter than the ships.

He noticed it from the boat, coming in past the southern point with the loading anchorage opening up ahead of him, and at first he could not say what was wrong with the look of the place, only that the proportion of it had failed somehow, the way a familiar face fails when a man has lost a great deal of weight too fast. Then he had it. The barque nearest the chutes — a Liverpool ship, iron, her topgallant masts struck down for the long wait — stood with her trucks higher than the cliff she was loading from. He had landed here in 'forty-nine with the white going up a hundred and eighty feet sheer off the surf, higher than anything that floated, a thing you craned your neck at off the deck of a full-rigged ship; and now a working barque rode taller than the northern face, and a man on her royal yard, had there been a man fool enough to be sent up one in this calm, could have looked down on the top of the deposit and seen the bare brown saddle of the island showing through it like scalp through thinning hair.

Forty feet. He made it forty at the high face, where the cut had been driven back into the last of the good ground, and the figure went through him the way figures always went through him, unbidden, before he had agreed to feel anything about it. He had stood on this beach a younger man and read a hundred and eighty feet off the cliff and stopped breathing from the size of it. He read forty now and his breath did not change at all, and he told himself that was because forty feet of guano at fourteen pounds the ton, running back from the face as far as it ran, was still a fortune past the imagining of the boy who had carried his father's net down to a harbour in Cornwall to be sold off the end of it. He told himself the figure was large. The part of him that did the sums and never lied to him, the part that had made him everything he was, told him the figure was small, and getting smaller, and that he had known the day it would get this small for seventeen years, having counted it himself one night with a lantern when it stood at a hundred and sixty.

He did not listen to that part today. He had got very good, this last while, at not listening to that part.

The boat ground up onto the shingle and he stepped out dry-shod the way he always stepped out, and the smell closed over him, and that at least had not changed. That was the one thing the island still did at its old strength. The reek came off the cut and the chutes and the lighters as thick as it had come off it in 'forty-nine, ammoniac and rotten-sweet and underneath the mineral thing that he had learned in another life was the very strength of it, the part the fields were starving for; and he stood on the beach and drew it down into his chest the way a man draws on a pipe he has smoked too long to taste, and he thought, as he always thought, *there it is, the money, you can smell the money,* and he was comforted by it the way other men were comforted by church bells.

He went up toward the scale-house. The path had not moved but the climb was nothing now, a few minutes' walk up a brown slope where it had once been a haul, and that was the worst of it, that a man could feel in his own legs how much of the mountain was gone. He passed the gang at the face. There were fewer of them than there had been, and the ones there were worked a poorer ground — he could see it in the colour of the spoil, browner, more rock in it, the rich black-white of the deep old deposit cut clean away years since — and he watched a digger drive a bar into the face and bring down a block that was half stone, and curse it, and break it anyway, because the weight was the weight whether the cargo was the strength of England or the rubble under it. He did not stop to watch them. He had never once in twenty-two years let himself stop to watch them. He kept the figure of the gang in the corner of his eye, the way you keep a draught-mark, and walked on.

*

The scale-house was a different man's house now. Tom Quick had run it when Penhale first came, a heavy Liverpudlian with a sunburnt neck and a clerk's love of a clean column, a man who weighed the rice on the same beam he weighed the white and called it fair; and Quick had drunk himself most of the way into the ground in the years between, and the company had a younger man on the beam, and the younger man stood up off his stool fast when Penhale came in, which Quick had stopped doing about fifteen years ago. Penhale noted it. He noted everything still. The young man's deference was worth a thing to him that he could not have named and would not have wanted named, and the wanting of it told him, if he had been listening, something about how the ground had moved under him this last while; but he was not listening, so it told him only that the boy knew his place.

"Mr. Penhale." The young man had the manifests ready. He always had them ready; word ran ahead of the boat. "The *Caradoc*'s topped off and warped out this morning. We've the *Antonia* under the chutes now and four more on the buoys. The figure for the season—"

"I have the figure for the season." He had had it before he left Lima. He had it the way he had the smell, in him, ahead of his consent. The figure for the season was the highest figure the trade had ever made in the history of the world, and the reason it was the highest figure the trade had ever made was the reason he had come down to stand on the island and look at it with his own eyes, because he did not entirely trust a figure that good to be read off a paper in a Lima counting-room. Fourteen pounds the long ton, and climbing yet, the price gone up every season for years now while the mountain came down, and any man who could not see what a beautiful thing that was had no business in the trade. Scarcity. There was the whole of it. There was less of the white in the world every year and the world wanted more of it every year, and the two lines of that, the having and the wanting, had crossed somewhere back in the sixties and gone on past each other in opposite directions, and the gap between them was where his fortune lived. The gap was the most beautiful number he knew. It was the only thing on the island that was still getting bigger.

He stood at the open door of the scale-house with the manifests in his hand and looked out over the anchorage, the ships on the buoys waiting their turn the way they had always waited, riding light, and the lighters going out white-laden to the *Antonia*, and the plumes of the cargo coming down the chutes off a cliff that a barque now overtopped, and he felt, standing there, richer than he had ever felt in his life. He let himself feel it. He had earned it. He read the high price off the season's figure the way a master reads a deepening load-line, as proof the venture rode well and would ride better, and he did not read the falling mountain at all, though it stood not two hundred yards from him with the brown showing through, because a man chose what marks he read, and he had chosen, and a hymn that he hated came up the back of his throat for no reason he would admit to, *we are but strangers here, we do not crave a home,* his father's voice off a chapel bench seventy years and half a world away, and he shut his teeth on it and it went down. It only ever came up when he was afraid. He was not afraid. He was rich. He had the season's figure in his hand to prove it.

*

He had a thing to settle in Lima, and the thing in Lima was his daughter.

She was waiting for him at the counting-room over the Callao mole when he came up two days later — she was always waiting, she had a way of being already in the chair he was coming to sit in, so that he arrived to find her risen from it just that moment, the warmth of her not quite gone out of the wood, a courtesy that was also a notice served — and she had the books open and a column run and a thing she wanted that she had decided he was going to give her, and he could see all of that on her the moment he came through the door, the way he could see the soundness of a hull through a coat of fresh paint. He had taught her to be read that little. She was better than he was at most of it now and she still could not keep him from reading the want off her, and he loved her for the want and despised the showing of it, both, in the one glance, and did not trouble to sort the two feelings out because he had never in his life sorted feelings out; he priced them and filed them and moved on.

She was twenty this year, near it, and turned out like money — Lima fashion to the last pin, the deliberate expensiveness of a woman who had worked out young that she would have to look twice the principal to be heard half the daughter at a table of men — and she had his eyes in her mother's face, the pale far-reading Penhale eyes that went to the horizon-line of a thing and stripped it, and behind them she was doing sums while she smiled at him, which he knew because he had stood in front of enough men doing sums behind a smile to know the particular stillness of it. It was his own stillness. She had got it off him the way she had got the eyes, without asking, and it was the nearest thing to tenderness he ever felt, to see his own hard usefulness looking back at him out of a face that was half Rosa's, and he would have died before he said so, and she would have died, he thought, to hear it, because she did not want to be told she was like him. She wanted to be told she was as good as him. She had built her whole self to be told it. He had never once told her, and he was not going to start today, and the not-telling of it was the largest sum in the room and neither of them ever entered it in any book.

"You stood on the island," she said. Not a question. She had smelled it on his coat.

"I stood on the island."

"And?"

"It's forty feet at the high face." He set the season's manifests down on the desk between them, on top of the books she had open, a small staking of the ground. "Less where they've gone after the deep ground. Browner spoil every season. The *Caradoc* loaded half rubble in her lower hold and her master said nothing because at fourteen pound the ton a man does not quarrel about a little rock in his rubble." He let that sit. "And the price is fourteen and climbing. You know all this. Why have you got that column run."

She did not flinch and he had not expected her to; he had built her better than that. She turned the book a half-turn toward him, the way the tally-man Mei used to turn a count toward Quick at the chute-head a lifetime ago, *here, look, I am not arguing, I am showing you a sum,* and Penhale registered, without pleasure, that his daughter and the Chinese broker had the same gesture, the gesture of a person who has learned that the only safe way to take ground off a powerful man is to make him think he is reading it himself.

"Because the price is fourteen and climbing," she said, "and the mountain is forty feet and falling, and I have run the two lines out, the way you taught me to run two lines out, and I want to know what you see where they cross."

"I see the richest season in the history of the trade."

"That is where they cross *now*." She put one gloved finger on the column, low, where the figures she had run forward ran off the bottom of the good ruled paper and onto the rough margin she had ruled herself in pencil. "I ran them on. Out past now. I want to know what you see *there*."

He looked at the pencilled margin. He did not need to read it. He had read it himself, the whole of it, on the cut face at a hundred and sixty feet with a lantern in his hand seventeen years before she was old enough to hold a pen, and he had carried the answer in him every season since the way the chest carries a breath of bad air it has decided not to mention. He knew exactly what stood at the foot of her pencilled column. The two lines did not go on crossing wider forever. They crossed, and the gap opened, and the gap was the fortune; but the mountain was a finite thing and the line of it ran down to a floor, ran down to bare rock, and when the having-line hit that floor it stopped, it could not go lower, there was no more white under the white; and a price, however high, was only a price on a thing that existed, and the day the thing did not exist the price was a number against nothing, a load-line painted on a hull that had already gone under. He knew it. He had known it longest of any man alive. His daughter had run it out in pencil on a margin and was holding it up to him across a desk like a count she would not argue, only show, and the showing of it put the hymn back up his throat so fast and so hard that for a moment he could not have said a word over it if the building had been on fire.

He crushed it. He had a lifetime's practice.

"I see a woman," he said, "who has run a column past the edge of the paper, which is a thing children do, and clever children oftenest, because they have not yet stood where the figures actually fall. There is no line in commerce that runs to the bottom of the page. Something always comes in before the bottom. A war. A new ground. A government that needs the money worse than the year before and finds a way to make more of it. You have run a straight line in a world that has never once been straight, and you have frightened yourself with it, which is what a straight line is for." He squared the season's manifests on top of her book, covering the pencilled margin, ending it. "We are richer this year than last and we will be richer next year than this, and the reason is the very thing you have drawn there and read backward. It is going. Of course it is going. A thing that is going, that men cannot do without and cannot get more of — that is not the end of a fortune, Inés. That is the whole of one. You have drawn the end of the world and it is only the description of money."

She looked at him a moment with the pale eyes he had given her, doing the sum behind them, and he watched her decide not to spend the rest of her hand today. She was too good to spend it into a closed face. She drew the book back from under his manifests, gently, and closed it, and the pencilled margin went away inside it, and she said, in the other voice, the daughter-voice she kept for when the principal-voice had been turned off the field, "Will you take the front room or shall I send your supper up," and he said the front room, and that was the end of it, and he had won, and he knew the exact shape of the thing he had won the way he knew the exact shape of every cargo he had ever lifted off a rock, which was to say he knew it was sound and he knew what it had cost and he did not let himself add the two together.

*

He took the front room and he took the supper up there alone, because he did not like to eat in front of people any more, his teeth being what they were, and he sat with the lamp and the season's figures and a glass of the Spanish brandy Rosa kept for him, and he counted.

He counted because counting was the one thing that had never failed him. A man could be sold by his own father off the end of a net for the price of a week's drink; a man could be stood in a line on a deck and have his teeth looked at and a figure said over him to another man with a book; a man could lose every soft thing he was born wanting and bury a son in the Callao ground that the son had been carried up out of with no name to it that the family ever heard said aloud — Daniel, in the war, five years gone now, blown to nothing at some battery wall defending the interests of a cargo, and Penhale had paid for the Masses Rosa wanted and had stood at the back of the cathedral in his dark coat and had felt, where the grief should have been, only the cold clean click of a column closing, *one short, close it up,* the same sound the island had taught him forty years ago, and had hated himself for it for exactly as long as it took to leave the cathedral and get back to the figures, which was not long — a man could lose all of it, every account but the one, and the one account that did not betray you was the count. Six and carry. The figure was the figure. The figure did not soften when you grieved and did not harden when you wanted, did not look at you the way his daughter had looked at him over the closed book, the way the digger A-Yün had looked at the sea the morning he set his bar down and walked off the cliff, the way the dead all looked, with that particular patience, as though they had run the column to the bottom of the page and were only waiting for you to get there too. The figure looked at nothing. The figure was clean. He had spent his life on the buying side of it for the cleanness of it, and he was not going to be talked off that side now by a pencil mark, not by his daughter's and not by the one in his own chest that he kept covered with the season's good news the way she had tried to cover her margin and he had covered it back.

He took out his handkerchief to wipe his mouth, and stopped, and looked at it.

It was the yellow one, the old one, the one he counted coin into out of long habit because a man raised the way he was raised did not put down good silver on bare wood; and there was a fineness on it, a dust, pale, the merest grey-white film worked into the cloth from a thousand handlings of coin that had itself been handled by men who had handled the white before it was ever coin, the chutes to the lighters to the holds to London to the bank to the bag to his palm to the cloth — and he had carried this same dust in his pocket for twenty-two years and never once thought a thing about it, it was only the trade, it was only money's grey, the way a fisherman's coat is salt and a collier's hands are black. He looked at it now in the lamplight, the pale film on the yellow cloth, and for no reason he would have allowed in daylight he was put in mind of the brown coming up through the white on the island, the scalp through the thinning hair, the thing wearing through. The same colour, near enough. He did not finish the thought. He had a great gift for not finishing thoughts; it was most of what had kept him whole. He folded the handkerchief over the dust so he need not look at it and put it away and drank the brandy off and went back to the figures, which were clean, and large, and his.

*

The thing about the scientists came to him a week later, in the way such things always came to him, sideways, off a paper, brought up by a man who had no notion he was bringing up anything but the day's gossip.

It was at the Club, the English one, the long room over the harbour where the trade gathered to eat indifferent mutton and price the world, and a Hamburg man Penhale half-knew, an honest merchant and a bore, had a German journal folded back to a page and was reading bits of it out in his careful English to whomever would hear, a paper by some naturalist or agricultural man, one of the Germans who came down now and again with their instruments and their patience to study the islands the way they studied everything, to death. The man had been out to the rookeries. The man had counted the birds — counted them, the Hamburg fellow said, laughing a little at the labour of it, *they have a method, they count the ground and reckon up from it, the German thoroughness* — and the man had got a number, and the number was smaller than the number the same sort of man had got a decade before, smaller by half, and the German had written a sober careful piece to say that the guano was not a mine but a crop, that the birds laid it down faster than a mountain ever was laid down by any other means, that it would keep coming, would keep renewing itself, the white falling fresh off the cliffs as fast as it was carried away — *but only*, the German wrote, *only if the birds were let to do it*; and the birds were not being let to do it, the birds were being driven off the very ground that men dug, the nests crushed under the barrows, the colonies cut into and scattered, fewer every season by half a decade's count, so that the trade was not spending a hoard, the German said, the trade was killing the thing that made the hoard, and would end not when the mountain was dug out but sooner, when the birds that built the mountain were gone.

The room thought it very funny. A crop. Bird-dung as a crop, to be farmed, the seabirds tended like Friesian cattle so they would oblige with more of it; the Germans would have you nurse a cormorant. Somebody said the price said otherwise — fourteen pound and climbing, and a thing that was running out did not sell cheaper every year, it sold dearer, so the market, which was wiser than any German with a spyglass, plainly did not believe a word of it — and the room agreed that the market was wiser than the German, and the Hamburg man folded his journal away good-naturedly, having entertained them, and the mutton came.

Penhale said nothing. He ate his mutton, what he could of it, and he priced the thing the way he priced everything, fast, in the cold clean back room of himself where no hymn reached, and his pricing of it was this: that it changed nothing. Suppose the German was right. Suppose it ended not in thirty years with the mountain but in ten with the birds. Then there was less of it than even he had reckoned, and less of a thing the world could not do without was dearer, not cheaper, so the price would climb the faster, and the gap would open the wider, and the years between now and the floor were worth more per year than any years the trade had ever known — which meant a man who understood it should not slow his digging by a single barrow but should dig as hard and as fast as the labour could be driven, should cut the last of it as quick as the chutes could carry it, should be the man who got the most of the last of it before the floor came up, whether the floor came up in ten years or thirty. The German's terrible news, run through the count, came out the same end as everything came out: *cut faster.* It always came out *cut faster.* He had known that since he was thirty-six with a lantern on the face, and the German had only put a date on the thing Penhale already lived by, and a date you did not believe was no different from a date that was wrong.

He did not believe it. That was the part he kept in the cold room and would not bring out. He had run the German's argument and found it sound and answered it with *cut faster*, and all of that was the man who held the pen; but under the man who held the pen, in the place the count did not reach, there was a thing that simply would not have it, would not have the birds be the wealth and the mountain be only their leavings, would not have it that the white he had spent his life standing on the buying side of was not a hoard he was spending but a living thing he was killing, because if that were so then the whole arithmetic of his life was wrong from the first figure. If the thing was alive then it had never been salvage. And if it had never been salvage then he had spent forty years valuing it wrong, stripping and logging and keeping and writing off a thing that was the opposite of wreckage, a thing that made more of itself if you only left it, the one kind of thing in all the world his eye had never once been able to see — and a man who had got that wrong, got the one great thing of his life wrong from the bottom up, was not a rich man at all. He was only a man who had stood a long time on a mountain, taking it down a barrow at a time, calling the taking wealth, while the thing that was actually worth having poured off the cliff over his head in a dark living river he had looked up at exactly once, on his first morning, and had filed, without heat, as more cargo coming.

The hymn came up. Right there in the long room over the harbour with the mutton going cold and the trade laughing about Friesian cormorants, his father's drink-thick voice came up the back of his throat unbidden and entire — *we are but strangers here, we do not crave a home; Heaven is our home* — and he was afraid, plainly and coldly afraid, more afraid than he had been since a deck thirty years gone, and the fear was not of the floor and not of the birds and not even of being poor again, though all of that was in it. The fear was that the boy on the chapel bench, the one who had not yet been sold, the one who still believed there was a home somewhere that did not run out, might have been the only one of all the men Penhale had ever been who had got the sum right.

He crushed it. He had a lifetime's practice and he was very good and it went down, the way it always went down, the hymn and the fear and the boy together, pressed back under the season's figure where they belonged. He finished what he could of the mutton. He was rich; the price said so; the price was wiser than any German. And in the morning he sent word down to the island, to the young man on the beam, that the gangs were to be driven harder through the good weather while it held, every barrow they could make, because the season was the best the trade had ever seen and a man did not leave a season like that lying in the ground.


## MANIFEST III — The Boom's Apex

### Chapter 18 · The Census-Taker
*Chincha North, off Pisco · 1872 · guano ≈ £14/ton (apex) · Chincha working face ≈ 40 ft (stumps)*

The square was where he had left it, which was the first thing that had gone right in nine days.

Doktor Konrad Maur had cut it into the flat rock of the north shoulder eleven years before with a cold chisel and a hand-drill and four brass pins set in lead, a metre square, true to the compass, the corners deep enough that no boot and no barrow and no decade of guano dust could lose them, and he had photographed it and drawn it and entered its bearings in the back of his field-book in his own crabbed Latin shorthand so that whatever fool came after him — and he had assumed a fool would come after him, the trade being what it was — could find the one fixed point on these islands from which a man might honestly measure a thing across time. He had been thirty-eight then and very sure of his method and a little vain of it, and now he was forty-nine and the method was the only thing left that he was sure of, and he knelt at the square in the white forenoon glare with his knees on a folded sack and brushed the dust off the four pins with a badger brush and found them sound, all four, and felt the small clean satisfaction a man feels when at least the instrument has not betrayed him. The rock had not moved. The pins had not moved. Everything that the pins were there to measure had moved, was moving, would not stop moving until there was nothing left to move; but the pins held, and a man could begin from the pins.

He weighted the corners of his counting-frame over them — a wooden lattice he had made in Lima, the metre divided into a hundred small squares of waxed thread so that the eye had something to hold against the swarm — and he settled his spectacles and bent over it and began to count nests.

There were none.

He counted them anyway, because the discipline of the count did not permit a man to write *none* before he had looked at every one of the hundred small squares and confirmed it, and he looked, square by waxed square, his lips moving in German on the numbers though there was no number to say, and what he found in the metre that eleven years before had held nine living nests of the guanay packed rim to rim was this: a crust. A grey-white crust of old dung gone hard, and in it, set like coins pressed into wax and then forgotten, the brittle stamped ghosts of the nests that had been — the low ring-shapes, the matted feather, here and there a fish-bone bleached to a needle, and in one corner of the metre, in the ninth small square from the left in the topmost row, a single eggshell, broken, very old, the inside of it stained the dark of a thing long dead. He looked at it a long time. Then he wrote in the field-book, in the column he had ruled for this square eleven years before and headed with its bearing and the word *Nidi*: a date, and the figure 0, and beside the figure, because the column called *Observationes* would not let him lie either by saying too little, the words *crusta tantum* — only the crust — and *testa una* — one shell.

Nine, the column said, in his own hand, dated 1861. Then a line for 1863, the year he had come back the second time, that said six. Then the long gap of the war years when no one would carry a naturalist to an occupied rock, and then a four, and a two, dated this morning's nearest prior visit, and now, under all of them, the nothing. The column ran down the page like a tide going out. He had ruled it to be read down. He had built the whole apparatus of his life — the pins, the frame, the photographs, the eleven years — precisely so that this column could be read down, by anyone, in a single glance, and mean what it meant without his having to raise his voice; and now it was ruled and dated and true and read down to nothing, and there was no one on the island to read it but himself.

He sat back on his heels on the folded sack and looked out past the frame at where the colony had been.

He could see the shape of where it had been. That was the cruelty of the thing, the scientific cruelty that he felt in his chest as a kind of pressure and that he distrusted because it was not a measurement. The whole seaward shoulder, two hundred and forty paces of it by the depth of ninety that he had walked and measured and re-measured across eleven years, lay before him in the flat morning light as a great pale field of the dead, the crusts of the nests packed as close as they had ever been, rim touching ruined rim, a quarter of a million pairs of them written into the rock in the negative, the way a thing is written by its absence — and over it, nothing. No bird. Not the noise. He had stood here in 1861 and not been able to hear a man shout three paces off for the din of them, two hundred thousand pair of the guanay sitting their nests wing to wing and quarrelling and the chicks crying to be fed and the dark commuting river of them pouring overhead out to the shoal and back, and the smell of the living colony, which was not the smell of the cargo but a wetter ranker thing, fish and warmth and droppings fresh, the smell of a place doing the work of making more of itself. He had hated the smell honestly in 1861 and put cloth over his face like the diggers, and he would have given a year of his remaining life to smell it now. There was only the dry crust-smell of the old deposit, the trade's smell, the ammonia that did not change. The colony was not thinned. He made himself use the exact word, against the pressure in his chest. It was not thinned. It was *gone*, from this shoulder, where eleven years before it had been the densest concentration of breeding seabird he or any man had ever set eyes on; the guanay that still bred on these three islands bred now on the far shoulders and the lee ledges where the cutting had not yet come, in numbers that he was here, across the next four days, to count — and he already knew, kneeling at the dead square with the badger brush in his hand, what the four days would tell him, because the column on the page had already told him, and the column did not lie, and the column was ruled to be read down.

He folded the frame. He moved on to the next station.

*

He worked the way he always worked, which was the only thing he knew how to do with the pressure in his chest, which was to break the unbearable thing into squares small enough to count.

This was Maur's whole method and his whole faith and, he was aware, his whole evasion. You did not stand on the cliff and grieve the colony. Grief was not a quantity; you could not put grief in a column and let another man read it down; grief was the diggers' word *thousands* and the chemist's word *inexhaustible* and every other word men used to mean *I have stopped counting*. You counted. You laid your metre square over one patch of the living ground and you counted the nests in the metre with your lips moving, and you paced the breadth and the depth of where the living birds still were, and you multiplied, and you wrote the figure small and dated it, and you did not feel anything about the figure until it was down, because a man who felt about the figure while he was getting it got the figure wrong, and a wrong figure helped no one and could even be used against you by men who wanted the figure to be wrong. So he counted. He spent the morning of the second day on the southwest ledge where the boobies still bred, the *piquero*, white-breasted, plunge-divers, less particular than the cormorant about the nearness of men, and he counted them with a kind of relief because they were still there to count, thousands of pairs of them, and he paced and multiplied and wrote it down. He spent the afternoon among the great pelicans on the low southern rocks, the *alcatraz*, two metres of wing, sitting their few large nests with their enormous patient prehistoric faces, and counted them too. And on the morning of the third day he went out in a hired boat with two Chincha boatmen who thought him mad — *el alemán que cuenta los pájaros*, the German who counts the birds, he had heard them call him on the beach, not unkindly, the way you speak of a harmless lunatic — and had himself rowed slow along the seaward foot of all three islands to take the long census, the river-count, the birds at sea.

That was the count that finished it.

Because the river was thin. He had no square for the sea and no pins; the open-water birds were the one population he could only estimate, by the old rough method, by the width and density of the dawn commute as it poured off the cliffs to the shoal — and he had timed it that dawn from the boat with his watch in his hand, counting the birds across a measured gap of sky against the lightening east for ten minutes by the second-hand, the way he had counted it in 1861 and 1863, the same gap, the same hour, the same method, eleven years apart, so that the two figures could be set side by side and read. In 1861 the river across that gap had been so dense he had given up the individual count after four minutes and estimated by the band, a black unbroken band the breadth of three of his hands held at arm's length, pouring without a break for the whole of the time he watched, and he had reckoned the breeding population of the three islands then, by that count crossed against the colony counts, at something near twelve millions, and had written the figure down and then, in the privacy of the boat, said it aloud once in German to hear what so large a true number sounded like in the human mouth, and it had sounded like nothing the mouth was built to say.

This dawn he had counted the birds one at a time across the same gap of sky and not run out of fingers.

Not quite. There were more than his fingers. But they came in strings and gaps now, in skeins, a knot of thirty and then a stretch of pale empty sky and then a straggle of eight, where eleven years before there had been the unbroken band, and he had counted them across the ten minutes and done the arithmetic against the colony counts of the two prior days, soberly, twice, checking it, wanting it to be wrong; and the figure that came up was six. Six millions, near enough, where there had been twelve. Halved. In a little over a decade, with the war's idle years folded in when no man cut at all, the breeding population of the richest seabird ground on the earth had been cut in half, and the half that remained was breeding on the margins, driven off the great central shoulders by the one thing it could not abide and the trade could not stop doing, which was the digging — the barrows over the eggs in the nesting season, the men and the noise and the carts on the very flat tops of the islands where the flat-ground nesters, the guanay above all, would not stay to raise a chick. He sat in the stern of the boat with the field-book on his knee and the figure under his hand and the two Chincha boatmen resting on their oars and watching him with the patience of men paid by the day, and he understood the whole of it at last, not as a feeling but as a structure, complete, the way you understand a clockwork when the back is off.

The birds were the deposit. Not a figure of speech: the literal fact, the only fact that finally mattered, that the white mountain the whole world was fighting over and borrowing against and growing rich on was the slow exudate of the living colony, a thing the birds laid down at some small annual rate, year over year over a thousand years, out of the fish they ate; and that the men of the trade were not, as they believed and as the circulars said, mining a finite hoard of stone that would one day simply run out and leave them to find another. They were doing a thing far stupider, which only a man who had counted the column down could see. They were spending, all at once, in a single generation, the laid-up product of a thousand years of the birds' work — the principal — and at the same time, by the very act of spending it, by the digging itself, they were killing the birds, which were the only engine that had ever made the stuff or could ever make it again. They were burning the granary to get at the last of the grain, and calling the fire prosperity, and the price rose as they did it because the world could see the grain getting scarce and could not see the birds at all. Stop the cutting in the nesting season — close the islands, as the old Indians had closed them, who had understood this with no chemistry at all and had made it death to trouble a nesting bird, the earliest law of its kind he knew of anywhere on the earth, a wild people protecting a wild thing because they had grasped that the thing was the wealth and the dung only its interest — close the islands, and the colony would come back, slowly, over a human lifetime or two, and lay the deposit down again forever, a renewing rent on a living estate. Keep cutting, and in a few more years there would be neither deposit nor bird, only bare rock and an empty sky, and no chemistry and no capital and no second civilisation would ever make another ounce of it, because the thing that made it would be extinct on these islands, and you could not assay back into existence a bird that was gone.

He knew it entire. He had the figures. He sat in the boat and held them, and felt, against all his discipline, the pressure in his chest become for a moment something very like terror, the specific terror of a man who can read a clock that no one around him believes is a clock.

*Reverse course, gentlemen, well clear of the rock, slow,* he said to the boatmen, in his careful Spanish, and they bent to the oars, and as the boat came round under the seaward cliff of the north island he made them hold it a moment so that he could look up at the working face, because a census-taker measured what killed his subject as carefully as he measured the subject, and the face was what killed them.

---

*Forty feet of the white stands above the cut on the great north shoulder where two hundred feet stood when the first ships came; the rest is gone, out to the holds, out around the Horn, into the soil of three continents and the bread of men who will never see a cormorant. On the flat top, where the guanay nested a quarter of a million pairs to the shoulder, the gangs are at the dregs of it, the barrows running the last of the deposit to the chutes; and where the birds will not nest now there is nothing to disturb, so the digging there is undisturbed. The guanay that are left breed on the far lee ledge, six millions over the three islands where there were twelve a decade gone, half of them gone in eleven years; and at dawn the thinned river of them still pours off the cliff to the cold green shoal and back, a herring in, a grain of the white down, the only ledger that ever balanced on these islands kept now by half the clerks it once had. The water below the cliff is cold and right and dense with the anchovy, as it has always been; the failure is not in the sea, not yet. The failure is on the rock. A bird flies up off the lee ledge with a fish crosswise in its bill and beats over the bare cut shoulder where its kind no longer nest and out toward the holds-ward sea, and does not know it is the interest on a spent estate, and does not know it is being counted, and could not say the figure if it did. The figure is six. It was twelve. A man in a boat below the cliff has it in a book.*

---

He had himself put ashore at Pisco that evening and took the diligence up to Lima the next day with the field-book buttoned inside his coat, because the figure was no good in a book on a shelf in Hamburg; the figure was only good if the men who owned the islands and the men who governed the country that owned the men who owned the islands could be made to read it down the column and understand that the column was a clock. He had told himself this for eleven years. He believed it still, in the diligence, with the brown desert going by and the green ribbon of the river-valley and the white islands sinking behind him into the haze, the way a man believes the thing that has organised his whole life and that he cannot now afford to disbelieve.

They received him very civilly.

That was the part Maur had not been able to prepare himself for, across eleven years and four voyages, though he had been received civilly each time and ought to have learned. He had imagined, when he was young and the method was new, that the difficulty would be doubt — that hard men of commerce would dispute his figures, demand his methods, accuse him of German sentiment or scientific fancy, and that he would meet the doubt with the pins and the photographs and the dated column and win the argument on the ground of fact, because the fact was on his side and the fact was unanswerable. He had armoured himself, all these years, against an argument. And there was no argument. That was the thing. He sat in a good room in a guano-financed building near the Plaza with three men who mattered — a deputy of the consignment, a Peruvian official with the guano portfolio whose name was in the newspapers, and an Englishman of one of the houses, courteous, attentive, with the appraising still pale eyes of his trade — and he laid out the column and the photographs of the dead square and the two dawn counts eleven years apart, and he gave them the figure, six where there had been twelve, and the mechanism of it, the birds as the engine and the digging as the thing that stopped the engine, clearly, without sentiment, in the plainest commercial terms he could find, which were not difficult to find, because the thing was at bottom an argument about capital and he made it as an argument about capital: *gentlemen, you are consuming your principal and destroying the means of its renewal in the same motion, and the price you take comfort from is the very proof of the scarcity that is ruining you.* And they listened. They listened with every appearance of grave attention, and the official nodded, and the Englishman asked one or two precise and intelligent questions about his method that showed he had understood it perfectly, and when Maur had finished there was a short respectful silence, and then the deputy of the consignment thanked him with real warmth for his important and valuable work, and said that the Doktor's findings would of course be studied with the greatest care, and laid the report — Maur watched the man's hands do it — face down on the side table, on top of two other reports already there, and turned the conversation, easily, courteously, to the price.

Because the price was fourteen pounds the ton this season and climbing, the highest it had ever stood in the history of the trade, and the deposit, whatever this German said of birds, was still coming out of the rock by the thousand tons a week and selling the instant it touched a London dock, and Peru had borrowed against the next decade of it sums that beggared the mind, and railways were going up across the desert on the strength of it, and every man in that room and every interest he represented was making more money than his father had ever dreamed a man could make, and not one of them, Maur understood, sitting in the good chair with his life's work face down under two other men's life's work on the side table, was lying. They were not corrupt and they were not stupid and they had not failed to understand him. They had understood him entirely. They had heard a true man give a true figure that meant the end of everything they had, and they had set it gently face down, because the figure was small and the price was large, and a thing that pays fourteen pounds the ton today cannot be heard to be dying, however carefully it is counted, by men who will be richer tomorrow than they were today. The fact was not on his side. He had been wrong about that for eleven years.
