The fact was perfectly on his side, the column ran down to nothing and could not be disputed, and it did not matter at all, because the fact had no price and the price was at its apex, and there was no instrument in that room or in the whole machinery of the trade that could weigh a true thing that did not pay against a false thing that did.

He gathered his photographs. The Englishman, at the door, was kind to him in the way of a man who has not stopped thinking after the others have, and shook his hand, and said, lower, almost privately, with something behind the pale eyes that might have been the ghost of belief, "You may be right, Doktor. I daresay you are right." And then, because he was a man of the trade and could not finish a sentence any other way, "But there's no money in being right too early. There never is." And he went back in to the price.

Maur walked out into the Lima evening with the field-book buttoned again inside his coat, against his ribs, where it had ridden for eleven years and would ride for whatever years were left, the pins and the dead square and the column ruled to be read down and read by no one; and he stood a moment in the gilded square in the warm dusk, among the carriages and the new gas-lamps and the great wealth of the guano age at its golden height, and he took the book out and opened it to the page and looked once more at the figure he had carried up from the rock, six under twelve under all the falling years, true, dated, unheard. Then he put it away, and went to find his lodging, and resolved, because there was nothing else a man of his kind could do, to come back in two years and count them again.


## MANIFEST III — The Boom's Apex

### Chapter 19 · Mei Loong-fa
*Callao, and a lesser island off Pisco · 1872–73 · guano ≈ £14/ton · Chincha working face ≈ 40 ft (stumps); his own island shallower*

The lawyer's man put the pen into his hand and Mei held it the way he had once held the slate-flake in the dark of the shed — as a thing he had not asked for and would use anyway.

It was a steel pen, new, with a bone shaft worn smooth where other hands had worn it, and the paper under it was thick and had a seal pressed into the foot of it in red wax the colour of a thing that had bled and dried, and the paper was in Spanish and in English both, two columns set side by side down the page, and Mei could read most of one and a fair part of the other now, twenty-three years on, and he read them, and that was the whole of it, that he could read them. He sat in a counting-room in Callao with the harbour out the window and the white dust of nothing on the floor because there was no white here, only the smell of ink and sealing-wax and the clerk's cold coffee, and he read the paper before he signed it, every line, slow, his head turned a little to bring the page to his right eye, while the lawyer's man waited and a fly worked the rim of the cup, and the reading was the thing he had paid for. Not the island. The reading.

The island was three hundred soles a year on the lease and a tenth of the cargo to the consignment and the rest his, and it was a stump of rock off the Pisco coast that the Chincha company had worked down to its last good face and let go, the way a man lets go a mine when the easy reef is out, and the deposit on it was not deep — forty feet at the high face when he climbed it, less where the cut had already eaten in, and below the white the bare red rock showing through in places like a bone through a wound that would not close. He had walked it before he signed for it. He had stood on the working face with the lessor's agent at his elbow naming him a price and he had read the rock the way Penhale read a hull, the way the wrecker read a wreck — what it was worth, stripped, and how long the stripping would take, and whether it would pay before it was gone — and the answer was that it would pay, at fourteen pounds the ton in London and the price still climbing, it would pay handsomely and for not very long, and Mei had known both halves of that answer standing on the rock and had signed for it anyway. He had the figure in his head the whole time the agent talked. He had had a figure in his head since he was a runner on the Amoy wharf, and the figure now said: *short and rich. Like a man who buys a horse already foundered to win one race.*

He signed. *Mei Loong-fa.* He wrote it in the characters first, on the line, because the line was his now and he would put on it what he chose; and then under it, because the law wanted it, in the Roman letters he had learned off the staves of cargo, he wrote it again the way the manifests spelled him, *Mei Long-Fa,* with the hyphen the English liked, and the two of him sat on the line one above the other, the man from the lane and the man the books knew, and he looked at the two of them and did not feel what he had thought, the once or twice he had let himself think of this, that he would feel.

He had thought he would feel the ship.

He had carried, twenty-three years, against his ribs, a folded paper with the character for eight on it that a broker had drawn for him in spilled wine, and he had carried it past the time the salt got into it and past the time the folds wore through and past the time the rest of the writing on it went to a grey smear no one could read, and he carried it still, in a flat tin box now to keep the last of it from going to powder, because it was the first ledger that had ever been kept on him and he did not throw away ledgers. He had thought, the once or twice, that to sit on the owning side of a paper at last — to hold the pen that the man with the book had held, to be the thumb and not the chin — would be to close that first paper out. To finish the eight years that had never finished. To stand, finally and forever, where the heavy man stood.

He signed, and the lawyer's man sanded the ink and turned the page for the witnesses, and Mei sat with his two names drying on the line and felt nothing close. The first paper went on owing what it owed. The ship did not come back to be paid. He understood, sitting there, that he had got the arithmetic of it wrong — that a debt was not a thing you cancelled by becoming the creditor of a different man; that you could buy the whole coast and the first paper would still have eight years on it and a grey house at the end of them with no one in it; that the man who had been cargo did not stop having been cargo by buying cargo of his own. He had spent his whole second life believing the only safe place was the side that held the pen. He held the pen. The flake of slate and the steel pen were the same hand, he had always known that, he had cut the first notch with one and signed the lease with the other. He had only not known, until the wax was dry, that the hand had been on the wrong side of nothing the whole time, because both sides were inside the same arithmetic, and the arithmetic did not care which end of it you stood at, only that you stood in it, and he had never for one day of twenty-three years stood out of it.

"Your island, señor," the lawyer's man said, pleasant, blotting.

"Yes," Mei said. He put the lease inside his coat, against the tin box, the new paper against the old one, and went out into the harbour light, and coughed, once, into his hand, and looked at what came up, and it was clear, this time, which it was not always, and he wiped the hand on his coat and went down to find a boat.

*

He was kinder than Quick had been. He knew it for a fact, the way he knew the height of a working face or the weight of a barrow, because he had been under Quick and now he stood where Quick had stood and he could set the two of them side by side on the one beam and weigh them, and the weighing came out in his favour, and it bought him nothing, and that was the whole of what the island taught him that the rock had not.

He fed them better. He had the rice weighed full on the same beam that weighed the cargo — full, an honest catty, not the short measure inside the contract that Quick had run and called fair — because he had lain on the boards once with the short measure in his belly and he knew to the grain what the difference was, and it cost him a little and he paid it. He did not flog. He had seen flogging make a slow man slower and a sick man dead and he had no use for a tool that lowered the day's weight, and so where Quick had reached for the lash Mei reached for the count, moved a failing man to a lighter face, docked where he had to dock and no more, kept the worst air off the new ones a week so they would last past the week, and a man under him lived, on the average, some unmeasured fraction longer than a man under Quick had lived. He had done the figure. The fraction was real. He had bought it with the rice and the no-flogging and the week's grace, and he could not have said what he had bought it with the money for, except that he could not run the workings the way he had been run and look at the stick at night.

It changed nothing. That was the thing the fraction did not buy out of. The air still came off the broken face the same caustic breath it had come off the Chincha face the morning it took his eye, because it was the same substance, a thousand years of the white rotting, the very strength they were starving the fields of England for, and a man who breathed it kindly for a master who fed him full died of it as surely as a man who breathed it under the lash, only slower, only by the fraction, and the fraction did not reach the lungs. Mei knew this. He had known it on the wharf at Callao before he ever signed; he had known it walking the forty-foot face; he knew it now, standing in the lee of his own scale-house in the late forenoon with his own gang strung along his own cut, leaning into their barrows in their rags with the cloths bound over their faces against the dust, and the chutes running, and a lighter taking his cargo out to the *Wanderer* riding light in the swell with her yards crossed sharp, and the price of it that very week thirteen pound the ton at the chute and fourteen landed in London and climbing, the fortune at its very loudest over a rock cut nearly to the bone, and his men breathing the strength of it down into themselves a barrow at a time and aging by the day in the air that made him richer. He stood and watched them earn him money with their breath and he kept, as he had kept everything for twenty-three years, the count.

The gang was Chinese, most of them, the last of the trade. The traffic was ending — a ship had been stopped at a Japan port the year before and a great noise made over it in the foreign papers, and there was talk the whole thing would be shut inside a year or two, and the price of a man at the Callao barracks had gone up with the scarcity the way the price of the white went up with the stump, five hundred soles the head now and rising, near double his own price of three hundred pesos in '49 — and Mei had paid it, had stood in the barracks shed at Callao and bought men, walked the line the way the man with the book had walked the line on the deck thirty years before the man with the book in Cornwall and twenty-three before Mei's own line on the beach, and looked at the teeth and the eyes and the set of the shoulders, and named a figure to the broker, and the broker wrote it down. He had not been able to do otherwise and have a gang. There was no clean way to staff a rock that killed the men who worked it; the system did not offer one; you bought men or you had no cargo, and he had bought men, and he had walked the line with his head turned to bring each face to his good eye, and some of the faces had been from villages a half-day from villages he knew, and one of them, a thin boy of seventeen with a burn-scar up one forearm from a kiln or a forge, had made him stop in the line a moment, the way A-Yün had stopped him once across the years, and he had bought the boy, and the boy was on the face now, and Mei knew his name.

He knew all their names. That was the difference Quick had never had and would not have wanted, that Mei knew every name on the rock, because he kept two books now as he had always kept two — the company's, in ink, where the men were a number and a weight and a ration and a date of purchase, the purchase that finished paying when the man went under the white; and his own, in wood, against his ribs, where the men were names, and the second book was the only place on the island a man's name was kept, and Mei was the master who killed them and the clerk who remembered them, both, the same as he had been on the day he was promoted off the face into the keeping of the books of the dead, except that now the men dying to make the figure at the top of the company's book grow were dying to make *his* figure grow, and the names he cut into the wood at night were names of men he himself had bought off a line and set in the air, and the stick that had begun as a thing he was carrying home to lay on his mother's stone table — *I was somewhere real, these men were real, they ended* — had become the death-roll of his own enterprise, kept by the proprietor, in his own hand, and there was no one alive he could explain that to, and he did not try.

*

The boy with the burn-scar went down on the rock in the afternoon, and he did not die, and that was worse than if he had.

Mei was at the face when it happened, which he need not have been — an owner could stand in the scale-house and let the work come to him on the beam — but he stood the face most days, in the dust, because a man who would not stand his own face had no business owning one, and because he could read a gang off the cut the way he could not read it off the ledger, the way a man going slack a week before he broke showed it in the cut before it showed in the weight. So he was near when the boy's bar went into a bad seam and a block came away wrong and the cloud of it blew back full into the boy's face, the dry white breath of the broken white, and the boy dropped the bar and put his hands to his eyes and made a sound, a short high sound, the sound a man makes the first time the air does the thing it does, before he has learned that the sound costs breath he cannot spare and stops making it.

Mei knew the sound. He had made it himself in 1849 at the Chincha face on the third day, with A-Yün two places down, and the overseer had not come and no one had come, you worked through it or you went short, and he had worked through it half-blind and shifted his stance a half-step so the next block would gust to his right side and saved his right eye perhaps a month, and learned, that day, without knowing he was learning it, which eye he would keep. Every man at the face chose, once, which eye to keep. He had chosen his right. The choosing was a thing you did on a day you could never afterward name.

He went to the boy. He was the owner and he went to the boy himself, and he took the boy's wrists, gently, away from his eyes, because a man clawing at the scald only ground it deeper, and he tipped the boy's face up to the light the way the man with the book had tipped his face up to look at his teeth, except that Mei was looking for the thing the man with the book had never once looked for, which was whether the eye could be saved, and he saw that the left one had taken the worst of it and was already going from a weeping to the thick scald and the swelling and the film, and that the boy, if he stood the face another season, would stand it crooked, favouring one side, like every old hand Mei had ever watched, like Mei.

"Don't rub it," Mei said, in the boy's own tongue, the village words, the tongue further forward in the mouth. "It makes it worse. Hold still. Let it run."

The boy held still. He was seventeen and he was frightened and he held still because the man holding his wrists spoke to him in the words his mother had used and was not the lash, and his good eye found Mei's face and read it, the way Mei had read faces all his life, for what the man would do, and Mei watched the boy read him and watched the boy understand that the man holding his wrists was the owner of the rock — that the kind voice and the village words and the careful hands belonged to the man who had bought him off a line at Callao and set him in this air for a tenth and a lease and the rest his — and Mei saw the understanding go across the boy's good eye and find no purchase and slide off, because there was no room in it, the boy was seventeen and his eye was burning out of his head and the only kindness on the rock was the hand of the man who had brought him to the rock, and what was a boy to do with that, and Mei had no answer for the boy any more than the rock had ever had one for Mei.

He had the boy taken off the face for the week. He moved him to the lighter air at the chute-head where the cargo only passed and was not broken, and he did not dock him, and it would save the boy's right eye perhaps a month, the way the half-step had saved Mei's, and the month was real and it was all there was. The boy would stand the face again when the week was out because the cargo had to be cut and there were not men enough to cut it without him, the trade ending, the men dear, and Mei would put him back in the air that was taking his sight, kindly, full-fed, un-flogged, and the boy would age by the day and at the end of the season or the next be a crooked old man of nineteen seeing the world out of one eye, and Mei would cut his name into the wood when the rock had finished with him, in his own hand, the proprietor and the clerk, and there was no version of the figure in his head, and he had run every version, in which the boy's eye was saved and the cargo got out and the lease paid and the fields of England fed, because the substance that fed the fields was the substance that burned the eye, they were the one substance, there had never been two, and the whole of the arithmetic he had stood inside his entire life came down, here, to a boy's wrists in his two hands and a film coming over a left eye in the afternoon light, and he let go of the boy's wrists and stood up and turned his own head to bring the cut to his own good eye, and called for the gap in the line to be closed up, and the barrows went on.

*

Carmen came out to the island on the supply boat in the dry month, because she came where she chose now and no one in either house had the standing to stop her, and she stood at the scale-house while Mei weighed the day's last cargo, and she watched him weigh it, and she said nothing, which was how he knew she had seen.

She was fifteen, near sixteen, and she had her mother's eyes and her mother's watching and a stillness in her when she was working a thing out that Mei knew was his own, that she had taken off him across the years the way a child takes a habit off the air, the turning quiet, the holding of the count behind the face — and it unsettled him to see it on her, his own banked watching looking back at him out of A-Lian's eyes, because it meant she was doing to him now what he had done to every overseer and broker and weigher he had ever stood in front of: reading him for the lie in the count. She had the comb in her hair. She always had the comb in her hair, the plain dark comb, the plum-flowers worn smooth, A-Lian's one thing from the lane, the thing his mother had pushed into A-Lian's hand on the morning the men came, and it sat in Carmen's dark hair on his own island in the dust off his own cut, the lane and the rock in one place, and Mei weighed his cargo and did not look at it.

"You feed them full," Carmen said. She had been on the island an hour. She had walked the cut and the chute-head and the shed and the scale-house with her quiet on her, and this was the first thing she said, and she said it flat, not a question, the way you read a figure off a page.

"Yes."

"And you don't whip them."

"No."

"And the man at the chute-head with the bandage on his eye — that's new this week. You moved him out of the dust."

"For the week."

"For the week." She turned the two words over the way he turned a figure he did not yet trust. She was looking out at the cut, at the gang coming down off the face in the last light, the cloths still bound over their faces, the dust on them like a second skin gone grey, and she watched them come, and Mei watched her watch them, and he saw her doing the sum, the sum no one had asked her to do, the sum she had been doing since she was a child in the Lima house with her two grandfathers' two tongues in her mouth, the appraisal in one and the count in the other, the worth and the dead, and never able to make them come out even. He had watched her fail to make them come out even since she was eight years old at her mother's deathbed. It was the truest thing about her and it was killing her by inches and he loved her for it and could not say so.

"You're better to them than the man who had you," she said.

"Yes."

"And they die anyway."

He did not answer that, because it was not a question and because the answer was on the rock all around them, in the air, in the bandaged boy, in the height of the cut, and she could read it as well as he could, better, in two tongues; and because the answer in his own tongue was a thing about the dead and what was owed them that he had never said to a living person and would not say across a scale to a girl of fifteen, even this girl, especially this girl, who had her mother's cough faint in her chest already, he had heard it on the boat, the dry catch, the small wrong sound under her breath that he knew the whole long account of and she did not yet, and that he would cut the wood for someday if the order of the deaths went the way it should not, and he stood with that in him and weighed his cargo and said nothing.

She came round the beam to him then, and she put her hand out, not to him, to the thing at his belt, and she did not touch it, she only held her hand near it, the way you hold a hand near a coal, and she looked at him, and she asked the only question she asked the whole visit.

"Show me the stick."

He had never shown it to anyone living but Quick, and to Quick only to tell him he was not on it. He had shown the company's two books to a hundred men. The wood he had shown to no one, because the wood was the book the company was built to lose and a thing the company would have laughed at or feared, a count of dead coolies kept in a man's coat, worth nothing, weighing nothing, a tally of purchases that had finished paying — and he stood now with A-Lian's daughter's hand held near it in the last light on his own killing rock, and he took it out.

There were two sticks. The first was full, every face of it cut and the cuts gone grey and smooth with the years and the oil of his hands, the top notch worn nearly round — *Cheng A-Yün, tile-maker, a village a half-day west, a wife, a son who would be a man of thirty-four now and never know* — and the second was younger, the pale wood not yet greyed, filling. He put them both in her two hands. She took them the way she took a thing she meant to weigh. She turned them in the light. She found the notches with her fingertips, the way he found them in the dark, and he watched her count them, her lips not moving, the count going behind her face, and he saw her reach the fresh ones low on the second stick, the bright new cuts, and stop.

"These are new," she said. "This year. This island."

"Yes."

She looked up from the wood at him, and her face did the thing he had watched her face do at her mother's deathbed, the thing where the two tongues in her finally said the one sentence together and it was unbearable, and she did not say it, because she was his, because he had taught her without meaning to that you held the worst count behind the face — and Mei stood and let her see it, the whole of it, the only confession he had ever made or would, that the stick that began as a thing carried home to prove that men had been real had become the proprietor's own register of the men he himself had bought and set in the air and would cut into the wood when the rock was done with them, and that there was no clean way out of it that he had ever found in twenty-three years, no figure that came out even, and that he had bought the rock anyway, at the top of the price, knowing, the way a man buys a foundered horse to win one race; and Carmen read all of it off his still face the way he had taught her to read a face, and she closed her hands over the two sticks, and she did not give them back.

"I'll keep these," she said. Not asking. The way he did not ask.

"Not yet," Mei said.

She looked at him. She understood the *not yet,* he saw her understand it — that there was an order to it, that the wood passed when the man who cut it could cut no more, that she was asking for a thing she could only be given the once and at a cost she could read in his chest as well as he could — and she opened her hands and gave them back, both sticks, into his two hands, and the giving-back was its own kind of promise, and they both knew which one, and neither of them said it, because they were the same in this, the two of them, the girl with the comb and the man with the wood, and the thing they were the same in was that they kept the worst account behind the face and rendered it to no one until the day the order of things made them.

The supply boat's man called up from the shingle that the tide was making. Carmen went down to it. Mei put the two sticks back inside his coat, against the tin box with the worn-out eight in it, the death-roll against the first ledger, and weighed the last of the day's cargo onto the lighter, and the lighter took it out to the *Wanderer,* and somewhere across the water the price stood at fourteen pound the ton and climbing, the loudest it had ever been, over the lowest the rock had ever stood, and Mei watched the white go out to the ship a barrow at a time off a rock he owned now, free and clear but for the tenth and the lease, and turned his head to bring the going-down of it to his good eye, and coughed, into his hand, and this time did not look at what came up.


## MANIFEST III — The Boom's Apex

### Chapter 20 · Inés Penhale
*Callao and Lima · 1873 · guano ≈ £14/ton · Chincha North working face ≈ 40 ft (stumps)*

She had learned to be early. A man came into a room at the hour appointed and took whatever chair was left, but a woman who meant to be more than a chair came in before the room was a room at all, while it was still only furniture and a clerk laying out the inkstands, and chose. So Inés Penhale was at the long table in the counting-room over the Callao mole a half-hour before the Frenchmen, and she had taken the chair at the head of it, her father's chair, the one with the worn arms, and she sat in it now with the morning coming grey off the harbour and a folio open in front of her that she did not need to read, because she had it by heart, and she waited for the men who were coming to tell her she could not sit there.

The chair was a calculation, like everything. If she rose when they came in, she had borrowed it, and a thing borrowed could be asked back. If she did not rise — if she let them find her in it, and went on with her pen, and named the figure before they had got their hats off — then it was a thing she sat in, and a man who wanted it would have to take it from her in front of the others, and the kind of man who came to a counting-room at eight in the morning to talk about two hundred thousand soles did not, as a rule, want to be the man who pulled a chair out from under a daughter. They would rather lose the half-percent. She had built a good deal of her life on that exact preference, on the narrow margin between what a man would do and what he would be seen to do, and it had never once failed her, and she did not entirely trust it for that reason.

She heard them on the stair. Échale, she thought — here they come — and turned a page she did not read.

*

The deal was the *Margarita Luz*, four thousand long tons consigned out of the lesser islands, Guañape and the scrapings off Macabí, and it had three masters because that was what the trade had come to. There was the house she nominally served, which had its commission and its bond. There was the Dreyfus money behind it all, the French house that had bought the whole of Peru three years ago with a loan and now held the guano of the future the way a pawnbroker holds a coat, against a debt the country could not see the bottom of. And there was the buyer, a Liverpool consignee's agent named Tregenza who had come down the coast to settle a disputed quality, four thousand tons that had been cut from a deposit so thin and so far from the old rich Chincha face that it carried more sand and shell in it than guano ought, and assayed two points under what the contract said, and Tregenza wanted twelve per centum off for it or he would refuse the cargo and let it rot in the lighters, and he had the contract on his side, and he knew it.

He did not know what she knew, which was that there was nowhere else for him to go.

That was the whole of the morning, and she had seen it the night before with the books open and the lamp low, the way her father saw a wreck — all at once, entire, the worth of the thing and the throat of it together. Twelve years ago a Liverpool man who did not like his cargo got back on his ship and sailed forty miles up the coast to a rich face and a clean grade and loaded out of the Chincha in a fortnight. There was no such face left. The Chincha north island that had stood a hundred and eighty feet of white over the sea the year she was born was forty feet of stained stump now, picked at by gangs working the dregs, and the rich easy guano of the world was gone into the holds of twenty years of ships, and what was left was this — sandy, far, dear, and the only thing there was. Tregenza could refuse the *Margarita Luz* and there was not another four thousand tons of clean grade between here and the Lobos he could have instead, not this season, not at any price, and a man who refused a cargo and then could not replace it had to go home to Liverpool and explain to a board why his ship had crossed two oceans in ballast and come back the same. He thought he was here to dock her price. He was here because he had no choice but to be here, and she was the only one at the table who knew which of those it was.

"Mr. Tregenza," she said, when they were seated and the clerk had poured, and she let her English come out as flat and northern and unornamented as her father's, because Tregenza would discount a Spanish accent the way he discounted a moist cargo, by reflex, without thinking, and she did not intend to give him the discount. "You've assayed her two points light. I don't dispute it. The Macabí cut runs sandy and I'll not stand here and tell you it doesn't."

She watched that land. A man came braced to fight for his first inch and you gave it to him before he asked, and it unbalanced him, because now he did not know what the fight was about.

"Then we're agreed on the twelve," Tregenza said, too quickly.

"We're agreed on the two points. Two points is a touch under three per centum on the nitrogen, and the price is by the nitrogen, so you'll have your three. I'll allow three on the grade and not argue it." She turned the folio toward him, her own figures, clean. "The other nine you want is for the sand, and the sand isn't in the contract, Mr. Tregenza, the contract says merchantable Peruvian guano and this is merchantable, it's only poor, and poor and unmerchantable are two different words and you and I both know which one your lawyer can hang a refusal on, and it isn't this one." She let that sit. Then, mild, the way you set a thing down so gently a man hears the weight of it more for the softness: "And if you do refuse her, you'll want to send a fast boat ahead of you to find the four thousand tons you mean to load instead, and I'd send it today, because there's a Bremen house asking me about that same cargo and they don't assay it, they weigh it, and they'll be glad of her at the contract price, sand and all, by Friday."

There was no Bremen house. There was the idea of a Bremen house, which was a different thing and served as well, and would never have to be produced, because a man does not call your bluff on a Friday cargo when the calling of it costs him the only guano on the coast and the not-calling costs him nine per centum he was never entitled to. She had done the sum on him before he sat down. If/then. If he is bluffing, he folds at the Bremen house; if he is not bluffing, he cannot afford to be, and folds anyway. There was no branch of it where she lost.

Tregenza looked at her a long moment. He was not a fool, which she had counted on; a fool would have refused out of temper and she would have had to find the Bremen house in truth. He was a careful man who had just understood that the careful thing was to pay, and that he had been brought, very politely, to a place where the careful thing and the thing he wanted were not the same, and that the person who had brought him there was twenty-two years old and a woman and had given him a fair three points so that the nine she kept would look, in the minute-book, like a thing he had conceded rather than a thing she had taken.

"Three on the grade," he said at last. "And the moisture clause as it stands."

"The moisture clause as it stands," she agreed, and did not smile, because a smile would have told him she had won and she meant for him to walk down to his boat believing he had merely been sensible.

She signed it. She signed it *Inés Penhale, for the house,* and the *for the house* was the thing, because it was not *Penhale's daughter* and it was not *p.p.* anyone and it was not a clerk's hand under a principal's name. It was her own name, signing four thousand tons, for the house, in front of two Frenchmen and a Liverpool agent and the clerk who would copy it into the letter-book, and when Tregenza had gone and the Dreyfus men had gone, pleased, because the cargo moved and the bond held and the French did not care a sou for the nine per centum so long as the wheel turned, she sat in her father's chair in the empty room with the signed paper in front of her and felt the thing she had built her whole life to feel arrive, and stay for the length of a breath, and go.

It went. That was the part no one had told her about the seat. You got it, and it was real, and it was yours, and the men on the stair would say her name now in a different tone, and none of it weighed a thing in the chest, because the chest had wanted something else the whole time and had only used the seat as the name for it.

She knew exactly what it had wanted. She was too much her father's to lie to herself about a figure. The chest had wanted him to be in the room.

*

On the wall opposite her father's chair, where he had hung it himself so that he could lift his eyes from any cargo and see it, was the first manifest.

It was framed in plain dark wood, the bill of lading of the *Petrel*, one hundred and forty tons of guano shipped from the Chincha Islands in the year forty-nine, in her father's young hand, the first cargo he had ever consigned in his own name. He had been an inspector then, a nobody clawing at the bottom of the trade, and the figure at the top of that bill — a hundred and forty tons, nine pounds the ton — was a small thing, a single ship, a beginning. He had kept it where he could see it for twenty-four years, and she had understood, as a girl, that he kept it the way other men kept a christening-gown or a wedding ring, as the relic of the day a thing began — except that her father had no christening-gown out and no ring on, and the thing that had begun on that day, the only birth he commemorated, was the figure. Not the child. The figure. He had framed the first ton of the fortune and hung it where his eyes could reach it, and he had never once, in her hearing, framed or hung or kept a single thing belonging to a person.

She had learned to read off that manifest the way another child learns the catechism off the wall of a church. It had taught her the whole of her father's love, which was that love, in him, was a column, and a thing he loved was a thing whose figure grew, and the way to be loved by him was to be the figure that grew fastest. She had set out at nine to be that. She had made herself into the cargo that appreciated — quick at the books, cold at the table, indispensable, the asset that returned the most — and it had worked, it had worked precisely as the manifest had promised it would, and her father had come, over the years, to look at her the way he looked at a sound hull and a rising price, with the nearest thing to warmth that the column allowed, and she had spent fifteen years calling that thing his love and had only lately begun to suspect that it was his appraisal, and that she had wanted the one and earned the other, and that they were not, after all, the same, no matter how perfectly she had built herself so that they would be.

He had not come down to Callao for the *Margarita Luz*. He no longer came down for much. He was five-and-fifty and his lungs had begun the dry catch that she had heard, all her life, in the men who came back off the islands, the same catch, and she did not let herself name what it was, the way he did not let himself name it; they were alike in that, in the things they refused to call by their names. He kept to the Lima house and the high view and the cigars he was forbidden, and he sent her down with the books and a free hand, and a free hand was a great gift in the trade and she knew its worth, and she had wanted, instead, just once, for him to come down and stand at the back of the room with his hat in his hand and watch her take the nine per centum off a Liverpool man, and say nothing, only see it, only be there to have seen. He would have priced it correctly. He would have seen exactly what she did and how, the Bremen house and the fair three points and the *for the house*, and he would have known it for the best piece of trading anyone in his house had done that season, better than he could do it now, and that was the whole of what she wanted — to be seen doing the thing she had become him in order to do, by him, while he could still see it. The free hand was him saying she did not need him in the room. She had spent her whole life proving she did not need him in the room precisely so that he would want to be in it. And he had believed her.

She closed the folio.

*

She rode up to Lima in the afternoon, in the carriage with the worn springs that her husband kept meaning to replace and never did, through the dust and the long files of the trade — the ox-carts and the rail-flats stacked with the bagged guano coming up from the mole, the new railway her country had built on the loaned money, the locomotive English and the rails English and the engineer English and the debt that paid for all three a thing with no nation at all, a number in a French ledger and a London bond — and she watched it go by, the gilded machinery of the boom at its very height, and did the only sum that mattered, which no one else at any table she sat at would do.

The price was fourteen pounds the ton. It had never been higher. The Dreyfus men spoke of it the way you spoke of weather that would hold forever, fourteen and climbing, the richest fertilizer on earth and a continent that could not get enough of it, and every man she dealt with read the high price as the proof of the high worth, as the floor under all the borrowing, as the reason the loans were sound and the bonds were good and the country would pay. They read fourteen pounds as strength.

She read it as a top.

She had read it as a top for two years now and told no one but her own books. The price was not high because the trade was strong. The price was high because the thing was running out. Fourteen pounds the ton was what scarcity looked like wearing the mask of prosperity; it was the white mountain at forty feet of stump fetching more by the ton than it had fetched at a hundred and eighty, because there was so little of it left and the world still wanted it all, and a thing that grew dearer as it grew scarcer was not an estate, it was a fire that threw the most light just before it ate the last of the wood. The men around her saw a fortune compounding. She saw a stock running down and a price running up to meet the running-down, and she knew — the way her father had once known a hull was holed below the waterline while she still rode high and looked sound — that the two lines were going to cross, and that when they crossed, the price would not come gently down off fourteen. It would fall through the floor, because the floor had never been there; the floor was only the borrowing, and the borrowing was secured against the very guano that was nearly gone, so that the day the last good cargo loaded was the day the bonds discovered they had been written against an empty island, and on that day there would be no price at all.

She had run it forward in the books a hundred ways. There was no way it did not end in zero. The nitrate had begun to come up out of the Atacama desert and undersell the guano for the poorer crops; the Chincha was stumps; the lesser islands were shallow and would not last a decade; the country had mortgaged thirty years of a thing that had perhaps ten years left in it, and the arithmetic of that was not difficult, it was only unbearable, and men did not do unbearable arithmetic, they did the arithmetic that let them sleep, and the arithmetic that let them sleep said fourteen pounds and climbing.

The smart play was to sell. Not the cargoes — the leases, the bonds, the standing in the trade, the whole apparatus, everything the family held that was a claim on the future of the guano, to sell it all now, at the top, to the men who still read fourteen pounds as strength, and to be out and into nitrate or land or London consols before the lines crossed. She had begun, quietly, to move that way herself. She had taken her husband's name and his Echeverría connection in part for exactly this — a Peruvian merchant house with land and a foot in the nitrate, a vessel she could move value into when the time came to move it off the guano — and she did not pretend, to herself, that the marriage had been anything else. Bertrand was a pleasant man and a useful one and they dealt well together and she had married the connection the way she did everything, with a clear eye on the figure, and if there was a coldness in that she had inherited the coldness from the man who framed manifests and not children, and she did not see why she should be ashamed of using the only kind of love she had been taught, which was the kind that improved a position.

But she could not move the family off the guano, because the family was her father, and her father would not go.

She had put it to him once, carefully, in the language he understood — not *the birds are running out,* she did not have that language, no one did, the words for that did not exist yet at any table — but *the price is a top, the deposit is finite, the smart money sells the wreck before it sinks, you taught me that, you taught me a man stands on the buying side and never goes down with a hull, sell now while they still think it floats.* And he had looked at her, her father, with the dry catch in his chest, and he had said no.

He had not said *you are wrong.* That was the thing she could not get past. He had not argued the figure. He had done the figure himself in fifty-four, on the cut face at night with a lantern, she knew that now, he had been the first man in the world to price the bottom of the white mountain and he had chosen to mine it faster knowing — and he had said no to her not because the deposit was not finite but because to sell it as a wreck would be to say aloud that it was a wreck, that it had always been going to run out, that the fortune he had spent his whole life building was a thing with a floor and a finish and a bottom like the cargo-deck he had been sold onto at eleven — and he would die before he said that. He would, she understood, sitting in the carriage with the boom going by gilded and doomed in the window, quite literally rather die than admit the mountain could end, because the mountain ending and his own life ending had become, somewhere in him, the same fact, and a man who has stood his whole life on the buying side of the scale does not climb down onto the other side just because the arithmetic asks him to. He had taught her to read a holed hull. He had not taught her what to do when the master of the ship was your own father and he had decided to go down with it on purpose, with his hand on the wheel, calling it sound to the last, because the alternative was to have been wrong.

*

The Lima house smelled, as it always faintly smelled, of the islands.

Her mother had given up, years ago, the war against the smell. It was in the curtains and the upholstery and the timber, the thin ammoniac ghost of the source, and Rosa had stopped scrubbing for it and learned, as everyone in the family had learned, to stop smelling it, which Inés knew was not the same as its going away — it only meant you had become a person who lived in it and did not know. She came into the cool of the sala and her mother was there, and the girl Carmen was there, and the two of them were at the long table with a thing spread between them, and Inés stopped in the doorway the way she always, somehow, stopped at the sight of Carmen, and watched before she was watched.

The child — she was sixteen now, not a child, but Inés was only six years older and had never managed to think of her as anything but the watching girl in the doorway of nine years ago — Carmen had the household accounts open, Rosa's housekeeping book, and she was checking it, and she was checking it correctly, Inés could see the little movement of her finger down a column and the pause where a figure did not sum, and there was a comb in her dark hair, a plain foreign comb, old, that Inés had seen her wear since she was small and had never asked about, and Carmen looked up and met her eyes across the room exactly as she had met them across the sala doorway the year the war began, child to child, two watchers, each one reading the other and giving nothing back.

They were the two who could do the sum. Inés had known it since Carmen was seven. She herself did the sum in soles and tons and bonds, the market sum, the one that ended in *sell;* and the girl, she suspected — she had watched her on the booby ledges as a child with old Asher and his bird-book, had watched her wear that foreign comb to a Lima dinner like a flag, had watched her go quiet the exact way the old Chinaman Mei went quiet, the man who was her great-uncle and not her grandfather and somehow more her grandfather than Liddell would ever be — the girl did some other sum, a sum Inés did not have the figures for, that ran in lungs and the dead and the things the manifests left out, and that did not end in *sell* and did not end in *hold* and Inés could not see where it ended at all, only that the girl was doing it, the way Inés was doing hers, in a family of people who refused to do any sum that kept them awake.

"You've a hand for it," Inés said to her, of the accounts. In Spanish, because the sala was her mother's room and her mother's tongue.

"It doesn't sum," Carmen said. "There's two soles fifty out, here." She turned the book. "Either the fish-woman shorted us or Mamá's got a draft entered twice."

"Then find which," Inés said. "Don't tell me the column's wrong. Tell me which one's lying."

The girl looked at her, and something passed — not warmth, neither of them ran to warmth — but a recognition, the kind that passes between two people who have each discovered, separately, in a house full of people determined not to know things, that they are the ones who cannot help but know. *We are going to need each other,* Inés thought, with the cold clear part of her that was always running the position, *she and I, before this is done. The day the lines cross, when Father goes down with the wheel in his hand and the bonds find the island empty, it will come down to the two who could read it. The one who would sell and the one who would — whatever it is she would do.* And she filed the girl the way she filed everything, the way her father had taught the whole trade to file a thing, stripped to its use and kept: *the only other one in the house who sees the floor.* Kept.

Her mother was watching the two of them with the look she had, the look of a woman who had borne one of them and sheltered the other and had spent her life watching the people she loved turn into ledgers in front of her. Rosa caught Inés's eye and said nothing and went back to her sewing, and in the not-saying was the whole of it, because Rosa knew, better than anyone, what the seat had cost; Rosa had watched her daughter build herself into the asset that would be loved and had known from the first hour, from the new-year fog the night Inés was born, that it would not work, that the thing being built to be loved would only ever be appraised, and had loved her anyway, helplessly, the way you love a child walking toward a thing you cannot stop. Inés had the seat now. She had won it that morning, signed *for the house,* taken the nine per centum, read the top of the market alone in a room of men who could not, and she had come home with it to a sala that smelled of the islands and a mother who pitied her and a girl of sixteen who was the only person in the family who would still be standing, clear-eyed, when the whole thing went to zero — and the seat weighed nothing, still, in the chest, where the thing it had been built to buy was not, and would not be, because the man it was meant to buy was up the stairs with a dry catch in his lungs, framing the first ton of a fortune that was going to end, and refusing to be wrong about it long enough to love her instead.

"He'll want the *Margarita Luz* figures," her mother said, of Liddell, mild, meaning *he is awake; go up.*

"He'll want them tomorrow," Inés said. "Tonight he can want them and not have them. It's a thing he should learn the taste of."

And she set the signed paper on the table beside the housekeeping book the girl could not make sum, the four thousand tons and the two soles fifty side by side, the cargo and the shortfall, the top of the market and the lie in the column, and she went to find her own room before she went up to his, because she did not, tonight, trust her face to climb the stairs and be appraised and found sound and not, even once, simply met.


## MANIFEST III — The Boom's Apex

### Chapter 21 · Asher Mei
*Macabí Island and the Chincha group, off the Peruvian coast · 1873 · guano ≈ £14/ton · Chincha North working face ≈ 40 ft (stumps)*

The right number this spring was five and a quarter. Asher knew it before he had finished the count, the way a man who has weighed cargo all his life knows the weight of a sack before the beam stops swinging, and he did the count anyway, all of it, square by square, because a number you knew before you counted it was not a number, it was a fear, and a fear had no place in the book.

He was on the high spine of Macabí with the wind coming hard off the cold water and the colony spread below him, and he laid down his square — two of his own forearms each way, the same arm he had measured the world with since he was twelve, longer now, a man's arm, but he had never re-cut the square because to re-cut it would break the whole long line of the count and make this spring no longer comparable to that one — and he counted the nests in it, and got four, and shifted, and got three, and shifted, and got four, and took the middle. And he paced the breadth of the nesting ground, which his boots knew now the way other men's feet knew a road home, and it was shorter than last year, shorter by a third of his pacing, because the guanay had drawn in off the seaward edge where the loose ones had nested and packed themselves tight into the centre the way a fire draws down to its last coals when you stop feeding it, and a fool on the cliff would have looked at that tight packed centre and said *thousands, look at them all, thousands,* and been comforted. Asher was not comforted. He counted the ground. The ground was what told the truth. When the ground a colony stood on shrank year over year while the birds packed tighter to fill the eye, that was a colony dying, and a man who counted the birds and not the ground would not know it until the morning he came out and the rock was bare.

Five and a quarter million, near enough, over all of it now. Macabí and Guañape and the Lobos and what little still nested on the Chinchas where the digging had been at them thirty years. He wrote it small at the back of the book where the frightening numbers lived, under the twelve million he had got the year he was thirteen, under the nine and the eight and the seven and the six of the years between, a column going down the way a draught-mark goes down a hull that is settling, and he capped the pencil and put the book inside his coat — a good coat now, a Lima merchant's broadcloth, ink at the right cuff that no laundress had ever got out and that he had stopped asking them to — and stood up into the wind, and his lungs caught.

A small dry catch, low down, the one he had carried since before he could remember carrying anything. He put his wrist to his mouth without deciding to. He had been born in the worst air on earth, in a shed in the barracks-lane on a rock his father dug, and a man could buy boots and broadcloth and a counting-house off the Calle de Mercaderes and English enough to read a bond and a Latin name for every bird below him, and the air he was born in stayed in his chest like a notch cut into wood. He had stopped minding it. He minded other things now. He minded the column going down.

"Five and a quarter," said Carmen, behind him, not a question. She had paced her own square thirty yards off along the spine and she came up to him with the wind taking her hair, the dark plain comb in it that did not match the good Lima dress and never had, the comb she had worn since she was eight years old that he had told himself a hundred times he would ask her about and never once had, because to ask would be to open the column he kept blank.

"Five and a quarter," Asher said.

"It was eight when I started coming out." She had been coming out four years, since she was twelve, and she counted nearly as fast as he did now and faster than he did at the boobies, which nested loose and could not be squared and had to be guessed by an eye that had the knack, and she had the knack. "Eight then. Six last spring. Five and a quarter now. The drops are getting bigger."

"Don't say it like the bleeding's steady, like a man could plan against it." He started down the spine. "It crept for three springs and ran in the fourth. That's not a slope a man walks down at his own pace, however gentle it looks from up here — and a colony doesn't come apart the way it built up. It built up grain by grain over a thousand years. It comes apart all at once, the one spring the shoal moves and they can't feed the young, and then it isn't a slope at all, it's a cliff." He heard himself and shut his mouth. He did not say all of it, as a rule, to anyone. But Carmen was the one person alive he could say a frightening number to and watch her take it down behind her eyes the way he took it down behind his, and not flinch, and not weep, and not tell him he had a morbid mind, which was what his wife said, gently, who was a good Lima woman and did not count anything and did not need to.

Carmen walked beside him not speaking. He had taught her that too, without meaning to, the year she was twelve: that you did not fill a count with talk. You let the number sit. Below them the colony roared and stank — the live stink, the wet sharp working stink of five million birds and a quarter-million more making the thing right now this morning that the men two islands over were dying to dig up from a thousand years ago, the same stink forty years apart, the one they could sell and the one they couldn't and no man but Asher seemed ever to have noticed they were the same — and out past the cliff the dawn-river of the guanay was pouring to the shoal, thinner than it had been, he could see it was thinner, a man who had watched it every dawn of his life could see the sky was less full than it had been when he was a boy, and the boobies dropped on the water like flung knives, and far down the swell a single barque lay at the loading with her hatches canvased over against the dust and her crew working the hold in the short shifts that were all a man's lungs could bear, and it was all of it, every part of it, worth fourteen pounds the long ton this season, the most it had ever been worth in the history of the world, the price climbing as the birds thinned, the two lines crossing each other on a chart only Asher seemed to keep, and he had no one to show the chart to but the girl beside him, who already knew.

"My grandfather is buying another lease," Carmen said.

"Which grandfather."

"The one with the stick."

Asher said nothing for a while. The path went down off the spine toward the workings on the lee side, where his father's gang was at the morning's cut.

"He should not," Asher said at last.

"I know."

"The price is at the top. He's buying at the top." It was the merchant in him, the part he had built, the part that read bonds and freight and knew to a shilling what a thing was worth. "Anyone who can read a column knows the price is at the top because the easy guano is gone, and when the price is at the top from scarcity, not from demand, the next move is down, and a man buys low and sells high, he does not buy high because he is afraid of being left out. My—" he stopped. He had been going to say *my aunt Inés,* and the word *aunt* sat oddly because he traced no such lines on purpose, but it was true, the cold one, the broker, was the only Penhale who saw it the way he saw it. "The broker is selling. She's getting out. She knows."

"She knows the price," Carmen said. "She doesn't know the birds. Does she know the birds?"

"No."

"Then you both know half of it." Carmen stopped on the path and looked at him with her mother's watchfulness that she still did not know she had, the watchfulness Asher had recognized in a Lima child on a booby ledge eight years ago and never forgotten. "She knows the price is at the top and getting out is clever. You know the birds are falling and there's no top to come back to. Put the two halves together and getting out isn't clever, it's just—" she did the wrist to her mouth, the small dry catch, the one that was his too, the one that had killed her mother in a Lima front room when Carmen was eight. "It's just the only true thing. There's nothing here. The whole thing's already over and the price hasn't been told yet."

Asher looked at her a long moment. She had said in three sentences the thing he had spent his life not saying, the thing under the true thing, the fish coming up under the lure.

"Don't say that to either grandfather," he said.

"I wouldn't. They wouldn't hear it. Liddell can't — he thinks the mountain can't run out, because if it can run out then he was never rich, and a man who was once cargo cannot afford to find out he was never rich. And the other one paid forty years to own the thing that owned him, and you don't tell a man the thing he finally owns is worthless." She walked on. "One taught me a price is the truth and one taught me a count is the truth, and they never once agreed, and they both raised me. That's all I am. I'm the place where their two books don't add up."

*

The workings on Macabí were not the Chinchas. Asher came down into them the way he came into them every spring, and every spring they were lower and meaner and the men in them more desperate, because Macabí was a scraping. The good deep deposit was gone off the Chinchas, cut down from a hundred and eighty feet in the year of Asher's birth to forty feet of stumps now, the painted high marks of old loadings stranded up the bare rock thirty feet above this year's cut like tide-lines on a wall after the sea has gone for good; and so the trade had come up the coast to the lesser islands, to Macabí and Guañape and the Lobos, where the deposit had never been deep, where you scraped fifteen feet and twenty feet and hit the rock, and the air off a shallow cut was no kinder than off a deep one, it was the same buried breath of a thousand years pressed to stone, and the men who dug it now were the dregs of a dying labour-supply, the last few gangs the brokers could still land before the Chinese trade closed for good this very year under the scandal of it.

His father's gang was forty men. Asher knew the number because he kept it, privately, the way he kept the birds — he had never told his father he kept it — and forty was down from the fifty his father had landed two years ago, and the ten were not gone home. He came down the cut to where the gang was at the face and he saw his father before his father saw him, which was always how it was, because his father saw out of one eye and turned his whole head to use it and so was always a half-beat late seeing anything on his blind side, and Asher came down on the blind side on purpose, he had done it since he was a boy, it let him watch the man a moment first.

Mei Loong-fa stood at the foot of the cut with a length of pale wood in his left hand and a clerk's slate in his right, and he was counting his gang onto the morning's work. He did not dig now. He owned the lease; he stood where the heavy Englishman had stood; he counted the men on and he would count them off, and the pale wood in his left hand was not the company's slate, it was the other count, the one he kept against his ribs and showed no man, the one the whole island had always whispered about and no one but Carmen had ever named to Asher's face — *he keeps a count on a stick, he won't show me what it counts* — and Asher had known whose it was since he was thirteen, and had never once asked, because there was a wall between him and the man at the face that they had built together over twenty-one years out of one silence laid on another, and the stick was on the far side of the wall.

His father was older. That was the thing Asher saw, on the blind side, every spring, with the small cold shock of it: that the silent half-blind man at the face was an old man now, the rope of him gone thin, the cough on him — Asher could see the cough on him at thirty paces, the way he held his ribs still and breathed shallow over the slate, the same trick Asher used over the bird-book, the same trick Carmen used at a dinner-table, the one breath held careful so the catch would not come, the family's one inheritance handed down in the body like a comb — and that the old man was standing in a dying scraping at the very top of the market counting forty men onto rock that would be bare in a year, with a stick in his hand that held the dead of two empires' worth of digging, buying another lease.

"You came down the blind side," Mei said, without turning. He had heard the boots. He always heard the boots.

"I came to count the colony. It's five and a quarter million." Asher said it the way he said everything to his father, flat, a fact, the only register the wall let through. "Down from six last spring. Three-quarters of a million off it in the one year."

"I know what it is." Mei turned his head and looked at his son out of the one eye. "I have watched it longer than you. I watched it go dark over the shoal the morning I landed and I have watched it thin for twenty-four years. You are not the first man to count the birds."

"No," Asher said. "There was a German, last year, on the Chinchas. He counted them. He wrote a paper and sent it to the trade and the trade put it in a drawer. I read it. He counted six and a quarter for the whole group and I had six and a half off my own squares the same spring, which means he counted well, for a man who'd been here a season." He had not meant to say it. He never meant to say the things the wall let through, they came out of him hard and dry like the others. "We got nearly the same number, the German and I, separately. Two men who never met, counting the same dying thing the same careful way, and getting the same answer, and being put in the same drawer. There must be something in counting it right that the men who buy it cannot stand to hear."

Mei looked at him for a moment. Something moved in the old face that Asher could not read, because he had spent his life not learning to read it.

"You count it right," Mei said. It was not praise. It was a weight set on a scale. "You always counted it right. You counted it right when you were a boy on the seaward shoulder where I could not go because the chute could not reach it, and you would come back at dusk with your numbers and not show me, and I would see you had been counting, and I would think—" The cough took him then, low and grinding, and he held his ribs and rode it, and got it down, and did not finish what he would think. He looked at the stick in his own hand instead, and then at his son, and the wall stood between them as it always had, built course by course out of every spring Asher had come down the blind side and every dusk the boy had come back with his numbers and not shown them, and neither of them put a hand to it, because neither of them knew, after twenty-one years, where the door in it had ever been.

"You should not buy the lease," Asher said.

"You came all the way out to tell me that."

"I came to count the birds. I'm telling you the lease while I'm here. The price is at the top. The colony's bleeding off a fifth of itself a year and getting faster. There's no bottom to the birds and the price is going to follow them down. The broker is selling everything she can. Anyone who reads a column—"

"You read a column," Mei said. "I read a man." He set the slate down on a barrow. "Your column tells you the thing is over, and you are right, it is over — it was over the spring the first wheel went over the first nest, and that was before you were born. But a column does not tell you what to do with a thing that is already over. I buy the lease because the man who sells it to me sells out of fear, and I have spent my whole life on the buying side of a fear, because the other side is where a man with a book walks down a line and puts his thumb under your chin. You do not stand on that side. I learned that the first week of my life on this rock, off an Englishman who is up the coast dying of it now, and I will not start now because a column says the price is high." He picked the slate back up. "When you have spent forty years being the thing that was bought, you do not refuse to buy. Especially not a worthless thing — because a worthless thing no one can take from you. There is nothing left in it now for anyone to come and weigh."

Asher had no answer to that. It was not a column. It was the man, and he could not read the man, and the man had just told him so.

"Carmen is up on the spine," he said instead.

"I know. I saw her dress on the rock when she paced her square." The old eye almost warmed. "She counts the ground, not the birds. You taught her that."

"She worked half of it out herself."

"She worked all of it out herself," Mei said. "You only gave her the square." He turned back to his gang, the forty men waiting at the cut with their cloths bound over their faces and their bars in their hands, the dregs of the last gangs the world would land here, and he raised the slate, and began, in the fourth language Asher had ever heard him speak, to count them onto the morning's work, his lips moving on the numbers, the pale stick that was the other count held loose against his side, and Asher stood on the blind side a moment longer, watching the old man count the living onto the rock that would soon hold their names, and then he went back up the cut to find Carmen, because there was nothing in the column or in the man that he could carry away, and the only thing on the whole island either of them could bear to count was still the birds.

*

It was Carmen who brought him word, two days later, on the Chincha mole where Asher had gone to check a cargo of his own — a small consignment he was floating on his own account, a merchant of his own making, the boy who had sworn no country would weep for him grown into a man who weighed bonds — and she came down the mole through the loading-dust with her hand to her mouth against it and the comb crooked from the wind off the water, and she said, "The Englishman's dead. The old overseer. Quick. He died on Chincha North in the night."

Asher knew the name the way everyone in the trade knew it. Quick. The heavy man. The man his father had named, once, the only time, without naming him — *an Englishman who is up the coast dying of it now.*

"How," Asher said. Not because it mattered. Because it was what you asked, to put a small thing in front of the size of a thing.

"The way they all go. Drink and the islands." Carmen's voice had the flatness he had taught her, the Remarks-column flatness, but he saw it cost her something, because she counted faces and the face she was reading now was her own grandfather's, the one with the stick. "He'd been failing two years. He had nothing left. The company let him keep a room on the Chinchas because there was nowhere else to put a man who'd run the scale-house thirty years, and he sat in it and drank and weighed nothing, and in the night his heart or his liver or the whole worn engine of him just—" she stopped. "He'd outlived his use. The company had no column for him any more. He was the man who'd weighed every soul that came onto these islands for thirty years and at the end there was no one to weigh him. They found him this morning."

Asher stood on the mole with the dust blowing and the white stumps of the Chinchas behind her — forty feet of bare worked rock where the mountain had stood a hundred and eighty in the year he was born, the high marks of the old loadings stranded thirty feet up the stone like the ghost of a tide that would never come back in — and he thought of the heavy man he had seen perhaps a dozen times, the overseer who had pulled his half-blind silent father off the digging-face twenty-four years ago and made him a tally-man, the small mercy that was also the sentence, the first turn of the whole wheel that had ended with his father owning a lease and standing where the heavy man had stood. And he understood, the way he understood the slope of the column, that the wheel had just turned its last quarter; that the machine had finished with the man who ran it and would not even slow; and that his father — the boy who had been bought, the digger promoted into the keeping of the books of the dead, the broker, the owner — was now, this morning, the only man left alive who had stood at that face when the mountain was whole, and that the seat the heavy Englishman had sat in thirty years was, as of last night, finally his father's: the scale, the count, the punishments, and the room you died in when your use was gone.

"Who'll run Chincha North now," Asher said.

Carmen looked at him.

"Your grandfather," she said. "He bought the lease that week. The one he shouldn't have. He signed for the dead man's island and the dead man was gone before the ink had cured." She did the wrist to her mouth, the catch, the inheritance. "He didn't know Quick was dying when he signed. Or he did. I don't know which would be worse." She turned and looked back up the dust at the bare stumps of the white mountain, forty feet of nothing where there had been a sky-blocking heap of it once, worth fourteen pounds the ton this season and going down, the birds going down ahead of it, five and a quarter million now and falling, and said nothing more, because she had given him the count and the man was on it and there was no figure left over to add.

Above them the dusk-river of the birds was coming home over the worked-down stumps, thinner than last year, thinner than the year before, five and a quarter million coming in over a mountain that two empires had once gone to war to own, and Asher took out the book without quite deciding to, and turned to the back where the frightening column went down the page, and wrote under the date, in his small upright hand, the truest thing he had: *5¼. Quick dead. The seat is my father's now. There is nothing in it.*


## MANIFEST IV — The Borrowing

### Chapter 22 · Carmen Penhale-Mei
*Lima and the Callao mole · 1874 · guano ≈ £12/ton and softening · Chincha North working face ≈ 15 ft (scraping)*

The sum did not balance, and this time Carmen had the books to prove it.

She had been doing the sum since she was twelve, in the back of copybooks, in the margins of other people's columns, in her head at dinners while the men said *fourteen pounds* the way they said grace, and for five years it had been a child's sum — two columns and no unit between them, the soles in one and the nameless cost in the other, and no way to carry from the first to the second because she had never been let near the page where the carrying was done. Now she was seventeen and Inés, who had no use for a household ledger she could check herself in half the time, had handed her the Lima house accounts to keep — *here, you have the eye for it, and I have the harbour* — and Carmen had discovered the thing about the real books that the child's sum could never have told her. The real books balanced. That was the horror of them. Every page added straight down to the farthing, the dresses and the masses and the carriage-springs and the wages of the cook, all of it squared and ruled and closed, and not one line in any of them carried the second column at all. The cost she had been trying to add since she was twelve was not a number that wouldn't add. It was a number that had been left off the page. The books were honest the way a scale is honest that weighs only the cargo and never the man, and she sat over them now with the lamp drawn close and understood, with the cold settling feeling of a thing arriving that has been on its way a long time, that she had spent five years trying to make an error of arithmetic out of what was a decision about what to count.

She turned the page. Coughed, low, into her wrist — the small dry one, the family's one — and put it down before it could be more than the one. There was no one in the room to hear it. Her brother was asleep across the patio in the other wing, Sam who was twelve now and slept like a thrown stone and did not cough, had never coughed, had been three when their mother died and four when the war took their father and so had been kept in Lima houses his whole life and breathed only Lima air, while Carmen had been taken out once, at six, in a sea-green dress, to see what built her dresses, and had breathed for one afternoon the air that built them, and now had the cough and Sam did not. She had worked that out too, on a deck, at thirteen, watching him not cough. It was the cleanest entry she had ever made and it was in no book either. The two of them were a controlled experiment in the one thing the family refused to weigh, and she was the one with the weight in her chest.

She set the house accounts aside and took down the other book.

She was not supposed to have it. Inés kept the real ledger of the house of Penhale at the counting-room over the mole, in the iron press, and Carmen had it tonight only because she had asked to check a figure that did not need checking and Inés, distracted, going at the Dreyfus papers with both hands, had let her carry it home in a leather case under her arm the way you let a clever child carry a thing too heavy for it so it will learn the weight. Carmen had learned the weight. She put the great book down on the table beside the little honest household one, and opened it to the front, where the first manifest was, the *Petrel*, one hundred and forty tons, the year forty-nine, nine pounds the ton, in her grandfather's own hard young hand — the first thing he ever sold off the white, the birth he had commemorated when he commemorated no birth of a child — and then she turned, slowly, to the back, to this season, where the same hand, older, shakier, the down-strokes gone thin, had stopped writing and Inés's writing had taken over, and she read the last full year of the house of Penhale, and did the sum, the real one, with the unit at last in her hand.

The unit was *owed*.

She had it from the other grandfather. From the one with the stick, who had taught her, without either of them ever saying it was a lesson, that there was a count the company's books were built to lose, and that the losing was the point of them. Liddell had taught her that a price was the truth. Mei had taught her that a count was the truth. They had never once agreed and they had both raised her and she was the place where their two books did not add up, and tonight, with both books open on one table under one lamp, she finally did what neither of them could do, which was read them together. Liddell's said the house was worth a great deal. Mei's stick, which was not on the table but which she could find in the dark of her own memory, notch by notch, past a hundred of them, said what the great deal was made of. And between the two books ran the thing she now had the figures to see and had never had them before: that the house was not worth a great deal. The house was *owed* a great deal, and owed it against a thing that was running out, and the two were not the same, and her grandfather Liddell did not know the difference, or knew it and had decided, the way the household books had decided, simply not to enter it.

She found the loans first. They were not hidden — they were entered plainly, because Inés was too good to hide a thing in her own books, she only declined to total certain columns where the total would be read by men over her shoulder. Advances against the next shipment. Advances against the shipment after. A bond floated in London two years gone, secured on the Macabí lease, against guano not yet dug, by men in a city that had never seen a bird; and the railway paper, the great Dreyfus paper the whole city was drunk on, in which the government had sold forward two million tons of a thing the islands no longer held two million tons of, and pledged the difference to a French house, and spent the loan on rails laid up into mountains that grew nothing — and her grandfather's house was wound all through it, lender and borrower at once, owing London and owed by Lima, a knot of paper with a falling number at the centre of it that every strand had been tied off against. She ran her thumb down the column the way she had run it down the stick the first time, three years ago, when she had found her mother's notch low and set apart with the room left above it, and she felt the same thing now that she had felt then, which was the count touching the thing it counted. Each line was a season of the white going out. And the white was nearly gone.

She knew how nearly.
