That was the second book she did not need on the table, because it was in her own lungs and on a deck in her own memory: the Chincha north island cut to a stump, fifteen feet of working face where there had been a hundred and eighty the year her grandfather wrote the first manifest, fifteen feet and scraping, and the lesser islands shallower than that, and her great-uncle gone and bought two leases on the bottom of it because a man who had spent forty years being the thing that was bought could not stop himself buying even a foundered thing. She had the depth from Asher, who counted the ground because the ground told the truth. She had the price from Inés, who read the apex as a top. And she had, tonight, from the page under the lamp, the thing neither of them carried whole — the leverage; the paper; the fact that the house had borrowed against the white at the very season the white ran out, so that when the white was gone the house would not merely be poorer, it would *owe*, and owe more than it had ever been worth, against a mountain that no longer existed, to men who would want it in coin.

She closed the great book. She sat for a while with her hands flat on the cover.

Then she took a clean sheet, and ruled it, two columns, the way she had done it at twelve, except that now she had the figures for both. In the left she put what the house was owed — the bonds, the advances, the value the books gave the leases. In the right she put what the leases would fetch the day the price reached the bottom that Inés said was coming and Asher said was already here, which she made, conservatively, generously, kindly, nothing. She put nothing in the right column. And she carried the one into the other, the way she had never been able to carry it as a child, and the answer was not a number that would not add. It was a number with a minus in front of it, large, and clean, and true, and she sat looking at it, the first honest sum she had ever finished, and felt no triumph at all, only the dry catch starting again low in her chest, which she swallowed, because there was no one to hear it and because it was, she understood now, the only line of the account she would never be able to falsify.

*

In the morning she went out to the mole to give the ledger back, and met the nitrate.

She had heard the word for two years and not minded it. *Salitre.* Saltpeter. A thing from the south, from the Atacama, from the bone-dry nitrate fields below Tarapacá, that did some of what the white did, in the ground, for less. The men at the dinners said it the way men say the name of a rival's daughter — with contempt, to keep the fear down — and Carmen had filed it and not weighed it, because at twelve and thirteen it had been a column she could not yet read. Now she walked out onto the Callao mole with the leather case under her arm and the dust of the loading blowing fine and white off the lighters as it had blown her whole life, and she saw, at the far berth where the guano ships had always lain, a ship loading the other thing.

It was nothing to look at. That was what stopped her on the boards. A barque like any other, German colours, taking on sacks — sacks, sewn and stamped and stacked in a hold a man could stand in without a cloth over his face, because the thing in them did not burn the lungs, did not need short shifts, did not need the crew up in the tops to breathe. Carmen stood and watched a stevedore carry a stamped sack up the plank on his shoulder, whistling, his face bare, and she understood the whole of it in the time it took him to reach the hatch. The white had to be torn out of a cliff by men who died of the air of it, ferried down a chute, lightered out, loaded by gangs in twenty-minute shifts under canvas because the dust was poison; and the thing in the sacks was dug dry out of a desert where no bird had ever flown and bagged and carried up a plank by a whistling man, and it did, in the ground, most of what the white did, for less, and there was a great deal of it, and it was not running out. She had spent her whole life inside a sum about how much the white cost. She had never once asked what it would be worth on the day a thing came along that did the same work and cost nothing like as much, in lungs or in shillings, because until this morning on the mole she had not let herself believe that anything could.

"You're in the way of the carts, señorita," a Genoese voice said behind her, not unkindly, and she stepped aside, and the carts went by with the white, the same white, fifteen pounds the ton last she had heard and twelve this morning by the figure chalked on the harbour-master's board, twelve and a fraction and the fraction with a stroke down through it where someone had marked it lower since, the price coming down for the first time in her life, the one line that had only ever gone up in twenty-five years bending at last toward the thing the men at the dinners would not name, and she stood on the mole between the two ships, the poison cargo and the clean one, the falling price and the steady one, and did, standing up, in her head, without paper, the last carry of the sum she had finished in the night. The white was worth what it was worth only as long as it was the only thing that did the work. It had just stopped being the only thing. Therefore the leases the house had borrowed against were worth less than the books said this morning and would be worth less again tomorrow and would be worth nothing on the day the German barques were all loading sacks and none of them lay off the Chinchas at all. And the house had borrowed against them at the top. The whole apparatus, the forty years of it, was a wager that the white would stay the only thing, made by men who had watched the mountain shrink their whole lives and never once let themselves believe the mountain was the thing they were spending.

She carried the ledger up to the counting-room over the mole and gave it back to Inés.

*

Inés took it without looking up. She was a principal in fact now, twenty-three and the only one in the family who saw the floor as clearly as Carmen saw it, and they were the two reckoners, the market and the moral, and they had never needed many words.

"Did your figure check," Inés said.

"There was no figure. I wanted to read it."

"I know." Inés put the book in the press and turned the key. She looked tired in a way she did not let men see, the glitter banked. "What did you make of it."

Carmen looked at her aunt who was only six years older, who had built her whole self to be valued by a man who could value and not love, and had got the seat she built herself for and found it weighed nothing in the chest, and who was right about the money and could do nothing with being right because the man who held the seat above her would not let her sell.

"I made it owing more than it's worth," Carmen said. "Against a thing that's nearly out. To men who'll want coin."

"Yes." Inés's mouth did something that was not quite a smile. "I've made it that for a year. I put the case to him. Sell the leases now, sell the lot, take the paper to a buyer who hasn't done the sum, and walk out with coin before the price tells everyone what you and I already know." She turned from the press. "He said no. He won't sell a thing as a wreck. To sell it as a wreck is to say it was always going to be a wreck, and a man who'd sooner — " she stopped, and chose the cooler word, because she was Inés " — a man who has built his whole life on the buying side of a scale cannot stand at the end of it and let the world weigh him as a man who bought wrong. So we hold. We hold a falling thing because the founder cannot survive admitting it falls. And in a year, or two, the men in London who lent us the coin will do the sum you did last night, and they will do it faster than you because it is their coin, and they will want it, and there will be nothing to give them but a mountain that isn't there." She picked up her gloves. "You did it in a night. It took me three years to make myself believe it, because I didn't want it to be true any more than he does. The difference between us is I want out and he wants to die rich, and the difference doesn't matter, because the man with the seat wants to die rich, and so we all will not get out."

"Then why tell me," Carmen said.

"Because somebody who isn't me will hold this house at the end of it," Inés said, "and it had better be somebody who's already done the sum honestly once, so they don't waste the only year they'll have learning to. You've done it now. Good. Don't unlearn it." She drew the gloves on. "And don't say it to either of the old men. They won't hear it, and you'll only have wasted breath you can't spare." She glanced, once, at Carmen's chest, the way she glanced at a figure, and away, and that was the only acknowledgement either of them ever made of it.

*

She went to the old men anyway. Not to say it. To look at them, the way you go and look at a hull you already know is taking water, to see how much of the season it has left.

Liddell was in the Lima house, in the room where he sat now that he came less and less to the mole, the first manifest framed on the wall where he could see it from his chair, the *Petrel*, the birth he commemorated. He was fifty-six and looked older, the big hands gone loose on the chair-arms, and when Carmen came in he turned his whole head to her the way her great-uncle turned his, except that Liddell turned his because the man he was had begun to leave the body and not because of any blinded eye, and he said, "There's my best cargo," which was the highest word he had, and meant it, and could not have said a warmer one to save his life. She sat with him a while. He talked, as he talked now, of the trade as it had been, the queue of ships fifty deep, the white going up two hundred feet into a hard sky — he said two hundred; it had been a hundred and eighty, she had it from three men who had measured it, but he said two hundred and she let him — and once, only once, the talk failed him and he sat looking at the framed manifest on the wall and she saw the thing cross him that crossed him more often now, the dry catch in his own chest, not the family cough, not yet, but the frame failing, and a line came up out of him under his breath, a fragment of something sung, *we are but strangers here*, and he caught it and crushed it the way she had watched him crush it her whole life, and his face shut, and he said, "Don't read in the bad light, you're no use to me with weak eyes," which was the only way he knew to say he was glad she was there.

She did not say the sum. There was no column in him to enter it in. He had spent forty years being the man who held the pen and could not, now, become a thing that was written down, and the mountain running out was a thing that would write him down, and so the mountain could not be running out, and she understood that the denial was not stupidity and not even greed exactly but the last load-bearing wall of a man who had built himself out of one terror at the age of eleven and could not pull the wall without the whole house of him coming down. She kissed him, which he tolerated as a tariff, and went out.

The other grandfather she found in his two rooms behind the cathedral, where he had come up to be near the surgeons who could do nothing, because the islands had begun in earnest now to finish what they had started on him a quarter-century before. The cough had him badly. He sat very still when she came in and breathed shallow over it, the trick he had taught her without teaching her, the one she used at dinners, the held breath that keeps the catch down, and she sat down across from him and did not fill the room with talk, because he had taught her that too — that you did not fill a count with talk; you let the number sit.

The pale stick was on the table between them. The second one, the one that was his own gang's now, the dead he had bought off the Callao line and set in the air himself, filling up beside the first one that was full. He saw her looking at it and did not move it.

"You read the great book," he said. He knew. He always knew. "Inés let you carry it home."

"I read it."

"And?"

She thought about what Inés had said, *don't say it to either of the old men.* And she looked at the man across the table, the half-blind survivor who had bought a foundered island at the top of the price because the only freedom the rock had ever taught him was to own the thing instead of be it, and who was coughing his way out of the world a stick of the dead at a time, and who had never once been able to say his grief aloud and had handed it to her instead in notches, and she found she could not lie to him by silence the way she could to Liddell, because Liddell did not want the truth and this one had spent his whole life wanting nothing else and being given nothing but the lie.

"It's owing more than it's worth," she said, flat, a fact, the only register the thing would bear. "Against the white. And there's a thing in sacks now, from the south, that does what the white does for less and doesn't run out. The price turned this morning. Twelve, and a stroke through it." She let it sit. "It's over. The whole thing. It was over before, you all knew it was finite, but it's over now in a way the price is going to make everyone know. And you bought two leases on the bottom of it."

He was quiet a long time. The cough moved in him and he rode it down. The one clouded eye and the one good one both rested on her, and she had spent her life unable to read what moved in that old face because she had learned to watch it before she had learned what watching was for.

"I know what I bought," he said at last. "I bought a thing no man with a book will ever want again. There is a freedom in that you will not understand until you are older, and I hope you never are old enough to understand it." He put his hand on the pale stick, not lifting it, only resting it there. "You did the sum. Good. Your aunt does the sum and wants to sell. Your brother will do the sum and want to run. They are not wrong. There will be no coin in it and a wise person gets out." He looked at her. "But you are not asking me whether to sell. You came to look at me. You want to know how long I have."

She did not answer, which was an answer.

"Long enough to give you a thing," he said. "Not yet. There is an order to it. The wood passes when the man who cut it can cut no more." His hand stayed on the stick. "When it comes to you, you will want to do something with it. Make it right. Even it up. You are the kind that thinks a thing can be made to balance if you only find the right unit. I will tell you now, so you have it before you have the stick: there is no unit. I looked for one for forty years. I counted the dead so there would be one true book in a country whose every scale was built to forget them, and the count is true, and it does not balance against anything, because the thing they paid was not a thing that can be carried into the other column. You cannot pay it back. You cannot even weigh it. You can only refuse to lie that it was paid." The cough took him then, long and grinding, and he held his ribs and waited it out, and when it let him go his voice was thinner. "That is the only honest entry left. Not to make it right. To stop saying it was."

Carmen sat with that. Outside, beyond the cathedral, the city went on being drunk on the railway paper, the carriages and the gilt and the two million tons sold forward against a mountain fifteen feet high, and somewhere down at the mole a German barque was loading sacks a whistling man could carry, and the price was coming down for the first time in twenty-five years, and her grandfather Liddell was sitting in front of a framed manifest refusing to be weighed, and this grandfather was dying of the air of the thing they had all got rich on, with the count of the dead under his hand and no unit to carry it by, and she had finished, last night, the first honest sum of her life, and it had a minus in front of it, and the man across the table had just told her that the minus was the truest figure she would ever write and that her whole faith — that the sum could be done and finished and made to come out — was the thing she would have to give up before the stick would do her any good.

She did not weep, because he had not, and because she had learned from him that you did not, and because the cough was starting again low in her own chest and she needed the breath. She reached across and put her hand on the stick, beside his, not taking it — *not yet* — and held it there a moment, the giving-back that was its own kind of promise, the two of them with their hands on the count of the dead in the bad light of the two rooms behind the cathedral, and neither of them said anything, because there was nothing left to count out loud, and because the number was already sitting between them, owed, and falling, and true.


## MANIFEST IV — The Borrowing

### Chapter 23 · Asher Mei
*The open Pacific and the lesser islands, off the Peruvian coast · 1875 · guano ≈ £11–12/ton (nitrate undercutting) · Chincha North working face ≈ 15 ft (scraping)*

The water was a degree and a half too warm, and Asher had known it for nine days before his thermometer would say so, the way a man who has weighed cargo all his life knows a sack is short before the beam stops swinging — but a thing a man knew before the instrument said it was not a fact, it was a fear, and a fear had no place in the book.

So he had brought the instrument. He sat in the stern of a hired Chincha boat with the early light coming flat and grey off the swell and lowered the brass-cased thermometer he had carried out from Lima on a marked line, a fathom and then two and then five, holding it down by his watch the full four minutes it took the column of mercury to forget the air and remember the sea, and then drew it up hand over hand through water that should have been cold and was not, and read it, and read it again with his thumb under the figure to be sure the swell had not thrown the glass, and wrote it in the back of the book where the frightening numbers lived. Sixteen and a half degrees on the centigrade scale, here, off the seaward foot of the north island, in the first week of the southern winter, where the cold green Humboldt water came up off the deep all year and held the coast at thirteen and fourteen and fed the shoal that fed everything. Sixteen and a half. Two degrees, near enough, above the figure he had taken in this same place with this same instrument the last three winters running, and a degree and a half above the worst of those, and there was no error in it, because he had checked the glass against a Lima apothecary's standard before he sailed and the line was true to the fathom and the swell was light and he had taken it three mornings now and got the same wrong number each time.

He capped the pencil. The two boatmen rested on their oars and watched him the way the boatmen always watched him, with the patience of men paid by the day for a passenger they had long ago decided was a harmless lunatic — *el que cuenta los pájaros*, the one who counts the birds, the son of the half-blind China-man who owned a lease up the coast, a Lima merchant in good broadcloth who paid them silver to row him slow under the cliffs at dawn and lower a thing on a string into the water and write in a book. They did not know that the figure he had just written was the end of everything they had ever made their living off, and Asher did not tell them, because a man did not tell two boatmen a thing he had not yet made himself say plainly even in the book.

He looked up at the cliff instead. Fifteen feet of the white stood above the cut now on the great north shoulder, where a hundred and eighty had stood the year he was born on this rock and forty when he had counted the dead square with the German watching him three years gone; fifteen feet, a scraping, the gangs working the last of it with bars where once they had swung picks into a face that overtopped the ships. The painted high-marks of the old loadings stranded up the bare stone a hundred and sixty feet above the men, faint horizontal lines like the rings of a felled tree, like a draught-scale on a hull, showing what the island had drawn before it was unloaded down to the rock of itself. He had stopped reading the marks for grief. He read the water now. The marks only counted the white, and the white was nearly gone and was the smaller account. The water counted the birds, and the birds were the thing.

*

He had the boatmen put him ashore on the lee ledge of the middle island where the guanay still bred, because the colony counts had to be taken from the ground and the ground was where the truth was. He went up the worn path his boots knew better than any road in Lima, the book buttoned inside his coat against his ribs the way his father carried the stick and the German had carried his field-book, and he came out onto the breeding shoulder into the smell and the noise of it, and stopped.

The noise was wrong.

It was a thing he felt before he could have said what it was, in the body, the way the warm water had been in him nine days before the glass would say so. He had stood on breeding ground since he was twelve and the sound of a guanay colony in the laying season was a single thing made of a quarter of a million quarrels, a wall of it, so that a man could not hear his own boots; and the sound on the shoulder this morning had holes in it. Stretches of it had gone quiet. He laid down his square — two of his own forearms each way, the same square he had cut to his arm at twelve and never re-cut, because to re-cut it would break the long line of the count and make this winter no longer answerable to that one — and he counted the nests in it, and there were birds, there were still birds, the ground was still grey-black with them packed to the centre the way a colony packs when it is dying and a fool on the cliff would say *thousands, look, thousands* — but he was not counting the birds. He was counting what the birds were doing. And what a third of them were doing, in the square and in the next square and along the whole near edge of the shoulder where the loose nesters sat, was nothing.

They sat the nest. The bird sat the nest the way a guanay sits, low, hunched, the red of its face dulled, and under it, when Asher knelt and looked — he was let to, here; the diggers had driven the birds off the flat tops but the lee ledge they left alone because there was no white there worth the carrying — under the near bird was a chick, hatched, ten days perhaps, the down still on it, and it was not crying. That was the hole in the noise. A guanay chick of ten days in the laying season cried without stopping, a thin needling shriek into the wall of the adults' din, because it was hungry the way only a thing growing that fast is hungry; and this chick was not crying because it had stopped, because a chick stopped crying when crying had got it nothing for long enough, and the adult sat over it and did not fly, because there was no use flying, because the thing the flying was for had failed.

He made himself count it. He laid the square and counted the nests in the metre — there were eleven, packed, which a man who counted the birds and not the ground would call a thriving colony — and against the eleven nests he counted the chicks alive and crying, which was the only count that signified, and got two. Two in eleven. He paced the breadth of the breeding ground, shorter again than last winter, his boots taking the count, and he worked the long shoulder square by square through the cold forenoon with his lips moving on the numbers and his face giving nothing, because a man who felt about the figure while he was getting it got the figure wrong, the German had been right about that, it was the only way to bear it and the only way to be believed; and when he had it he sat back on his heels on the bare rock among the quiet sitting birds and the dead and dying young and did the arithmetic against the open-water count he had taken at dawn from the boat, and the figure came up a little under three millions over the three islands, down from three and a half the spring before and six the spring before that and twelve when he was thirteen, the column going down the page the way a tide goes out — but the figure was not the thing this morning. The figure had been falling for years from the digging and he had a column of it and could read it. This morning the figure was falling from the sea, which was a different thing, and a faster, and a thing no column of his had a line ruled for, because the digging took the colony's ground and the sea took the colony's food, and a colony could pack tighter and breed on the margins against the loss of ground for a long while, the way it had, the way it was packed below him now — but there was no margin to which a bird could move to be nearer a shoal that had gone down into the cold dark out of reach.

He wrote it. Date, place, count, and in the Remarks column, in his small upright hand, the words the figure could not carry: *Water 16.5°, three mornings. Anchovy gone deep. Adults not feeding. Two chicks living in eleven nests counted. This is not the cut. This is the water.* He looked at it a moment. Then, because the column would not let him lie by saying too little any more than the German's would, he added, low, the thing under the thing: *The cut killed the half of them. The water will take the rest, and it will not take eleven years to do it.*

*

---

*The cold water has gone off the coast. For a thousand years and more the deep green upwelling has stood along this shore in all seasons and lifted the cold up out of the dark, and in the cold has bred the anchoveta in shoals the breadth of provinces, a finger long, silver, past counting; and on the anchoveta has fed the guanay, the booby, the great pelican, the daily commuting river of them off the cliffs and back, three million now over the three islands where there were twenty when the first ships came, and out of the fish through the birds has come the white that the world weighs and borrows against and goes to war for. The chain has four links and it hangs from the cold. This winter the cold is gone. A warm water has come down from the line and lain over the green like oil on a well, two degrees, three, no more — a small wrong number a man could hold in a brass tube on a string — and under it the anchoveta have done the one thing they do, which is to follow the cold, down, out, into the deep dark beyond the reach of a bird that must see its fish and take it in the top fathom of the sea. The shoal has not died. It has gone where the wing cannot go. And on the rock the guanay sit their nests over the hatched young and fly out at dawn the way they have flown out every dawn since before there was a Peru, and they quarter the warm flat empty water that should be black with fish and is not, and they find a few, and they come back with a few, and the chick that needs a fish an hour is fed a fish a day, and stops crying, and on the third day or the fifth is dead under the parent that will not leave it; and the parent sits on, because the thing it is built to do is sit and feed, and the sea has taken away the half of the equation that the wing was for. The failure is not on the rock now. The rock was the cut, and the cut is done, the white nearly gone. The failure is in the sea, where no barrow ever went. A bird beats out over the bare scraped shoulder at first light with nothing in its bill, and does not know the water has changed by a degree and a half, and could not say the figure if it did, and the figure is three, and it is falling, and the man who has it in a book is the only one on the coast who has weighed the warm water and understood that it weighs more than all the rest.*

---

*

He took it to Echeverría's table that night because Echeverría's table was where the family's business now decided itself, in the Lima house with the worn carriage and the foot in the nitrate, and because Inés would be there, and Inés was the only Penhale who would understand half of what he had and act on it, even if it was the wrong half.

He laid the column on the cloth between the candles and the coffee and he told them, flatly, in the plainest commercial terms he could find, which were not difficult to find because the thing was at bottom an argument about a failing supply and he made it as one: that the easy guano was gone and they all knew it; that the price was wobbling, eleven pounds and twelve, the Atacama nitrate taking the bottom out from under it season by season; that this they had reckoned and Inés had reckoned soonest and was right to be getting out. And then the other thing, the thing that was his and no one else's at that table. That the birds were down to three millions from twelve in a dozen years, which they had heard him say before and discounted as the bird-man's melancholy; but that this winter the water off the islands stood two degrees too warm, that the shoal had gone deep, that the young were dying unfed on the ledges where the digging had never reached — and that this meant the thing was not running down, it was being killed, all at once, at the root, by the sea itself; that a colony came apart not the slow way the column showed but the way a thing falls off a cliff, in one season, the one season the shoal moved; and that whatever they meant to do with the islands they had better do it knowing there might soon be no islands worth the name, no white worth carrying and no bird left to make more.

Echeverría heard him out with real courtesy and asked after the boatmen's charge for the hire. Inés heard him with her whole sharp attention and got, Asher saw, exactly half of it, the half her instrument could read — that the supply was failing faster than even she had priced, that the warm water if it held would crater the freight market inside the year, that here was one more reason, the strongest yet, to sell every claim and bond and barrel the house held before the rest of the trade smelled it. She had her pencil out before he had finished. "Then it's worse than I told my father," she said, "and he's a greater fool than I knew to forbid the sell. How long will the warm water hold?"

"I don't know. A season. Two. No one has ever counted it before — there's no column for it." He heard the thing he was failing to make her hear, and tried it once more, plainly. "Inés. It isn't a market. It's the engine. If the warm water holds two seasons the birds are gone, not thinned, gone, and a thing that's gone doesn't come back at a price. There'll be nothing to sell because there'll be nothing there. The selling's not the clever move and the wrong move, it's just—" he stopped, because he was about to say the thing that was Carmen's, the thing that was true and that no one at this table would hear: that the selling was the only honest move left because there was nothing to save. He did not say it. He said, "—it's later than the price knows."

"The price never knows," Inés said, not unkindly, writing. "That's how a body makes money in it. You've given me the best reason yet to be out by spring. The birds I'll leave to you." She looked up at him a moment, and something in the cold clever face nearly softened, the way it nearly softened when she looked at Carmen. "You count a true thing, Asher. You always did. It's only that the men who buy it can't be sold a thing that doesn't pay, and a dead bird doesn't pay. Maur learned that. You'd think the family would have learned it off Maur." She went back to the column, to the half of it she could use. "Eleven pounds and falling. We sell."

He gathered the book. He had told a true man's truth at a table of able people who were not fools and not cruel, and they had taken the half that had a price and set the other half gently aside, the way the men in the good room near the Plaza had set the German's life work face down on top of two other men's, because the bird had no price and the warm water had no price and there was no instrument in the whole machinery of the trade that could weigh a thing that did not pay against a thing that did.

*

Carmen was waiting in the patio when he came out, sitting in the dark on the lip of the dry fountain with the plain dark comb crooked in her hair the way it had been crooked since she was eight, eighteen now and able to wait in a dark patio without fidgeting because he had taught her, the year she was twelve, that you did not fill a count with talk.

"They wouldn't hear it," she said. Not a question.

"Inés heard half. The right half wrongly." He sat down beside her on the cold stone. "She'll sell. She'd have sold anyway. I gave her a better reason and she took the reason and left the bird on the table." He found he had the book still in his hand and he opened it in the faint light off the house and turned to the back and showed her, because she was the one person alive he could show the column to and watch her take it down behind her eyes the way he took it down behind his, and not flinch, and not weep, and not say he had a morbid mind. "It's the water. Not the cut now. The cut's done — the cut killed half of them years back. This is the water. Two degrees warm and the shoal goes down out of reach and the young starve on the rock the diggers never touched. I counted two chicks living in eleven nests this morning. Two. The sea's doing in one winter what the barrows took a dozen years to do, and there's no barrow to stop and no lease to lapse and no island to close that fixes it, because the harm's three miles down in the warm dark where I can't put a square."

She was quiet a long time, reading it. He let the number sit. Out beyond the wall the Lima night went on, the carriages, the gas-lamps, the great fortune of the guano age in its last gilded season pretending it was not over; and he watched her face take down the figure and then take down the thing under the figure, which was the thing he had not been able to say at the table, and he saw her get to it, the way she always got to the thing under the thing, the fish coming up under the lure.

"Then there's nothing to save," she said. The flat counting voice he had given her, but he saw it cost her. She did the wrist to her mouth, the small dry catch, the inheritance, the one that was his too and her mother's that had killed her mother in a front room in this city when she was eight. "We've been arguing it for a year as if it were a choice. Save the islands or sell them. Let Grandfather's lease lapse back to the birds and forfeit the paper, or sell it to a man who'll strip the last of it — and I kept thinking the first one was the honest one even if it ruined us, because at least the birds would come back, slowly, over a hundred years no one I love will live to see, but they'd come back." She turned and looked at him in the dark. "And you've just told me there's no first one. You can't save what the water's already killing. If the warm water holds the birds are gone before the lease even lapses, and then closing the island saves nothing because there's nothing on it, and selling the island sells a rock. It was never two roads. It was one road with the price hidden until now."

"I didn't say all of that," Asher said.

"You showed me the water." She closed the book gently and put it back into his hands. "I worked the rest of it out myself."

He could not answer that, because it was true, and because she had said in the dark in three sentences the thing he had spent the whole evening failing to make a table of clever people understand: that the sea had reached in over the heads of every man arguing over the islands and quietly foreclosed the one decision the family thought it still had to make. Above the dark roofline a few late birds were crossing toward the coast, gulls only, scavengers, the kind that did not need the shoal, and Asher watched them and took the book back out without quite deciding to and turned to the page and wrote, under the day, the truest thing he had, in the small upright hand: *Three, falling. The water, not the cut. Told them. Inés sells. The choice is gone — the sea took it. There is nothing left to save, and we have one more winter to find that out for certain than we will be glad of.*


## MANIFEST IV — The Borrowing

### Chapter 24 · Liddell Penhale
*Lima · 1876 · guano ≈ £11/ton and softening · Chincha working face ≈ 12 ft (the dregs)*

The letter came up from Callao with the morning's papers, and Liddell Penhale read the papers first.

He did it without deciding to, the way he had once put on the clean collar before going ashore to take a man's chair, because a man who read the market before he read his grief lost nothing by the order and a man who reversed it lost the morning. The papers told him what he already knew and told it in the only language the city still had for it, which was the language of a thing that would not stop falling. *El Comercio* had the new figure for the nitrate at Iquique and would not say what the figure meant, the way a doctor will give you the pulse and not the prognosis. The government had suspended the half-yearly on the external debt. The phrase they used was *suspensión temporal del servicio*, temporary, and Liddell read the word *temporary* and his mouth did the thing it did when a master told him the dust in the hold was nothing, a small dry tightening at one corner, because he had been selling *temporary* his whole life and knew exactly what it weighed. The whole of Peru had borrowed against the next ship and the ship after that and the birds that would make the cargo for the ship that came after those, and now the birds were a rumour and the next ship was a wreck and the men in London who held the paper wanted to be paid in something that was not a promise about excrement, and there was nothing on the islands to pay them with but excrement, and the price of that had stood at fourteen pounds the ton in the year the girl was twelve and stood this morning at eleven and softening, eleven and going, and he could feel it going under him the way you feel a deck go under you when the cargo has shifted and not yet told the hull.

Then he read the second letter, which was Rosa, and which said she had died in the night.

It was Constancia's hand, the younger girl's, the round careful hand of a child who had been taught to write by nuns and had never had a reason to write him before. *Mamá murió esta noche, sin dolor.* Without pain. He sat with it in the grey light off the patio and he found that the part of him that did the reading went on reading, went back over the line, weighed the word *sin dolor* and judged it a kindness the girl had put in to spare him or to spare the dead, because Rosa had been ill a year and there had been pain, he had paid for the things that were given against the pain, the Frenchman's drops and the woman from the Malambo whom Rosa trusted further than the Frenchman, and a year of paying for a thing did not buy you the right to be told at the end that the thing had not been needed. He knew it had been needed. He had signed for it. He sat there and he could not make the fact be a feeling. He turned it over and over, the way he had turned the *Marianne* over twenty years before, sound hull, foul bottom, what's she worth, and he could find in himself nothing that was not an assessment.

*She kept a good house,* was the first whole thought that came, and it came in his own voice, flat, and he heard it as if another man had said it across a counting-room, and he was ashamed of it the way you are ashamed of a sum that comes out wrong in front of a clerk — not for the cruelty of it, he had no entry for the cruelty, but for the *poverty* of it, that thirty years of a woman should resolve in him to four words about the keeping of a house, that he should reach for her and bring up a valuation. He had married salvage, men said of him, behind the hand, in two cities; he had heard it; he had never troubled to deny a true account. But it was the other thing they did not see, which was that the salvor cannot give back what the salvor takes. You stripped the wreck and you logged it and what was left was a hull that no longer floated and was no longer worth the stripping, and you did not weep over a stripped hull, you could not, there was nothing in you that had not already been spent on the appraising. Rosa had been a sound vessel and he had taken the freight of her, the legitimacy, the house, the daughter who was the best thing either of them ever made, and somewhere in the taking the part of him that might have grieved her had gone over the side with everything else he had thrown overboard at eleven to keep the ship light, and there was no getting it back up off the bottom now. He knew this about himself with a clarity that was the nearest thing he had to feeling, and the clarity did not help, the clarity was the wound, and he sat in his daughter's careful childish hand and could not cry for the mother of his child, and he understood that this, precisely this, was what it had cost to stand his whole life on the buying side of the scale.

A line came up in him then, off a chapel bench forty years and a hemisphere gone, in his father's drink-thick voice — *we are but strangers here, we do not crave a home, Heaven is our home* — and he sat very still and let it surface and crushed it, because it only ever came when he was afraid, and he would not be afraid in his own patio over a thing that was already done. But it had come. It had logged something while it was open, the chest, the way a draught-mark logs the load whether the master wills it or not. He folded the girl's letter along its fold and put it in the breast of his coat, against the place where the cough sat that he did not call a cough, and he went to find his daughter, because there was a thing that had to be stopped before the day got into it, and the only person in Lima who could stop it was the only person who wanted to do it, which was the difficulty.

*

Inés was at the long table in the front room with the books open, and she had been at them since before light; he could read it on her the way he read the marks on a cliff, the candle burned to a stub though the day was up, the second coffee gone cold at her elbow, the particular stillness of a person who has done a sum three times and got the same answer three times and does not like it. She had Rosa's letter already. He saw it folded by her hand and saw that she had wept and stopped, because there was no wet on her face but the lashes had that stiffness, and he felt a thing move in him that was not grief and was adjacent to it, a kind of respect, that his daughter had buried her mother before breakfast and come to the table, because that was what he had made her and she did it better than he had ever done it. He had never in his life been able to tell her this. He had said *she'll do* when she was an hour old and Rosa had named her over his silence, and *there's my best cargo* when she was twelve, and these were the largest words of love he owned and they were both of them words off a manifest, and Inés had built her entire glittering self to deserve the second one and he had watched her do it and had never once told her that the deserving was not the love, that the love was the helpless thing under the appraising that he could not get at any more than he could get at his grief for her mother. The same drowned thing. He stood in the door and looked at the back of her bent head and the grey coming in her dark hair already at six-and-twenty, no, five, she was five-and-twenty still, and he thought: *I made a beautiful instrument and I cannot play it and it cannot play itself and we will both of us die having only ever been weighed.* And then he came in and sat down across from her at the books, because the thought was no use, and the books were the morning's work.

"You've seen it," she said. She meant the suspension. She did not mean her mother. They would not, either of them, mean her mother out loud; that was understood between them, a thing they had in common like the eyes.

"I've seen it."

"It's not temporary."

"No."

"They'll not resume the service. There's nothing to resume it with. Dreyfus has stopped his remittances, the railways have eaten the loans, and the loans were the guano and the guano is—" She turned a page, found the column she wanted, turned the book so he could read it, the way she had turned the *Concordia* settlement to him under his own hand when she was thirteen. He did not look at the column. He knew what was in it. "—the guano is fifteen feet of scraping on the Chincha north and twelve at the high face and dropping, and the lesser islands are worse, and the price is eleven and the nitrate is undercutting it on every contract we bid, and I lost the Hamburg order Tuesday to a saltpetre house out of Valparaíso that delivered a better nitrogen at nine. Nine, Father. We cannot meet nine. There is no working of these islands that meets nine." She put one finger on the turned page, on the figure, and held it there, not arguing, only showing, and he saw that she had got the trick of it from somewhere, the showing-not-arguing, and he did not let himself think from whom, because the man it had come from was a Chinese broker who was dying behind the cathedral and that was a separate account he did not open before noon. "We sell," she said. "We sell the leases, we sell the bonds we hold, we sell the agency, we take the Echeverría land and the nitrate stake Bertrand has and we are *out*, with capital, before this is bare rock and worth nothing and we are a name that owes London money we cannot pay. I have the buyers. I have had the buyers for a year. You forbade it for a year." Her voice did not rise. That was the worst of it, that her voice did not rise; she had learned that off him too. "Mother is dead this morning and I have done the books and I am telling you, while there is still a thing to sell, *sell it.*"

He looked at the figure now. Twelve feet. He had read a hundred and eighty off that same cliff in the year he landed, in the year this girl was not yet born, and he had read a hundred and sixty off it with a lantern in fifty-four, alone on the cut face in the dark, and he had done the sum that night that no other man in the trade had done, the sum on the floor, the number of seasons that stood between the cut and the bare rock, and he had got it right. He had known. He was the one man who had known. And the knowing had not made him cautious; the knowing had made him *quick*, get rich before the floor, cut faster, because a man who has stood in a line to have his teeth looked at does not slow down when he learns there is a floor, he runs at the money while the running is good, and he had run, and the running had made the house. And now the girl he had run it for was sitting across the books telling him the floor was here, was this morning, was twelve feet and eleven pounds and a saltpetre house at nine, and that the last clever thing the house could do was to admit it out loud to a buyer and take the buyer's money for it.

And he could not.

He sat with the why of it and the why was not in the books. The books said sell. Every figure his daughter had set in front of him said sell, and his daughter was the best reader of figures he had ever made or met, better than he was now, and he knew she was right the way he knew his own hand. To sell now, at eleven, at twelve feet, with the buyers she had kept warm for a year — that was the one move left that salvaged the house, and salvage was his whole religion, salvage was the word he had built a life on, you stripped the wreck before she went under, you did not stand on the deck out of sentiment and drown with the cargo, only a fool did that, only a man who had let a thing get into him that was not arithmetic. He had taught her that. He had taught the whole trade that. And he could not do it, and he sat there and felt the inability in him like a stone in the chest beside the cough that was not a cough, and he made himself look at the why, because he was a man who looked at things, it was the only courage he had ever owned.

To sell the islands as a wreck was to say the islands had always been a wreck. To take a buyer's money for twelve feet of dregs was to write, in the one ledger that could not be falsified, that the mountain ran out — that it had a bottom, that it had *always* had a bottom, that the hundred and eighty feet of it he had stood under on the deck of the *Petrel* with the smell of the fortune in his throat had been, from the first morning, a finite hoard being spent, a wreck on a longer fuse, and that every ton he had ever logged had brought the bottom nearer, and that the whole of it, the house, the daughter, the forty years, the running, had been a man getting rich off a thing that was running out and calling it a fortune. And if that was true — and it was true, the girl's figures said it was true, he had said it himself with a lantern in fifty-four — then he had never been rich at all. He had been a man spending a corpse. He had been, the whole time, on the wrong side of the only scale that counted, and the eleven-year-old on the deck with the thumb under his chin had been the last time anyone weighed him truly, and the man with the book had been right, the boy had a price and the price was finite and the boy had spent his whole life pretending he had got off the scale when he had only ever changed which pan he stood in.

He could not write that. He would die first. He understood, sitting across the books from his weeping dry-eyed daughter on the morning her mother died, that he would actually, literally die first, that he would hold these leases until they were bare rock and worth nothing and the house owed London money it could never pay, that he would let the one clever move go past because making it meant admitting the floor, and that this was madness by every figure in the books and was the only sane thing his soul could do, because his soul had been built at eleven on a single beam, *be the man who does the weighing, never the thing weighed,* and to sell now was to step, finally, willingly, onto the pan with the chin and the teeth, and a man does not do that. A man would rather drown.

"No," he said.

She had known he would say it. He saw her know it; she had come knowing it; she had done the books and buried her mother and come to the table to be told no by her father one more time, because she was constitutionally unable to stop being right at him even when she had given up on his hearing it, and that too she had off him, the inability to stop showing the true count to a man who would not read it. They were exactly alike and it had ruined them both.

"Father." Just the word. Not a plea — she did not plead, he had not built her to plead — but the word set down on the table between them like the last figure in a sum, the figure that is the answer, the one you cannot argue with because it is only the truth added up. "She's gone. There is nothing here now but the money, and the money is going, and if you hold these you will leave me a *debt*. You will leave the name a debt. Is that—" and here her voice did the thing at last, not rising, the opposite, dropping to almost nothing, "—is that the cargo you want to leave me. After all of it. A wreck with the bottom out, and your name on the manifest."

The hymn came up in him hard, the whole of it now, his father's voice and the chapel and the cold bench and the words rising in his chest with a pressure that was very near the pressure of the cough, *we are but strangers here, we do not crave a home,* and he did not crush it fast enough, it got nearly to his teeth, and he had to set his jaw against it the way he set his jaw against the catch in his lungs when prices were mentioned, and he sat there with the fear of being poor and owned and weighed rising in him in the shape of a hymn, on the morning his wife was a day dead, refusing the one thing that would save his daughter, because saving her cost him the only thing he had ever been able to keep, which was the lie that he had been rich.

"The mountain doesn't run out," he said. It was not an argument. He heard himself say it and heard that it was not an argument, that it was the thing under the argument, the beam itself spoken aloud, and he watched it land on her and watched her understand, finally, all the way down, what she was dealing with, which was not a businessman making a bad call. It was a man who would die to keep from learning he had been wrong. "It looks low. It's looked low before. There's deposit back from the face on the lesser islands no survey's touched, there's—" He stopped. She was looking at him the way the girl Carmen looked at him, the way the half-Chinese child looked at him over the top of whatever sum she was always doing, the look that saw the beam and did not say it saw the beam, and he could not bear it from his own daughter, the being *read*, and he stood up from the books. "I've said no. We hold. Go and—" He gestured, a man's helpless gesture at the whole apparatus of death, the priest, the dressmaker for the black, the notices, the things that women did with a death that he had no entry for. "Go and see to your mother."

She gathered the books. She did it precisely, squaring them, the way he had taught her, and she did not look at him again, and at the door she stopped with the ledgers against her like a child carrying schoolbooks, five-and-twenty and grey already coming in her hair, and she said, not turning, in the flat cargo-voice that was the only inheritance he had managed to give her clean, "I'll do the notices. Carmen's coming up from behind the cathedral. The old man's worse. She'll want to see you." And then, because she could not help it, because it was bred into her as deep as it was bred into him: "Eleven and a half, this morning, on the Liverpool wire. Not eleven. It dropped while we sat here." And she went out, leaving him the half-shilling like a coin on a dead man's eye, the true figure to the last, because between the two of them a true figure was the nearest thing to *I loved her too* that either of them could put on the page.

*

Carmen came in the afternoon, up from the two rooms behind the cathedral where the broker lay dying, and she came in with the white dust of the long road on her hem and the small dark foreign comb in her hair that she had worn since she was a child, and she sat with him without saying much, which was her gift and the reason he could bear her when he could bear no one. She had her mother in her, the dead Chinese woman he had never let himself think about, the same square watchfulness; and she had the cough, which he heard her swallow twice in the hour, the small wet catch she had learned to take down before it showed, the same catch that sat in him now and that he took down the same way, and he did not let himself put the two catches side by side, hers and his, because that was a sum he would not do, that was the worst sum in the house and he had spent the morning refusing an easier one.

"Inés told you," he said. "About Rosa."

"She told me." Carmen looked at her hands. Nineteen now, he made her, the braid-child, the orphan he had taken in as a good asset and loved the only way he had, which had not been enough and had not been nothing. "I'm sorry, Grandfather." And then, because she was the only person alive who would say a true thing to him gently, "Uncle Mei is sorry too. He said to tell you. He can't come. He hasn't the breath for the stairs now." She said it flat, the way she said everything, but he saw what it cost her, and he saw the thing he had been refusing to see all day, which was that there was a second floor in this house and it was in the old broker's chest, that the man who had counted the dead on a stick for forty years was himself nearly counted now, was twelve feet of dregs in his own body, and that the girl in front of him belonged to that dying man as much as she belonged to Liddell, more, and that she was going to be left holding both of them, the English wreck and the Chinese one, the two old men who had come up from the bottom of the same scale and spent their lives proving you could climb it and were proving now, in two failing chests across the city from each other, that you could not, that the scale took its weight back at the end no matter which pan you had clawed your way onto.

"He counted straight," Liddell said. It was the highest thing he knew how to say about a man. Carmen heard it as such; he saw her hear it; her eyes went bright and she swallowed, the cough or the other thing, he could not tell.

"He did," she said.

He wanted, for one moment, with a force that frightened him, to say the rest of it to her — to say *your grandmother kept a good house and I cannot cry for her and I think I have left it too late to learn how, I think I left it on a beach forty years ago, and I am going to hold these islands until they are bare because I cannot afford to know what you and Inés and the old man behind the cathedral all already know, and I would rather leave you a debt and a lie than leave you a true accounting of what I have been* — but the words were not in him, there was no manifest that carried that freight, and what came out instead, in the flat low voice, was: "Tell him I asked after him."

And the girl nodded, and took it for what it was, which was the most he had, and she got up to go back across the city to the dying man, and at the door she did a thing her aunt had done that morning and that the dead woman would have done and that he himself would never do, which was to cross the room and put her hand for a moment on his shoulder, the white dust still on her hem, the comb dark in her hair, the cough held down in her young chest, and she said nothing at all, and went out into the failing light toward the broker's rooms; and Liddell Penhale sat alone in the house the island money had built, on the day his wife had died and the debt had come due and the price had dropped a half-shilling while he sat, and he took out the girl Constancia's letter with its line about the absence of pain, and he could not weep over it, and the hymn rose in him again and he let it come this time, all the way, *we are but strangers here, we do not crave a home, Heaven is our home,* a drowning man's hymn in an empty room, and he thought, in the one part of him no figure could reach, that the old broker behind the cathedral would be dead before the month was out and would die knowing the true count, would die having written the real number down on his stick where it could not be falsified, and that he himself would outlive the man and die later and richer on paper and never once, to the last breath, let himself add it up.


## MANIFEST IV — The Borrowing

### Chapter 25 · Mei Loong-fa
*Lima, behind the cathedral · 1877 · guano ≈ £11/ton · Chincha working face ≈ 12 ft (dregs)*

The count he was keeping now was his breath, and it was the easiest count he had ever kept, because it went only one way and he did not have to set it down.

He lay on his back in the smaller of his two rooms, the one without the window, because the other room had the light and the light was a thing he could no longer use and the dark cost less, and he listened to himself go in and out and made of it a sum the way he had made a sum of everything for twenty-eight years. So many in the hour. Fewer than the hour before. He did not need the slate for it. He had carried whole afternoons of a rice factor's accounts in his head as a boy and never written a figure until the day was done, and he could carry this, the small descending column of his own breath, without his hand, which was good, because his hand had begun in the last days to do a thing he did not give it leave to do — to lift, a little, off the coverlet, the fingers working as if at a notch, cutting a wood that was not there — and he had to watch it the way he watched the men a week before they broke, for the sign of the thing it would do next.

The cough sat in him low, an old lodger that paid no rent and would not be turned out. He had known it twenty years. It had come into his chest the second winter of the war and he had thought, then, that he had a few seasons before it finished its arithmetic, and he had been right, and the few seasons had been long, longer than A-Yün got, longer than A-Lian got, who had it from a child and went at twenty-seven, and he had used the seasons. That was the thing he could set against the column when it ran out. He had used them. He had bought a rock and stood on it and fed his gang full and known to the grain what the full measure was, and it had changed nothing, and he had done it anyway, and a man who did a thing knowing it changed nothing and did it for the dead and not the living was a man who had at least chosen his uselessness, which was more than the boy on the deck in '49 had been let to do.

He breathed. He counted. The white was in it. He could not see it but he could feel it, the dry catch at the bottom of each breath where the air would not finish going down, the same caustic breath that had come off the broken face at the Chincha the morning it took his eye, the strength they had starved the fields of England for, gone now into him a barrow at a time across a working life and lodged in the soft of him where no scale could weigh it out. The fields had eaten the white and grown their corn. He had eaten it too, breath by breath, off the same air, and it had not grown anything in him but this, the slow filling of a lung with the thing he had spent his life selling, and there was a joke in it somewhere that A-Lian would have found and said aloud and made him almost laugh, but A-Lian was twelve years under the Lima ground and he could not find the joke himself. He had never been the one who found them.

*

The doctor came in the forenoon and Mei heard him on the stair before the door, the heavy careful tread of a man paid by the visit who did not want to be there, and he set his hand flat on the coverlet so it would not be caught lifting.

"He should be in the light," the doctor said to someone in the doorway, not to Mei, the way they spoke of him now, over him, the way the man on the beach had said *three hundred the head* over his shoulder as if Mei were a hog. "The air is better by the window."

"He won't have the light," said Carmen.

"Then at least—"

"He won't have it. Leave him the dark. He knows what he wants."

The doctor came and put the cold disc of the instrument against Mei's chest and listened, his head bent, and Mei lay still under it and read the man's face out of his right eye, the only ledger left him, and saw the figure come up in the doctor's mind and saw the doctor decide not to say it, and was almost grateful, because it spared him having to arrange his own face to receive a number he already had. He had had the number a long time. He had been the man who held the number and did not say it, all his life; he could let another man have the office for once.

"How long," he said, in the English, to spare Carmen the saying.

The doctor straightened. "Señor—"

"How long. I am not a child. I have weighed worse things than this and said them to the man's face." His voice came thin off the top of the air, the breath under it short, and he heard how little of it there was and used what there was. "Tell me the figure or send me a man who will."

The doctor looked at him then, properly, the first time, and something in him gave way to the thing in Mei that had stood at a thousand weighs and would not be lied to over a count. "Days," he said. "A week. Perhaps less, if the lung fills."

"It is filling."

"Then less." The doctor packed his bag. At the door he said, to Carmen, the thing they always said, the absolution they handed out of their bags free with the visit, "He is not in much pain."

When he was gone Mei lay and listened to the not-much-pain settle back over him like silt, and thought: a man who says that has never been on the rock. There was no pain in the breath running out. The pain was elsewhere and it was old and it had nothing to do with the lung. The lung was only arithmetic. He had made his peace with arithmetic in a wineshop in Amoy before he was nineteen, watching a broker draw the figure for eight in spilled wine; arithmetic he could lie down inside of and be still. It was the other account that would not let him be still, the one in the wood, the one that did not balance and never would, and that account was the reason his hand kept lifting off the coverlet to cut a notch in the air, because the hand knew, even now, that there were men it had not got down, men whose names had gone over the cliff with them and never been carved anywhere, and the hand wanted to be at its work while there was still a hand.

*

He had the boat in him, those last days. Not the passage-boat, the long one, the hold; that one he had put down years ago, having outlasted it. The other one. The supply boat, the dry-month one, that went out to the island, his island, the stump off the Pisco coast that he had bought to be the man who owned the thing, and the deck of it under him in a swell, and the white coming up out of the water ahead the way it had come up the first morning when he had thought it snow.

The island stood lower in his memory now than it had stood the last time he climbed it. That was the white doing its own arithmetic, the one he had read off it for twenty-eight years, the one that only went down. Forty feet at the high face when he signed for it. Fifteen the last season he had stood on it, the cut eating into the red rock below the white so that the rock showed through in long sores that would not skin over, the deposit going the way the Chincha had gone, which his men brought him word of when the supply boats crossed — the great mountain that had stood a hundred and eighty feet the morning he landed, that had been the richest ground in the world, cut now to a dozen feet of dregs that the last gangs scraped off the bare stone for a price that had begun, this last year, to do a thing the price had never done in his lifetime: to fall. The nitrate was in it. There was a salt they dug in the desert to the south now that did the work cheaper, and the white that had been worth more by the ton than any cargo in the history of the world was worth eleven pounds this season and slipping, and the men who had said it could not run out were beginning, very quietly, where they thought no one heard, to count the years left to them the way he had heard Penhale's kind count them in a wineshop tone, *short and rich,* except that now it was only short.

He had bought at the top. He had known he bought at the top. He had told the boy with the notebook, his son, the one who counted birds, *you read a column, I read a man,* and he had read the seller's fear and bought because a man who sold out of fear was a man Mei would never again let himself be, and he had been right about the fear and the rock had been just as foundered as the boy said, and it had paid for one race and would not pay for another. He did not regret it. A man could not regret the only freedom the place had taught him to want; that would be to regret having wanted to stand up off the boards. But he understood, lying in the dark with the boat in him, the thing he had begun to understand at the lawyer's table with the wax not yet dry — that he had got the arithmetic of his whole second life wrong, that he had thought there was a side of the scale a man could stand on and be safe, the buying side, the side that held the pen, and there was not, there had never been, the white killed the man who sold it as surely as the man who dug it, only slower, only by the fraction that did not reach the lung, and now the fraction had run out in him too, in the owner, the proprietor, the man who fed his gang full, and he was going down off the same air as the boy of seventeen with the burn-scar whom he had set in it, kindly, full-fed, for a tenth and a lease and the rest his.

The boy was on the island still, or under it. Mei did not know which. He had been too sick this last season to go out and read the cut and the gang off the working face the way a man should who owns a face, and he had let the clerk bring him the figures, and the figures had a number for the gang that was smaller this month than last, and Mei had not asked which names the difference was, because to ask was to have to cut the wood, and his hand was no longer steady enough on the slate-flake to cut wood, and a notch cut by a shaking hand was a notch that lied about the man, made him smaller or larger than he had been, and Mei would not lie to the dead in the one book that had never lied to them. So there were men, this last season, who had gone under his rock and not been got down, because the clerk of the dead had been too busy dying to clerk them, and that was the thing that would not let him be still, that and one other.

*

Carmen sat with him in the afternoons.

She came after the counting-house let her go — she ran more of the family's affairs now than her aunt liked, the only one of them with the books open and her eyes open over them, the only one doing the true sum while Inés liquidated and Liddell, failing in his own house across the city, denied to his last shillings that the mountain could end — and she came in the dim and sat in the chair by the bed without lighting the lamp, because she did not need to see him to sit with him and he could not see her either way, and they sat in the dark like two people on a deck at night who do not need to look at the water to know it is there.

She had the comb in her hair. He knew it without seeing it, the way he knew everything about her, by the small sounds and the long knowing — the plain dark comb gone amber, the close teeth, the line of plum-flowers worn smooth along the spine by a thumb, his mother's thumb first and then A-Lian's and now hers, twenty years of it now in this girl's hair, the one thing that had come whole out of the lane and the hold and the rock and the dying, the homeland made into an object small enough to keep. His mother had pushed it into A-Lian's hand the morning the men came, on the lane that ran down to the harbour well, and said the thing he had only learned A-Lian had carried all these years from Rosa, after, that a girl with a comb like that in her hair was a girl from somewhere and no one could make her be from nowhere. The lane was sold. The grey house was gone these twenty-eight years, his mother under foreign ground he had never seen and his father too and the brother who drowned carrying the net, all of them somewhere he had set out at nineteen to come back to and never did, and the only thing left standing of any of it was a comb in the hair of a girl who had his sister's watching eyes and his own banked stillness and the cough, faint still, the dry catch he could hear under her breath when she leaned to settle the coverlet, the same lodger that had moved into him and her mother before him, taking up its quiet residence in the soft of her, beginning its long count.

He had heard it on the boat the day she was fifteen and he had not said. He would not say now. There was nothing in the saying that the body would not say for itself, in its own time, the only ledger no man could falsify, the one that crossed from the woman who dug the dust at eleven to the brother who sold it for forty years to this girl who had breathed it in a sea-green dress at the age of six, held up to the working face by her own father to see what built her dresses. The account ran in the blood and the breath and it did not skip. He could not stop it. He had never been able to stop anything; he had only ever been able to keep the count of it, and even that he was losing now, the slate-flake too heavy for his hand.

"Sam wants to sell," Carmen said into the dark. Not because he needed telling. Because she needed to say it to someone who would not tell her what to do.

"Sam is your brother."

"Sam is right about the money." He heard her turn the words over the way he had taught her to turn a figure she did not trust. "The money's gone, or near gone. He says take what the leases will still fetch and walk away clean and be poor and breathing instead of rich on paper that's about to be worth nothing. He's not wrong. Inés isn't wrong. They've both got the number right." A pause, the small dry catch in it. "And it's the wrong sum."

"Why."

"Because their sum only has the one column." She said it low and flat, the way she said the true things, and he lay and listened to his own banked watching come back at him out of A-Lian's voice and was not unsettled by it now, the way he had been on the island when she was fifteen; he was past being unsettled; he was glad of it, the way a man is glad of a thing he has made that will stand after him. "They can balance it because they leave out the part that won't go into soles. They always could. That's how you balance it — you leave out the men. Liddell balanced it for forty years by leaving them out. Sam can sell clean because he doesn't carry them. He was three when they died; he never learned the other column; he's the only one of us who can add it up and have it come out, because to him there's only the one book." Her chair creaked. "I can't make it come out. I've never been able to make it come out, not since I was eight years old at my mother's bed counting her breaths down. I'm the place where the two books don't add up."

Mei lay still a long time, his breath going in and out, the small column dropping.

"You write it down," he said at last. It was the thing he had said to her when she was twelve and he had let her hold the old dark stick for the first time and she had found her mother's notch low on it, set apart, the room left above it. It was the nearest thing to a blessing he had; it was also the nearest thing to a warning, and she knew it for both, because he had taught her, without ever meaning to, that the two were the same. "You keep the column that won't go into soles. Even when it won't balance. Especially when it won't balance. A book that balances is a book that left something out. You keep the one that doesn't."

"I know," she said.

"It is a hard thing to keep."

"I know what it is. I've watched you keep it my whole life."

"Then you know I could not put it down," Mei said, "and that is why I am giving it to you, and not to spare myself, and not to make it yours instead of mine. I am giving it to you because you are the only one of them who already knows what it weighs, and a thing like this you cannot give to a person who thinks it weighs nothing. They would lose it. They would balance it. You will not be able to."

*

He had her bring the box.

It was the flat tin box he had kept the worn paper in, the contract with the figure for eight on it that a broker had drawn for him in spilled wine and that the salt and the years had taken down to a grey smear no eye could read, kept all the same, because it was the first ledger anyone had ever kept on him and he did not throw away ledgers. The box was on the shelf in the dark room and Carmen found it by feel and brought it to the bed and put it into his hands, and his hands knew the weight of it, and he held it on his chest where the breath rose and fell under it and did not open it yet.

"The lease is in here," he said. "Both. The Pisco-coast island and the Chincha one, the one that was Quick's, the one I bought at the top the day before the old man died, which the boy with the notebook said I should not buy, and he was right, and I bought it. They are yours now. The lawyer has the paper that makes them yours; this is the deed and the lease both; you will take them to him." He breathed. "You will do with them what your sum tells you. Not Sam's sum. Not Inés's. Yours, the one that does not come out. If your sum tells you to sell them, sell them, and I will not be on the rock to mind, and a man who is dead has no use for a rock anyway. But you will know what is in them before you decide. You will know what the rock is. You have seen it. You stood on my face and you saw it." He turned his head toward where her breathing was, the old habit, bringing her to his good ear now that the eye was no use at all in the dark. "There is no clean way to be rid of it that I ever found. I looked. Twenty-eight years I looked for the side of it a man could stand on and have clean hands, and there is not one, and I am telling you that so you do not waste your years looking the way I wasted mine. The hands are not clean. They were never going to be clean. The question is only what you do with dirty hands, and that is a different question, and I do not know the answer to it, and I am too far gone to find it, so I am handing you the question, which is the only honest thing I have to hand anyone."

He opened the box. He took out, from under the grey worn paper, the two sticks.

The first was full — every face of it cut, the cuts gone smooth and grey with the years and the oil of his hands, the top notch worn nearly round, *Cheng A-Yün, tile-maker, a village a half-day west, a wife, a son who would be a man of thirty-nine now and never know.* The second was younger, the pale wood greyed only at the top where his hands had worn it, the rest of it filling, the new cuts low and bright — his own gang's dead, the men he had bought off the Callao line and set in the air himself, the proprietor's own death-roll kept in the proprietor's own hand. And there was a third, that she had not seen, the newest, hardly begun, two notches only, that he had started this last season when the second filled, and had thought he would fill himself, and would not now.

He put all three into her two hands. He felt her take them the way she took a thing she meant to weigh, the way he had taught her, the only inheritance he had managed to teach her on purpose. He felt her thumb find the notches in the dark, the way his own thumb found them, the way a man reads who has no eyes. He heard her counting, her lips not moving, the count going behind her face the way it went behind his, the held count, the worst account, rendered to no one. He heard her reach the third stick, the two bright new cuts on the almost-bare wood, and stop.

"There's room left," she said. Her voice was steady. She had her mother's will in her, the will the islands could not break and only the lungs, in their slow time, could; he had heard it in A-Lian to the last hour, the steadiness over the unbearable, the refusal of pity even at the cost of the lung. "On this one. There's room left above the two."

"Yes."

"Men who haven't been got down yet."

"Some," Mei said. "This season. I was too sick to climb the face and read the gang, and the clerk brings me a number and not the names, and there are men gone under my rock that I could not get down because the hand—" He stopped. His hand, on his chest, had lifted again, off the tin, the fingers working at the wood that was not there, and he let her see it, the only confession he had ever made or would, the hand that wanted to be at its work while there was a hand.
