"The hand will not cut clean any more. And a notch cut by a hand that shakes is a lie about the man. So they are not down. They went over and the account did not keep them, and to be lost out of the one book that did not lose the men is to be doubly lost, and I cannot bear it, and I cannot fix it, and that is the other thing that will not let me lie still."

Carmen was quiet. Then he heard her move, and felt her take his shaking hand off his chest, gently, the way he had taken the boy's wrists off his eyes on the rock so the boy would not grind the scald deeper, and she folded it closed and laid it back on the tin and held it there under her own, still, so that it could not lift.

"I'll get them down," she said. "The ones from this season. You tell me what you have of them — the gang number, the dates the clerk brought, what you remember, a village, a scar, a man who stood next to a man. Whatever you have. I'll find the rest from the clerk's figures and the barracks talk, the way you taught me to find a figure that isn't written. And I'll cut them. Not now. When my hand is steady and the count is sure. I'll put them on the stick above the two, in the room you left, and they won't be lost. None of them will be lost. I'll keep the column that won't go into soles, for as long as I have a hand, and when my hand goes I'll find the one who knows what it weighs and give it on the way you're giving it to me, and it won't stop. The count won't stop. I promise you it won't stop."

He lay with his hand closed under hers and his breath going out small, and he did not weep, because he had decided about weeping a long time ago in a wineshop in Amoy and the decision had held for twenty-eight years and would hold to the end; but a thing in him that had been clenched since the ninth day on the rock when A-Yün set his bar down gently and walked off the world — a thing that had stayed clenched through A-Lian's notch and his own cough and the buying of the rock and the boy's burning eye, the whole long clenched holding of an account no one had ever offered to help him carry — let go, a little, in the dark, the way a hand long cramped lets go when another hand at last takes the weight; and he understood that this was the thing the place could not arrange against him, after all, the one sum it had not been able to make come out in its own favour: that the count would outlive the counter, that he had carved a thing he was carrying home to a dead house on a sold lane to prove that men had been real, and it had not got home, and it had got somewhere else instead, into the hands of a girl with his sister's eyes who already knew to the grain what it weighed and would not let it be lost.

"There is a man," he said. His voice was nearly gone now and he gave it the last of the air. "The first one. At the top of the first stick. The one worn round. Cheng A-Yün. A tile-maker. He set his bar down so it would not fall and trouble anyone, and then he walked off the cliff into the sea, and the place did not even stop, and I had to carry the count straight after, with my own hand, and that is the first notch and the reason there is a stick at all. You will keep his name. He had a son of eleven. The son is a man now, thirty-nine, in a village a half-day west of mine, and he never knew, and the only place in the world his father is kept is on that stick, in that notch, worn round. You will keep him. Tell me you will keep the first one."

"I'll keep the first one," Carmen said. "Cheng A-Yün. Tile-maker. A son of eleven. I have him. He's kept."

Mei breathed. The column dropped another figure. Somewhere across the city the price stood at eleven pounds and slipping, and on a rock off the Pisco coast that was his and now hers the white stood at a dozen feet of dregs over the bare red sores of the stone, and the last gangs scraped it for a cargo that fetched less this month than last, and the great mountain at the Chincha was cut to nothing, and the birds — he had heard the boy with the notebook say it, the boy his son, that there were fewer of them every spring and that the water had gone wrong warm this last year and the fish gone deep and the birds coming back to the nests with nothing — the birds were going the way the white had gone, and the boy could not make anyone hear it any more than the German with his squares could, and none of them, not the boy nor the German nor Inés with her number nor Sam with his clean exit, would live to be proved right or wrong by the rock itself, which kept its own slow account and rendered it to no one, and least of all to Mei, who would not live to see the thing that broke him broken, who would die a week or less before the price he had stood inside his whole life finished falling, the rage he had banked for twenty-eight years going down into the ground with him unspent and unvindicated, the place taking even that, even the small cold mercy of being there when it ended.

He minded it less than he had thought he would. He had thought, the once or twice he had let himself think of dying, that he would mind being cheated of the end, of not standing on the bare rock to see the white that had killed A-Lian and A-Yün and the boy with the burn-scar and would kill this girl in her slow time turn worthless under the men who had said it could not, that he would want, more than anything, to be there for that. But the want had gone where the homeward want had gone before it, curdled and then quiet, and what was left in its place, in the dark, under the girl's steady hand holding his still, was smaller and harder and enough: that the count would not stop. That was all. That he would not be there did not matter, because the not-being-there was only arithmetic, and he had made his peace with arithmetic before he was nineteen. The other account, the one that did not balance, the one in the wood — that one had found a hand to keep it that knew what it weighed. The place had not got that. He had got that out of the place. One thing, in twenty-eight years, he had got out, and it was the only thing worth getting, and it was in her hands now, all three sticks, the room left above the two.

He let the breath go. He did not pull the next one in for a moment, just to feel the column stop where he chose, the way a man at the chute-head holds a figure a beat before he sets it down. Then he pulled it in, because it was not time, not quite, the doctor had said days, and a man who had kept the count this long would not falsify his own at the very end by hurrying it.

"Light the lamp now," he said. "I have changed my mind about the dark. I cannot see it. But I would like, while there is a hand, to set my hand on the wood she is holding, and I would like to know the lamp is lit, for her, so she is not sitting alone in the dark with a dying man and a stick of the dead. Light it for her. Not for me."

He heard her strike the match. He felt the small warmth of it find the side of his face, the side with the good ear, the side he turned things toward; and he could not see the light but he knew it was there, the way he had always known the things he could not see, by the long knowing and the small sounds; and he laid his shaking hand over her steady ones on the three sticks, the worn round notch at the top and the bright cuts low and the room left above the two, and he kept it there, and the count went on dropping, easy, one way only, the easiest count he had ever kept; and the girl with his sister's eyes and his sister's comb and the slow lodger already moved into her chest held the wood and held his hand on it and did not let go, and the lamp burned in the room he had said he wanted dark, and the price slipped, and the white stood at a dozen feet on a rock that was hers now, and somewhere out past the curve of the world the warm wrong water was rising over the shoal where the fish had gone down, and the count went on, and would go on, in a steady hand, above the two, in the room he had left.


## MANIFEST V — The Collapse

### Chapter 26 · Inés Penhale
*Callao / the open Pacific · 1877–78 · guano ≈ £11 and falling with no floor · Chincha working face ≈ bare rock*

*The beach below the north island is two hundred yards of dead birds. They have come ashore the way the anchovy used to come ashore, in a tide, except the anchovy are not there; that is the whole of it; the cold water that fed the shoal has gone warm by a degree and a half, and the shoal has gone down into a dark the wing cannot follow, and the guanay that went out at first light over an empty sea have come back over an empty sea and not fed, and gone out again, and come back, until the going out cost more than the coming back was worth, and then they have come down. They lie with the wings half open as if the falling caught them in the middle of a thing. There is no mark on them. The crop is empty and the breast under the feathers is a keel with no flesh left on it, and the smell that comes off two hundred yards of them is not the old smell. The old smell was ammonia, the smell men called money. This is the other smell, the one that was always underneath, that the dry cold air had held down for a thousand years and the warm wet wind now lets up off the rock and the surf-line both. There were twenty million of them over these three islands when the first ships came. There are not a hundred thousand now, and the figure is falling, and the birds do not know it is falling, and could not say it if they did. The water is warm. They cannot reach the fish. That is all they know, and it is not even that; they know only the going out and the coming back with nothing, and then the coming down.*

---

Inés Penhale stood at the harbour-master's board at Callao with her gloves in one hand and read the figure that was not there.

It was not there because there was no figure to chalk. The board ran a column for guano the way it ran a column for sugar and copper and hides, a price per ton against the day's date, and for twenty-eight years that column had carried a number every market morning, and the number had only ever climbed, and last winter it had begun, for the first time in her life and her father's, to fall — and now it carried nothing at all, because a price is the figure a buyer will give, and there was no buyer. The clerk had drawn a short horizontal stroke where the eleven had stood. A stroke meant *no sale offered*. She had seen the stroke against hides in a glutted year and against copper when the mines flooded. She had never seen it against the white. She stood and looked at the small chalk dash on the slate, the whole of the trade's worth rendered as the absence of a worth, and she did the only sum the morning permitted, which was a sum with nothing in it.

"They'll not even bid it down," said Tregenza, beside her. He had come up the way men came up to her now, not to deal but to stand near someone who had been right, as if the being-right rubbed off, as if it were not the most useless quality a person could own at this date. "I had a man this week would have taken the *Antonia*'s cargo at six. Six. I'd have given it him at six and counted the freight saved. He's gone quiet. They've all gone quiet. The Liverpool houses are sitting on a year of it landed and rotting in the sheds, and they'll not take another ton at any figure, because the figure's going to nothing and a man who buys today owns less tomorrow." He said it the way a man says a thing he has rehearsed in the night to make it bearable by the morning. "It's the salitre. The nitrate took the bottom out and now there's no bottom to take."

"It is not the salitre," Inés said.

"How's that?"

"The salitre took the price." She had her eyes on the harbour, on the ships. "The water took the trade." She did not explain it to him. She was done explaining things to men who had not wanted them explained when the explaining could have saved them. She had spent four years explaining and it had cost her every hour of those years and changed not one decision, and she had learned the lesson the trade taught everyone in the end, which was that being right was a kind of cargo you could not sell either.

She looked at the anchorage. There had been a season, the season she came into her power, when she had stood at this same rail and counted the riding-lanterns of the ships waiting on a berth and made it forty, fifty, sixty, a town set down at sea, every hull empty and rolling high and light, every one of them here to be filled with the thing her family sold. They were still here. That was the part that turned the stomach. The ships were still here, a score of them at least, swinging at anchor in the grey forenoon, and they were still empty, and they were not waiting to load. They were waiting for orders that would not come, for a charter that had lapsed, for an owner in Liverpool or Bremen or Boston deciding whether it was cheaper to send them home in ballast or sell them where they lay. A barque she knew, the *Caradoc*, that had loaded off the Chinchas every season for a decade, lay with her yards sent down and a flag she did not recognise at the gaff, which meant she had changed hands at a loss, which meant somewhere a man's whole stake in the trade had been struck through with a chalk stroke like the one on the board behind her. The town set down at sea was still set down. It had simply stopped being a town and become a queue of things no one any longer had a use for.

*

She had the books with her, in the leather case, because she carried the books everywhere now the way a sick man carries the thing that proves his sickness, not to read them — she had them by heart — but to have them to hand when a creditor's clerk came to the door and required to see them, which happened daily.

She went from the harbour to the counting-room, the good rooms off the plaza that the island money had paid for, with the dark cabinets and the long table where she had out-traded Tregenza himself once over a moisture allowance, and she sat down with the case unopened and let herself, for the length of one breath, hate her father.

It was not a feeling she indulged. *Hate* was an extravagance, like grief; both were positions a person took up and then had to defend, and she had built herself out of the conviction that a position you could not defend was a position you should not have taken. But the books were a *minus* now — not a small house's minus, not a bad-season minus, but the great Penhale minus, the one she had carried home from this table four years ago and laid out clean before her father and before the dying Mei and before Carmen, who at seventeen had done the same sum herself and got the same answer: that the house was not *worth* a great deal, the house was *owed* a great deal, against a thing that was running out, to men who would in the end want coin, and that the only honest move on the board was to sell the whole apparatus at the top — the leases, the consignment contracts, the ships, the standing in London — to sell it all while the price was loudest and the fool's eyes saw a fortune, and to take the value off the dying thing and into something that did not breathe ammonia.

She had been right. She had been right at the top and she had been right a year later and she had been right the last time she put it to him, in the spring, with her mother three weeks in the ground, when she had laid the Liverpool figure on the table — *eleven and a half, and it dropped while we sat* — and run the whole case one final time, cold, complete, the work of a person who had been certain since she was twenty-two that this exact morning was coming. And her father had looked at the figure and looked at her, with the dry catch already in his chest that she had heard begin in his lungs three years before, the cough that had started in the diggers and crossed into the man who sold them and was now in the buyer who had stood his whole life on the buying side of the scale, and he had said the thing he always said, the thing that was not an argument because it could not be answered, because it was not about the market at all. *The mountain doesn't run out.*

He had not believed it. That was what she understood now, sitting with the case shut, that she had not let herself understand in the spring. He was the man who had priced the floor of that mountain in 1854, alone on the cut face with a lantern, when she was three years old — Mei had told Carmen and Carmen had told her — he had stood on the white and read the high-water marks and done the sum and known to the season how many years stood between the cut and the bare rock, and he had told no one and chosen to dig faster. He had always known it ran out. He knew it better than she did; he had known it longer than she had been alive. And he would die before he would say it aloud, because to say *the mountain runs out* was to say *I spent a thing that ended*, and to say that was to say *I was not the man who bought; I was a man who sold a corpse a barrow at a time and called the proceeds a fortune*, and a man who had built his entire self, every stone of it, out of one terror at eleven years old on a gang-ship deck — the terror of being the thing on the scale instead of the man who weighed it — could not survive the discovery that he had been weighed wrong all along, that the white had appraised *him*, by the lungful, and found him soft, and was logging him out now the same as it logged out the diggers, only slower, only later, only richer on paper. So he chose the paper. He chose to die rich on a thing worth nothing rather than live having been wrong, and because he chose it, and because he was her father and held the leases in his own name and would not be moved, she had not got out at the top, and now there was no top and no bottom and no buyer and no out, and the figure on the board was a chalk stroke, and she had been right the whole way down, and her rightness was worth precisely what the *Antonia*'s cargo was worth, which was the stroke, which was nothing.

*

The clerk came at noon. Not a creditor's clerk — her own, young Soto, who had stopped wearing the good coat because there was no longer anyone to impress and the good coat cost in the keeping. He had the morning's letters and a face she read in a glance, because reading faces was the one trade left to her that still paid.

"The Casa Dreyfus people," he said.

"What of them."

"They will not roll the bill. The November bill. They say —" he had it in the letter and he did not want to read it, so she took it from his hand. The French house that had held Peru's whole guano by the throat since '69, that had advanced the government millions against guano not yet dug and birds not yet hatched, was calling its paper, because the government had defaulted last year and the guano behind the paper was warm water and dead cormorants and bare rock, and a debt secured against a thing that no longer exists is a debt a prudent house collects today, from whoever it can reach, and the Penhale acceptances were a thing it could reach. She read the figure they wanted by November. She read it twice, not because she had misread it but because the first reading had been a refusal and the second was the acceptance, and she made herself do the second the way she made herself do everything, cleanly.

"We cannot meet it," she said.

"No."

"With the ships sold, the Lima house mortgaged, the leases —" the leases were the joke, the leases were Mei's two islands that had come to Carmen, bare rock and rotting dregs, a deed to a thing that owed its lessee money it could never make. "We cannot meet a tenth of it. They know we cannot meet it. They are calling it because calling it lets them put us in the column of *attempted* before they write us off, so that when the whole edifice goes down they can show their own creditors they pursued every claim." She set the letter on the table, squared it to the edge, a habit of order in a room where order had stopped meaning anything. "It is good practice. I would do the same in their place." She would have. That was the thing about the trade she had loved and the thing it had cost her: she could always see the move from the other side of the table, see it clean and admire its economy, even when the move was the one that finished her.

Soto stood. He was nineteen, she thought, perhaps twenty, the age she had been when she signed her first deal *for the house* in her father's private black book under his hand, and she looked at him and thought, without sentiment, that he had given his late boyhood to a thing that was ending, the way the diggers had given their lungs and the birds were giving their lives, all of it poured into a column that the warm water was now striking through with one long stroke, and that he at least would walk away breathing, which was more than most of the people the white had used could say.

"There's the matter of the gangs," he said.

"What gangs. There are no gangs. There is no one to dig for, Soto. The cargo no one buys does not want digging."

"On the leased island. Your grandfather's — Señora Carmen's island, the Pisco one. There are men on it. The last contract gang, the dregs of the dregs, the ones the company put off there two seasons back to scrape the last of it, and the wages have not been paid since the spring, because the consignment that paid them has stopped paying, and they are —" he stopped. "They are still digging. They have not been told it's worth nothing. There is no one on the rock to tell them, and the boat that brings the rice has not gone out in a month, and they are still cutting the dregs and barrowing it to the chute against a cargo that will never be loaded, for wages no one will ever pay, because no one has gone out to tell them to stop."

Inés sat very still. She thought of the figure on the board, the chalk stroke, *no sale offered*, and she thought of men on a bare rock thirteen miles off Pisco cutting a worthless white off a worthless stone into a worthless heap, by the barrow, by the body, in the same caustic air that had taken the sight from Mei's eye and put the cough in the lungs that crossed into her father and into Carmen, all of it being done at this exact hour for a thing that the slate behind her at the harbour declared had no worth at all, by men who had not been told, because telling them required someone to make a voyage no one would now pay for. It was the cleanest figure the trade had ever shown her. It was the whole of it in one line. The cost was still being paid, in full, in breath, on the far side of a sum that had already come out to nothing.

"Someone must go and pay them off," she said.

"There is nothing to pay them with."

"Then someone must go and tell them, and bring them off, before the rice does not come a second month and they begin to do what the men on these islands have always done when the rice stops." She knew what they did. Everyone in this trade knew what they did; it was in the company's old logs as *one short, close up*; it was the first notch on the stick that had come to Carmen. "Send word to my niece. It is her island and her gang and she will want to be the one. And tell the boat's master I will stand the freight myself, out of what is mine and not the house's, before I let it be said a Penhale left a gang on a rock to starve for a cargo we knew was worth nothing." She heard herself say it and could not have told whether it was decency or only the last reflex of a person who had built a whole self around squaring the column, around leaving no figure unaccounted, even now, especially now, when the only figures left were the ones that could never be paid.

Soto went out. She sat alone with the shut case and the squared letter and the chalk stroke standing in her mind, and she found that her hand had gone to the cabinet without her telling it to, to the small framed thing she kept there, that her father had given her the year she came into her power and that she had never decided whether she kept out of love or out of the same instinct that made the diggers keep the contract paper they could not read — the *Petrel*'s manifest, the first one, his first guano bill of lading, 1849, a hundred and forty tons of the white at nine pounds the ton, the figure at the top of it the seed of the whole forty years, the figure that had grown and grown and compounded into the gilded house and the ships and the seat at the table she had fought a life to be given. There was a fine white dust ground into the fold of it, into the creases, that had been there as long as she had owned it and that she had taken, when she was younger, for the dust of age. It was not the dust of age. It was the white. It was a little of the cargo itself, gone into the paper that recorded it the way it had gone into the lungs that breathed it, the ledger and the body keeping the same account in the same substance, and she sat in the ruined counting-room and held the first manifest in her two hands and did the sum that joined the figure at its top to the figure on the board at the harbour, nine pounds to a chalk stroke, the whole rise and the whole fall, and she did not weep, because weeping was a position she could not defend, but she sat for a long time with the dust of it on her gloves and understood that she had been the best reader of the board the trade ever produced and that it had not saved a single thing, not the fortune, not her father, not the years she had spent being right, not the men still cutting the dregs at this hour off Pisco for a stroke of chalk, and that the only ledger she had never been able to make come out — the wanting to be the one her father valued — was the one that had cost the most and would never now be paid either, because the man who held it was dying across the city of the cough, denying the bottom, and she had built her whole life to be weighed by him and the scale had gone to bare rock with her still standing on it, waiting to be told what she was worth.

Out past the curve of the world the warm water lay over the cold, and the shoals were down in the dark, and the birds went out over the empty sea and came back over the empty sea and came down on the beaches in their tens of thousands, and the white stood at a chalk stroke, and on a rock off Pisco the last gang barrowed the worthless dregs to the chute against a cargo that would never be loaded, and did not know, and no one had yet gone out to tell them.

She put the manifest back in the cabinet, and squared the letter, and rang for the boat to be made ready.


## MANIFEST V — The Collapse

### Chapter 27 · Liddell Penhale
*Lima, the Penhale house · 1878 · guano unsellable, no figure on the board · Chincha working face ≈ bare rock*

---

He woke before light with the smell of the money in his throat, and for a while he did not know which year it was, and was content.

It came in across the dark the way it had come in across the water that first morning off the islands, an hour before the lookout called land — ammonia and rotted fish and the sweetish mineral thing underneath — and Liddell Penhale lay still in the bed in the high room above the plaza and read it the way he had read it for forty years: not what it was, but what it was worth. A man who could still smell the cargo was a man with cargo to sell. He lay and breathed it and felt the old arithmetic begin to open up in him the way a hold opens with the hatches off and the lamp held down inside, the price and the tonnage and the queue of ships, and it was a comfort, the figures coming, because the figures had always come, and a man to whom the figures came was a man on the buying side of the scale.

Then he drew the breath all the way in to do the sum, and the breath caught.

It caught low, on a sharpened edge, the way a line catches on a cleat when the load runs out too fast, and it dragged a cough up out of him before he had consented to it, and the cough went on longer than a cough should, and bent him in the bed, and when it was done he lay with his eyes shut and his big hand pressed flat to the front of his shirt and understood that the smell was not on any wind. There was no cargo coming across the water. The window was shut against the night air on the doctor's order and the room was four walls of a house in Lima a hundred miles and twenty-four years from the islands, and the smell that he had woken to was coming up out of his own chest, off the cloth he had coughed into, a fine grey-white that he did not need the light to know was there.

He had smelled the money his whole life from the outside. It had got in.

He got the handkerchief up to his mouth and held it there and waited the next one out. The same handkerchief. He knew it by the weight of it and the worn place at the corner where his thumb went; it was the one he had counted coin into across a thousand mornings on the islands and in the counting-rooms, drawing the sovereigns across the table and pinching them into the kerchief by tens and twenties, and the white had got into the weave of it years ago, the dust of the cargo ground down into the cloth the way it ground into everything, into the food and the rigging and the seams of a man's hands. He had taken it, those years, for the dust of money. He brought it down now in the grey before-dawn and could not see the colour and did not need to. There was the old white in the weave and there was a fresh wet white that was his, and they were the same white, and he lay in the bed and was, for the length of one held breath, very precisely afraid.

A line came up in him then, unbidden, in his father's drink-thickened voice off a chapel bench forty years gone — *we are but strangers here, we do not crave a home* — and he heard it surface and was angry, because it only ever came up when he was afraid, and he had spent his whole life not being afraid, and the hymn coming told him a thing about himself that the arithmetic would not. He crushed it. He thought about the price instead. He could not get the price. He reached for it the way a man reaches in the dark for a tool he has set down a thousand times in the same place, and his hand closed on nothing, because there was no price; the board at the harbour carried a chalk stroke where the number had stood; and the reaching itself frightened him worse than the cough had, so he stopped reaching, and made himself think instead about the mountain, which did not run out, and the chest went quiet, and he slept.

*

When he woke again it was full day and Carmen was in the room.

She was at the window with the shutter cracked the width of a hand, standing the way she stood, very still, looking down into the plaza, and he watched her a while before she knew he was awake. Twenty-one this year. He did the figure without meaning to, the way he did every figure; born the year he priced the floor of the mountain on the cut face with a lantern, Daniel's girl, A-Lian's girl, the girl who had come to him at nine with the foreign comb in her fist and a cough already in her that he had heard the first night and logged and said nothing of, because there was nothing to be done with a thing once it was in the lungs, you could only mark it, the way you marked a strained plank — kept, with a fault, watch it. She had grown up between his house and the Chinaman's. She had his own weighing eye; he had seen it on her since she was small, the way she looked at a thing and stripped it to its worth, and it had pleased him, that eye, the way a sound tool pleased him, and it was the thing in her he had never been able to buy or bend, because she weighed *him*, and got a figure he did not like, and would not tell him what it was.

"There's my best cargo," he said, to hear his own voice come out level, and it did, mostly.

She turned from the window. "You were coughing in the night," she said. "Maria heard you through the wall."

"A man my age coughs."

"You were coughing for an hour."

He let that lie where she put it. She came across and looked at him with the flat assessing look that was his own look given back, and he saw her go down the inventory of him — the colour of him, the way the breath sat in him, the cloth folded on the night-table that he had not had the strength to hide — and he saw her reach the bottom of the column and not say the figure, and he respected her for it the way he had never been able to respect Inés for the opposite, for always saying the figure. The two of them. His daughter who said the bottom and his ward who held it. He had built the one and the other had built herself, and between them they had read him out to the last decimal, and there was nothing he could do about either of them now from a bed.

"What's the day," he said.

"Tuesday."

"The board."

She did not answer at once. She went and squared the cloth on the night-table, a small unnecessary motion, ordering a thing that did not need ordering, and he knew the gesture because it was Inés's gesture and it meant the answer was bad and the answerer was deciding how to give it. "There's no figure on the board," she said. "There hasn't been a figure on the board for guano in three weeks. There's no buyer to make one."

"Then it'll come back when there's a buyer."

"Grandfather."

"It always comes back," he said. "I have seen it down. Forty-nine it was at nine and they said it would not hold nine. Sixty-four the war had it where no man could get a ton off the rock and it went to thirteen, *up*, mind, up, because the thing was wanted and a wanted thing finds its level. It is at a stroke of chalk because the Frenchman has stopped his remittances and the government has welshed and every house from here to Liverpool is sitting on its hands waiting for the next man to bid first. Let one of them lose his nerve and bid and you will see your figure back on the board before the month is out." He had got some wind behind it by the end and the wind cost him; he felt the catch waiting at the bottom of it and stopped short of the bottom, where the cough lived. "It is a panic. I have seen panics. The mountain doesn't run out."

She stood and looked at him.

"You know it runs out," she said. Quietly. Not unkind. The way you say a true thing to a man you are not trying to win against. "You priced the floor of it in fifty-four. Uncle told me. On the cut face with a lantern. You knew to the season how many years it had in it, and you told no one, and you dug faster." She let that sit. "You've always known it runs out."

He had a number of things he might have said to that. He might have said the Chinaman was a liar, except Mei had been a great many things and never once a liar; the truth was the only currency the man had ever fully trusted, and he had paid it out in full, to the end, behind the cathedral, dying, with the bare count in his hands. He might have said the girl had it wrong, except she did not have anything wrong; he had taught her the trade himself, by example, by never once pretending a figure was other than it was — except this figure, this one figure, which he had carried alone for twenty-four years and meant to carry into the ground. He looked at her, and the cough came up and took the saying out of his mouth and bent him over the kerchief, and when it let him go he lay back and breathed shallow and even, the way he had watched Mei breathe in the last years, going still to keep the air from finding the raw place, and he understood that he had learned that from the man too, the way you go still to live a little longer, and he hated that he had learned it from a digger.

"Open the shutter the rest of the way," he said. "Let me see the day."

She opened it.

*

The light came in hard and white and for a moment it was the islands.

It was the white of the cliff at the high face the first morning, bone-white, salt-white, two hundred feet of it standing up out of a sea the colour of pewter, the white that threw the sun back at a man off a deck and gave the hands snow-blindness in a flat calm — and he was on the deck of the *Petrel* in his shirt with the smell of the fortune in his throat and the figures opening up in front of him, nine pounds the ton and climbing and eleven hundred ships a year if there were berths, and the size of it stopped his breath, not the stink, the size. He had taken hold of the rail. He remembered taking hold of the rail. And over the islands and out to seaward the air had gone solid and dark with the birds, a river of them pouring off the cliffs and bending out over the water in a column that did not end, going up and out and down to where the sea was darker still with the shoal they were after, every bird in it diving and rising and pouring back, and the noise of it had come across the water over the slap of the swell, a high continuous roar like surf in a cave, the sound of a number too large to be a sound.

A thousand years of birds, the Bristol fool had said, and said it the way a man says a thing he finds quaint. And Penhale had looked at the pouring dark and understood at once that he was looking at the cargo in an earlier form, still aloft, that the birds were not in the way of the fortune, the birds *were* the fortune, dropping it out of the sky a speck at a time over more years than there had been a Cornwall, and that the dead weight of all that nothing, dried white and stacked into the sky, would be worth more by the ton than anything ever hauled off a Cornish rock in the whole history of theft.

He had been right about that. He wanted that entered, wherever such things were entered. He had been *right*. The smell had been money and he had bottled it and the figure at the top of the first manifest — a hundred and forty tons of the white at nine pounds the ton, the *Petrel*, larboard side, the season of forty-nine — that figure had grown and grown and compounded across forty years into this house and the ships and the seat in London and the girl at the window who could read a board, and a man who builds a thing that large out of bird dung and his own two hands and a clear eye for worth is not a man who was *wrong*.

The birds had thinned. He knew they had thinned; he was not a fool and had never been a fool; the German with the bird-as-crop paper had stood up at the English Club years back and made his count and made his point, and Penhale had priced the point at a glance and the point was *cut faster*, and he had been right about that too, because the point of a mountain that is running out is to get the most of it down to the water before the next man does, not to leave it for the birds to sit on. The flocks were down now to nothing off the islands. Carmen's boy — the other one, the Chinaman's son, Asher, with his notebook — had a figure for it, near zero, the sky over the rock gone empty for the first time in living memory, and the beaches under the islands lined with the dead birds come ashore in their thousands where the warm water had taken the fish out from under them. He knew all of it. He had let none of it touch the one figure he kept, because the bird-count was the bird-count and the mountain was the mountain, and the mountain did not run out, and if he held that — if he held only that — then the white on the board was a panic and the white on his handkerchief was an old man's cough and the white he was looking at in the window was the high face of the islands in forty-nine with the fortune pouring off the cliffs, and he was thirty-one and on the buying side of the scale with his whole life in front of him.

He held it. It was the last load-bearing wall of him and he set his back against it.

"Get the manifest," he said.

Carmen turned from the window. "Which manifest."

"The first. The *Petrel*. Inés has it framed in the counting-room; she keeps it by the cabinet. Send for it."

"Grandfather, the counting-room's —" She stopped. He saw her decide not to say what the counting-room was now; that the rooms off the plaza that the island money had paid for were full of a creditor's men three days a week going through the cabinets; that Inés sat among them with the books by heart and let them look; that the framed first bill of lading was very possibly already entered on some Frenchman's inventory of the estate, *one bill of lading, framed, of no value*. "I'll send Maria," she said.

"Send her."

*

It came up to the room in the early afternoon, the glass cold from the lower house, and Carmen put it in his two hands.

He had not held it in years. Inés held it; it was Inés's by long practice, the way the whole apparatus had become Inés's while he sat in the chair and pretended to oversee a thing she ran better than he ever had; and he had given it to her himself the year she came into her power, not as a gift, he did not give gifts, but as evidence, the way you hand a man the deed so he understands what he is standing on. He held it now and tipped it to the white light from the window and read it, and his eye, which had gone soft for a printed page these last two years, found the figures sharp, because the figures were the last thing in him that did not fail.

*Petrel. Of Bristol. Chincha Norte. One hundred and forty tons guano. £9 the long ton.* His own hand at the foot of it, younger, harder, the down-strokes driven deep. He had stood on the beach with his account-book under his arm and the smell closing over him entire and written it, and somewhere up the white cliff a barrow had tipped and a plume of the fortune had come down the chute and slid past him to the waiting boat, and he had begun, in the one room of his head where no hymn could reach, to count the mountain down. A hundred and forty tons at nine pound. Twelve hundred and sixty pounds, the first cargo, less the freight and the inspector's cut and the consignee's third. He had it still, the figure, clean, after twenty-nine years; he could have done the freight in his head this minute with the breath he had left.

There was a fine white dust ground into the fold of the frame where the paper met the glass.

He saw it because the cold glass had brought a little damp up out of the air and the damp had wet the dust and it had bloomed, a faint pale smear in the crease, and he looked at it. He knew that dust. He had taken it, when he was younger and the manifest hung where he could see it, for the dust of age, the grey that gets into an old paper. It was not the dust of age. It was the white. It was a little of the cargo itself, a few grains of the hundred and forty tons, gone into the paper that recorded it the way the rest of it had gone into the lungs that breathed it and the cloth he had counted the proceeds into and was coughing into now; and he lay in the bed with the first manifest in his two hands and the fresh wet white drying on the kerchief at his throat, and the two whites were a hand's span apart, the one in the fold of the paper and the one on the cloth, and they were the same white.

He did the sum. He could not help doing the sum; it was the one thing the body would not take from him; and the sum was very short and had nothing in it. Nine pounds the ton at the top of the paper in his hand. A stroke of chalk on the board at the harbour. The whole rise and the whole fall, the *Petrel* to the stroke, and in the fold of the one and in the weave of the other the same few grains of the same white, the ledger and the chest keeping the one account in the one substance — and the figure the account came out to, when a man held both ends of it at once in a bed in Lima with the breath catching at the bottom of every draught, the figure was that he had not bought the white. He had breathed it. Every season, forty of them, he had stood on the buying side of the scale with the pen in his hand and the thumb under the chin, and the white had stood on the other side and weighed *him*, by the lungful, a few grains a day into the cloth and the chest, and had found him the same as it found the diggers, only slower, only later, only richer on paper, and was logging him out now the same as it had logged out A-Lian who coughed it up at twenty-seven and the Chinaman who coughed it up at forty-seven, the same beam, the same iron, the man with the book entered in his own book at the last among the cargo.

He felt the shape of it go through him, the thing he had no entry for, the thing the line of men on the cliff had put in his chest that first morning ahead of his consent, that he had felt once before exactly, thirty-one years gone come Michaelmas, when he was eleven and stood in a line on a deck and a man with a book walked down it and put his thumb under each chin and tipped the face to the light to look at the teeth and the eyes and said a figure to the man beside him who wrote it down. He had understood, at eleven, with a clarity that had never left him and had made him rich, that he had a price. That he was an entry. That a sum had been done with him in it. And he had spent fifty years learning to be the man with the book so that it could never be done to him again, holding the pen and not the teeth, doing the appraising and never being the thing appraised — and here at the end the white had done it anyway, quietly, a few grains at a time, while he stood on the deck he thought was safe, and had appraised him, and found him soft, and logged him.

If he let that in, it was true.

If he let that in, then he had not built the fortune; he had spent a thing that ended, a barrow at a time, a lung at a time, his own among them, and called the proceeds a fortune, and there was no fortune, there had never been a fortune, there had been a mountain of dead birds that a clear-eyed man mistook for stone and sold off to bare rock while the live ones that made it died of being disturbed and starved, and the proof of it was a stroke of chalk and three coffins and the white drying on his own throat. A man could not survive that. A man who had built every stone of himself out of one terror at eleven years old — the terror of being the thing on the scale instead of the man who weighed it — could not, at the end, in a bed, take into himself the knowledge that he had been weighed wrong all along.

So he did not take it in.

He set his back against the wall, the last wall, and he held the manifest in his two hands and looked at the nine pounds the ton at the top of it, the seed of all of it, and he made himself think: *a hundred and forty tons. Twelve hundred and sixty pound. The first of how many hundred thousand.* He made the figure the floor under him and the figure held, the way it had always held, and the bottom did not open, and the white on the board went back to being a panic that would pass and the white on the cloth went back to being an old man's cough and the white in the fold of the frame went back to being the dust of age. The mountain did not run out. He held only that. He held it with the last of the strength he had, the way a man holds a rail under a thing larger than himself, and the hymn came up under it — *we are but strangers here, we do not crave a home* — and this time he did not crush it, because he had not the wind to crush it and reckon both, and he let it come, all of it, the whole verse off the chapel bench, his father's voice and his own, *Heaven is our home* — and it was the only home in the song that did not run out, the only country in it that a man could not be sold off of by the pound, and he heard himself reach for it, the bottom of his life, the way the diggers reached east for their ancestors and the Chinaman had reached for his count, and he was afraid the whole length of it, and the figure held, and the breath caught at the bottom of the verse and did not come up again clean.

"Grandfather," Carmen said.

The manifest had gone over in his hands, the glass flat on his chest, the nine pounds the ton turned down toward the white that was drying there. She took it up off him. He felt her take it. He wanted to tell her the freight on a hundred and forty tons but the figure would not come out past the catch; he had it, he had it clean, he simply could not get the wind under it to say it; and that was the last thing that frightened him, that the figure was there and the breath was not, that the account was perfect and there was no longer any way to read it out, and he understood with the part of him that had always done the cold sum that this was the bottom he had priced in fifty-four arriving at last, only it was not the mountain's bottom, it was his, the marks run all the way down to bare rock, the load over the line, the hull taking water past the scale painted up her side.

She had her hand flat on his chest where his own had been, over the white on the cloth, and she was saying something, his name, or the boy's name to send for the doctor, and he could not get the wind to tell her not to spend the money on a doctor, that a thing once it was in the lungs could only be marked, kept with a fault, that he had marked it himself the first night she came to him with the comb in her fist and known there was nothing to be done. He got his hand up and got it onto hers, the big weathered hand over the girl's, and held it there on his chest, on the white, and he felt the manacle scar pull at his wrist the way it pulled when prices were mentioned, the badly-healed place from the deck where the iron had been, the first entry ever made in him, and he rubbed his thumb against her knuckle the way he rubbed the scar, once, twice, an old motion, the only tenderness in his whole life he had never first converted into worth, and could not now, because there was no longer time to do the conversion, and so it stood, bare, unpriced, his hand on hers on the white on his chest, and he held it, and he held the figure, and he held the home in the song that did not run out, and the white light came in off the window hard as the cliff at the high face, and somewhere up it a barrow tipped, and a plume of the fortune came down the chute toward the waiting boat, and he reached for the rail.

*

Carmen sat a long while after with the first manifest in her lap, glass-side down, before she turned it over.

The dust had bloomed in the fold where the cold had wet it, a pale smear in the crease, and there was a fresh fleck of the same white on the glass over the figure where his breath had carried it up from the cloth in the last hour. She did not wipe it. She held the paper and read the nine pounds the ton at the top of it in the hard white light and thought about the stroke of chalk on the board at the harbour that was the other end of the same line, and she did the sum that joined the two figures, nine pounds to nothing, the whole rise and the whole fall, the way she had been doing sums no one asked her to do since she was twelve. It came out the way it always came out now. It came out to the white in the fold of the paper and the white on the glass and the white drying on the cloth at the throat of the man in the bed, the same grains, ground into the ledger and into the lung, two records of the one thing, and to the fact that the one record could be framed and hung and entered on a Frenchman's inventory of the estate at *no value*, and the other could not be falsified, and was finished now, and had carried the account to the end in a body the way it had carried it in A-Lian and in her uncle, three coffins of the same white, and would carry it next, she knew, by a few grains a year, in her own chest, which had begun its catch at six in a sea-green dress held up to the high face to see what built her dresses, and would carry it after that — the cough was the one thing in the whole estate that came down clean, generation to generation, owing nothing, hiding nothing, keeping the true count when every other book lied.

She set the manifest on the night-table and squared it to the edge, the glass-side up, the nine pounds the ton turned to the light, and she put two fingers to the front of her own chest and breathed in slow and even and shallow, the way she had watched both old men learn to breathe at the end, to keep the air from finding the raw place, and she felt the small catch waiting at the bottom of the draught where it had been waiting for fifteen years, and she did not cough, because there was no one left in the room to hear it but the dead man, and she had the count to get down before the doctor came, and the room above the two to keep, and the thing he had said over the board to carry now whether she believed it or not, because it was hers now, the whole of it, the leases and the ledger and the comb and the three sticks and the cough and the one figure he had carried alone for twenty-four years into the ground — *the mountain doesn't run out* — and she sat with it in the white light and knew, the way she had known since she was thirteen on the Macabí face, that the sum could not be done, and that someone had to refuse to lie that it had been paid, and that the someone was her, and that there was no one now above her in the house to do it instead.


## MANIFEST V — The Collapse

### Chapter 28 · Wei Ah-Sip
*A stripped island off the Pisco coast · 1878–79 · guano unsellable, no figure asked at the chute · working face ≈ bare rock*

---

The paper they pressed into his hand on the Callao mole had a number in it he could read, and the number was eight, and he had laughed at it, low, so the crimp would not hear, because he had paid the eight once already.

He had been eight years a digger and a cane-cutter and a man who scraped the last salt islands, eight years off a paper exactly like this one, sold out of Amoy at nineteen for three hundred pesos a head into a contract he could not read, and he had served the whole of it and come up alive at the end, which was a thing so few men did that the agents in Callao had no honest column for a freed man and treated him, when his time was out, as a clerical error walking around. He had meant to go home. He had stood on the Callao waterside at the close of his eight years with the worn paper folded in his shirt that said he was free, and he had found that the lane he came from had been sold for debt while he dug — he had this off a Fujian cook in a chophouse by the customs, the same way a man gets all the worst news, sideways, over food — and that there was no ship for a man with no money, and that a Chinese with an expired paper and empty hands was, on that mole, in that year, the cheapest thing for sale that had ever been free. The new crimp had not even bothered to lie well. *Eight years,* the paper said, the same eight, drawn up fresh by a man who knew the freed ones had nowhere to stand, and Wei Ah-Sip had set his thumb to it in the company's ink because the alternative was the harbour, where men with no paper at all were going off the mole at night into the green water, and he had a better use for himself than the harbour had. He always had. That was the curse of him and he knew it. A man who could always find a use for himself was a man the place could always find a use for.

So he came back. Forty-eight years old by the false reckoning the agents kept and older than that in the body, with the left lung a wet sack that pulled wrong on the cold mornings and the joints of his hands gone to knots from the bar, he came back onto a lighter at Callao in a gang of nine and was rowed out to a rock he had never worked, a small stripped island off the Pisco coast that the company had leased off a dead man's estate, and he stood up in the boat as it came under the cliff and read the cliff the way he read everything, by the count, and the count was wrong.

There was no white.

He had dug six islands and every one of them had stood up out of the sea white, bone-white, salt-white, a hundred feet of it or eighty or forty at the worst of the scraped ones, the cargo itself stacked into the shape of a hill so that a man could read the years left in it off the face like a draught-mark. This one was red. It was the red of the bare rock under, the iron-stained stone the white sat on, and the white was a skin on it now, a crust no thicker than a roof-tile in the deepest pockets and gone entirely off the high shoulders where the wind got it, and the high marks of the old loadings stood stranded up the bare red stone in faint pale lines, tide-lines, the rings of a felled tree, twelve and fifteen and twenty feet up a cliff that had nothing left under the lines to give. He stood in the boat and read it and understood, before his foot was on the rock, the thing the crimp had not told him and the company would not: that he had been brought to dig a mountain that was already spent. He filed it. It went where everything went, into the place behind his face where he kept the count, and the count of this island was a thing he could do in one look, which he had never been able to do with an island before, and the doing of it told him the worth of his own eight years to the men who held the paper, which was that they had spent the white and would now spend the men on the dregs of it, by the barrow, the same as ever, because a man cost less than a ton and always had.

*

The first morning at the face taught him the second wrong thing, after the rock, and the second wrong thing was the air.

He knew the air. He had been given his blindness in instalments on the first islands, a hand's breadth a season, the eyes that wept and then crusted, the throat that took the scald and would not swallow it down, the cough the air called up out of a man like a creditor calling a payment he had not known he owed; he had paid all of that, years back, on the white, and he had thought of the cough and the half-clouded eye as a tax already settled, the way a man thinks of a scar. But the air on the dregs was not the air on the white. The white had burned. The white was alive with the ammonia that was the smell men called money, a clean caustic burn that took the eyes and the throat and the lungs but smelled, under it all, of the thing that was worth a fortune across the sea. This air did not burn. This air was sweet. It came off the worked rock thick and sweetish and rotten, the smell that had always been there under the money smell, the one the dry cold air had held down for a thousand years and that the warm wet wind of this bad season now let up off the stone in a fog you could taste, and Wei Ah-Sip stood at the bare red face with the bar in his knotted hands on the first morning and breathed it and knew it for what it was the way he had known the rock. It was the smell of a thing that had stopped. The white that burned was a thing being made, still, somewhere, by the birds, dropped down out of the sky a grain at a time the way it had been dropped for longer than there had been an Amoy. The white that rotted was a thing finished. He had smelled the one for eight years and called it the worst smell in the world. He breathed the other now and would have given an eye, the bad one, to have the burn back, because the burn had been money, and a thing that smelled of money was a thing with money in it, and this rotted and was sweet and had nothing in it at all, and they were going to work the gang to death getting it down to the water just the same.

He made his weight. He had always made his weight. He cut the crust off the red stone with the bar and shovelled it into the barrow and ran the barrow along the bare worked rock to the chute, and the work was worse than the white had ever been, because the crust came up in flakes and powder and not in blocks, so that a man cut three times as long for a barrow's load, and the dregs had to be carried twice the distance, the cliff-edge stripped so far back that the chute-head stood a stone's throw from the face across a flat of bare iron rock that rang under the barrow-wheel, and the company's quota did not know any of that. The quota was a number on the rice-boat. It came off the same paper the eight had come off, and it asked of a man on bare rock the weight a man had given on a hundred feet of soft cargo, and it docked the rice of the man who could not, by the book, and the overseer who ran the scale on this island was a hard sunburnt Peruvian who could not hold a sum over a long forenoon and lost the thread of his column when he was spoken to and cursed and started again from the top. Wei Ah-Sip saw it the second day. He saw it the way he had seen everything since he was nineteen, a long time ago, on a different island, off a different overseer, and he did with it what he had done then, which was nothing, yet, only filed it: *that one cannot keep a count, and a man who cannot keep a count needs one who can, and needing is the one hunger on a rock that a barrow can feed and live.* He had got off the face once before in his life by being the man who could keep the count. He did not know yet whether there was a face left to get off of, on a rock this bare, or whether the keeping of the count here would only make him, again, the clerk of the killing — the man who sat at the high shelf above the flood and subtracted the night's dead out of the morning's roll before the first barrow rolled, with his own hand, on the overseer's order. He thought there was nothing left to be promoted into. He thought the rock was too far gone for the company to bother keeping books on the men who died scraping it. He was wrong about that, the way a man is wrong who still believes the place has a bottom of cruelty it will not go below. The place always had a lower shelf.

*

He kept his own count from the first night. He could not have said why; it was not hope, hope was for men who believed the waiting would end in a thing; it was only that a man who has kept a count for thirty years cannot stop, the way a man cannot stop the small steady tremor in his hands, and the count was the one wall he had ever found to lean the days against, even a wall he knew was painted on a cliff.

He had no stick. He had nothing of his own but the worn first paper, the true one, the freed-man's discharge that the agents treated as a clerical error, folded in his shirt against his ribs where the new false eight rode with it, the two papers together, the one that said he had paid and the one that said he had not, and he kept his count the only way the rock left him, in his head and on the inside of the false paper, where there was a margin of clean white, with the point of a nail he had bent and hidden in the seam of his trousers. He cut the count into the paper the way another man, a long time ago, on a richer island, had cut it into a stick, though Wei Ah-Sip did not know that man and never would, did not know that the rock he scraped had been bought at the very top of the trade by an old Amoy digger who had owned it for one foundered year and died of the same lung, and that the count Wei Ah-Sip now kept in nail-scratches on the inside of his contract was the same count, begun again, the third or fourth or fortieth time the place had taught a man to keep it. He did not know the count had a history. He knew only the law of it, which was the same law the other man had made without ever speaking it: that the count was for the dead he had seen. Not the dead he supposed. He did not scratch the paper for the men he heard had gone over in the night, because a man could not keep an honest count of what he had not watched leave; he scratched it for the ones he carried down the slope afterward, or counted out of the morning's roll at the chute-head when the overseer's column broke, the ones he could put a name to or, where there was no name, a village and a trade and a way of standing — *the Swatow man, a fisher, two fingers gone off the left hand; the boy from the same boat as me, a basket-weaver's son, would not eat the third day and was dead the fifth; the old one nobody knew, who carved a kitchen-god out of the rice-soap and prayed to it and went off the cliff at the new moon, clean, four steps, the way the sea took men who had decided about the day.*

He scratched that one deep. He had watched it. The old man had stood a long moment at the very edge of the bare red cliff with the surf working below and looked out at the open water and at the sky, and Wei Ah-Sip, running the barrow along the high flat, had looked where the old man looked, and that was the third wrong thing, the worst one, the one he had been not-looking at since the first morning the way he had taught himself, years ago, not to look at the women's line on the beach, because a man cannot carry the rock and a thing like that both and live.

He let himself look now, because the old man was looking, and a man owed the dying that much, to see what they saw before they went.

The sky was empty.

*

*There is no bird over the rock at dawn.*

*There has not been, this is the first morning, and the men who have worked these islands a lifetime stand at the bare face and feel the wrongness of it before they can name it, the way a man feels a silence in a house where a clock has always ticked. For as long as there has been a man on these rocks, and longer, since before there was a man, the dawn over the islands has been the birds: a dark river of them pouring off the cliffs at first light and bending out over the water to the shoal, a column that did not end, going up and out and down to where the sea was dark with the fish, and the noise of it coming across the water over the surf, a high continuous roar like surf in a cave, the sound of a number too large to be a sound. Twenty million of them over these three islands when the first ships came; twelve, when the boy who counts them was a boy; six; three; and the warm water has taken the rest, the cold green upwelling gone warm by a degree and a half so the shoal went down into a dark the wing cannot follow, and the birds went out over an empty sea and came back over an empty sea and did not feed, and went out, and came back, and came down at last along the beaches in a tide where the anchovy used to come ashore. The beaches were lined with them a season ago. Now even the dead are gone, picked by the few that are left and washed off by the surf, and the rock is bare and the sea is bare and the sky over both is the one thing no man here has ever seen it be. It is empty. The dawn comes up grey behind the mainland and the dark shoulder of the Andes a hundred miles inland and breaks over the islands and there is nothing in it, no river off the cliffs, no column, no roar; only the light coming up on the bare red stone and the flat empty water and the small wet sounds of the surf, which a man can hear now, every separate slap of it, because there is nothing over it any longer to drown it out. There is no bird over the rock at dawn. The silence is the loudest thing on the islands, and there is no one to keep its count, except that one of the diggers, an old man at the face with a bent nail, has begun, this morning, to scratch the empty sky into the margin of a paper he cannot read, with a mark that is not a number, because there is no number left to cut: a single short stroke for the nothing, the way the company's slate carries a single short stroke where the price of the white once stood.*

---

He did not scratch the sky in the end. He started to, and stopped, because the nail wanted saving and the count was for the dead, not for the air, and the empty sky was not a thing that had died that he had watched leave; it was a thing that had simply stopped being there, like the white on the cliff, like the burn in the air, like the worth of the cargo, all of it gone the way a tide goes out around a stake driven in the shallows, so slow you swear each morning it stands where it stood, until the day you come down and the stake is gone and the water stands where the stake had been, and you cannot say which tide did it.

The old man went off the cliff at the new moon and Wei Ah-Sip carried that count alone, because there was no overseer's column for it now; the overseer had stopped keeping one. That was the lower shelf he had been wrong about. On the fourth day a different thing had happened than the thing that happened on the white, where the company sent a clerk and a fresh quota on every rice-boat and bothered to count the men because the men were dear and the cargo was worth a war. No clerk came to this rock. The rice-boat that should have come on the second week did not come on the second week, and when it came at last it came late and short, and the man who brought it would not look at the gang, and he carried no fresh quota and no new contracts and no clerk, and Wei Ah-Sip, who could read a boat-master's face the way he read a cliff, stood on the chute-head landing and read this one and understood the whole of it at last, the rock and the air and the smell and the empty sky and the short late boat all resolved into one figure, the way the price and the water and the dead birds had resolved on a slate at Callao into a single chalk stroke he would never see. The company had stopped counting because the company had stopped selling. He was digging a cargo no one would buy. The chute ran the dregs down into a lighter that ferried them out to a barque at anchor, the way it always had, and the barque lay there week on week and did not fill and did not sail, because there was no port that wanted her load, no figure on any board to put against her tonnage, no eleven pounds and no nine and no six, nothing; and the men of the gang who could not read a boat-master's face dug on in the belief, which the company let them keep because it cost the company nothing, that the white at the bottom of the world was still worth a fortune across the sea, and that the digging of it was therefore a thing that meant something, a labour that fed a trade, the same labour that had fed it for thirty years and killed the men who did it, except that now it fed nothing, and killed them just the same.

He did not tell them. He thought about it, at the face, in the rotted sweet air, with the empty sky over him and the bent nail against his ribs: that he could go down the barracks-lane in the dark and say it, *they are not selling it, we are scraping a rock for nothing, the trade is dead and they have not told us because dead men cost less than fed ones,* and that it was the truest thing he knew, the figure all the other figures came out to. And he did not say it, and the not-saying was the one thing on the rock he chose, the only freedom the place had ever left him, and he weighed it the way he weighed everything. Because a man who knew it was for nothing would go off the cliff, the way the old one had, the way the men had gone off the white when the place took the last wall out from under them; and a man who did not know it, who believed each barrow fed a fortune, would make his weight in the morning and live the day, and Wei Ah-Sip had decided, a long time ago, on a different island, that he had a better use for himself than the cliff, and he made the same decision for the gang without their leave, the way the place had always made decisions for him without his, and he was not sure, scraping the red stone under the empty sky, whether the keeping of it from them was a mercy or only the last small corruption of a man who had survived everything by being useful, including this, the keeping of a hopeless gang at its work for a company that had already written them off. He could not weigh it. There was no unit for it. He knew only that he would keep his count, the dead he had seen, scratched into the margin of the paper that said he had not yet paid; and that no clerk would come for it; and that when his own hand could no longer hold the nail there would be no one to pass the count to on this rock, no stick, no granddaughter, no further entry, the count ending with the man who kept it the way the white had ended and the birds had ended and the price had ended, all of it run down to bare rock together, the dregs over the red sores of the stone, the empty water, the empty sky.

He made his weight. The barrow rang on the bare iron flat. Up the chute a lighter took the worthless white and carried it out to a barque that would not sail, and the dawn came up grey over the islands with nothing in it, and Wei Ah-Sip stood at the face where, thirty years before and a hundred miles north, on a richer rock under a sky black with twenty million birds, a younger man who would die owning an island had stood and cut the first notch of a count into the first stick — and he did not know that man, and the count went on anyway, begun again on bare rock in nail-scratches on a contract no one would ever read, the dead carried by a man no one would ever count, under a sky that was, for the first morning in the memory of anything that breathed there, silent.


## MANIFEST V — The Collapse

### Chapter 29 · Carmen Penhale-Mei
*Lima, the Callao counting-room, and the open Pacific to Mei's island · 1879 · guano unpriced — no figure on any board · working face = bare rock*

---

There was no figure to do the sum against, and Carmen, who had waited her whole life to do the sum, sat in the dead counting-room over the mole with both her grandfathers' books open in front of her and understood that the waiting had been the easy part.

She had thought, when she was twelve, that the thing standing between her and the truth was that no one would let her near the page where the carrying was done. She had thought, at seventeen, that the thing standing between her and the truth was that the price had only ever gone up, so that no one in the house had ever once had to find out what the white was worth on the day a man stopped wanting it. She had been wrong both times, and she knew it now the way you know a thing you have walked all the way around: the price had not gone up for five years, and it had not merely come down, it had gone out, the way a tide goes out around a stake driven in the shallows, gone past low and past the bare mud and off the edge of the slate entirely, so that the harbour-master's board down on the mole, the board she had stood under at seventeen and watched the twelve get a stroke chalked through it, no longer carried any figure for guano at all. It carried nitrate. It carried sugar, and cotton, and the Liverpool wire's quotation on bar silver. Where the white had stood for thirty years there was a clean rubbed place on the slate, and below it, in the harbour-master's idle chalk, the thing she had not been able to stop looking at when she came up the boards this morning: not a price, not even a zero, only a short horizontal stroke, the mark a clerk makes in a column where there is nothing to enter and the line must still be ruled.

She had the same stroke in front of her now, in three different hands.

She had the great house-of-Penhale ledger open to its last page, where Inés's writing had run out a year ago because there was nothing left to write — the last full entry a sale that had not cleared, a barque's load of Macabí dregs consigned to a Hamburg house that had gone bankrupt before the cargo rotted at anchor, the figure entered and then struck and then, in Inés's hand, the one word *protested*, which was the language of the bill of exchange and meant the money was never coming and everyone had known it. She had the little honest household book beside it, which she had kept since she was seventeen and which still balanced to the farthing, because the cook still had to be paid whether or not the white was worth anything, and the dresses still had to be made, except that there were no more dresses, and the cook had gone in the spring to a house that could pay her, and the last entries in Carmen's own hand were a column of small economies, the carriage sold, the second cook gone, the good front room shut up where her mother had died, the masses for Liddell paid for out of money that was not the house's because the house had no money, money lent against the only thing the house still held that a man in Lima would still lend against, which was the name, which was running out faster than the white had.

And she had the third book, which was not a book.

It lay on the desk where she had set it down when she came in, beside the two ledgers, in its tin box, and she had not opened the box yet, because she knew exactly what was in it and she was keeping it the way her great-uncle had taught her to keep a thing, close enough to hold, not close enough to burn. Three sticks of pale hard wood, the longest worn round and dark at the grip from forty years of a hand that was dead now, the second filling, the third hardly begun — two notches only, cut by a hand that had already started to shake, so that the second of the two ran a little crooked, a flaw she could find with her thumb in the dark and that she had decided, the night he gave it to her, she would never sand smooth, because a notch cut by a hand that shook was not a lie about the man; it was the truest thing on the stick. And under the three sticks, folded so old it had gone soft as cloth, the paper with the eight on it. And under that, the deed. Two leases, in the one folded packet — the Pisco-coast stump he had bought in seventy-two because the only freedom the rock ever taught him was to own the thing instead of be it, and the Chincha north lease he had bought in seventy-three off a dead man's estate at the very top of the price, like a man buying a horse already foundered to win one race. Both of them hers now. Both of them, this morning, by the figure that was not on any board, worth precisely the stroke the harbour-master had chalked where the price used to be.

She had come up here to do the sum and decide. That had been the whole of her, since she was twelve: she would learn what the fortune cost, in full, on both sides, in both tongues, and then she would decide, with the figures in her hand, whether to save the islands or sell them before the price reached the bottom. She had believed, more than she had believed anything, that the question had an answer and that the answer was a thing you arrived at by counting honestly enough.

She unfolded her own clean sheet, the way she had at twelve, the way she had at seventeen in the night with the lamp drawn close, and she ruled it down the middle, two columns, and she sat with the pen, ready at last to do the thing she had waited fifteen years to do, and found that there was nothing to put in either column.

That was the discovery. It was not the discovery she had spent fifteen years preparing for. She had prepared to find the cost unbearable. She had not prepared to find it spent.

*

Sam came at noon, as he had said he would, and he came up the stairs two at a time the way he had done everything since he was a boy, full of the body Lima had given him and the lungs Lima had given him, seventeen now and a head taller than she was and not coughing, never coughing, and she heard him on the stairs before he was in the room and had time, in the few seconds of his coming up, to put the cold flat look on her face that she had learned from the man who had owned the sticks, because she did not want her brother to see, before she had to say it, that she already knew what she was going to do.

He had a buyer.

He did not waste it on greeting.
