He had their father's directness and none of their father's bad luck about the date he was born into; he came in and pulled the other chair around and sat with his forearms on his knees and told her, fast, the way you tell a thing you have rehearsed against an argument, that there was a man — a Yankee, out of one of the houses that had been authorised under their own Guano Islands Act years back to grab any rock no nation had nailed its flag to — who was buying up lapsed Peruvian leases for the phosphate that was left in the bottom dregs, the stuff too poor to ship as guano but rich enough, ground and bagged dry, to sell into the Southern cotton states as a cheap manure, and that the man would take both of Mei's leases off their hands for a sum. Not a fortune. A sum. Real coin, American coin, against paper that every other man in Lima now treated the way you treated the protested bill in the ledger downstairs, as a thing that meant the money was never coming.

"How much," Carmen said. Because that was the first thing, always; she had it from Liddell; a price was a fact and you got the fact before you got anything else.

He told her. It was, against forty years and a hundred and thirty notches and two lives gone into the ground of it, a small number, and it was, against the stroke chalked on the board down on the mole, an enormous one, because it was a real number and the board had none. She sat and looked at it. She let it sit, the way she had been taught not to fill a count with talk.

"And the gang," she said.

He did not understand the question at first, which told her something about Sam she had always half-known and had never had to weigh until now: that he was not a bad man and not even a hard one, only a man the family had managed, by an accident of which year you were born in and which air you breathed, to raise clean. He was three when their mother died of the white. He had breathed Lima air his whole life and he did not carry it in his chest, and so he had never had to learn the second column, the one their great-uncle kept in wood, because the first column had never once tried to kill him.

"There is a gang on the Pisco island," she said. "Nine men. They came onto it last winter off the Callao line. They are scraping the dregs of a thing no one will buy, for a wage the house is two seasons behind on paying, and the rice-boat is short and late, and they do not know that there is no buyer, because no one has told them, because a man who knows it is for nothing goes off the cliff and a man who believes each barrow feeds a fortune makes his weight in the morning and lives the day." She had not known she would say this. She had it from the deed. She had it from the small careful accounting that had come up to the counting-room with the leases — the agent's report, three lines, in the matter-of-fact hand of a man who reported wastage — and she had read it the night Mei died and carried it the way she carried everything now, behind her eyes, where the figures lived. "If we sell to your Yankee, he will work them harder, because the phosphate's in the deep dregs and a man cuts three times as long for a barrow of it, and he will pay them off the books of a country that has gone bankrupt and stopped pretending to count Chinese, and they will die on it faster than they are dying now, and the sale will be clean, and we will walk away with coin, and it will be the last thing the house ever bought and sold, and it will be men."

Sam was quiet. He was not stupid; he had never been stupid; he was only undamaged, which in this family was nearly the same thing and far rarer.

"They're going to die anyway," he said at last. It was not cruelty. It was the truest thing he knew, the figure all his other figures came out to, the same figure their great-uncle had come out to on a different rock thirty years before — *a man who did not know it was for nothing made his weight in the morning and lived the day* — and Sam had arrived at it honestly, from love, because he wanted his sister to take the coin and be poor and breathing instead of rich on paper that was about to be worth nothing, and she knew it, and she could not fault the sum, because the sum was right.

"Yes," she said. "They are going to die anyway. That isn't the question."

"Then what's the question." And he said it without heat, leaning toward her, his good lungs even in the asking. "Carmen. There's no fortune to save. You've done the books. I've done the books, the rough of them, and you've done them down to the farthing the way you do, and they come out the same: the white's worth nothing, the leases are worth nothing, the name's nearly used up, and we owe more than the whole of it would fetch if it fetched anything, which it won't. There's one number on the table that's real, and it's the Yankee's, and it's small, and it's enough to get us out from under the paper and leave us poor instead of ruined. Inés sold three years ago and got out clean; she saw it before any of us and she was right. I'm asking you to do the thing she did. I'm asking you to do the thing the man who owned those sticks would tell you to do, because he told you himself — Sam wants to sell, and Mei said *Sam is right about the money* — you told me he said it. He said I was right."

"He did," Carmen said. "He was telling the truth. You are right about the money."

"Then sell."

She looked at her brother for a while. Outside, beyond the shut window, the mole went on being a working harbour for everything that was not guano — the nitrate barques loading their sewn dry sacks, the whistling stevedores with their bare faces, the cargoes that did not need short shifts and did not need the crew up in the tops to breathe and did not run out — and somewhere down there the harbour-master's chalk stroke sat in the rubbed place where the price had been, and somewhere a hundred and fifty miles south on a bare rock nine men were making their weight under an empty sky for a thing no one would buy, and she felt the dry catch start low in her own chest, the small one, the family's one, the one she had been swallowing in company since she was twelve, and she let it come once, into her wrist, and put it down before it could be more than the one, and she saw Sam's eyes go to it and then go away from it, the way Inés's had, the way everyone's did, because it was the one line of the account that no one in the house had ever known how to enter.

"I'm going out to the island," she said. "Before I answer the Yankee. I want to stand on it."

"There's nothing on it." Sam was on his feet now, and the impatience was love, she knew it was love. "There's nothing to stand on. It's bare rock. I've seen the agent's report, it's bare red rock with a skin of dregs on it and a sky with nothing in it. What is there to go and look at."

"That," Carmen said. "I want to go and look at that."

*

The boat she hired at Pisco was a small worn coaster with a Genoese master who took the leases' name without expression and named a price for the passage that told her the man knew exactly what the island was now and exactly what she was — a woman going out to look at a ruin she had inherited, which was a fare he had run before, in this bad year, more than once. She paid it. She had brought the tin box. She would not have been able to say why she brought the tin box on a boat to a bare rock except that the sticks were the one thing in the house that was not paper and did not lie, and she had promised the man who cut them that the count would not stop, and she found she could not go out to the place where the count began without carrying it.

She wore the comb. She had worn it since she was eight years old, in her hair, the plain dark foreign comb with the plum-flowers worn smooth, the one her mother had carried out of Amoy on the morning the broker took her and had pressed into Rosa's hand to give to Carmen *when she asks where she is from*, and Carmen had asked, and had been told, and had worn it for fourteen years over a head that read as one thing in Lima and another in Cornwall and as foreign in both, and out on the open water now, with the cold green sea running under the worn coaster and the wind off it coming clean and salt and empty, she put her hand up once and touched it, the way you touch a thing to know it is still there, and felt the small worn teeth of it, and thought about how it weighed less than the dust on a banknote and was the whole of a person's home.

The sea was wrong. She knew it was wrong because Asher had taught her to know it — Asher who had stood off the Chincha north island four years ago with a brass thermometer on a line and read the water two degrees too warm three mornings running, and had come back grey and said the cause was the sea now, not the cut, that the cut had killed the half of them years back but this was the water, and that it would not take eleven years to do what the water was going to do. The water had done it. She trailed her hand off the gunwale and the sea ran warm over it, warmer than the cold Humboldt green had any business being a hundred and fifty miles down a coast that the cold current was supposed to own, and she took her hand back in and did not say anything, because there was no one on the boat to say it to and because saying it changed nothing, and she watched the empty water go by, mile on mile of it, the surface of it bare, no dark river of birds pouring out from any cliff to any shoal, no column going up and out and down to where the sea was thick with fish, because the sea was not thick with fish, because the shoal had gone down into a warm dark the wing could not follow, and the birds had gone out over an empty sea and come back over an empty sea and not fed, and gone out, and not come back.

The island stood up out of the water in the late forenoon, and it was red.

She had been told it would be. She had had it from the agent's report and from Sam and from her own knowledge of the figures, the working face cut from a hundred and eighty feet of white the year her grandfather wrote the first manifest down to bare rock now, and she had thought, holding the figures, that she understood it. She had been doing the sum on this mountain since she was a child; she knew it the way she knew her own breath, that it had stood and that it had fallen, season on season, the only line in any of the books that had only ever gone one way. But the figures had not told her the color. The figures had given her the depth and the price and the years and they had not given her that the bare rock under the white was the red of old iron, ox-blood red, the red of a sore, and that the high marks of the old loadings stood stranded up the bare red stone in faint pale lines, the rings of a felled tree, twelve and fifteen and twenty feet up a cliff that had nothing left under the lines to give, and that the smell of it came off across the water before the boat was under the cliff and was not the smell she had breathed her whole life.

She had breathed the white. The white burned — she had it from a single afternoon when she was six, a sea-green dress, her father holding her up to the working face *to see what built her dresses*, the dust that opened in her at six and was in her chest still and would be in her chest until it killed her — the white burned, a clean caustic ammonia scald that took the eyes and the throat, and that smelled, under the burn, of the thing that was worth a fortune. This did not burn. This was sweet. It came off the worked rock thick and sweetish and rotten, the smell that had always been there under the money-smell, the one the dry cold air had held down for a thousand years and that the warm wet wind of this bad season let up off the stone in a fog you could taste, and she stood up in the boat as it came under the cliff and breathed it and knew it for what it was without anyone to tell her, the same way she had known, on the mole, that the chalk stroke was not a price. It was the smell of a thing that had stopped. The white that burned was a thing being made, still, somewhere, dropped down out of the sky a grain at a time. The white that rotted was a thing finished.

*

There were nine men on the island and she did not, at first, understand that they were working.

She came up the worn timber landing at the chute-head with the Genoese behind her carrying the tin box because she had let him, and she stood on the bare red flat that rang under her boot the way the agent's report had said it would ring, stone-floor flat where the cliff had been cut all the way back, and she watched the gang at the far face, nine small figures cutting a crust off red stone with bars, shovelling flakes and powder into barrows, running the barrows the long stone-throw across the rung flat to the chute, tipping, running the empty barrows back, and it was, exactly, the work — the work she had carried in her since she was twelve, the work the company's books were built to lose, the work her great-uncle had done at nineteen and that had taken the sight from his left eye and the breath from his chest, the work her mother had done at eleven, the barrow and the bar and the chute, going on, this morning, under a sky with nothing in it, for a cargo that was already, before it reached the chute, worth the stroke a clerk chalks in an empty column.

The overseer came over to her. He was a hard sunburnt Peruvian who could not, she would learn within the hour, hold a sum over a long forenoon, and he came over because a well-dressed woman off a hired boat with a man carrying a box was a thing that happened on this rock perhaps never, and he asked her business in the flat Spanish of the coast, and she gave him the only word that would mean anything to him, which was the word she had hated her whole life and that meant, on this rock, in this year, that she could do as she liked.

"I'm the owner," she said.

He took her to the gang because she asked to be taken. She did not have a reason she could have given Sam; she had only the thing Mei had given her instead of a reason, the count, and the rule of it, which was that the count was for the dead you had *seen*, not the dead you supposed, and that you could not keep an honest count of a man you had not watched leave. She had a hundred and thirty notches in a tin box and she had never once, in all her life, watched one of them happen. She had taken them on the wood, taken them on faith, taken them off an old man's shaking hand. She stood at the face now in the rotted sweet air with the comb in her hair and the box behind her and she looked, for the first time, at the living men whose deaths she had promised to keep, and she made herself look, because she owed the living that much before she went home and decided what to do about them.

They were nine. She did the thing she could not stop doing, the thing she had from both of them, from the one who counted worth and the one who counted men: she read them. A Swatow man with two fingers gone off his left hand, a fisher by the way he stood. A boy who could not have been older than Sam, narrow, with the burn-scar already up one forearm and the eye on that side beginning to weep and film, the eye he had already, without knowing he had done it, chosen to lose, standing a half-step crooked at the face the way her great-uncle had stood crooked at a face thirty years gone so the broken block would gust its breath to the side of the eye he meant to keep. An old man, nameless, who had carved a small kitchen-god out of a cake of the rice-soap and set it in a niche of the red rock at the face, the one who watches what the house eats and tells, and who prayed to it, low, in a dialect she did not have, and went back to his barrow. And the rest — five more, named to themselves and to each other and to no book, making their weight in the morning under the empty sky for the fortune they believed they were feeding.

She stood among them a while. The cough caught her — the working air, the rotted sweet stink of it, into a chest that had taken the white at six and never let it go — and it caught her hard, harder than the small dry one she swallowed in company, and she could not put it down, and she bent over it with her hand on her own knee the way her great-uncle had taught her without teaching her, the held breath that keeps the catch down, and she rode it, and the men at the face looked at her and then did not look at her, the way the diggers had not looked at her at Macabí when she was thirteen and wept in the scalding air, because a digger who could not afford to weep could not afford to watch someone do it, and she understood, bent over her own knee on the bare red rock, coughing the white she had breathed at six, that she had at last, after fifteen years, found the unit.

There was none. That was what the man with the sticks had told her in the two rooms behind the cathedral, and she had not believed him, not all the way, because to believe him was to give up the faith she had built her whole self on, that the sum could be done and finished and made to come out. *There is no unit,* he had said. *You cannot pay it back. You cannot even weigh it. You can only refuse to lie that it was paid.* And she had thought it was an old man's despair, the thing the islands left in a man instead of a future, and she stood on the islands now, the actual islands, the bare red sore of them, with the count in a box and the cough in her chest and nine men making their weight for nothing in front of her, and she found it was not despair and not even sorrow. It was arithmetic. It was the truest figure in the book and it was the one she had spent fifteen years trying to make into something else.

The choice she had carried out here in her two hands, the choice she had believed in since she was twelve — save the islands or sell them — was not a choice. There was nothing to save. The birds were dead; the warm water had taken the last of them while she sat over ledgers in Lima; the sky over the rock was the one thing no one who had ever worked here had seen it be, and it would be a hundred years, two hundred, longer than there had been an Amoy, before the cold came back and the shoal came back and a single bird came back to a ledge to begin again the thing that had taken a thousand years to make the mountain that was now red rock under her boots, and no one she loved would live to see a grain of it. And there was nothing to sell. The price was gone, not low, gone, off the slate, and the only number on the table was the Yankee's, and the Yankee's number was real precisely because it was a number for the dregs and the men, the last value to be wrung out of a finished thing, the phosphate in the deep flakes and the bodies it would cost to dig them, and to take it was to enter, in the last honest book the family would ever keep, one more line that said *the men were worth this much, ground down*, which was the line the company had been entering for forty years and calling it trade.

She had come out here to do the sum and decide between two roads. There were not two roads. There had never been two roads. There was one road, with the price hidden until now, and the price was already spent, all of it, the whole of it, the white and the birds and the breath, spent on a mountain that was red rock, by men who had watched it shrink their whole lives and never once let themselves believe the mountain was the thing they were spending — and the only thing left on the table that she could actually do, the only entry left that was not a falsification, was not to save and not to sell. It was to stop.

*

She did not decide it on the rock. She would tell herself, after, that she had not decided it on the rock, that she had carried it home and slept on it and done it cold, because that was the kind of thing she could live having done. But she knew, standing at the face with her hand on her own knee and the box behind her and the empty sky over her, that the deciding was finished, the way her great-uncle had known a thing was finished when the count came out one way only.

She opened the tin box. The Genoese had set it down on a low shelf of the red rock and gone back toward the boat to be out of whatever this was, and she opened it, there, at the face, and took out the three sticks, and held them — the long dark worn one, the filling one, the third with its two notches and the crooked second cut by the shaking hand — and she did not, this time, only hold them. She had promised the man who gave them to her that she would get the current season's un-notched dead down, the ones his hand had been too far gone to cut, the men gone under his rock that he could not keep because *a notch cut by a hand that shakes is a lie about the man*. She had the agent's three lines behind her eyes; she had the wastage; she had, from the overseer who could not keep a sum, the rough of the winter's roll. She took the bent nail she had carried out in her glove for exactly this, because she had known, without letting herself know it, what she would do out here, and she set the point of it to the third stick, below the two crooked notches, and she began, on the bare red rock under the empty sky, with the comb in her hair and the cough in her chest and the bare-faced boy choosing his eye six feet away, to cut the count her great-uncle's hand could no longer cut — the dead he had not been able to keep, taken down at last, clean and square and deep enough to find with a fingertip in the dark, in a hand that did not shake, because she was twenty-two and the white had not yet got into her hands the way it had got into her chest, and there was still, in her, a hand steady enough to keep the book true.

She cut the first one. She did not yet have all the names. She would get them; she would get them off the overseer and the gang before the boat sailed, a village and a trade and a way of standing where there was no name, because the count was so the name would have a place to stand, a thing in the world to be true against, and she had it from the man who began it that you carried what you could and you did not lie about the rest.

The boy at the face had stopped to watch her. She felt it before she saw it — the small change in the rhythm of the work, the way you feel a clock stop in a house — and she looked up, and the boy with the weeping eye, who was Sam's age, who had a burn up his arm and a half-step crook already in his stand, was looking at the woman in the good coat kneeling on the bare rock cutting marks into a stick of pale wood, and he did not know what she was doing and there was no reason on earth he should, and Carmen looked back at him and did not explain it, because there was nothing to say across the rotted sweet air that would mean anything, and she held the third stick and the bent nail in her two steady hands and felt, low and certain under the cough, the small banked weight of the thing she carried now in her own body and had not told Sam and had not told anyone, the thing that would be born by the time the year turned, the next one, the small new account, and she put her hand for one moment flat against herself where it was, and then she set the nail back to the wood, and the boy watched her cut the second notch, square and deep and steady, into the count that would not stop.


## MANIFEST V — The Collapse

### Chapter 30 · Carmen Penhale-Mei
*Mei's island, off Pisco, and a room in Lima · 1880 · guano priced at zero — no figure on any board · working face = bare wet rock*

---

The lease ran out at Lady Day, and she let it.

That was the whole of it, in the end, the thing she had carried home from the rock the year before and slept on and done cold the way she had promised herself she would: there was a date the paper expired, the twenty-fifth of March, the old quarter-day the English notaries kept even here, even now, even for a thing no English notary would touch any longer; and on that morning a man came up the stairs of the dead counting-room over the mole with a printed form and a renewal fee, the consignment house's last clerk, a thin polite boy who plainly did not know why he had been sent, and he set the form on the desk between the two open ledgers and the tin box, and Carmen looked at the renewal fee, which was small, and looked at the box, and did not sign. She thanked him. She said the house would not be continuing the Pisco-coast lease, nor the Chincha north, that the leases might lapse, that the rock could go back to whoever wanted it, which was no one. The boy wrote *not renewed* in his own little book in his own neat hand, the same horizontal nothing the harbour-master chalked where the price had been, and gathered his form, and went down the stairs, and that was a fortune ended — not with a sale, not with a stroke through a figure, but with a date passing and a woman declining to pay to keep a thing she could not have given away.

Sam had sold his own portion in the autumn. She had not stopped him. She had done the sum for him herself, in fact, the night she came back from the island, sat with him at the shut-up table in the good front room where their grandmother had died and where the dust-sheets were on the chairs now, and showed him on her clean ruled sheet that the Yankee's number was real and the leases' number was the harbour-master's stroke, and told him to take it, and he had stared at her across the candle like a man who has braced his whole weight against a door that is suddenly opened from inside.

"You're not coming," he said.

"No."

"You'll let them lapse."

"Yes."

"For nothing. You'll hold them to nothing and then let them go to nothing." He was not angry. He had stopped being able to be angry at her about it somewhere on the island they had never been to together; he only wanted, with the whole of his undamaged heart, for her to be poor and breathing beside him instead of this. "Carmen. You could take half of what I'm taking. He'd give it. He'd pay for the dregs and the deed and never once ask what's under it."

"I know what's under it."

"So does he. So do I. We all know what's under it. It's bare red rock and nine men and a sky with nothing in it, and a Yankee who'll grind the rock for the cotton states and let the men dig it, and he'll do that whether you sell him the deed or whether you let the deed lapse and he picks the island up off the ground next year for the asking, because no one's nailed a flag to it and no one will." He had their father's plainness and he was, she thought, almost certainly right, which was the thing she could not make him understand: that she did not believe she was saving the men, or the rock, or the birds, or any single grain of any of it; that she had been out and stood on it and knew there was nothing left to save and no clean exit and no road that the price had not already been paid down to the bone; and that what was left to her was not a way to make it come out, but only a way to keep one book that did not lie. "He'll have it either way," Sam said. "The only difference is whether we eat off it."

"Yes," Carmen said. "That's the difference."

He went in the spring with his coin in a Lima bank and a passage booked north, to a country that was not bankrupt and a trade that was not dead, and he kissed her at the door of the front room and held her a moment too long for a man who thought he was right, and she felt the lungs in him, the good Lima lungs, the breath that did not catch, and she let him go. She was not sorry. She would tell herself, after, that she had not been sorry, and for once it was true. He was the one of them the family had managed, by an accident of which year you were born in, to raise clean, and she wanted him to go on being clean somewhere the white had never been, and she would carry the rest.

*

She took the boat out again in the autumn, when the child was three months old and could be left a day with the woman who had been her grandmother's cook and had come back to her, not for wages the house could no longer pay, but because the child was the cook's now too, the way a child becomes the property of everyone who has held it; and she took Asher, because she had written to him at the counting-house he kept above the broadcloth warehouse and he had written back two lines in the hand she knew from twenty years of margins — *I will come. I want to count it once more before I stop* — and she had understood that he meant to close his own book the way she meant to close the family's, and that the two of them had agreed, without quite saying it, to do the closing together.

He came aboard the worn Genoese coaster at Pisco with the notebook under his arm, the marbled cover gone soft at the corners, the cover she had watched him start when she was eight and he thirteen on the booby ledges with the war's smoke on the water, and he had a brass thermometer on a line in his coat pocket the way he had carried one for thirty years, and he was forty-eight now and grey and the cough was in him too, the family's one, the quieter one, and he stood at the rail beside her as the coast went by and neither of them said anything about the sea, which was still warm, still wrong, the warm running over the gunwale where she trailed her hand the way it had run the year before, because the warm had not gone with the birds; the warm was the thing that had taken them, and it would be years yet, more years than either of them would have, before the cold came back and made the coast its own again.

"Three degrees over, off the north island, last I read it," Asher said, when the silence had gone on long enough to be comfortable. He said it the way another man says the time. "Still."

"Still," she said.

"It'll hold a while." He looked at the water with the flat naturalist's look he put on the way her grandfather had put on a flat appraising look, a thing to stand behind. "Then it'll go. The cold always comes back. It's older than the birds and older than us and a great deal older than the trade, and it doesn't know there was a trade." He was quiet. "It just won't be soon."

She had stopped, a year ago, being able to be comforted by that and had started being able to bear it, which she had decided was a better thing and possibly the only thing on offer. She watched the empty water go by, mile on mile of it, no dark river pouring off any cliff to any shoal, the surface of it bare to the bend of the world, and she thought about how she had stood at this rail the year before with the comb in her hair and the box at her feet and had not yet known the child was coming, and how she had the child now, three months and a head of dark hair and the dark watching eyes that read as one thing in Lima and another nowhere, and how she had heard, three nights ago, bent over the cradle in the dark, the first small dry catch in the small chest, the cough so faint it might have been a sleeping breath, except that she knew the sound, knew it the way she knew her own, the way her grandfather had known a thing was finished when the count came out one way only; and she had not wept and she had not been able to put it down either, and she had stood over the cradle with her hand flat on her own chest where her own catch sat, and understood that the one line of the account she had never been able to falsify had ruled itself down into the next book before that book was a month old.

She had not told Asher. She would, on the rock. There was a thing she meant to do on the rock, and he was the only one left alive she could do it in front of.

*

The island stood up red in the late forenoon, as it had the year before, ox-blood red, the bare worked stone the color of a sore, the high marks of the old loadings stranded in faint pale rings up a cliff that had nothing left under them, twelve feet and fifteen and twenty, the rings of a felled tree, the draught-scale of a hull that had gone all the way under; and the smell came off it across the water before the boat was beneath the cliff, the sweet finished rotting smell, not the clean caustic burn of the white being made but the thick warm reek of the white that had stopped, the thing the dry cold air had held down for a thousand years let up off the stone by the wrong warm wind; and Carmen stood up in the boat and breathed it and Asher stood beside her and breathed it, and Asher said, in the voice of a man reading a figure he had checked three times and did not want, "It's gone over. The whole of it. Last year it was scraping. There's no one cutting now."

There was no one cutting now.

That was the difference the year had made. The nine men were gone — the Swatow fisher with two fingers off his hand, the old man who had carved a kitchen-god out of rice-soap and set it in a niche of the red rock, the boy Sam's age with the burn up his arm and the eye he had already chosen to lose: gone, off the island, the gang paid off or not paid off into whatever a bankrupt coast did with men it had finished with, because there was no buyer and at last the word had reached even here, even the overseer who could not hold a sum over a forenoon had been made to understand that the dregs were worth the harbour-master's stroke and not a centavo over it, and the chute stood empty, the worn timber landing stood empty, the barrows lay on their sides at the foot of the dead face where the last men had set them down, and the bare red flat that had rung under her boot the year before rang under it again, hollow, the floor of a thing scraped out.

She came up the landing with the tin box, and Asher carried the brass thermometer to the water's edge out of an old habit he did not bother to explain, and read it, and wrote the figure in the notebook without comment, and came back. And they stood together on the bare rock where the white mountain had stood, the two of them, the conscience and the bird-counter, the two children of the braid who had been planted side by side on these same workings when they were eight and thirteen so that the family's love would have somewhere to go when the old men died, and who had outlived the old men and the trade and the mountain and were the last two of the whole apparatus left standing, here, on the spent principal, under the empty sky.

"How many," she said. She did not have to say of what.

He opened the notebook to the last ruled page. She had seen the column before, in the dark, in his counting-house, the descent of it: twelve million when he was a boy on the ledges; six the year the German came and counted and was not heard; three and a half the spring after; three, and falling, the spring of the warm water; and then the year of the great dying that he had not been able to count because there was no counting it, the beaches of the lesser islands lined with the starved birds, the sweet rot of them carrying out to sea, the column of his book broken off because the thing it counted had broken off.

"I've been out to all of them this year," he said. "Macabí. The Guañapes. Here. I do a dawn off each, the square, the way I've always done it." He was not looking at her. He was looking at the cliff, the bare red ledges going up empty into the empty light, the booby shelves and the cormorant ledges where the colony had nested wing to wing in a darkness that made the cliff itself look as if it moved. "Off the Chincha north I counted forty. Off Macabí, eleven. Here—" He stopped. He had the rigor in him still, the thing that had made him a man of his own making, the discipline that would not let him round a figure up to mean more than it was or down to mean less. "Here I have not counted one. In three dawns. There was a single guanay yesterday morning, low over the water to the north, and I had the glass on it the whole way and it did not come to the rock, it went on south, and I could not in honesty enter it against this island, because the count is for the birds you have *seen* on the ground you are counting, not the ones you suppose, and I have not seen one on this ground." He closed the book on his thumb. "So. None. Here, this year, none."

None.

She stood with it. She had a hundred and thirty notches in the tin box, the count of the dead her grandfather had begun on this island thirty years gone and she had carried on with her own steady hand, and she had Asher's broken column of the birds, the twelve million come down to none, and she understood, standing on the red rock between the two failed counts, that they had always been the one account, the way her grandfather had told her at the very end in the two rooms behind the cathedral when she had not believed him, that the notches and the tons had always been the same book kept twice, once in the white going out and once in the men going under the white; except that now she saw it was kept three times, the third in the birds, the white going out and the men going under and the birds going dark, three columns of the same single subtraction, and that no one had ever been meant to read them together because read together they did not come out to a fortune. They came out to a rock with nothing on it.

*

She set the box down on the low shelf of red stone where the Genoese had set it the year before, and she opened it, and she took the things out one at a time and laid them on the rock in the cold light, because she had brought them all out here for a reason she had not been able to give Sam and could give Asher only by doing it.

She took out the three tally-sticks — the long dark worn one, the grip of it gone round and black from a hand that had cut its first notch on this island in 1849 and was dust now; the second, filling; the third, with its few notches and the crooked second cut by the shaking hand that she had sworn never to sand smooth — and the season's dead were on the third now in her own square clean cuts below the crooked one, the nine men's count she had taken down off the overseer and the barracks and the agent's three lines, the Swatow fisher and the old man and the boy and the rest, every one of them carried home on the wood from the year before so that they would not be lost, so the names would have a place to stand. She took out the folded paper with the eight on it, gone soft as cloth, the fraudulent contract her grandfather could not read and had kept against his ribs for forty years. And she took out the deed, both leases in the one packet, the Pisco-coast stump and the Chincha north, that had lapsed at Lady Day and were worth precisely the stroke the harbour-master chalked, and were hers, and were no one's.

And she took out the last thing, which she had carried out wrapped separately in oiled cloth, and which was not from the box at all but from the dead counting-room, off the desk where it had lain beside the two ledgers all year: a single sheet of foolscap, very old, the ink gone brown, a bill of lading in a hand she had never seen written but had read a hundred times in the framed copy on the counting-room wall — *Petrel*, of Bristol, master so-and-so, one hundred and forty tons of Peruvian guano shipped in good order, the Chincha Islands, the year eighteen hundred and forty-nine — the first manifest, the very first, that her grandfather Penhale had written and signed on the beach of these islands the morning he came ashore and began to count the mountain down, the manifest that had been kept and annotated and carried for thirty years down through the house, the top of an account that ran out at the harbour-master's stroke. A hundred and forty tons, at nine pounds, climbing to ten. The number at the top of the figure that had grown until it ate the mountain.

She had brought the little brass hand-scale too. It was the household one, the one from the kitchen the cook weighed flour on, a beam and two small pans and a set of brass weights in a felt-lined box, because there was no scale-house on the island any more, the great beam was gone with the company, and she had wanted, she had needed, a beam.

She set the *Petrel* manifest in one pan.

She had thought about what to put in the other. She had thought she would weigh it against the last manifest, the final bill of lading the house ever wrote, the Macabí dregs to the Hamburg house that went bankrupt before the cargo rotted at anchor, the figure entered and struck and written *protested* in her aunt's hand, and she had brought that too. But standing on the rock she found that the last manifest weighed the same as the first — a sheet of foolscap, a few drachms of paper and brown ink, nothing, the weight of a thing that was worth a fortune and the weight of a thing that was worth nothing being, on a kitchen beam, exactly equal, paper against paper, the pans level. That was the joke at the bottom of it, the thing her grandfather Penhale had spent forty years refusing to weigh: that the manifest had never had any weight of its own at all. It had only ever borrowed it — from the white that went down the chutes, and the white had borrowed it from the birds, and the birds had borrowed it from the cold sea, a thousand years of herring and droppings dried under a dry sky, and the whole towering figure at the top of the *Petrel* sheet, the figure that had bought the Lima house and the cathedral pew and Inés's seat at the table and the sea-green dress they had held a child of six up to the working face to admire, had been resting the whole time on a thing that breathed and could stop, and had stopped.

So she did not weigh the first manifest against the last. She took the tally-sticks instead, the three of them, the count of the dead, and she set them in the second pan.

They weighed more than the manifest. Hard pale wood against thin old paper — of course they weighed more; it was not even close; the beam tipped and the pan with the sticks went down and rang on the brass arm and the *Petrel* sheet rode up light and useless, and she stood and looked at it, the bill of lading for the first hundred and forty tons of the fortune cocked up high and weightless above the count of the men it had cost, on a beam, on the bare rock, under the empty sky, and there was no figure that made them equal and there never had been, and that — the man with the sticks had told her, and she had not believed him, and now she stood on the spot where he had cut the first notch and believed him entirely — was the only honest entry the books would ever hold. *There is no unit. You cannot pay it back. You cannot even weigh it.* But she could. She had. She had weighed it, on a kitchen beam, and it did not balance, and the not-balancing was the truth, the first true thing the house had ever set down, the only line in four decades of the family's books that no one had cooked.

Asher had watched her do it without a word. He understood it; he was the only one who could have; he had been keeping the same kind of beam in a marbled notebook for thirty years, the birds in one pan and nothing in the other.

"Leave them," he said quietly. Not the sticks — he meant the manifests, the paper. "Leave the paper here. Let the rock have it."

But she did not leave the paper, because the paper was the proof, and she had not come out here to make a gesture the rock would dissolve in a season; she had come to keep a book. She put the *Petrel* manifest back in its oiled cloth and the last manifest with it and the deed that had lapsed, and she put the sticks back in the tin box on top of them, and she shut the box, the way her grandfather had taught her to keep a thing — close enough to hold, not close enough to burn — and she stood up on the bare red rock with the box under her arm and the comb in her hair, the plain dark foreign comb with the plum-flowers worn smooth that her mother had carried out of Amoy on the morning the broker took her and that weighed less than the dust on a banknote and was the whole of a person's home, and she looked at the empty cliff one more time before they went down to the boat.

*

The bird came while they were at the landing.

She did not see it first; Asher did, because Asher had spent his life seeing them; he made a small sound, not a word, the sound a man makes who has stopped expecting a thing and has it anyway, and his hand came up, and she followed it, and there, low over the water off the north shoulder where the column had poured for a thousand years and had not poured in three years, a single bird came in off the sea.

A guanay. Black, white-breasted, the bare red skin at the face just visible as it banked, a fast low pursuit-diver flying the way they flew, close to the water, beating and gliding, beating and gliding, and it came in off the empty sea toward the empty cliff, and Asher had the glass up and on it and did not breathe, and Carmen stood with the box under her arm and the child three months old in Lima with the cough already in its chest and watched the one bird come, and it did not go on south this time. It came to the rock. It went up the face in a long slant and it landed on a bare red ledge twenty feet up the cliff, a ledge that had held a nest wing to wing with ten thousand others in the year the mountain was a hundred and eighty feet high, and it stood there, one bird on the bare wet rock, and folded its wings, and did not do anything at all that a man could put in a book. It did not build. It did not call. There was nothing for it to build with and no one to call to. It only stood, a single small black-and-white weight on the stone, a few ounces of the living thing the whole towering fortune had been made of and spent and could not buy back, on a ledge that might, in a hundred years, in two hundred, longer than there had been an Amoy or a Cornwall, when the cold came back that did not know there had been a trade, hold a colony again and begin again the slow thousand-year work of making the mountain that the family had taken down to bare rock in thirty.

Asher wrote it in the notebook. She watched him do it — date, place, count, the column under *Remarks* — and she saw his hand stop over the count, the discipline catching even now, and she knew what he was weighing: that he had said *none* and meant it and entered nothing, and that here was one, seen, on the ground he was counting, and that the rule was the rule.

"One," he said, and wrote it. "Here. Today. One."

She put her hand for a moment flat against herself, against the place where the next account had been, the small new book three months ruled now and the catch already in its breath, and she did not say anything across the rotted sweet air, because there was nothing to say that would mean anything and because she had learned that from the man who began the count, the going-most-silent when you were most moved; and she stood on the bare red rock that had been a white mountain, with the box of the dead under her arm and the comb of the lost home in her hair and the one bird on the ledge above her and the child of the cough waiting in Lima, and she watched the bird, and she did not write it down, because it was not hers to write; it was Asher's; she kept the other count, the one that did not balance, the one she would carry home and keep true with a steady hand until the white got into her hands the way it had got into her chest, and then pass on, to the child, who carried it already, in the breath, before it could hold a pen.

The bird stood on the ledge. The cold sea ran warm under the boat. Asher closed the notebook on the single figure, and they went down to the water, and she carried the weight of it home.

---

*One bird stands on the wet rock where a mountain stood. It came in off a sea the color of pewter, low over the water, the way they have always come, and it found the cliff and went up it and stopped on a ledge twenty feet above the surf, on the bare red stone, and folded its wings. It is a guanay. It weighs a few ounces. There is no nest under it and there is none beside it; the ledge it stands on held ten thousand once, wing to wing, in a darkness that moved; the count off this rock at this dawn was none, and now it is one. It does not know the mountain was here. It does not know it is the first of anything, or the last, or that under its feet are forty feet of red iron sores where a hundred and eighty feet of white stood when the first ships came; it cannot read the pale rings stranded up the cliff that are the years, and would not be the thing it is if it could. It stands where the cold water is and the cold water is wrong, three degrees over, and will be wrong for years; and then, on a clock that does not keep the quarter-days, longer than there has been an Amoy or a Cornwall or a price chalked on a board, the cold will come back that does not know there was a trade, and the shoal will come back into the cold, and the birds will come back to the shoal, one and then a hundred and then the darkness that moves; and the white will begin again, a grain at a time, dropped out of the sky, the slow thousand-year making of a weight no one alive will ever lift or sell or carry. One bird stands on the wet rock. It is the whole of it, beginning. It does not look up.*
